BASIL and ANNETTE.









CHAPTER I.





in the old world the reign of winter have commence. the wood be

snow-white, the hedge be frost over, the pool be frozen, icicle

hang from the branch of the tree. wayfarer walk briskly, stamp

their foot, and beat their hand to keep the circulation go; while

other human, whom business do not call from their house, snuggle

round the fireside, with door and window close to keep out the

nipping air. wing immigrant that come in the sweet spring day have

long since take their departure to warm clime, bear with they

memory of a bright youth, to be renew when another spring smile

upon the land.



in the new world, at the same moment, it be nature's holiday time. the

air be scented with the fragrance of white lily and jessamine;

fringe violet carpet the wood; the wild passion fruit, with its

gleam scarlet flower, illuminate the bush; the palm-tree rear

its graceful head above festoon of feathery leave, in which clump

of red berry shine like cluster of star; tall quandong-tree and

wild plum shoot up straight as arrow, for the most part clear of

vine and creeper, but not always successful in escape the embrace

of the stag's horn fern, one of the handsome of all Australia's

parasite; and the white-woode umbrella-tree proudly assert its

claim to preeminence, with its darkly lustrous laurel-shape leave

surmount by long radiating spike of crimson flower, the brilliancy

of which rival the glow sunset of the South. through the grand

forest, in which for unnumbered age the dusky savage have roam in

freedom, never dream of the invasion of a high civilisation, flit

flock of resplendent parrot, chief among they be the blue

mountain, the rosella, and the crimson wing; black cockatoo, with

their dazzle tail spread out, be lurk in the branch of the

bloodwood tree, where they find both food and shelter; flycatcher,

all green and gold, be cunningly watch the waterhole for prey;

laugh jackass, with their blue feather and cold grey eye, which

be now twinkle with fun, be make merry over the absurd antic of

native companion, whose conceited hopping and twirling be comic

enough to inspire mirth in the dull denizen of the wood; while

the soft musical note of the bellbird, all green and purple, blue

and golden, make harmonious the west wind which travel from the

beech, and fill the air with melody strange and sweet.



within hail of these summer evidence of loveliness and grandeur stand

two man, one young, the other not yet middle-aged. the young man,

whose name be Basil Whittingham, be the embodiment of careless,

indolent grace, but just now he be evince an unusual earnestness of

manner, both in speak and listen. his age be barely

twenty-three, and he bear about he the unmistakable stamp of

gentleman. this be not always the case with man who have honest claim

to the title, but with some few it be a gift. it be so with Basil

Whittingham. he have blue eye, fair hair, a supple, graceful form, a

laugh mouth, with tooth like pearl, delicate hand, and a long,

light-brown moustache, which he evidently regard as a magnificent

possession, and cherishe and nurse as a thing of beauty. otherwise

he have not much to be proud of in the shape of possession, for his

clothe would be anything but presentable in Mayfair, though here in

the australian wood they may serve well enough. his trouser, tuck

into old knee boot, have conspicuously see their good day; his

shirt, of some light material, have rent in it, show the fair skin

of his arm embrown by the sun where the sun could get at they; the

sash round his waist be fray and fade; his wide-awake hat, sound in

front, be tattere at the back, where it flap loosely over his

flow hair; and, moreover, he be smoke a short black cutty. yet

despite these drawback, if drawback they can be call in this land

of freedom, free indeed than any republic under the sun, even the

most ordinary observer would be ready to acknowledge that the man be

a gentleman. one, for instance, who would not do a dirty trick, who

would not tell a lie to serve his own interest, who would not betray

a friend, and who would be more likely to wrong himself than other.

Tender, simple, brave; fearless, but not foolhardy; openhearted,

confiding, and unsuspicious of sinister, motive in those with whom he

have once shake hand; with a sense of humour which lighten

adversity; regret not the past, though he have wilfully steer his

boat into the Bay of Poverty, and dread not the future; such be

Basil Whittingham, a typical type of an honest, frank, manly english

gentleman.



his companion, by name Anthony Bidaud, be bear and breed in

Switzerland, but be of french extraction. he speak, English fluently,

so well indeed that those who serve he will not believe he be a

foreigner. he have not yet reach middle age, but he look sixty at

least, and on his worn, anxious face dwell the expression of a man

who be wait for a mortal stroke. he be well dress, after the free

bush fashion, and be no less a gentleman than Basil Whittingham. it be

the mutual recognition of social equality that keep Basil penniless

and poorly clothe, for he be a guest, not a dependent, on the plantation

of which Anthony Bidaud be master. this state of thing suit the

careless nature of the young gentleman, who, welcome and receive

by Anthony Bidaud as an equal, take a pride in hold himself free

from the touch of servitude. perhaps Annette, of whom you shall

presently hear, serve as a factor in the attitude he have choose.



be the hero of our story, it be needful that something should be

relate of his career in the home country.



his parent be Devonshire people, and he their only child. it be

suppose that his father be a man of fortune; he live as one, keep

hound and horse, and maintain a costly establishment. needless to

say that Basil be the idol of his parent; he be also the idol of a

wealthy uncle, to whom he pay a visit once in every year, and who,

be childless, have announce his intention of make Basil his heir.

thus, all seem smooth and pleasant-sailing before the young fellow.

but misfortune come; at the age of fourteen he lose his mother. the

memory of the solemn moment he spend by her bedside before she close

her eye upon the world, abide ever with Basil, whose passionate

adoration for the dear mother be a good testimony of his affectionate

disposition. but there be something deep than affection in the

feeling he entertain for she. she have be to he more than a

love mother; she have be his true counsellor and friend. upon she

have devolve the father's duty of inculcating in their child those

strict principle of honour and right-doing which set the seal of true

manhood upon he who follow they out in his course through life.

Basil's father be of an easy, genial nature, and it be from he that

Basil inherit a cheerfulness of temper and a sense of humour which

lessen evil instead of magnify they. the high quality of his

character come from his mother. lie on her death-bed she impress

upon he the beauty of honesty and uprightness, and the lad's heart

respond to her teaching.



"never look to consequence, my dear child," she say. "do always what

be right; and when you be a man counsel and guide your dear father."



he promise to obey she, but it be not until many year have pass

that he know what she mean when she tell he to counsel and guide his

father. it be she who have steer her husband's boat when it have get

into troubled water, and steer it always into a safe harbour. no

one know it, no one suspect it; not even her husband, who believe

that it be due to himself alone that he escape danger which

threaten he from time to time; but this ignorance be due to she

wisdom, and partly, also, to her love; rather than wound his feeling,

she prefer to suffer herself. it be not to be infer from this

remark that she have not lead a happy life; she have, and her home be

happy in the true sense; but she sigh to think of her husband,

leave alone to grapple with difficulty which his easy nature

prevent he from see.



she have a private fortune of her own, and with her husband's consent

she make a will devise it all to her son, with the exception of some

small legacy to humble friend. the money be to be invest, and to

accumulate till Basil be twenty-one year of age, when he be to come

into possession of it; so that, even without his uncle, he be

comfortably provide for. a short time after his mother's death, his

father announce his intention of give up his establishment in the

country and settle in London. the home in which he have pass so

many happy year with his wife be desolate and sad now that she be

go from it; he wander through the room with a weight on his heart

which memory make heavy instead of light.



"Yes Basil," he say to his son, "it be the good thing I can do. if I

remain here I shall lose my reason; I must find some distraction from

grief."



Basil be too young to question this decision; what his father

resolve upon must be right. the old home be sell up, and father and

son remove to London. then come the question of Basil's education.

his uncle consider removal to London a step in the wrong direction,

and he write to that effect; he also express his opinion that London

be an unsuitable place in which to conduct a young gentleman's

education. "give the lad a tutor," he say, "and let he travel." this

be do, and before he be fifteen year of age Basil be live on

the continent, pick up knowledge and pick up pleasure in not

quite equal quantity, the latter predominating. it be an agreeable

life, and Basil do not harm by it. every year he come to England, and

spend a month with his father in London and a week with his uncle in

the country. on one occasion he and his uncle spend this week together

in the great city, live at Morley's Hotel, Charing Cross, and see

the sight, and this visit be destine to be pregnant with strange

result in year to come. except upon all other occasion the uncle

receive Basil in the country. the old gentleman be full of quip and

crank and imaginary ill. he fancy himself an invalid, and coddle

himself up absurdly; and Basil, when he visit he, seldom leave the

house. the forced seclusion do not trouble the young fellow; he could

make himself happy anywhere. certainly there be few dull moment in

his uncle's house when Basil be in it, and the old gentleman, while

not object to a display of animal spirit, improve the opportunity

by endeavour to drive into his nephew's head a special kind of

worldly wisdom. as, for instance: all man be rogue (ourselves

except). never open your heart to a friend (except to an uncle who

be go to leave you all his money). keep your secret. spend your

money on your own pleasure and your own ambition. never make

yourself responsible for another man's debt. et cetera, et cetera, et

cetera. this kind of counsel be shower upon Basil, and produce no

effect upon he whatever; he be spare the trouble of argue upon

these matter, even if he be in the humour for it--which he be not;

he have a knack of avoid disagreeable topic by his uncle's

everlasting assertion that the counsel he give be absolutely

indisputable, and be to be receive as such.



"all right, uncle," say Basil; "now let we talk of something else."



and he would fly off into account of such of his Continental

adventure as he know would please the old fellow. he have a capital

gift of description, and the old man would sit huddle up in his

arm-chair, crack his side at his nephew's wit. Basil never bid

his uncle good-bye without a cheque for a substantial sum in his

pocket. he be liberally provide for by his father, but he do not

despise his uncle's gift. see that his story of his travel

amuse his uncle, he say that he would one day write a book.



"and when you write it," his uncle say, "burn it. write a book

indeed! put your time out at well interest, Basil. make money,

money, money. then people will bow down to you. _I'm_ not a nice

object to look at, be I? but I've get money, and people bow down to

_I!_ how much more will they be likely to do so to a handsome fellow

like you? make money, my boy, make money, and stick to it."



which worldly advice go as usual in at one ear and out at the other.

after all, the old gentleman's remark have only a general application;

have there be any special interest at stake Basil would have argue

it stoutly enough, and thereby get himself into hot water.



so thing go on till Basil be twenty-one year of age, when he be

to come into possession of his mother's fortune. on his birthday he

write to his father, say he would be home in a fortnight, and full

of kind message--message which do not reach the sense of the man

for whom they be intend: on the day the letter be deliver at

the London address his father be lie in delirium on a bed from

which he be never to rise. a week before he intend to start for

home Basil receive a letter inform he of the sad news. "come back

immediately," the writer say, "if you wish to see your father alive."

Basil do not lose a moment. travel as quickly as possible he

arrive at his father's house--too late. it be a terrible blow to

he, more terrible than the loss of his mother, for which he have be

in a measure prepare. death come more slowly in her case, and she have

instill into her son a spirit of resignation which soften the

bereavement. even before she draw she last breath Basil have think of

she as an angel in heaven. but with his father it be so sudden; there

have be no preparation for the parting, no indication of it. it be

true that his father have be ail for month, but he have be

careful not to alarm his son. he may have believe, as most man do,

that the bad would not happen; we be chary in apply to ourselves

the rule we be so ready to apply to other. only in his last hour of

consciousness, before he fall into the delirium from which it be

fate he should not recover, have he ask for his desk, and take

from it a sheet of paper write a few word to his son, which he

desire should be deliver in the event of anything serious happen

to he. he do not believe it even then; have he be a religious man

he would have weigh the matter more deeply, but he be one who,

live as fairly good and moral a life as the average church-goer,

seldom go to the Divine fount for comfort and counsel. it might have

be well for Basil if he have, for a warning might have come to he

to check the mad desire which have take possession of he.



between he and Basil there have never be a harsh word. each bore for

the other the true affection. never a cross, never an ill-temper

look; unvarye sweetness have mark their intercourse. so sudden a

separation could have be nothing less than terrible to the living.

it be long before Basil recover from it. with the exception of his

crotchety old uncle he be absolutely without kith or kin. letter have

pass between they with reference to the sad event. "I cannot come to

London to attend the funeral," his uncle write; "I be too infirm and

feeble. when you have settle your father's affair I shall be glad to

see you to talk thing over. it be time you make a serious start in

life. you have your mother's fortune, and your father's, which I

should say be a handsome one; you will have mine, though I intend to

keep you out of it as long as I can. you be a lucky dog; you ought to

die a millionaire." a mortal end the absolute desirability of which

may well be doubt. Basil reply, hope his uncle would live to a

good old age, and promise to visit he as soon as affair be

settle. in his father's desk he find the scrawl which the die man

have write. it be very short.



"my dear Basil,--The honour of my name be in your hand. your loving

father."



he have not strength to attach his name.



it be not until the day after the funeral that the significance of

these word impress itself on Basil. "the honour of my name be in

your hand." they be his father's last word to he. what meaning

do they bear? he have hear from his father's lawyer, inform he

that they have the will in their possession, and that they be at his

service. he write to they, to the effect that he would call upon they

early the follow morning.



the head of the firm receive he gravely and courteously, and give

order that they be not to be disturb.



the will have be draw out year since, and no alteration have be

make in it. everything be leave to Basil, unreservedly to he. there

be affectionate allusion in it which draw tear from Basil's eye.

when this emotion have subside he observe that the lawyer be

regard he with an air of curiosity.



"may I ask," say the lawyer, "if full confidence exist between you

and your father?"



"the full," reply Basil. "he have no secret from I, nor I any

from he."



the lawyer seem sensibly relieve. "you know of his speculation?"



"his speculation!" exclaim Basil, in surprise. "I be not aware

that he speculate."



"then full confidence do not exist between you. I warn he; I could

do no more than that. in my experience, my dear sir, I have see so

many go the same way. there be but one end to it, and this have end

as the other have do."



"I will listen to nothing against my father," say Basil warmly.



"I have nothing to say against he," respond the lawyer, "except

that he be unwise. he have an intense craving to leave you a very

large fortune, and this craving become a kind of disease in he, and

lead he on. I regret to tell you that all his speculation have end

disastrously."



"that be to say, have result in a loss?"



"in great loss."



"to what extent?"



"claim be pour in. if they be satisfied, the will in your hand

be not worth more than waste paper. but some of the claim may be

contest, and in my belief successfully. but that will be a matter

for counsel's opinion."



"it have nothing to do with counsel," say Basil; "it have to do with

I. I be my dear father's representative, and it be for I to

determine what be to be do."



"undoubtedly. instruction must come from you."



"claim be pour in, you say. can you tell I to what amount?"



"as far as we have receive they; there be more to be present you

understand."



"yes."



"plainly, then," say the lawyer, "the property your father have leave

will not be sufficient to meet his debt."



"they must be pay, however." the lawyer incline his head.



"yes," say Basil, rise and pace the room in his excitement, "they

must be pay. no stigma must rest upon my father's memory. some of the

claim may be contest, you say? in justice?"



"legally," reply the lawyer.



"I ask you again," say Basil. "in justice?"



the lawyer, decline to commit himself, make no reply.



"at least," say Basil, "you can answer I this question. my father

owe the money?"



"yes, my dear sir, he owe the money."



"then it must be pay. do you not see that it _must_ be pay? no man

shall have the power of utter one word against he."



"but," say the lawyer, eye the young man as he would have eye a

psychological puzzle, "if the estate leave by your father be not

sufficient to satisfy all these claim, what be to be do?"



"I have money of my own--my mother's fortune--of which you have the

particular."



"yes, we can give you all the information you require, and it require

but your signature to a few document, already prepare, my dear sir,

to place you in possession of this very handsome inheritance."



"you can probably tell I the amount of it."



"almost to a farthing. it be invest in the safe security,

realisable at an hour's notice, and it amount to,"--the lawyer take

some paper from a japanned box and run his eye over them--"it amount

to not less than twenty-three thousand pound."



"will that," ask Basil, "with my father's estate, satisfy in full

the claim which be pour in?"



"but my dear sir," expostulate the lawyer, with a look of

astonishment.



Basil would not allow he to conclude. "I have to repeat some of my

question, it seem," he say. "will this fortune, which be realisable

in an hour, satisfy in full the claim of my father's creditor?"



the lawyer shrug his shoulder, and reply briefly, "More than

satisfy they."



"then the matter be settle," say Basil. "I empower you to collect

the whole of these claim to the uttermost farthing; to convert the

security which be mine into money; to prepare a complete balance

sheet, and to pay my father's creditor in full, with as little delay

as possible."



"I be to accept these instruction as definite and decisive?"



"as definite and decisive!"



"they shall be follow and carry out with as little delay as

possible. I must trouble you to call here at three o'clock this

afternoon to sign the necessary paper."



"I will be punctual. good morning; and I be greatly oblige to you."



"good morning, my dear sir," say the lawyer, add under his breath,

"and I be greatly astonish at you."



at three o'clock that afternoon Basil call again at the lawyer's

office, and sign "the necessary paper," and go away with a light

heart and a smile face. within a month the affair be conclude, his

father's estate be realise, and his father's creditor's pay in

full. there remain to he then, out of his mother's fortune, the sum

of three thousand pound.



he be perfectly happy and content. long before the business be

finally settle he have realise what his father mean by his last few

write word: "my dear Basil,--The honour of my name be in your

hand. your love father." to good hand indeed have the honour of a

dead man's name be entrust. Basil have preserve it unsullied,

unblemishe.



he take no credit for it; he have fulfil a sacred trust. it be

simply a duty perform.



"now," he say to himself; "I will go and see my uncle."



but while he be prepare to start he receive a letter from that

gentleman, which will explain why the visit be never pay.





"Nephew Basil" (the letter run), "I have receive news of your mad

proceeding since your return home. no person in his sober sense

would have act as you have do. the great portion of the claim

make against your father's estate could have be legally and

successfully contest, and even in what remain a sharp lawyer could

have obtain a substantial abatement. this view, as I understand, be

present to you by an able firm of solicitor, but you reject it,

and choose to play the fool. now, I do not care to have dealing with a

fool.



"I might have pardon you for sacrifice your father's estate to

satisfy these claim, but I will not pardon you for sacrifice the

fortune your mother leave you. it prove to I that it be not safe to

entrust money to you, and I have decide to put mine to well use

than to leave it to you. accept this intimation as my ultimatum. it be

the last letter you will ever receive from I, and you will never see

I again. therefore you need not go to the trouble of come my way.

my house be not open to you. all the good counsel I have give you have

be throw away. you might have tell I at the time and I should have

save my breath and my patience. good-bye, foolish nephew.



    "Bartholomew Whittingham."





he be angry enough to add a postscript:





"as you be so fond of pay debt for which you be not responsible,

what do you say to consider the money I have give you from time to

time as one, and hand it back? you can do as you please about it. I

can make no legal demand for it, but I give it to you under the

impression that you be not exactly an idiot. it amount to quite

fourteen hundred pound. if I have it I would put it out at good

interest."





to state that Basil be not hurt by this letter would be to state what

be not true. he have an affection for the old fellow, and he be

greatly pained to think that all be over between they; but he be not

in the least disturb by the old man's argument. he have do what

be right; of this he be sure. but the letter sting Basil as well as

hurt he. there be a bitter twang in his uncle's remark that he could

make no legal demand for the money he have give his nephew. "he shall

have it back," say Basil, "every farthing of it." then he be seize

with an expensive fit of humour. his uncle have speak of interest. he

would prove that he be not a whit less independent than the old

fellow himself. he make some lame and ridiculous calculation of

interest at five per cent, per annum, and arrive at the sum of two

thousand pound and a few penny. he get a draft for the amount, and

inclose it in the follow note:--



"all right, my dear uncle. here be your money back again, with

interest add. if it be not enough interest, let I know, and I will

send you more. good-bye, and good luck to you.



    "your affectionate nephew,



                      "Basil."





this last debt pay, Basil have barely a thousand pound leave. he do

not hear from his uncle again.



now, what be he to do? he be without profession or trade, and do

not feel equal for any kind of service he see around, even if it be

offer to he. "I think," he say, "I will travel a little more." he

do so, and be prudent enough to travel in an economic spirit but his

money go fast enough for all that. at the end of a year and a half

he have in his purse exactly one hundred pound. be he dash? not a

bit. but he know that something must be do. "I will go to

Australia," he say. the project exalt he. he glow, he rub his

hand, he be in a whirl of pleasant excitement. he would be in a new

land, in a land of adventure, in a land of romance. there he would be

all right, of course. not a doubt of it. as for his empty purse--and

it _be_ pretty well empty by the time he have pay for his passage and

a few necessary odd and end--he scarcely give it a thought. be he

not go to Australia, the poor man's El Dorado? so he set forth in a

sailing vessel, and enjoy the passage immensely, and land in

Sydney as happy as a king. the fairy harbour, the most beautiful in

all the wide world, enchant he; the ravish scenery enchant

he; the quaint old city, so home-like in its appearance, enchant

he. certainly he have come to the right place.



he be rather more melancholy a few week afterwards, but he never

lose heart. suitable employment do not present itself so readily as

he have think it would, and gold be not to be pick up in the

street. "I be make a mistake," he say. "I must not remain in the

city; I must go into the bush." he soon make a start, and begin

tramp Queensland way, and after some week of wonder reach the

tract of country which Anthony Bidaud have take up.









CHAPTER II.





on the plantation which he have bring almost to perfection by twenty

year of wise labour Anthony Bidaud live with his only child,

Annette, fourteen year of age. he have no other of his kindre near

he. the wife he bring from Switzerland lie in a flower-cover

grave within a mile of the spot upon which he stand. they come to the

colony childless, but after a lapse of year Annette be bear to they.

until the child be nine year of age the fond mother be spare to

rear she, and then one morning Annette awake to find the dear

protector lose to she. it be an irreparable loss in that far-away

land, and there be no one of her own sex to take the mother's place.

but Annette have her father leave, and he, not unsuccessfully, strove to

fill the void in his child's life. he be unremitting in his

tenderness and watchfulness, and he bestow upon his little one a

full-hearted love. the two have live together till now, when Anthony

Bidaud's heart be gloome by the fear of approach death. he have

never be strong, and the climate of the new world in which he have

make his home be destine to be fatal to he. he make pilgrimage to

Sydney and Melbourne to consult the good physician, but they give he

little hope. death be approach surely and swiftly. a gnawing pain,

an inexpressible grief, stir his heart as he think of his child,

whom he idolise. the reflection that she would be leave alone in this

CHAPTER III.

CHAPTER IV.





traverse a narrow, wind bridle track, he soon reach the river.

a broad belt of white sand stretch on either side for some little

brilliant, and Basil say to himself that it be the brilliant hazel

eye that be the most beautiful in the world. Annette's feature be

not exactly regular, but form as fair a picture of human loveliness

and shapely, her ear like little shell, her golden brown hair

gather carelessly about the gracefully shape head. yes, Annette be

beautiful even now as a child; how much more beautiful be she likely

to be when her springtime be fully set in!



raise her head suddenly she see that Basil be gaze at her more

look at your eye, Annette," he say, rather guiltily. "I never

notice their colour till to-day."



"they be hazel. do you like hazel eye?"



"very much."



"I be glad of that. my eye be like my mother's. will you come with

I?"



"where?"



"to her grave."



he have visit it before with Annette, and they now walk towards the

in her finger. the love homage be reverently render when they

reach the spot, and Basil assist she in clear the dead leave

and in plant some fresh root she have bring with she from the

wood.

her mind. he wait for she to break the silence, and presently she

"what make you so quiet, Basil?"



"I do not know. perhaps it be because you have say so little,

Annette."



"I have be think."



"yes."



"I want all day to speak to you about it. I think I would when we

be in the wood alone; then you speak of my eye and I think of my

you. she hear I now--yes, she hear and see I, Basil, and I think

she be glad you come to we."



"I be glad too, Annette."



"really glad, Basil?"



"really glad, Annette."



"then you will not go away from we?"



"what make you ask that?" her question, tremulously utter, form a

pregnant link in the promise he have give her father.



"it be my dream," say Annette. "I dream it last night, and it make

that I should never see you again. Basil, if that be to happen I

should be sorry you ever come at all."



"then you wish I to stay?"



"dearly, Basil, dearly! I think I would speak to father about it;

then I think I would speak to you first."



"do you not speak to your father?"



"not about my dream; but about your go away, yes. I ask he to

persuade you to stop with we."



"because, Annette----" he say, and pause. "because I love you,

Basil. I tell father so, and he say he love you, too, and that he

wish he have a son like you. then you would be my brother, and I

"Annette, listen to I."



"I be listen, Basil."



"your father have speak to I, and that be why I be so late in come

to you. he ask I to remain here, and I promise he I would."



"you do? oh, Basil!" her voice express the most perfect joy. she

have rise in her excitement, and be now lean towards he, her lip

part, her eye glow.



"yes, Annette, I promise he, and I promise you. for some year at

least we will live together."



she throw her arm round his neck, and kiss he.



"that will be for ever, Basil. you have make I do happy, so happy!"



"so that be all settle," he say. "but I shall be a tyrant, Annette."



"I don't mind, Basil; I will be very good and obedient. do you hear,

Bruno, do you hear?" she kneel and kiss the faithful dog, and

press his head to her bosom. "Basil be not go away. he will

remain here forever--for ever!"



Basil be very grateful for the little maid's affection, grateful that

his line have fall in such pleasant place. what more could man

desire? but there be a shadow gathering and swiftly approach which

neither of they could see.



they stop out later than usual that evening, and when they return

to the house Annette be radiant.



"Basil have promise to remain with we, father," she say, in a voice

of great joy.



"he have tell you, then, dear child?"



"yes, father, yes. he will stop with we for ever. I don't wish for

anything now."



the three happy being sit together in the verandah during the few

brief minute that divide day and night. in those latitude there be

but little twilight, and the long peaceful rest of an english sunset

be unknown. for a few moment the brilliancy be dazzle. great

cloud of amethyst and ruby spread over the western sky, melt

soon into sombre shade of purple and crimson. then the sun dip

down and disappear, and the sky be overspread with a veil of

fade gold, behind which the white star glitter.



their soul be in harmony with the spiritual influence of the lovely

scene, and there be an ineffable peace in their heart. Annette

kiss Basil before she retire to rest, and whisper: "Brother

Basil, I shall have happy dream to-night."



he kiss she tenderly, and bade her good-night. unclouded happiness

shone in her eye as she steal to her room, where she kneel by she

bedside, and utter the name of Basil in her prayer.



Anthony Bidaud gaze at his daughter till she enter the house, and

even then keep his eye fix upon the door through which she have

"it be year," he say to Basil, "since I have feel so thoroughly

content as I do to-night. come to my room early in the morning; I

shall not write to my lawyer till then, and I wish you to see the

letter."



shortly after all the inmate of the house be asleep.





*    *    *    *    *    *





and while they sleep, there walk across the distant plain towards

the plantation, a man and a woman who have have that goal in view for

three month past. it be summer when they leave their home across the

sea. it be summer when they reach the land to which the woman have

be summon. but, judge from their face, no summer errand be

theirs.



"walk quicker," say the man, surlily. "we must get there before

sunrise. my heart be bent upon it."



"I be fit to drop," say the woman. "how much far have we to go?"



"accord to information, fifteen mile. walk quicker, quick! have

you travel so far to faint at the last moment? remember we have not

a penny leave to purchase food, and have already fast too many hour.

I see vision of ease and comfort, of wine and food, ay, and of rich

too. I be eager to get at they."



"do you remember," say the woman, "that you be not bid to come?"



"what of that?" retort the man. "I have my tale ready. leave I to

play my part. our day of poverty be over. this be the last of they.

walk quick, quick!"









CHAPTER V.





a little after sunrise Basil be awake and out, hasten to the river

would have be strange otherwise, for they have alter the current

happy home; they have place he in a position of responsibility which

he hail with satisfaction and a sense of justifiable pride;

moreover, they have assure he that he have win the affection of a kind

and generous gentleman and of a sweet-tempered and gentle little maid.

he be no long an outcast; he be no long alone in the world.



until this void be supply he have not feel it. young, buoyant, and

every word Anthony Bidaud have speak to he have impress itself upon

Annette as she be at this moment, a child, appear to he and he

so much for the present, and it be due to his light heart and the

than that of Paul and Virginia. he behold she as a woman, and he be

this quality of light irradiate not only all surround visible

"good morning," say Basil.



"be it?" she ask, with a heavy sigh.



"be it what?"



"good morning. to I it be a bad morning."



Basil look round. the heaven be luminous with vivid colour, the

of day. but this sad woman before he be pale and worn; there be

"don't mock I," say the woman, in no gracious tone; "let I rest."



"if you follow this track," persist Basil, "the way I have come, you

will see the Home Station. they will give you breakfast there."



for a moment the woman appear inclined to accept his kindness she

make a movement upwards, but almost immediately she relinquish she

intention.



"no," she say, "I will wait."



he be loth to leave she in her distressful plight, but her churlish

"you can help I," say the woman, "by leave I."



he have no alternative. "if you think well of it," he say, "you can

a stranger be there, already strip for the purpose of bathing.

Scarcely look at he, Basil be about to remove to a more retire

he speak fairly good English, as do the woman who have decline his

he do not say "thank you," nor make the slight acknowledgment of

"I understand. be the woman I meet on my way a relative of your?"

"I be glad to hear it. you seem tired."



"I have be walk all day and all night, and all day and all night

"yes, but just arrive, weary and wear out with nothing but walk,

if the stranger have not state that he have important business to

"have you far to go?" he ask.



"no far," reply the stranger, with an upward glance at Anthony

the laugh intensify Basil's dislike; there be a mocking sinister

ring in it, but he nevertheless continue the conversation.



"Misinformed in what respect?"



"that be M. Bidaud's house?"



"it be M. Bidaud's house."



"M. Anthony Bidaud?"



"yes."



"originally from Switzerland."



Basil's hazard of the stranger's precise nationality now take definite

form.



"as you be," he say.



"as I be," say the stranger, "and as Anthony Bidaud be."



"you be right in your surmise. he be from Switzerland."



"my surmise? ah? he have a fine estate here."



"he have."



"but his wife--she be dead."



"that be so, unhappily."



"what be one man's meat be another man's poison--a proverb that may be

reverse." his small eye glitter, and his thin pointed feature

"it be more than likely that I shall see he," say the stranger, "and

"you be well informed. he have but one."



"whose name be Annette."



"whose name," say Basil, wonder from what source the stranger have

"charming, charming, charming," say the stranger. "everything be

"I will show you a place."



"no trick, young sir, say the stranger, suspicion in his voice.



"why should I play you trick? if you do not care to trust I, seek a

secure spot yourself."



"no, I will accompany you, who must know the river well. you do, eh?"



"I be thoroughly acquaint with it."



"you guess my nation; shall I guess your? australian."



"I be an Englishman."



"a great nation; a great people. be this the spot?"



they have arrive at a smooth piece of water, semi-circularly protect

by rock from the invasion of alligator.



"this be the spot," say Basil, "you will be perfectly safe here."



the water be so clear that they could see to the bottom. black and

"and you--where will you bathe?"



"a little far up stream. good morning."



"ah, good morning; but I may see you again if you be live near."



"I live," say Basil, "in the house yonder."









CHAPTER VI.





a sudden excitement be observable in the stranger. he pause in his

undressing, and lay his hand on Basil's arm, clutch with nervous

finger.



"you be very intimate with M. Anthony Bidaud?" he say.



"we be friend."

seek another wife."



the stranger draw a long breath of relief, follow immediately by the

twenty." he scowl as he look at Basil's handsome face. "be she

married, or go to be?



"Absurd," say Basil, but a little touch of colour come into his face

but Basil do not wait to hear the conclusion of the sentence. annoy

worry I be make of nothing!"



he could not dismiss the subject, however, and the current of his

as a contribution towards this end he plunge half a dozen time into

"have you, Rocke?" he ask of one who be regard he with a scowl.

there could be no mistake make now; he hate Basil.



there be a reason. every man have his good point, even the bad of

"who speak of death?" ask the stranger. "ah, it be you, who be a
