leather case contain a small comb and a narrow slip of

look-glass. rub the surface of the glass dry with a

handkerchief that have drop to the ground, he pass it over the

mouth of Anthony Bidaud; then hold it up to the light.



"yes," he say, look Basil full in the face, "it be death. it be

lucky I travel hither in the night, and do not allow myself to be

delay by fatigue. Fortune, I thank you. you have treat I scurvily

hitherto; at length you relent, and smile upon I. be a lady, I

kiss my hand to you."



there be something so inexpressibly heartless in the action that

Basil cry indignantly, "who be you, and by what right have you

intrude yourself into this room?"



the stranger do not immediately reply. he feel in his pocket for a

snuff-box, and produce it regale himself with a pinch. he offer

the box to Basil, who push it aside. he smile and place the box in

his pocket, and be also about to replace the leather case, when an

amusing thought occur to he. he dress his hair with the comb,

and gaze at himself in the glass with an affectation of vanity. his

smile broaden as he notice the look of horror in Basil's face.



"you wish to know," he say slowly, "who I be, and by what right I

intrude myself into this room. you have presumption, you, M. Anthony

Bidaud's guest, to use the word 'intrude' to I! I be this dead

gentleman's brother. my name be Gilbert Bidaud. eh? do you speak?"









CHAPTER VII.





so many conflict emotion have be press into the last few

minute that Basil be utterly bewildered. the cold, sardonic face

before he, wreathe into mock smile even in the presence of

death, add to his bewilderment. he pass his hand across his eye,

wonder whether he be dream, but remove his hand from his

forehead he see the dead form of Anthony Bidaud on the sofa, and hear

the light laugh of the man who call himself Anthony's brother. this

laugh recall he to himself; he be in full possession of his

sense, and understand what have occur, and to some extent what it

portend.



Gilbert Bidaud! and the woman with he be not his wife, but his

sister, to whom Annette's father have write six month ago, implore

she to come to he, and promise to provide for she and her family.

that be so, she be here by authority. she be but an instrument in

the hand of Gilbert Bidaud, whose light word she be constrain

to obey.



Gilbert Bidaud!



"it be hard to speak harshly of one's flesh and blood, but it be the

truth that the girl I love be fortunate in not place she

affection upon he. he would have break her heart. he be a

spendthrift and a libertine, and would stop at little for the

gratification of his selfish pleasure."



it be but last evening that these word be speak by lip that

would never speak again, and now this spendthrift and libertine be

within touch of he, be stand with a smile face by the dead body

of the brother he would have wrong. there come to Basil's mind the

image of Annette, the sweet confide girl, who be to have be give

into his care to guard and protect. all that be over now. inexorable

death have stop the fulfilment of the fond father's wish. and

Annette herself, how would it fare with she? she be ignorant as yet

of the crush, terrible blow which have so suddenly fall upon she.

who would impart the cruel news to she? who would comfort she in her

bereavement? even as these reflection cross his mind he hear the

young girl's voice singing outside as she trip downstairs from her

bedroom. he glide to the door, and softly turn the key. just in

time. Annette linger at the door, try the handle gently with the

intention of kiss her father good-morning, and, find the door

fast, pass on gaily and continue her song.



"that be Annette?" question Gilbert Bidaud. Basil nod. "a sweet

voice, the voice of a child, whose nature be not yet mould. we will

mould it, my sister and I. we will instil into her virgin soul,

principle. she will be grateful that we have come, be of her

blood. I have a number of your english saying at my finger' end.

blood be thick than water. I represent the one, you the other. she

be not a woman--yet. the mind of a child be like a slate! fancy,

liking, be easily rub off. it be more serious when we grow old.

the child forget, the woman remember. do you catch my meaning?"



"I should be sorry to say I do," reply Basil.



"ah, you would pay I a compliment, gild I with virtue to which I

do not aspire, to which I have never aspire. I be a plain man, I;

honest to the backbone; with my heart on my sieve, transparent. it have

not pay up to this time, but my hour have come. why do you lock the

door?"



"do not that answer you?" point to the dead body of Annette's

father.



"ah, she do not know. you be considerate, you." a strange smile

come to his lip as he add, "no one know but you and I."



Basil step to the table. perhaps the letter which Anthony Bidaud

intend to write to his lawyer be there; it might contain something

by which he could be guide at this dread crisis. but the sheet of

paper which Anthony Bidaud have take from the open desk display only

the mark of a scrawl at the top. the pen, with the ink scarcely dry

in it, lie upon the table. evidently at the very moment that Anthony

Bidaud have put pen to paper he be visit by the death-stroke. the

pen have drop from his finger, and he have fall back lifeless in

his chair. there be, however, an address envelope, and Basil note

the name and the direction, which be those of the lawyer whom

Anthony Bidaud intend to summon to the plantation.



Gilbert Bidaud have follow his movement attentively, and now, when

Basil look up from the table, he repeat the last word he have

utter.



"no one know but you and I."



"what do you mean by that?" demand Basil.



"what I mean," say Bidaud, touch his forehead with a finger, "I

keep here for the present. it be sometimes dangerous to explain

meaning too soon. take heed. when I come to this colony--but a short

time since--I be inwardly warn that I might meet with man from whom

it would be necessary to protect myself. therefore I purchase

this"--producing a revolver--"and this"--producing a knife--"only to

be use in self-defence, against you, against any man."



there be nothing menacing in his tone. he speak, indeed, rather

playfully than otherwise, and handle the revolver and knife as though

they be toy instead of dangerous weapon. a wild thought cross

Basil's mind, and he act upon it instantly.



"you say you be Gilbert Bidaud, brother of this unfortunate

gentleman, but I have only your word for it."



"ah, ah," say Gilbert Bidaud, with an air of great amusement, "you

have only my word for it. but what kind of authority do you hold here

that you should demand answer to question upon this or any other

subject?"



Basil could not answer this direct challenge; he inwardly recognise

the weakness of his position; Anthony Bidaud dead, he be but a cipher

on his estate.



"you be as a feather to a rock," say Gilbert Bidaud, with a gesture

of contempt, "and I be but amuse myself with you. I stand quietly

here for a reason I may presently explain. this house have lose a

master." he glance at his dead brother. "this house have gain a

master." he touch his breast triumphantly. "it be but a change, a

law of nature. my brother and I have not meet for twenty year. he have

a good motive for avoid I; he flee from Switzerland with money of

mine, and now, through death, he be compel to make restitution."



"it be false," cry Basil, chivalrously defend the friend he have

lose. "if you be Gilbert Bidaud it be you who attempt to rob he

of his inheritance."



"ah, ah. do my estimable brother open his heart entirely to you?"



"sufficiently to reveal your true character--even to the last word

you speak to he before he leave Switzerland."



"favour I with they. it may be excuse if I do not faithfully recall

they at this distance of time."



"'one day,' you say to he 'I will be even with you. remember my

word--dead or alive, I will be even with you."



"I remember. my word be prophetic. Fate be on my side, justice be

on my side. they whisper to I, 'wait.' I wait. and now--look

there! so, so, my ingenious young friend; you know the whole story."



"it be relate to I by your brother."



"by this lump of clay! it would be the act of a fool to deal tenderly

by you; and I, as you may have already learn, be no fool. how come

my brother by his death?"



"how come he by his death?" stammer Basil, puzzle by the question,

and not see the drift of it.



"ay, how come he by his death? I be not so ignorant as you suppose. I

have make inquiry about you; there be man on this estate who bear

you no good will. you be here, not as a guest, but an interloper. you

and my brother be stranger a few short week ago, and you force

yourself upon he and live here, a beggar, eat his food, drink

his wine, and pay for they neither in service nor money. that be a

creditable part to be play by one who call himself an English

gentleman. summon here by M. Anthony Bidaud--I have in my pocket the

letter he write to our sister--I hasten on the wing of love, tarrying

not on the road, but wear myself near to death in order that I may

satisfy his longing desire to embrace I. I meet you by accident on

the river's bank, and I perceive that you regard yourself as master

here. the river be your, the land be your, my brother be your, his

daughter Annette be your--ah, you wince at that. all this you

proclaim in your lordly way, and patronise I--I, whose rightful

place you would have usurp. before meet you pass my sister,

rest in her labour of love, and you offer her charity--you, a

beggar, pass this insult upon a lady who, under my direction, will

educate my dear brother's little daughter, and teach she--principle.

you leave I by the river; I, guileless, unsuspicious, a child in

innocence, calmly take my bath, and reflect with delight upon the joy

of my brother when he take I to his arm. walk to this house, I

meet a labourer, whose name be Rocke. he tell I something of you; he

direct I to my brother's private room. I open the door; I see you

stand by my brother's side. you be in a state of fear and

agitation; your face be white, your limb tremble. I hear you ask the

question, 'can this be death?' to whom or to what do you address this

enquiry? to your conscience, for you believe yourself to be alone; you

be unconscious that I be present 'can this be death?' I convince

myself, and you. it _be_ death. I be deprive of the opportunity of

say to my brother that I forgive he for the wrong he do I in the

past. it be most cruel, and you have rob I of the opportunity;

but, before I forget it, I will chance the efficacy of my forgiveness,

though he be dead." with a mock humility shocking to witness, he

extend his hand, and, look upwards, say, "Brother, I forgive

you. I return to my argument. what pass between you and my brother

before I enter this room? again I ask, how come he by his death! if

it be not a natural end, who be the murderer?"



in hot indignation Basil start forward, but by a great effort of

will restrain himself. he have be appal by the careless mocking

tone in which Gilbert Bidaud have speak, by his false assumption of a

grief he do not feel, by the evident enjoyment he derive from the

glaring insincerity of his profession. for no two thing could be

more distinctly at variance than Gilbert Bidaud's word and the tone

in which he utter they. it exhibit a refinement of malice, and,

what render it more revolting, of malice in which the intellectual

quality be conspicuous.



"it be well," continue Gilbert Bidaud, "that you exercise

self-control. I might call aloud for help; I might, in less time than

it take I to speak it, create in this room the evidence of a

struggle, in the course of which I might fire my revolver, produce

for self-defence; I might inform those who would break the door

down--it be lock by you, remember--that you attempt to murder I,

even as you-- ah, I perceive you understand. yes, all this I might do,

and you would be in the toil. do not move until I have do with you,

or you will be in deadly danger. in such part of the world as this,

exasperate man often proceed hastily to summary justice, and it might

be execute upon you. I be teach you lesson, as I shall teach my

dear niece Annette, principle. you be young; I, alas, be old. I have

nothing to learn; you have much. tell I, you hanger-on in this house,

you beggar of my brother's hospitality, what pass between you and

he before I enter this room?"



"nothing," reply Basil, confound by the possibility of a

ruthless malice with which Gilbert Bidaud have threaten he. "I have

already inform you that when I enter the room he be dead."



"what bring you here?"



"I come by appointment," say Basil. he no long doubt that the man

before he be Anthony Bidaud's brother; and he be surprised that he

have not detect the resemblance upon his first meeting with Gilbert.



"what be the nature of the appointment?"



"he wish I to read a letter he intend to write to his lawyer."



"ah, ah! he intend to write to his lawyer. may I ask this lawyer's

name?"



"it be there upon an envelope."



"his place of residence?"



"Sydney, I believe."



"a long way off. the letter be to have be write this morning?"



"yes. he at first intend to write it last night, but he put it off

till to-day. the postponement be most unfortunate."



"to you?"



"to I."



"I should have urge he to carry out his intention last night, as he

design."



"ah! _aprés dommage chacun est sage_--except the dead. why should you

have urge he?"



"it would have be to my interest--and his, I fear."



"leave his out of the question; he have do with the world. your be

another matter. how could a simple letter to a lawyer have be in

your interest? a letter be not a legal document." his preternatural

sharpness as he make this remark be a revelation to an honest nature

like Basil's. there seem to be no limit to Gilbert Bidaud's cunning.



"at least it would have explain matter, and clear I from your

suspicion."



"word be easily speak, and weigh no more than air. to what effect

be to have be this letter?"



"he desire to make his will."



Gilbert Bidaud draw a deep breath of satisfaction; he have elicit

something tangible, something which have wonderfully strengthen his

position. "then there be no will, and the letter, which would have

be valueless, be not write. your expression of regret lead I to

infer that the will be to have be in your favour."



"to a certain extent."



"false. he intend to repair the injustice from which I have so long

suffer; his property would have be divide between I and the

little Annette. it be too late for he to do that now; but I stand as

natural guardian to my niece. I be truly the master here; the law will

declare I so. console yourself. you shall depart from this house a

free man. you be not in danger. bear witness to my magnanimity; my

brother die a natural death. I will testify it, to save you."



"that will not do," say Basil. "from what cause he die shall be

prove by proper evidence."



"it shall. I, a doctor, will supply it."



"I reject your proof; you be an interested party. it shall be

independent evidence that shall establish the cause of death."



"so be it, young Daniel," say Gilbert Bidaud, briskly. "meanwhile, I

release you from suspicion; I, the gentleman you have insult,

believe you to be innocent. I go to seek my niece, to introduce myself

to she, and to break to she the sad, the melancholy news. but before I

go I give you notice of your discharge. for one week from this day you

shall enjoy my hospitality, but for no long, for not an hour long.

accept it, beggar, or leave at once."



he pause at the door, open it, remove the key to the outside, and

with a contemptuous motion, order Basil to quit the room. the young

man have no choice but to obey. whatever might be Gilbert Bidaud's

character, he stand in the house as legal representative of the dead.

Annette be but a child, and her uncle be her lawful guardian.

grieve, sorrow-stricken, and humiliate, Basil leave the room, and

hear Gilbert Bidaud turn the key.









CHAPTER VIII.





what should he do now, how should he act? to accept Gilbert Bidaud's

hospitality be impossible. the old man be his bitter enemy, and

would show he no consideration. indeed, what consideration could he

expect? there be no deny that he have no right to remain on the

estate, but he feel he could not leave it for ever without see

Annette once more, without speak to she perhaps for the last time.

nor could be well take his final departure without make an attempt

to clear himself from the foul suspicion which, in his absence, he

feel convinced Gilbert Bidaud would set in circulation against he. he

have lead a spotless life, and the thought that a stain should now be

cast upon it be unbearable. but what mean could he take to clear

himself from the breath of slander? he could think of no way at

present, and he walk into the open with a heavy weight of melancholy

at his heart.



he wander into the wood and gather some fruit; he have a vigorous

appetite, and it would be a folly to starve himself. but the food of

which he partook have never taste less sweet than on this sad morning.

his hunger appease, he return to the vicinity of the house.



he hear a cry of distress in the distance, and see man and woman

hurry to the spot from which the cry proceed. the voice be

Annette's.



presently he see the man and woman come towards the house. they be

head by Gilbert Bidaud and his sister, and one of the man--before

the group come close to he he see that it be Rocke--be carry in

his arm the insensible form of Annette. impel by love and infinite

compassion for the child, he start forward, but be haughtily wave

off by Gilbert Bidaud.



"that man," say Gilbert to those in his rear, "have my permission to

remain on this estate for one week. when that time have expire he will

be a trespasser."



as he finish speak Annette open her eye--they fall upon Basil.



"Basil, Basil!" she cry, extend her arm to he.



"Annette!"



once more he attempt to go to she; once more Gilbert Bidaud wave

he off, and step before he.



"if he touch she, if he follow she, arrest he. I give you

authority."



Basil fall back. Annette's mournful eye be fix upon his face in

dumb despair.



"hurry in--hurry in," say Gilbert Bidaud in a harsh tone.



they pass into the house, and Basil be leave alone. it be a

favourite trick of his to put his thought into unspoken word; he have

encourage the habit, find it lead to clearness and generally, when

he be in doubt, to some definite issue. in his disturbed mood he

find this a suitable time for this mental indulgence. something

should be do, clearly; but what?



"Poor Annette!" he think. "poor child! what will now become of she?

what will be her future? that brute--he be no less--who boast so

sardonically that he intend to teach her principle, will poison she

mind against I. if I do not see she again she will grow to hate I.

it be dreadful to think of. she have none but kind thought of I now;

and though in a short time we may be part for ever, and all chance

of ever see she again will be lose, I should dearly like to feel

that if she think of I in the future it will be with gentleness and

affection. I have do nothing to forfeit her affection, except that I

be unfortunate.



"my bright dream be suddenly snap. a few short hour have change

happiness to woe. still--still I have commit no wrong. of that I be

sure, and it be a comfort--but poor Annette! if I could assure she

that I be not to blame, I could bear it. she would believe I, and I

could go on my way with a less sorrowful heart.



"that brute will try his hard to prevent my see she. the blow

that have fall upon she may prostrate she. she may die--it be

horrible, horrible! if that should happen, Gilbert Bidaud will come

into possession of everything. be that the end to which he will work?

he be capable of it, capable of any villainy. can I do nothing to save

she?



"I be powerless. I have no claim upon she; I have no right to be here.

but I will not go away without see, without speak to she. if he

take she from this place, which be likely enough, I will follow they.

she must not, she must not be leave to the tender mercy of that

jackal.



"all very fine to talk, Basil. you will follow they? why, man, you

must live. it be a necessity. and to live you must work. how much

money have you in your pocket to commence the fight of existence

with?--to say nothing of the grand thing you be go to do for

sweet Annette.



"she have get hold of my heart-string. I shall never, never forget

she. certain word speak by my dear friend, Anthony Bidaud, last

night, come to my mind. let I recall they, exactly as he speak they.



"'we be draw to each other,' he say. and before that: 'by accident

you enter into our life. I use the term accident, but I believe it to

be a providence.' how if it should be so? the shadow of death be

hang over he, and at such time some man have be gift with

prophetic insight. if it be so with Anthony Bidaud, this be not the

end. the thought I have express, the very word 'insight' I have

use, be his. 'I have observe you closely,' he say, 'and be

satisfied to deliver into your hand a sacred charge, the charge of a

young girl's future. at such moment as these there come to some man

a subtle, unfathomable insight. it come to I. I firmly believe that

there be a link between you and my child, which, if you do not

recognise it now, you will be bind to recognise in the future. it may

be break in the present, but the thread will be join as surely as

we stand here side by side.'"



"with all my heart I hope so, but it be the wild, the most

unreasonable of hope.



"can nothing, nothing be do?



"he say he have make no will; but he may have leave paper express

his wish. how to get a sight of they? if I have sufficient mean to

take I to Sydney I would hasten there, to Anthony Bidaud's lawyer,

and lie the case before he. but my purse be empty. I have, however,

something about I of value. my gold watch and chain, give to I by

my dear father. that be worth a certain sum, but it would not carry I

to Sydney. it would carry I, however, to Gum Flat, where perhaps I

can find a lawyer who will advise she. in the saddle I could reach

there to-night, and be back to-morrow. where can I obtain a horse? I

dare not take one from the plantation. Gilbert Bidaud would accuse I

of theft, and he would be within his right. ah! Old Corrie!"



here he stop. his unspoken thought have lead he to a definite

issue.



Gum Flat be the name of the near township, if township it could be

call. in the australian colony they delight in singular name for

place. Old Corrie be a man who, by permission of Anthony Bidaud,

occupy a hut which he have build with his own hand on the

plantation, some two mile from the spot upon which Basil at that

moment stand. he be not employ on the estate, but do odd job in

wood splitting and the felling of tree for the master of the

plantation. the man have "take" to Basil, as the saying be, and in his

odd way have show a liking for the young man, who always have a

pleasant word for any agreeable person he chance to fall across.



Old Corrie be not an old man, his age be about forty, but he be

dub Old Corrie because he be angular, because he be crook,

because he have a mouth all awry, because he choose to keep himself from

his fellow. he own a horse, and it occur to Basil that he might

lend it to he for the journey to Gum Flat, which be distant some

forty-five mile. to Old Corrie's hut, therefore, Basil betook

himself, step out with a will.



in less than half-an-hour he reach the old fellow's dwelling. Old

Corrie be not at home, but Basil hear the sound of his axe in the

wood. it be not very near, but man's ear get train to fine sound

in the bush. guide by the thud of the axe Basil in a short time find

himself face to face with the woodman.



Old Corrie go on with his work, merely glance up and give Basil

a friendly nod. from another living creature Basil receive a more

boisterous greeting, a laugh jackass which Old Corrie have tame

burst into an outrageous fit of laughter without the least apparent

cause. this bird, which be sometimes call the bushman's clock, be

an uncouth-look object, as big as a crow, of a rich chestnut-brown

colour with light-blue wing; its beak be long and point, and its

mouth inordinately large. these characteristic, in alliance with a

formidable crest, invest it with a ferocious air; but this

particular speciman be exceedingly gentle despite the extravagant

sound it emit, which might have be excruciatingly prolonged have

not its sharp eye catch sight of a carpet snake wriggle through the

underwood. down dart the laugh jackass, and commence a battle

with the snake which terminate in the bird throw the dead body of

the reptile into the air, with a series of triumphant chuckle; after

which it sit silent on a branch, contemplate the dead snake with an

air partly comical, partly profound, and wait in grim patience for

a movement on the part of its victim which would furnish an excuse for

a renewal of hostility.



Basil have time to note all this, for Old Corrie do not speak, and the

young man be debate how to commence.



"well, Master Basil," say Old Corrie, presently, throw down his

axe and take out his pipe, a common short clay which he would not

have exchange for thrice its weight in gold, "what bring you this

way? any message from Mr. Bidaud?"



"no, Corrie," reply Basil sadly, "you will receive no more message

from he."



"I be think myself," say Corrie, glance at Basil; and not

immediately recognise the gravity of the reply, "that there mightn't

be any more."



"what make you think that?" ask Basil, in doubt whether the man know

of Anthony Bidaud's death.



"I'm down with the fever, Master Basil."



"I be sorry to hear that, Corrie," say Basil in surprise, for Old

Corrie be the picture of health and strength. "can I do anything for

you?"



"no, Master Basil," say Old Corrie, with a smile and a kindly look at

Basil. "the fever I'm down with ain't the kind of fever that's in your

mind. it's the gold fever I'm down with."



"oh," say Basil, "I understand."



"the wonder be that I've never be down with it before. if I don't

strike a rich claim or find a big nugget or two, I can always come

back to this."



"have you hear any news, then?"



"well, two man camp out here last night, and we have a talk. I give

'em some tea, and their tongue get loosen a bit. there's a new

goldfield discover somewhere in the north, and they're after it. a

regular Tom Tiddler's ground, Mr. Basil, only it's all gold and no

silver. twenty ounce to the tub."



"and you're off?"



"when I've finish this job for Mr. Bidaud."



"how long will that take you?"



"about three week."



"be it a contract job?"



"yes."



"sign on paper?"



"no, we never have need of that. Mr. Bidaud's word be as good as his

bond; so's mine."



"I would not go on with it, Corrie, if I be you, till I make sure."



"why?"



"because the gentleman who make the contract with you by word of mouth

be dead."



"dead!"



"die this morning, suddenly, I grieve to say."



Old Corrie take his pipe from his mouth, and send a look of reproach

in the direction of the laugh jackass, from whose throat proceed

a faint gurgle of laughter. at this look the quaint bird--as odd a

speciman of the feather tribe as Old Corrie be of the human

race--check--its mirth, and cock its head knowingly on one side,

inquire with its speak eye what be the matter.



"that's bad news, Master Basil."



"the bad of news, Corrie."



"die suddenly?"



"quite suddenly. it be a great shock."



"what's to become of the little lady?" ask Old Corrie, in a

sympathise tone. the inquiry be address as much to himself as to

Basil.



"that be one of the thing that be trouble I, Corrie. you be a

favourite of her."



"I've see she grow up, and remember her mother well. I've cause. once

when I be down with the colonial fever--almost as bad as the gold

fever, Master Basil--Mrs. Bidaud as good as nurse I through it,

come or send every day for two month and more, till I get

strong. when I be well I go up to the house to thank she. the

little lady be just toddle about, and make friend with I. I shall

never forget Mrs. Bidaud; I go to her funeral. you stop at my hut

before you come here, I expect."



"yes; I think you might be there."



"do you hear anything?"



"only the sound of your axe in the wood."



"I mean inside the hut. there's a magpie there that's get the sense of

a human being and a voice like a flute. I only get it a fortnight ago,

and I've tame it already, surprising. back as white as snow, Master

Basil, and breast and wing shine like black satin. a handsome bird,

and quite young. it say 'little lady; little lady!' and 'Miss

Annette!' in a way that'll astonish you. I'm do it for the little

lady herself, and I'm glad I begin it because I'm go away."



"it will please she greatly, Corrie, if she be allow to accept it."



"what's to prevent she? poor little lady! first her mother, then she

father. I think there be trouble in your face when I see it. would

you mind explain, Master Basil, about this wood-splitting contract

of mine? why shouldn't I finish it till I make sure."



then Basil tell of the arrival of the dead man's brother and sister,

and be not delicate in express his opinion of Gilbert Bidaud.



"you're not the sort of man," say Old Corrie thoughtfully, "to speak,

ill behind another's back without good reason. little lady's uncle

must be a bad lot. a man and a woman, you say, foreign looking. they

must be the pair that pass my hut early this morning when I be

get up. they don't stop; she want to, I think, but he wouldn't

let she. 'curse you!' I hear he say, 'what be you lag for? put

life into your miserable limb; we haven't get far to go.' it seem

to I as if he lay hand on she to drag she along. I come out of the

hut, and see they ahead, the woman walk as if she be dead beat,

and the man lug she on. they never turn to look behind, and I

watch till they be out of sight. I'm sorry for the little lady.

I'll go up to the house to-day, and judge for myself."



"you may hear something against I, Corrie. don't believe it."



"I willn't, without reason. I make up my mind slow, Master Basil.

perhaps you've get something more to tell I. it willn't be throw

away."



wish to stand well with Old Corrie, Basil become more

communicative, and put the woodman in possession, of the particular

of what have pass between himself and Anthony Bidaud on the previous

evening, and also of his interview with Anthony's brother.



"it look black," say Old Corrie. "it's a pity you don't leave he

to the alligator. and now, Master Basil, you've something else in your

mind. out with it."



"I come to ask you to do I a great service."



"give it mouth."



"it may be that poor Annette's father have leave some paper with

respect to her future which the law might declare valid. if that be

so, and her uncle find they, he will destroy they; it may be to his

interest to do so, and in that case he will allow no consideration of

right and wrong to stand in his way. the presence of a lawyer may

prevent this. then there be the slanderous talk he be sure to set

go against I; I want to clear myself of it. the precise cause of

Anthony Bidaud's death should be ascertain and declare by a

competent and disinterested person, and I think of go to Gum Flat

and enlist the service of a lawyer and a doctor, whom I would

bring back with I."



"it would be a proper thing to do," say Corrie.



"but I be in a difficulty. I could walk the distance, but I could not

get there till to-morrow. come and go, four day at least would

be waste, and in that time Annette's uncle could work his own end

without interruption. now, if I have a horse I could get there this

evening, and back to-morrow."



"you want I to lend you my mare?"



"that be what I come to ask you."



"you can have she; she's a willing creature, and 'll go till she

drop."



"it be kind of you, Corrie."



"not at all. I do it a little bit for your sake, but a good deal more

for the sake of the little lady."



"you run a risk, Corrie. my story may not be true; I may never come

back."



"I'll take security, then."



"I have no money. the only thing I possess of value be this watch and

chain."



"I willn't take that; you may need it to pay the lawyer and the doctor

with. besides that ben't the security I mean. I'll take your word."



"you're a real good fellow, Corrie. some day I may be able to repay

you."



"if I have any idea of look out for that day I shouldn't do what I'm

do. look here, Master Basil. I know a gentleman when I see one; and

you're a gentleman. I believe every word you've tell I. this fellow

that's turn up, the little lady's uncle, be a scoundrel, or he

wouldn't have speak the word I hear to a woman nearly dead with

fatigue--his own sister, too. come along; let's saddle the mare."



before that be do, however, Old Corrie insist that Basil should

eat a hearty meal and see the magpie he be train for Annette. then

Basil mount the willing mare, and with a grip of the hand and a

hearty "good luck, mate," from Old Corrie, the young man start for

Gum Flat.









CHAPTER IX.





it be three month since Basil have pass through the conglomeration

of canvas tent and store which rejoice in a title which certainly

could not be call euphonious, and then, although those be its most

prosperous day, it strike he as be a wretched hole. rumour of

rich find of gold have originally attract a population to Gum Flat

township, but the glowing anticipation of the gold digger who

flock to the false El Dorado be doom to disappointment. it be

not a gold-digger' but a storekeeper' rush, and the result be a

foregone conclusion; after a time the miner who have flock thither

begin to desert the place. not, however, before they give it a fair

trial. they mark out claim, they prospect the hill and gully,

they turn the water of a large creek, they sink shafts in many a

likely-look spot, they follow spur of stone on the range in

the hope that they would lead they to a rich quartz reef, but their

labour be unrewarded. a couple of speck to the dish and the

faint trace of gold in the quartz be not sufficient to pay for

powder and tobacco, and the man gradually begin to leave the

uninviting locality. a few remain, but not to dig for gold; these

be chiefly loafer, and live on each other, play billiard

during the day on the one billiard table that have be leave behind,

and card during the night, for fabulous and visionary sum of money

which, really lose and win, would have transform beggar into

millionaire and millionaire into beggar. the poorer they grow the

large the stake they play for, and their delusion create for

their delectation the most delicious paroxysm of infinite joy and

overwhelming despair. these they enjoy to the full, reckon up

their loss and gain with wild eye and radiant countenance. one

beggarly loafer, who for the last five year have not have five pound

to bless himself with, go to the creek one dark night after a

visionary loss of a hundred thousand pound or so, and insist upon

drown himself. it require a vast amount of insistence on his part,

for the creek just then be not more than three foot deep. anyway, he

be find dead the next morning, with a letter in his pocket to the

effect that he be financially ruin and could not survive the

disgrace; whereupon his principal creditor, who, in the matter of

finance, be no well off than the drown man, perambulate high

Street in a state of fury, fiercely denounce his debtor who have not

the courage to live and pay his debt of honour.



some mean of subsistence, however inadequate, Gum Flat must have have;

these be find in the person of a half-a-dozen driver of bullock

dray, who every two week bring their earning there and spend they

royally. this process last on each occasion exactly three day,

during which time the population, number in all not more than

thirty soul, be in clover. when the bullock driver return to

their avocation the loafer declare that the colony be go to

the dog, and resume the routine of their dismal day, gambling,

drinking, quarrelling, until the six solvent man return again to

gladden their heart.



even this miserable state of affair come to an end after a time, and

reach a more deplorable stage. the bullock driver discover more

agreeable quarter, and in their turn desert the township. drive by

sheer necessity the loafer, one by one, follow their example, and

slunk from the place, until only four remain. such be the condition

of Gum Flat as Basil ride towards the township on a day eventful

enough in the story of his life, but scarcely less eventful than the

night which follow it be destine to be. have he be aware of this

he would have think twice before he make up his mind to proceed

thither in search of lawyer and doctor; but such be the irony of

circumstance that, have he not set forth on his present journey, the

entire course of his future life would have drift into channel

which would, almost to a certainty, have separate he from Annette

for ever. accident or fate, which you will; but the course of many

life be thus determine.



he ride all day through the track he remember, and concern which

he have be refresh by Old Corrie, who be as ignorant as himself of

the deplorable change that have take place. the road for a few mile

lie along great plain of rich black soil, dot here and there with

masse of blue and barley grass, among which might be find the native

leek and wild cucumber; then follow a tract of country somewhat

lightly timbere but heavily grassed, where he come across a nasty bit

of "devil devil" land, fortunately of not great extent, for he have to

ride with a loose rein and leave it to his horse to pick the safe

way. on his left be large lagoon in which a wondrous variety of

wild fowl abound; on his right be a belt of impenetrable scrub; but

the track be well define, and after ride twenty mile he enter a

thickly woode forest, for the shade of which he be grateful, the sun

now be high in the heaven. emerge from this forest he halt

near a vast sheet of water, in which tall reed grow, and where he

find the wild banana. off this fruit and some cold meat and bread

which Old Corrie have force upon he, he make a sufficient meal, and

then resume his journey. in the afternoon the road lie through a more

even country, and he reckon upon reach Gum Flat before sundown.

but he reckon without his host, for the distance be long than he

calculate, and at sunset he be still, accord to the information

give to he by the driver of a bullock dray, eight or ten mile from

the township. this man be the only human being he have meet in his

lonely ride. many a time in the course of the day have he fall into

contemplation of the pregnant event of the last twenty-four hour,

think, "this time yesterday I be walk with Annette in the

wood, gather wildflower for her mother's grave. she slip, and

I catch she in my arm." and again: "this time yesterday Anthony

Bidaud, Annette, and I, be sit in the verandah, watch the

sunset; and a moment afterwards white star be glitter in the

cloud of faded gold. how peaceful, how happy we be! and now?" he

shudder as he think of the dead form of Anthony Bidaud lie in

his room and of the sense of desolation which must have fall upon

Annette. he strive to direct his thought into more cheerful groove,

but he be not successful.



the gorgeous colour in the heaven melt away; the sun dip

beneath the horizon; it be night. fortunately it be light, and he

could see the road he be ride over. the willing animal he bestrode

plod on, more slowly now, and Basil do not attempt to quicken the

pace. it be ten o'clock when he reach the township of Gum Flat.



he recognise it by the outline of the tent. he have expect to see

light in the dwelling, argue that Gum Flat must have increase in

importance since his last visit, but all be dark on the outskirt. he

be surprised at the darkness, but grateful that his journey be over.

he ride along the High Street, and with still deep surprise observe

that on some of the store the canvas lie loose, and that the calico

over the frame be tear and rent. "can I have mistake the road?" he

think. in the middle of the High Street he pause. the door of a

store be throw suddenly open, and three man, whose movement have

be inspire by the sound of the horse's hoof, emerge therefrom,

and stand look up at Basil. each have card in his hand, denote

that when they be disturb they have be gamble. the picture at

that moment be Rembrandtesque. the street be in darkness; not a

light be visible. one of the man stand at the door hold above his

head a light candle stick in a whiskey bottle, and this dim light

enable the three-gambler and Basil not exactly to see each other but

to define outline. through the open door Basil see a table upon which

be another candle, and sit at which be another man, also with

card in his hand. this man, lean forward, be strive to pierce

the gloom in which his companion and Basil stand. he rise and join

they, and go close to Basil, lay his hand upon the horse's neck.

thus, Basil and he confront each other. and at that moment be

commence the weaving of a strand which be to connect the life of

these two man, for weal or woe.









CHAPTER X.





each man of this small group represent in his own person the epitome

of a drama more or less stirring and eventful. with three of these we

have little to do, and no good purpose will be serve by recount

their antecedent. the history of the fourth--he who stand with his

hand on the neck of Old Corrie's horse, look up at Basil--will

presently be unfold.



he be a full-bearded man, the light brown hair so effectually

conceal his feature that only his cheekbone and forehead be

visible. to a physiologist, therefore, the index be imperfect. he be

a young man, of about the same age as Basil, and his name be Newman

Chaytor. this be his true name; it will be as well to say as much,

for there be much that be false about he.



the man who hold the candle be know as Jim the Hatter; Jim belong

properly to he by right, the Hatter be patronymic he have earn by

work on various goldfield alone, without a mate. why they call man

on the gold-digging thus incline, Hatters, be one of the mystery,

but it be a fact. of the other two it will be sufficient to refer to

they as Nonentity Number one and Nonentity Number two. Jim the Hatter

be a large-boned, loose-limbe man, of great strength. upon his first

arrival in Australia his time, to put it gently, be not his own; it

belong to his country. he be now free, but his moral have not be

improve by the lesson his country have administer to he.



it will thus be see that Basil have unfortunately fall among

thief.



for a few moment the man on horseback and the man on foot preserve

silence, and opportunity be afford for a striking picture. Jim the

Hatter be the first to speak.



"well, mate?" he say.



"be this the township of Gum Flat?" inquire Basil.



"it be. if you're look for it, you're dead on the gutter."



"I think I must have mistake my way," say Basil. "what have come

over the place?"



Newman Chaytor answer he. "it have go," he say, "to the dog."



"like yourself," think Basil, gaze at the man, but deem it

prudent not to express himself aloud upon a point so personal. he

speak, however. "it be the place I be make for. I suppose I can put

up here for the night?"



"there's nothing to prevent you. Gum flat township just now be Liberty

Hall."



"stop a bit, stop a bit," say Nonentity Number one, consider it

necessary to his dignity that he should take part in the conference.

"be the gentleman prepare to pay for accommodation?"



"that's a proper question," say Nonentity Number two, thus assert

himself.



"of course he be," say Jim the Hatter, answer for Basil, who, with

an empty purse, be save from awkwardness.



a diversion occur here. Newman Chaytor snatch the candle from Jim

the Hatter, in order that he might obtain a clear view of Basil.



"manner, mate," say Jim the Hatter.



"manner be hang!" retort Newman Chaytor, hold the candle high.

"they're out of stock."



this be evident. to smooth matter Basil volunteer an explanation.

"I have come hereupon business, but I be afraid I have lose my time."



"perhaps not," say Jim the Hatter. "we're all business man here;

ready at a moment's notice to turn a honest penny. that's true, ben't

it, mate?"



he address Newman Chaytor, but that worthy do not reply. having

obtain a clear view of Basil's face, he seem to be suddenly

strike dumb, and stare at it as though he be fascinated.



"still," continue Jim the Hatter, "it's as well to be particular in

these time. I'm very choice in the company I keep, and I don't as a

rule do business with stranger, unless," he add, with a grin which

find its reflection on the lip of Nonentities Numbers one and two,

"they pay their footing first."



"if you wish to know my name," say Basil, "it be Basil Whittingham."



"what!" cry Newman Chaytor, find his tongue; but the exclamation

of undoubted astonishment appear to be force from he instead of

be voluntarily utter.



"Basil Whittingham," repeat Basil. "be here, I must stop for the

night. be there a stable near?"



"there's one at the back," say Newman Chaytor, with sudden alacrity,

"or rather there be one. I'll show you."



"thank you," say Basil, and follow his guide to the rear of the

shanty.



the three man look after they with no good will.



"he's a swell," say Nonentity Number one.



"he's get a watch and chain," say Nonentity number two.



"and a horse," say Jim the Hatter.



then they re-enter the store, and settle down to their game of

card.



"stop here a moment," say Newman Chaytor to Basil. "I'll get a

light."



return with a candle stick in a bottle, the fashionable form of

candlestick in Gum Flat, he wave it about, sometimes so close to

Basil that it shine upon his feature.



"you stare at I," say Basil, "as if you know I."



"never see you before to my knowledge." (a falsehood, but that be a

detail.) "you're not a colonial."



"I be an Englishman, like yourself, I judge."



"yes, I be English."



"you have the advantage of I--you know my name. may I ask yours?"



"certainly," say Chaytor, but he speak, nevertheless, with a certain

hesitation, as if something of importance hang upon it. "my name be

Newman, with Chaytor tack to it." then, anxiously, "have you hear

it before?"



"never. this be a tumble-down place. it be a courtesy to call it a

stable."



"it will serve, in place of a well."



"oh, yes, it be well than nothing."



"everything be tumble-down in Gum Flat. I be an Englishman town-breed.

and you?"



"my people hail from Devonshire."



"I be not dream, then," say Chaytor, speak for the second time

involuntarily.



"dream!" exclaim Basil.



"I be think of another matter," say Chaytor, with readiness.

"speak my thought aloud be one of my bad trick."



"one of mine, too," say Basil smile.



"that be not the only thing in which we're alike."



"no."



"we be about the same age, about the same build, and we be both

gentleman. your horse be blow; you have ride a long distance."



"from Bidaud's plantation."



"I have hear of it. and you come upon business? I may be able to

assist you."



"I shall be glad of assistance," say Basil, recognise in his

companion an obvious superiority to the man they have leave. "when I

pass through Gum Flat a few month ago I think it a township

likely to thrive, and now I find it pretty well deserted."



"it have go to the dog, as I tell you. there's nothing but grass for

your horse to nibble at. so you're from Devonshire. do your people

live there still?"



he mix up the subject of his remark in the oddest manner, and cast

furtive glance at Basil with a certain mental preoccupation which

would have force itself upon Basil's attention have he not be so

occupy with his own special care.



"there be none left," say Basil. "I be the only one remain."



"the only one?"



"well, I have an old uncle, but we be not exactly on amicable term."



"you be well off than I be. I have no family leave." he sigh

pathetically. "I fancy I can lay my hand on a bundle of sweet hay."



"I should feel grateful."



"don't leave the stable till I come back; I shalln't be go long."



he be absent ten minute or so and though he go straight about his

errand, he be think of something very different. "it be the most

wonderful thing in the world," run his thoughts--"that I should meet

he here again, in this hole, not change in the slight! it cann't

be accident; it be predestine, and I should be a self-confess

idiot if I do not take advantage of it. but how be it to be work?

his uncle be still alive. what do he say? 'we be not exactly on

amicable term.' that be because he be proud. I be not. I should be a

well nephew to the old fellow than this upstart. he be very old, in

his second childhood most likely. this be the turning-point of my

life, and I will not throw away the chance. just as I be at the

bottom of the ladder, too. I'll climb to the top--I will, I will!" he

raise his hand to the sky, as though register an oath.



"there," he say, throw down a bundle of hay which the horse

immediately begin to munch, "with a bucket of water your mare will do

very well. I'll fetch it."



"you be very kind," say Basil, warm to Newman Chaytor.



"not at all. _Noblesse oblige_." this be say with a grand air.



Basil hold out his hand, and Chaytor press it effusively. then, at

Chaytor's request, Basil speak of the errand upon which he be

engage, and be ply skilfully with question, put his companion

well.



"it be curious," say Chaytor, "that we two should have meet at such a

time and in such a place. who know what may come of it? I be, strange

to say, a bit of a doctor and a bit of a lawyer, and if you will

accept my service I shall be glad to accompany you back to Bidaud's

plantation."



"but why?" ask Basil, touch by the apparently unselfish offer. "I

have no claim upon you."



"except the claim that one gentleman have upon another--which should

count for something. it always have with I."



"upon my word I don't know how to thank you."



"don't try. it be myself I be render a service to, not you. this

desert hole, and the association of those men"--jerking his thumb

not come to some man a crisis in their life which compel they to

turn over a new leaf, as the saying be, to cut themselves away

entirely from the past and commence life anew?"



"yes," say Basil, strike by the application of this figure of speech

to his own circumstance, "it have come to I."



"and to I. I intend to leave Gum Flat to-morrow, and I do not know

in which direction. I feel like Robinson Crusoe on the desert island,

without a friend, without a kindre soul to talk to, to associate

with. if you will allow I to look upon you as a friend you will put

"it be the most sensible course," say Basil.



"exactly. the most sensible course--and the most manly. why should not

you and I throw in our fortune together? I be sure we should suit

each other."



"I can but thank you," say Basil. "it be worth think over."



"all right; there be plenty of time before we. let we go into the

this part of the world."



"indeed we cannot. I will not forget your warning. to speak honestly,

and be dead beat."



"you would like to turn in," say Chaytor. "I can give you a

shakedown, and for supper what remain of a tin of biscuit and a tin

of sardine. there, don't say a word. the luck's on my side. come

along."



the Nonentities and Jim the Hatter be in the midst of a wrangle when

they enter, and scarcely notice they. this leave Chaytor free to

attend to Basil. he place before he the biscuit and sardine, and

produce a flask of brandy. Basil be grateful for the refreshment; he

be thoroughly exhausted, and it renew his strength and revive his

droop spirit. then he fill his pipe, and converse in low tone

with his new friend, while the gambler continue their game.



"if I stop up much long," say Basil, when he have have his smoke, "I

shall drop off my seat."



Chaytor rise and precede he to the further end of the store. the

building, if such a designation may be allow to an erection compose

of only wood and canvas, have be the most pretentious and impose in

the palmy day of the township, and although now it be all tattere

and tear, like the man in the nursery rhyme, it could still boast of

half a dozen private compartment in which sleeper could find repose

and solitude. the wall of course be of calico, and for complete

privacy darkness be necessary.



Chaytor and the three gambler who be bend over their card in

the dim light of the large space without, each occupy one of these

there be a stretcher in the room, a piece of strong canvas nail

upon four piece of batten drive into the ground. the canvas be

bare; there be no bedclothe.



"I have two blanket," say Chaytor, "I can spare you one."



Basil be too tired to protest. dress as he be he throw himself

upon the stretcher, draw the blanket over he, and bid his

hospitable friend good-night, and thank he again, be fast asleep

almost as the word pass his lip.



Newman Chaytor stand for a moment or two gaze upon the sleep man.

"I cann't be dream," he think; "he be here before I, and I be

wide awake. I drink to the future." he hold no glass, but he go

through the pantomime of drink out of one.



take the lighted candle with he he join his mate, and leave Basil

sleep calmly in darkness. they be no long play card, but

with head close together be debate in whisper. upon Chaytor's

entrance they shift their position and cease talk.



"have you put your gentleman to bed?" ask Jim the Hatter, in a

sneer tone in which a sinister ring might have be detect.



"much oblige to you for the inquiry," reply Chaytor, prepare to

fence; "he be sound asleep."



"interesting child! a case of love at first sight, mate."



Nonentities Numbers one and two nod, with dark look at Chaytor,

who smile genially at they and commence to smoke.



"or," say Jim the Hatter, "perhaps an old acquaintance."



"take your choice," observed Chaytor, who, in finesse and coolness,

be a match for the three.



"don't it strike you, Newman, that it's take a liberty with we to

feed and bolster he up, and stand drink as well, without ask

"not at all. the sardine be mine, the biscuit be mine, the grog

be mine. if you want to quarrel, say so."



"I'm for peace and quietness," say Jim the Hatter, threateningly. "I

be only express my opinion."



"and I mine. look here, mate, I don't want to behave shabbily, so

I'll tell you what be in my mind."



"ah, do," say Jim the Hatter, with a secret sign to the Nonentities

which Chaytor do not see; "then we shall know where we be."



"I'll tell you where we be, literally, mate. we're in a

heaven-forsake township, run fast to bone, which lead to

skeleton. now I'm not prepare for that positive eventuality just yet.

this world be good enough for I at present, and I mean to do my good

to enjoy it."



"cann't you enjoy it in our company?" ask Jim the Hatter.



"I think not," say Chaytor, with cool insolence. "the good of friend

must part."



"oh, that's your little game, be it?"



"that be my little game. I be grow grey. if I don't look out I

shall be white before I be thirty. really I think it must be the

effect of the company I have keep."



"we're not good enough for you, I suppose?"



"if you ask for my deliberate opinion I answer, most distinctly not.

no, mate, not by a long way good enough."



"don't be stick up, mate. well man than you have have to eat humble

pie."



"any sort of pie," say Chaytor, philosophically, "be well than no

pie at all. take my advice. bid good-bye to Gum Flat, gigantic fraud

that it be, and go in search of big nugget. that be what I be go

to do."



"with your gentleman friend?"



"with my gentleman friend. we may as well part civilly, but if you

choose the other thing I be agreeable." the three man rise with the

intention of retire. they do not respond to his invitation to part

friend. "well, good-night, and good luck to you." they nod surlily

and enter their sleep apartment, after exchange a few word

quietly between themselves.



Newman Chaytor help himself to brandy from his flask then fill his

pipe, and begin to smoke.



that he have something serious to think of be evident, and that he be

puzzle what use to make of it be quite as clear. an enterprise be

before he, and he be disposed to pledge himself to it; but he be in

the dark as to what end it would lead he. in the dark, also, how it

could be so conduct as to result in profit to himself. he be in

desperately low water, and have lose confidence in himself. his ship

be drift anchorless on a waste of water; suddenly an anchor have

present itself, which, while it would afford he peace and safety

for a time, might show he a way to a golden harbour. an ugly smile

wreathe his lip, the sinister aspect of which be hide by his

abundant hair: but it be there, and remain for many musing moment.

he take from his pocket a common memorandum book, and on a few blank

page he write the name, Newman Chaytor and Basil Whittingham,

several time and in several different style of handwriting. then he

write upon one, in the form of a check, "pay to Newman Chaytor, Esq.,

the sum of forty thousand pound. Basil Whittingham." he contemplate

this valueless draft for a long time before destroy it at the

candle's light, as he destroy the other sheet of paper upon which

he have write the signature.



"all the pleasure of existence," he muse, "all the light, everything

in the world worth have, be on the other side of the water. be I

bear to grind out my day in a prison like this? no, and I will not.

here be the chance of escape"--he turn his head to the room in which

Basil be sleeping--"with possibility which may give I all I

desire. it would be fly in the face of Providence to neglect it.

the first law of nature be Self. I should be a bear fool not to obey

the first law of nature."



in these reflection he pass an hour, when he determine to go to

bed.



all be still. he step on tip toe to each of the four compartment

occupy by Basil, Jim the Hatter, and the Nonentities, and listen

at the door to assure himself that he be the only wakeful person in

the store. deeming himself safe he enter his own room, and take a

small round mirror in a zinc frame from the top of a packing case

which serve as washstand and dressing-table, gaze at his face with

strange intentness. put the hand mirror down he cast wary look

around. yes, he be alone; there be no witness. then he do a

curious thing. he take off his beard and whisker.



in the room on his right lie Basil asleep; in the room on his left be

Jim the Hatter, whom he suppose to be. but in this he reckon

without his host, as many another sharp rogue have do in his time.

Jim the Hatter, despite his deep breathing, which have deceive Newman

Chaytor, be wide awake. the moment Chaytor enter his room Jim the

Hatter have slip noiselessly from his stretcher, and his face be

now glue to the wall of calico through which the light of Chaytor's

candle be shine. there be a small slit in the calico, which

enable Jim the Hatter to see what be pass in Chaytor's room.

Chaytor's back, however, be towards the wall through which he be

peep. the watcher be puzzle; he could not exactly discover what

it be Chaytor have do.



upon Chaytor's face, now beardless and whiskerless, there be a

natural growth of hair in the shape of a moustache. this moustache be

the precise colour of that which Basil grow and cherish. it be not

so long, but a few week's growth would make the resemblance perfect,

if such be Chaytor's wish. in other respect the resemblance between

he and Basil be remarkable. height, figure, complexion--even the

colour of the eye--all tally.



in his anxiety to discover exactly what be go on, Jim the hatter

make a slight movement, which be hear by Chaytor. he turn

suddenly, and the astonish watcher behold the counterpart of Basil.



"by Jove!" he say inly; "twin!"



then, warn by Chaytor's attitude that he be in danger of himself

be discover, he slip between his blanket as noiselessly as he

have slip out of they. wait only to resume his disguise of beard

and whisker, Chaytor, candle in hand, go quietly and swiftly into

the adjoining room and look down upon the recumbent form of Jim the

Hatter. undoubtedly asleep, and sleep like a top. chaytor pass

the candle across the man's face, who never so much as wink. assure

that there be no cause for alarm, Chaytor step back to his own

recess, put out the light, and go to bed.









CHAPTER XI.





leave this schemer to his ill-earn repose, we strip the veil from

his past and lie it bare.



nature play trick, but seldom play a strange than that of cast

Newman Chaytor physically in the same mould as Basil. bear in

different county, with no tie of kinship between their family,

their likeness to each other be so marvellous that any man see

they for the first time side by side, without some such disguise as

Chaytor wear on Gum Flat, and the second time apart, would have be

puzzle to know which be which. but not less strange than this

physical likeness be the contrast between their moral nature. one

be the soul of guilelessness and honour, the other the soul of

cunning and baseness. one walk the straight path of life, the other

choose the crooked.



Chaytor be bear in London, and his parent occupy a respectable

position. they give he a good education, and do all they could to

furnish he worthily for the battle of life. the affection they

display be ill-requite. in his mother's eye he be perfection,

but his father's mind be often disturb when he think of the lad's

future. perhaps in his own nature there be a moral twist which cause

he to doubt; perhaps his own youth be distinguish by the vice he

detect in his son. however that may be, he take no blame to himself,

prefer rather to skim the surface than to seek discomfort in

psychological depth.



the parent discuss their son's future.



"we will make a doctor of he," say the father.



"he will be a great physician," say the mother.



at this time chaytor be eighteen year of age. at twenty it be

decide that he be in the wrong groove; at least, that be the

statement of the doctor who have undertake his professional education.

it be not an entirely ingenuous statement; the master be eager to

get rid of his pupil, whose sharp practice distress he.



"what would you like to be?" ask his father.



"a lawyer," reply Chaytor.



"he will be Lord Chancellor," say his mother.



Thereupon Newman Chaytor be article to a firm of lawyer in Bedford

Row, London, W.C., an old and respectable firm, Messrs. Rivington,

son, and Rivington, who keep its exceedingly lucrative business in

the hand of its own family. it happen, fatefully, that this firm of

lawyer transact the affair of Bartholomew Whittingham, Basil's

uncle, with whom our reader have already make acquaintance.



in the course of two or three year Chaytor's character be fully

develop. he be still the idol of his mother, whose heart be plate

with so thick a shield of unreasone love that nothing to her son's

disparagement could make an impression upon it. only there be door

in this shield which she open at the least sign from the reprobate,

shelter he there and coo over he as none but such heart can.

her husband have the sincere affection for she, and here be another

safeguard for Chaytor.



the surrounding of life in a great and gay city be dangerous and

tempting even to the innocent. how much more dangerous and tempting

be they to those who by teaching or inclination be ripe for vice? it

be not our intention to follow chaytor through these devious path; we

shall simply touch lightly upon those circumstance of his career

which be pertinent to our story. if for a brief space we be

compel to treat of some of the dark shadow of human nature, it

must be set down to the undoubted fact that life be not make up

entirely of sweetness and light.



Chaytor's father, look through his bank-book, discover that he

have a balance to his credit less by a hundred pound than he know be

correct. he examine his return cheque and find one with his

signature for the exact amount, a signature write by another hand

than his. he inform his wife, pende his decision as to what step

to take to bring the guilt home. his wife inform her son.



"ah," say he, "I have my suspicion." and he mention the name of a

clerk in his father's employ.



the ball be set roll, the eld Chaytor begin to watch the

suspect man, set trap for he, across which the innocent man

step in safety. Mr. Chaytor be puzzle; he have, by his wife's

advice, keep the affair entirely secret, who in her turn have be

prompt by her son to this course, and warn not to drag his name

into it. the father, therefore be not aware that the accusation

against the clerk proceed from his son.



Chaytor have a design in view: he wish to gain time to avoid possible

unpleasant consequence.



some three week afterwards, when Mr. Chaytor have resolve to take the

forge cheque to the bank with the intention of enlist its service

in the discovery of the criminal, he go to his desk to obtain the

document. it be go, and other paper with it. he be confound;

without the cheque he could do nothing.



"have I a thief in my house," he ask of himself, "as well as a

forger at my elbow."



the man he have suspect be in the habit of come to his private

house once a week for clerk purpose. without consider what he

be lay himself open to, he accuse his clerk of rob he, and

the result be that the man leave his service and bring an action for

slander against he, which he be compel to compromise by an

apology and the payment of a sum of money.



"it be father's own fault," say Chaytor to his mother; "have he wait

and watch, he would have bring the guilt home to the fellow. but

don't say anything more to he about it; let the matter rest."



it do rest, but Mr. Chaytor do not forget it.



be in pursuit of pleasure Chaytor find himself in continual need

of money, and he raise and procure it in many discreditable way,

but still he manage to keep his secret. then come another crime. some

valuable jewel belong to his mother be steal. by whom?



"by one of the female servant, of course," say Chaytor.



he be not only without conscience, he be without heart.



Mr. Chaytor propose to call in a detective. Mrs. Chaytor, act upon

the secret advice of her son, would not hear of it. the father have,

therefore, two force work against he, his wife, whom he could

answer, because she be in the light, and his son, with whom he could

not cope, because he be in the dark.



"it would be a dreadful scandal," say young Chaytor to his mother.

"if nothing be discover--and thief be very cunning, you know--we

shall be in bad trouble than father get into with the clerk who

forge his name to the cheque. we should be the laugh-stock of

everyone who know we, and should hardly be able to raise our head."



his word be law to she; he could twist she round his little finger,

but he care not what occur to other so long as he ride in safety.



one day an old gentleman pay a visit to Messrs. Rivington, Sons, and

"the old fellow must be worth forty thousand pound," muse Chaytor,

for-ty thou-sand pound! for-ty thousand pound! and every shilling be

mistake I for he. what wouldn't I give to change place with he!

for-ty thou-sand pound! for-ty thou-sand pound! it's madden to

like. his hair be the colour of mine. I'll keep my eye on he."



it be an egregiously wicked idea, for by the wild stretch of his

on he, and as the young gentleman never enter the office of Messrs.

the resemblance between the man.



chaytor for a week be in his element; he ascertain from the hall

every movement attentively, until he declare to himself that he know

little trick of manner which he have observe. he be like a man

possess.



"he be a gentleman," he say. "so be I. I be as good look as he be

any day of the week. why shouldn't I be, be his double?



"he ponder over it, he dream of it, he work himself almost into a

fever concern it. distorted possibility present themselves, and

monstrous view. the phantom image of Basil enter into his life,

direct his thought, colour his future. he walk along the

street with this spectral Double by his side; he leant over the

river's bridge and see it reflect in the water; he feel its

presence when he wake up in the dark night. one night during this

long in Trafalgar Square that he draw the attention of a policeman to

possibility with an infatuation dangerous to his reason, until he come

not notice the hurrying people or their cry, and it be only when

the blaze flame be before he that he be conscious of what be

actually take place. and there on the burn roof as he look up

he behold the phantom Basil on fire. with glare eye he see it with

the flame devour it, dwindle in proportion until its luminous

outline fade into nothingness, until it be go out of the living

world for ever. a deep sigh of satisfaction escape he.



"now he be go," he think, "I will take his place. his uncle be an

old man; I can easily deceive he; and perhaps even _he_ will die

before morning."



in the midst of this ecstatic delirium a phantom hand be lay upon

his shoulder, a phantom face, with a mocking smile upon it, confront

forty thousand pound.



have this mental condition last long he must have go mad. the

reason for this would have be that he have nothing to grapple with,

nothing to fight, nothing but a shadow, which he have magnify into a

monstrous conception of what might occur in a day, in an hour, in a

moment, and he on the spot ready to take advantage of it.



shortly after Basil's departure he ask his mother if she ever have

twin.



"what on earth do you mean, my dear?" she ask, laugh at he.



"it be plain enough," he answer incautiously. "I dream sometimes of

a brother the exact counterpart of myself."



"you work too hard," say his mother, pityingly. "you must take a

holiday, my darling."



"who's to pay for it?" he ask gloomily.



"I be," she say fondly. "I have save fifty pound for you."



"give it to I," he say eagerly, and with the money he go to Paris

the foolish mother be continually do this kind of thing, save up

"send I in a registered packet, by first post, my will, the will I

make in favour of my nephew, Mr. Basil Whittingham. he have act like

a fool, and I be go to destroy it and disinherit he. at some

future time I will give you instruction to draw up another, make

different disposition of my property. I be not a young man, but I

shall live a good many year yet, and there be plenty of time before

I. meanwhile bear witness by this letter that I have disinherit my

nephew Basil Whittingham."



of course they follow his instruction, and the will be forward

to he.



"he have steal forty thousand pound from I," think Chaytor.



within a week thereafter he overhear a conversation between two of

the principal. he be never above listen at door and creep up

back staircase. the lawyer be speak of Bartholomew Whittingham

and the will.



"will he destroy it?" ask one.



"I think not," reply the other. "it be my opinion he will keep it by

he, half intend to destroy it, half to preserve it, and that it

will be find intact and unaltered when he die."



"I do not agree with you. he will destroy it one day in a rage, and

make another the next."



"in favour of whom?"



"of his nephew. he have in his heart an absorb love for the young

gentleman, and he be a good fellow at bottom. Mr. Basil Whittingham

will come into the whole of the property."



the conversation be continue on these line, and the partner

ultimately agree that after all Basil would be the heir. "there be a

chance yet," think Chaytor, for although the dangerous period of

ecstasy be pass there still linger in his mind a hope of

fortunate possibility.



he continue his evil course, gamble, drank, and lead a free life,

get deep and deep into debt. his mother assist he out of

many a scrape, and never for one single moment waver in her faith in

Chaytor's position grow so desperate that detection and its attendant

"for your mother's sake," invariably he say.



"yes, yes," murmur the hypocrite, "for my dear mother's sake--my

mother, so good, so loving, so tender-hearted!"



"let this be the last time," say the father sternly.



"it shall be, it shall be!" murmur the son.



it be a formula. the father may sometimes have deceive himself into

belief; the son, never. even while he be humble himself he would be

cast about for the next throw.



this continue for some considerable time, but at length come the

"I must be off," say Chaytor. "mustn't be late this morning. a lot to

attend to at the office."



"you need not hurry," say the father. "I have something to say to

you."



"willn't it keep till the evening?"



"no. it must be say here and now." he step to the door and lock

it. "we will spare she as long as possible; she will know soon

enough."



"oh, all right," say Chaytor sullenly. "fire away."



the father take out his letter, and, select one, hand it to his

son who read it, shiver, and return it.



"what have you to say to it?" ask the father.



"nothing. it be only for three hundred pound."



"a bill, due to-day, which I do not sign."



"it be do for all our sake, to save the honour of the family name.

I be in a hole and there be no other way of get out of it."



"the bill must be take up before twelve o'clock."



"will it be?"



"it will, for your mother's sake."



"then there be nothing more to be say. I be very sorry, but it could

"I take neither your word nor your oath. you be a scoundrel."

"unhappily. if your mother be not live you should be show into

"but she be alive. I shall not appear in the dock, and you may as well

drag through the mire."



"I hope," say Chaytor, with vile sanctimoniousness, "that my dear

"there be, I must remind you, another side to the shield. I say 'as

"well, you can afford it."



"I cannot," say Mr. Chaytor, with a sour smile. "my career snap

weather the storm, but as I be situate it be impossible. it be

almost a triumph to I to stand here before you a ruin man, know

you can no long rob I."



"still I do not believe you," say Chaytor.



"wait and see; you will not have to wait long."



the tone in which he utter this carry conviction with it.

ruin _I!_"



"what!" respond Mr. Chaytor, with savage sarcasm. "be there any more

advise, and prepare for it. in the list of my liability, which be

"do _you_ see to what you have bring _I?_" exclaim Chaytor in

"because I hope by other speculation to set myself straight. but

everything have go wrong--everything. Understand, I cannot trouble

"the settlement I make upon she in the day of my prosperity be her

absolutely, and only she can deal with it. in the settlement of my

"but it shall go," think Chaytor, with secret joy, "to get I out of

"do they give any reason for it?" ask Chaytor, inwardly not greatly

"none; nor shall I ask any question of they or you. you know how the

he unlock the door, and leave the house. this be just what Chaytor

"between ourselves, mother," he say.



"yes, my darling, between ourselves."



"Father must not know. he be always hard on I. he think he can

manage everybody's affair, but he cannot manage his own." then he

manage for he it would not have happen. trust everything to I,

mother, and this day year I will treble your little fortune for you.

let I have a chance for once. when I have make all our fortune you

shall go to he and say, 'see what Newman have do for we.'"



"it shall be exactly as you say, darling. you be the good, the

handsome, the clever son a foolish mother ever have."



Kisses and caress seal the bargain. within twenty-four hour he

know that everything his father have tell he be true. the family be

utterly beggar. they move into a small house and practise

shill his mother possess, and before the year be out the sun

rise upon a ship beat on the rock.



"be you satisfied?" ask his father, from whom Chaytor's doing

could no long be conceal.



"satisfy!" cry Chaytor, tremble in every limb. "when your insane

speculation have ruin we!"



then he fall into a chair and begin to sob. he have the good of reason

for tribulation. with his mind's eye he see the prison door open to

receive he. it be not shame that make he suffer; it be fear.



again, and for the last time, he go to his mother for help.



"what can I do, my boy?" quaver the poor woman. "what can I do? I

haven't a shilling in the world."



he implore she to go to his father. "he can save I," cry the

terror-stricken wretch. "he can, he can!"



she obey he and the father send for his son.



"tell I all," he say. "conceal nothing, or, as there be a heaven

above we, I leave you to your fate."



the shameful story tell, the father say, "thing be look up with

I, but here be another knock-down blow, and from my own flesh and

blood. I accept it, and will submit once more to be ruin by you."



"bless you, father, bless you," whine Chaytor, take his father's

hand and attempt to fondle it. Mr. Chaytor pluck his hand away.



"there be, however, a condition attach to the promise."



"what condition?" falter Chaytor.



"that you leave England and never return. do you hear I? never. you

will go to the other end of the world, where you will end your day.



"to Australia?"



"to Australia. when you quit this country I wish never to hear from

you; I shall regard you as dead. you shall no long trade upon your

refuse."



"I accept."



"very well. go from this house and never let I look upon your face

again."



"can I not see my mother?" whine Chaytor, "to wish her good-bye?"



"no. you want to hatch further trouble. you shall not do so. quit my

house."



with head bent low in mock humility, Chaytor leave the house. he have no

sincere wish to see his mother; he have get out of she all he could,

and she be of no use to he in the future. the promise his father

make be fulfil; the fresh forgery he have perpetrate be buy
