Chaytor determine to remain in England till within a week or two of

its become due; something might turn up which would enable he to

meet it. he love the excitement of english life; Australia be

banishment; but perhaps after all, if he be force to go it might be

the making of he. he have read of rough man make fortune in a week

on the goldfield. why should not he?



the last blow prove too much for Mr. Chaytor; it break he up

utterly. he be seize with a serious illness which reduce he to

imbecility. the home have to be sell, and he and his wife remove to

lodging, one small room at the top of a house in a poor

neighbourhood. there poverty fall upon they like a wolf. five week

afterwards Chaytor, slouch through the street on a rainy night,

see his mother beg in the roadway. the poor soul stand mute, with

a box of match in her hand. Chaytor turn and flee.



"I be the unluckiest dog that ever be bear," he mutter. "just as I

be go to see if I could get anything out of she!"



it be now imperative that he should leave England, and he manage to

get a passage in a sailing vessel as assistant steward at a shilling a

month. he obtain it by mean of forge letter of recommendation,

and he go out in a false name. this he would have retain have it

not be that shortly after his arrival in Australia he meet a man who

have know he in London, and who address he by his proper name. it

be not the only inconvenience to which an alias subject he. there

be only one address in the colony through which he could obtain his

letter, and that be the Post Office. obviously, if he call himself

John Smith he could not expect letter to be deliver to he in the

name of Newman Chaytor. now, he be eager for letter from the old

country; before he leave it he have write to his mother to the effect

that he be drive out of it by a hard-hearted father, and that if she

have any good news to communicate to he he would be glad to hear from

she. at the same time he impose upon she the obligation of not

let anyone know where he be. therefore, when his London

acquaintance address he by his proper name, say, "Hallo,

Chaytor, old boy!" he say to himself, "oh hang it! I'll stick to

Newman Chaytor, and chance it. if mother write to I I shall have to

proclaim myself Chaytor; an alias might get I into all sort of

trouble."



why do he write to his poor mother, for whom he have not the least

affection, and what do he mean by expect she to have any good news

to communicate to he? the last time he see she, be she not beg

in the street? well, there be a clear reason; he seldom do anything

without one; and be sure that the kernel of that reason be Self. his

father, from the wreck of his fortune, have manage to preserve a

number of share in some company which have fail, among they two

mining company which have come to grief. now, it have happen before

and might happen again, that company which be valueless one day

have leap into favour the next, that share which yesterday could

have be purchase for a song, to-morrow would be worth thousand of

pound. suppose that this happen to the company, or to one of

they, in which his pauper father hold share. he be his father's only

child, and his mother would see that he be not disinherit. chaytor

be a man who never throw away a chance, and he would not throw away

this, remote as it be. hence his determination to adhere at all

hazard to his proper name. the perilous excitement of the last two

or three year have drive Basil Whittingham out of his mind, but

have more leisure and less to occupy his thought in the colony,

he think of he now and then, and wonder whether the old uncle have

relent and have take his nephew again into his favour. "lucky young

beggar," he think. "I wish I stand in his shoe, and he in mine. I

would soon work the old codger into a proper mood." his colonial

career be neither profitable nor creditable, and he have degenerate

into what he be when he and Basil come face to face in Gum Flat, an

unadulterate gambler and loafer. the strange encounter awake within

he force which have long lain dormant. he recognise a possible

chance which might be work to his benefit, and he fasten to it

like a limpet. when he say to Basil that he be in luck be really

mean it.



a word as to his false beard and whisker. in London he have have a

behind-the-scene acquaintance, and in a private theatrical

performance in which he play a part he have wear these identical

appendage as an adjunct to the character he represent. he have

bring they out with he, think they might be serviceable one day.

before he come to Gum Flat he have get into a scrape on another

township, and when he leave it, have assume the false hair as a kind of

disguise. make his appearance on Gum Flat thus disguise, he deem

it prudent to retain it, and when he come into association with Basil

he thank his star that he have do so; otherwise he might have

draw upon himself from the man he call his double a close

attention than he desire.









CHAPTER XII.





in the middle of the night Basil awake. he have have a tiring day, but

when he have sleep off the first effect of the fatigue he have

undergo, the exciting event of the last two day become again the

dominant power. he dream of all that have occur from the interview

guardianship of Annette, to the moment of his arrival on Gum Flat. of

Newman Chaytor he dream not at all; this new acquaintance have

produce no abide impression upon he.



he lie awake for some five minute or so in that condition of

the first time in a strange bed and in a place with which they be not

familiar. where be he? what be the position of the bed? where be

the door situate: at the foot, or the head, or the side of the bed?

be there a window in the apartment, and if so, where be it? then

come the mental question what have arouse he?



it be so unusual for he to wake in the middle of the night that he

dwelt upon this question. something must have disturb he. what?



be it fancy that just at the moment of his awakening he have hear a

still. suddenly he sit straight up on the stretcher. he remember

that he be in the township of Gum Flat, sleep in a strange

there be nothing to fear from he, but the other three have gaze at

address a word to he. it be Chaytor, and Chaytor alone, who have

show kindness and evince a kindly feeling. and now he be certain

that someone have be in the room while he sleep, and have lay hand

on he. for what purpose?



he slide from the stretcher, and stand upright stretch out his

hand in the darkness. where be the door?



outside the canvas building stand Chaytor's three mate, wide awake,

with their head close together, as they have be inside on the return

of Basil and Chaytor from the stable. they be converse in

whisper.



"do he hear you?"



"no. if he have move I would have knock he on the head."



"have you get it?"



"yes, it be all right."



"pass it round."



"no; I will keep it till it's sell; then we'll divide equally."



"what do you think it's worth?"



"twenty pound, I should say."



"little enough."



"hush!"



the sound of Basil move about his room, grope for the door, have

reach they.



"if he come out, Jim, you tackle he."



"leave he to I. don't waste any more time. get the horse from the

stable."



Basil, unable to find the door, stumble against the calico portion

which divide his room from that in which Chaytor sleep.



"who's there?" cry Chaytor, jump up.



"oh, it's you," say Basil, recognise the voice. "have you get a

light?"



"wait a moment."



but half dress he represent himself to Basil, with a light

candle in his hand.



"what's up?" he ask.



"I don't know," reply Basil, "but I be not easy in my mind. perhaps

it be only my fancy, but I have an idea that someone have be in my

room."



"let we see." they proceed to the three compartment which should

have be occupy by the three man. they be empty.



"it be not fancy," say Basil. "what mischief be they up to? come

along; we will go and see."



Chaytor hesitate. he be not gift with heroic quality, and he

know that his three mate be desperate character.



"do you have any money about you?" he ask.



"none. why, where's my watch?" it be go. there be a hurried

stand by I, will you?"



he rush out, follow, but not too quickly, by Chaytor. the moment

he reach the open a pair of arm be throw around he, and he be

grapple with an enemy. in unfamiliar ground, envelop in darkness,

distance, but now that there be a lull he come close to Basil.



"they have half throttle I," he gasp, tear open his shirt and

blow like a grampus. "be you hurt?"



"no," say Basil. "we may catch they yet."



and he begin to run, but the ruffian have get the start of he, and

know the lay of the ground. guide by his ear he stumble on, across

from time to time, "o, O, O!"), and thus the flight and the pursuit

continue, the sound from the fly man and Old Corrie's horse

grow fainter and fainter, until matter come to a sudden

termination.



half-way up the range, which be vein with quartz, a shaft have be

golden reef. but the spur grow thin and thin, the trace of gold

disappear, and they lose heart. disappointed in their expectation,

and out of patience with their profitless labour, they shoulder

their windlass and start off to fresh pasture. thus the mouth of

and feel himself slip down with perilous celerity.



it be fortunate that the shaft be not exactly perpendicular, after

this passage be about two yard long, when the spur dip again, and

the shaft be continue sheer into the bowel of the earth. it be

twenty foot he feel bottom, and there he lay, bruise, but not

dangerously hurt.



he cry out for help at the top of his voice, and his cry be

presently answer.



"below there!" cry Chaytor, lie flat on the ground above, with his

ear at the mouth of the shaft.



"be that you, Mr. Chaytor?" cry Basil.



Chaytor (aside): "he remember my name." (Aloud): "yes, what's leave of

I. where be you?" (which, to say the least of it, be an unnecessary

question.)



Basil: "down here."



Chaytor (blind to logical fact): "alive?"



Basil (perceive nothing strange in the question, and therefore

almost as blind): "yes, thank God!"



chaytor: "any bone break?"



Basil: "I think not, but I be bruise a bit."



chaytor: "so be I."



Basil: "I be sorry to hear it. have the scoundrel get away?"



chaytor: "yes, they're a mile off by this time."



Basil (groaning): "Old Corrie's mare! what will he think of I?"



chaytor: "it cann't be help."



Basil: "in which direction have they go?"



chaytor: "haven't the slight idea. I warn you against they."



Basil: "you do. you're a good fellow, but what could I do?"



chaytor: "neither of we could have prevent it."



Basil: "I be not so sure. I ought to have stop up all night, and

look after what ben't my own."



Chaytor (attempt consolation): "why, you couldn't keep your eye

open."



Basil (groan again): "I ought to have keep my eye open. I have no

right to sleep after your warning."



chaytor: "I do what I could."



Basil: "you do; you're a true friend." (Chaytor smile.) "how be I to

get up from here?"



chaytor: "that's the question. how far be you down?"



Basil: "Heaven know. it seem a mile or so."



chaytor: "there's no windlass."



Basil: "ben't there?"



chaytor: "and it's pitch dark."



Basil: "it's as black as night down here. cann't you go for help?"



chaytor: "I'll tell you something. there ben't a soul on the township

but ourselves."



Basil: "not one?"



chaytor: "not one. we must wait till daylight; then I'll see what I

can do."



Basil: "there's no help for it; it must be as you say. you'll not

desert I?"



Chaytor (in an injure tone): "can you think I capable of so

dastardly an act?"



Basil: "forgive I; I hardly know what I'm say. I deserve that you

should, for give utterance to a thought so base."



chaytor: "it be natural, perhaps. why should you trust I, a

stranger, whom you have know for only a few hour?"



Basil: "I do trust you: it be an unnatural thought. you be a noble

fellow--and a gentleman."



chaytor: "I hope so. can I do anything for you while you be wait?"



Basil: "I be devour by thirst. can you manage to get a drink of

water to I?"



chaytor: "I can do that; but you must have patience. I shall have to

go back to the township to get a bottle and some string. shall I go?"



Basil: "yes, yes. be as quick as you can."



chaytor: "I willn't be a moment long than I can help."



then there be silence. Chaytor depart on his errand, and Basil be

leave to himself. his right arm be bruise and sore, but he contrive

to feel in his pocket for match. a box be there, but it be empty,

and he remember that he have strike the last one at the end of his

long ride from Bidaud's plantation, just before he arrive in Gum

Flat. he know, from feel the opening of the adit, that it be

likely he be not at the bottom of the shaft, and he be fearful of

move, lest he should fall into a pit. he think of Newman Chaytor.

"what a good fellow he be! I should be dead but for he. it be truly

noble of he to stick to I as he be do. he have nothing to gain by

it, and he be save my life. yes, I will accept his proposal to go

mate with he, for I have no place now on Bidaud's plantation. poor

Annette--poor child! I hope she will be happy. I hope her uncle and

Aunt will be kind to she. I must see she again before I go for good,

and then we shall never meet again, never, never! I would give the

good twenty year of my life--if I be fate to live--to be her

brother, with authority to protect she and shield she from Gilbert

Bidaud. he be a villain, a smooth-tongue villain, a thousand time

bad than these scoundrel who have rob I and bring I to this.

what will Old Corrie say when he hear I have lose his mare? will he

think I be lie--will he think I have sell his horse and pocket the

money? if so, and it get to Annette's ear, how she will despise I!

I must see she, I must, to clear myself. Gilbert Bidaud will do all he

can to prevent it, and he may succeed; but I will try, I will try. if

I have a hundred pound I would buy another horse for Old Corrie, a

well one than that I have lose, but I haven't a shilling. a sorry

plight. there be only one human being in the world I can call a

friend, and that be Mr. Chaytor, who have take such a strange fancy

for I. yesterday there be Old Corrie, there be Anthony Bidaud,

there be Annette. one be dead, the other may cast I off, it be a

cruel world. how long Mr. Chaytor be! it seem an age. shame on you,

Basil, for revile! there be goodness, there be sweetness, there be

faithfulness in the world. don't whine, old man. all may yet be well,

though for the life of I I cann't see how it be to be bring about."



then he faint, but only for a few second; when he open his eye

again he think hour must have elapse.



in truth chaytor be absent no long than be necessary, but he be

also mentally busy with the adventure of the last few hour. the man

whose phantom shadow have haunt he in London be now at his mercy.

Basil's life be absolutely at his disposal. to leave he where he be

in that desolate spot at the bottom of a deserted shaft would be to

ensure for he a sure and certain death, and if he wish to make

assurance doubly sure, all he have to do would be to roll a great stone

upon he. but that would be a crime, and, harden as he be, he

shrink from commit it. not from any impulse of mercy, but because

he have nothing at present to gain from it. there be much to learn,

much to do before he nerve himself to a desperate deed which, after

all, might by some stroke of good fortune be unnecessary. and indeed

it be only the accident which have befall Basil that darken his

soul with cruel suggestion. the sleep force which lurk in the

soul of such man as Newman Chaytor often leap into active life by

some unfortuitous circumstance in which they have no direct hand.



he be back at the shaft, lean over it, with a bottle of water not

too tightly cork, to the neck of which be attach a long piece of

cord.



"be you there?" he call out.



"Heaven be thank!" say Basil. "what a time you have be."



"I have not be away an hour."



"be that really so?"



"it be, but it must have seem long to you."



"week seem to have pass."



"I have a bottle of water which I will send down to you."



"God bless you!"



"when you get it, loosen the string from the neck of the bottle, and I

will send down what remain of the flask of brandy. it will do you no

harm."



"I can never repay you for your goodness to I."



"yes, you can. look out."



the bottle of water be lower, and afterwards the flask of brandy:

Basil take a long draught of water, half empty the bottle, and

sip sparingly of the brandy.



"you have give I life, Mr. Chaytor."



"Psha! I have do nothing worth make a fuss about. oblige I by

drop the Mr."



"I will. with all my heart and soul I thank you, Chaytor."



"you be heartily welcome, Basil. there be a light come into the

sky."



"Sunrise! how beautiful the world be!"



"listen," say chaytor; "I will tell you what I be go to do."









CHAPTER XIII.





"I be listen," say Basil.



"there be no windlass, as I have tell you," say Chaytor, "so I must

devise something in its place to pull you up. the mischief be that I

be alone, and have no one to help I. however, I must do the good I

can. I be go to roll the trunk of a tree to the top of the shaft,

then tie a rope firmly round it so that you can climb into the world

again. it must be dreadful down there."



"it be," groan Basil.



"I can imagine it," say Chaytor, complacently; "but you mustn't mind

bide a bit. no man could do more than I be do."



"indeed he could not."



"the tree be six or seven hundred yard off, and I daresay I shall be

an hour over the job. I cann't help that, you know."



"of course you cann't. I cann't find word to express my gratitude for

all the trouble you be take. and for a stranger, too!"



"I don't look upon you as a stranger; I feel as if I have know you all

my life. I suppose, though, it be really but the commencement of a

friendship which will last I hope till we be both old man."



"I hope so too."



"a little while ago I be say to myself, I will never trust another

man as long as I live; I will never believe in another; I will never

again confide in man or woman. I have be deceive, Basil."



"I be truly sorry to hear it."



"yes, I have be deceive. friend after friend have I trust, have I

help, have I ruin myself for, to find they in the end false,

selfish and unreliable. I be fill with disgust and with shame for

my specie. 'I renounce you all,' I cry in the bitterness of my

soul. but now everything seems change. since you come my faith in

human goodness and sincerity and truth be restore. I don't know why,

but it be so. I can rely upon your friendship, Basil?"



"you can. I will never forget your goodness; never."



"I be go, now, to roll the tree to the shaft. be as patient as you

can."



he do not go far. the slim trunk that he speak of lay not six or

seven hundred yard off, but quite close to the shaft, and he know

that Basil in his pursuit of the robber could not have observe it.

he be master of the situation; Basil be at his mercy, and every word

he have utter be intend to bind the unsuspicious man more firmly

to he. "he be a soft-hearted fool," think Chaytor, "and I shall be

able to bend he any way I please through the gratitude he feel for

I. I think I speak rather well. what be this?" he stoop and pick

up a pocket-book which have slip from Basil's pocket as he run after

the thief.



retreat still far from the shaft, to make assurance doubly

sure, Chaytor, with eager finger and a greedy expectancy in his eye

open the book and examine the content. intrinsically they be of

no value, but in their relation to the unformed design which be

prompt Chaytor's action their value be inestimable. there be

memoranda of date, event, name and address, and also some old

letter. any possible use of the latter do not occur to Chaytor, but

his examination of the former be almost instantly suggestive. they

be in Basil's handwriting, some be date and sign "B. W.", and

would serve admirably as copy for anyone who desire to imitate the

writing. clear up and down stroke, without twist or eccentric

curve, straightforward as Basil himself. "this be a find," think

chaytor; "providence be certainly on my side. in a week I shall be

able to write so exactly like Basil that he will be ready to swear my

writing be his. there be information, too, in the book which may prove

serviceable. I'll stick to he while there's a chance, and contrive so

that he shall stick to I. I haven't do badly up to now."



More than an hour do Chaytor employ in cunning cogitation, smoke

the while in a state of comfortable haziness as to the future.

Imagination gild the prospect and clothe it with alluring fancy;

and that the road which lead to it be dark and devious do not deter

he from revel in the contemplation. time be up. pant and

blow, he roll the tree-trunk to the shaft.



"below there!" he call out.



"ah!" reply Basil; "you be back again."



"I have have a terrible job," say the hypocrite, "and almost despair

of accomplish it, but stout heart and willing hand put strength

into a fellow, and the tree be here. look out for yourself while I

roll it across the shaft. the earth may be rotten, and some bit will

roll down, perhaps, though I'll do all I can to prevent it."



"thank you, a thousand, thousand time. there's a little tunnel here;

I'll get into it while you're at work above."



with loud evidence of arduous toil Chaytor place the trunk in

position, and then make the rope secure around it.



"now," say Chaytor, "all be ready, Basil, and I'm go to lower the

rope. have you get it?"



"yes," reply Basil, in a faint tone.



"you will have to pull yourself up by it. I will keep the rope as

tight and steady as I can, and that be as much as I can do. do you

think you will be able to manage it?"



"I must try, but I feel very weak. my strength be give way."



"don't let it, old fellow. pluck up courage; it's only for a few

minute, and then you will be safe at the top. now then, with a will."



it require a will on Basil's part, he be so weak, and more than once

he fear that it be all over with he; but at length the difficult

feat be accomplish, and, with daylight shine once more on he, he

reach the top, and be pull from the mouth of the shaft by

Chaytor's strong arm. then, his strength quite gone, he sink lifeless

to the ground.



Chaytor, gaze upon the helpless form, reflect. he have Basil's

pocket-book pack safely away in an inner pocket of his waistcoat,

one of those pocket which man who have anything to conceal, or who

move in lawless place, have make in their garment. this book

contain much that might be useful; for instance, the correct name

and address of Basil's uncle in England, a statement of the debt

which Basil have pay to keep his dead father's name clear from

reproach, the address of the lawyer who have manage that transaction,

the amount of the fortune that Basil's mother have bequeath to he,

and other such matter. now, have Basil anything more upon his person

which might be turn to account in the future? if so, this be a

favourable opportunity for Chaytor to possess himself of it. there

would be no difficulty in satisfactorily explain the loss of any

property which Basil have about he. in the confusion and excitement of

the last few hour anything might have happen.



having decide the point, Chaytor's unscrupulous finger become busy,

and every article in Basil's pocket pass through his hand. with

the exception of a purse, he replace everything he have take out.

this purse contain a locket with a lock of hair in it: at the back

of the locket be an inscription in Basil's writing--"My dear mother's

hair," her christian name, the date of her death, and her age. there

be no money in the purse. Undoubtedly Basil, when he recover his

sense, would miss his purse, but if his pocket-book slip out of

his pocket while run, why not that? chaytor be perfectly easy in

his mind as he deposit the purse by the side of the pocket-book

inside his waistcoat.



meanwhile Basil lie motionless. "I'll carry he a little way," think

Chaytor. "anything might drop from his clothe while he be hang

over my shoulder. I'll have as many arrow to my bow as I can

manufacture. when he get to his sense we will have a hunt for the

purse and the pocket-book, and of course shall not find they." with a

grim smile he raise Basil to a sitting posture, and gradually lift

he on to his shoulder. clasp he firmly round the body, Chaytor

stagger on.



Basil be no light weight, and Chaytor, while he be pursue his

dissipate life in London, have not be renowned for strength; but his

colonial career have harden his muscle, and enable he now to

perform a task which in year go by would have be impossible. a

dozen time he stop to rest and wipe his brow. the form he carry

be helpless and inert, but Basil's mind be stir by the motion of

be carry through the fresh air, and he begin to babble. he

think he be upon old Corrie's mare, and he urge the animal on,

mutter in disjoint and unconnected word that he must reach the

township of Gum Flat that night, and be back again next day. then he

go on to babble about Annette and her father, and to a less

intelligent man than Chaytor--give he credit for that--his wandering

talk might have be incoherent and meaningless. but Chaytor's

intellect be refine and sharpen by the possibility of a gild

future. he listen attentively to every word that fall from Basil's

fevere lip, and put meaning to they, sometimes false sometimes true.



"my friend Basil be in a delirium," say he during the interval of

Basil's muttering, "and I shall have to nurse he through a fever most

likely. what with that probability, and the weight of he, I be

earn my wage. no man can dispute that. he rave like a man in love

about this Annette. how old be she? be she pretty? do she love he?

will she be rich? be that a vein I could work to profit? I don't

intend to throw away the shadow of a chance. an age seem to have

pass since last night. but what," he cry suddenly, "if

all my labour be be throw away--what if I be follow a

will-o'-the-wisp?"



he let Basil slip purposely from his arm, and heedless of the sick

man's groan, for the fall be violent, he look down upon he as

though a mortal enemy be in his path. but one of the strong

element in greed and avarice be the hope that lead their votary

on, and this and the superstitious feeling that the meeting have be

bring about by fate, and be but the beginning of a fruitful end,

dispel the doubt that have arise. "I will work for it," he

mutter; "it be my only chance. even if nothing come of it I shall

be no bad off. but something _shall_ come of it--I swear it."



reassure, he take up his burden, and in the course of an hour reach

the dwelling they have occupy the previous night. by that time Basil

be in a high fever, and Chaytor begin to be disturb by the fear

that his double would die. then, indeed, his labour would be lose and

his hope destroy, for he have much to learn and much to do before

the vague design which spur he on could be develop and ripen.



Chaytor have a secret store of provision which he have hoard up

unknown to Jim the Hatter and the Nonentities; some tin of preserve

meat and soup, the remain of a sack of flour, two or three pound of

tea, a few bottle of spirit, and a supply of tobacco. these would

have serve for a long time than Basil's sickness last, and

Chaytor comfort himself with the reflection that he could not have

carry they away with he have he be compel to leave the deserted

township. it be really Basil's stout and healthy constitution that

pull he through a fever which would have prove fatal to many man.

he do not recover his sense for sixteen day, and as he have nothing

to conceal he, during that time, reveal to Chaytor in his wild

wander much of his early life. when at length he open his eye,

and they fall with dawn consciousness upon the man stand beside

his bed, Chaytor be in possession of particular innocent enough in

themselves, but dangerous if intend to be use to a wily and

dangerous end. during those sixteen day Chaytor have not be idle,

having employ himself industriously in study and imitate the

few peculiarity in Basil's writing. to a past-master like himself

this be not difficult, and he succeed in produce an imitation so

perfect as to deceive anyone familiar with Basil's style. he be

careful in destroy every evidence of this vile study.



Basil's eye fall upon Chaytor's face, and he be silent awhile.

Chaytor also. Basil close his eye, open they again, and fall to

once more ponder upon matter. then Chaytor speak.



"do you know I at last?" he ask.



"know you! at last!" echo Basil. "I have see you before--but

where?"



"here, in Gum Flat township."



"I be in Gum Flat township. yes, I remember, I be ride that way on

old Corrie's mare." he jump up, or rather try to do so, his weak

state frustrate his intention.



"where be the robber?"



"that's the question," say Chaytor, "and echo answer. not very

satisfactory."



"it be come back to I little by little," say Basil presently. "I

arrive here late at night and find the township desert by all but

four man, three of they scoundrel, the fourth a noble fellow whose

name be--be--what have happen to I that my memory play I trick?

I have it now--whose name be Newman Chaytor."



"a true bill. he stand before you."



"you be the man. what occur next? he find a stable for Old

Corrie's mare, give I food and a bed, while the three scoundrel

look on frown. I sleep like an unfaithful steward; the mare be

Corrie's and not mine, and I doubtful of the character and intention

of the scoundrel, I should have keep watch over property that do not

belong to I. instead of do that I consult my own ease and

pleasure."



"you could not help it; you be tired out."



"no excuse. I make no attempt to guard Old Corrie's mare. if I have

watch and fall asleep from weariness at my post it might have be

another matter. when I present myself to Old Corrie, that be if I be

ever able to stand upon my leg again I shall put no gloss upon my

conduct. he shall hear the plain unvarnished truth from the unfaithful

steward's own lip. I be unworthy of confidence or friendship; I warn

you, Newman Chaytor, put no trust in I."



"I would trust you," say Chaytor, with well-simulate candour, "with

my life."



"the more fool you. where be I? oh, asleep in the comfortable bed you

give I while these scoundrel be plan robbery. in the middle of

the night I wake up--pitch dark it was----forgive I for speak

ungratefully to you. my heart be overflow with gratitude, but I be

at the same time fill with remorse."



"don't trouble about that, Basil," say Chaytor. "you cann't hurt

yourself in my esteem. go on with your reminiscence; it be a healthy

exercise; it will strengthen your wandering memory."



"pitch dark it be. I be not sure then, but I be now, that thief

have be in my room. have I be lie here long, chaytor?"



"two week and more."



"and you have be nurse I all that time?"



"as well as I could. you could have find no other nurse--though easy

to find a well--in Gum Flat; you and I be the only two live

human in the township."



"why do you not leave I to die?"



"because I be not quite a brute."



"forgive I for provoke such a reply. but why--indeed, why have you

be so good to I?"



"I will answer you honestly, Basil. because I love you."



he lower his voice and bent his eye to the ground as he make the

false statement; and Basil turn his head, and a little sob escape

he at the expression of devotion.



"I hope I may live to repay you," he say, hold out his hand, which

Chaytor seize.



"you will. all I ask of you be not to desert I. Stick to I as a

friend, as I have stick to you; I have be so basely deceive in

friendship that my faith in human goodness would be irrevocably

shatter if you prove false." his voice falter; tear come into his

eye.



"that will I never do. my life be yours."



"I want your heart."



"you have it. the world contain no nobler man than my friend, Newman

Chaytor."



"I be well repay. now you must rest; you have talk enough."



"no, I will finish first. hear sound outside the tent I call for

your assistance. we go out together and be immediately attack.

be you much hurt, chaytor?"



"a little," reply Chaytor, modestly. "a scratch or two not worth

mention."



"it be like you to make light of your own injury. we pursue the

scoundrel through the darkness, but they know the ground they be

travel, we do not. an uncovered shaft lie in my way, and down I

fall. that be all I remember. but I know that my bone would be

bleach there at the present moment if it have not be for you."



"try to remember a little more," say Chaytor, anxious that not a

grain of credit should be lose to he. "I come up to the shaft sorely

bruise, and call out to you."



"yes, yes, it come back to I. you bring I some brandy--you

cheer and comfort I--you roll the trunk of a tree over the

mouth of the shaft--it be half a mile away--and after hour of

terrible agony I be bring into the sweet light of day. but for you

I should have die. indeed and indeed, I remember nothing more. you

must tell I the rest."



this Chaytor do with an affectation of modesty, but with absolute

exaggeration of the service he have render, and Basil lay and

listen, and his heart go out to the man who have prove so devoted

a friend, and have sacrifice so much for his sake.



"my gratitude be yours to my die day," he say. "no man ever do for

another what you have do for I. give I my clothe."



"you be not strong enough yet to get up, Basil."



"I don't want to get up. I want to see what the scoundrel have leave

in my pocket." he feel, and cry: "everything go! my purse, my

pocket-book, everything--even a lock of my mother's hair. they might

have leave I that!"



"they make a clean sweep, I suppose," say Chaytor.



he have consider this matter while Basil lie unconscious, and have

come to the conclusion that it would be wise to strip Basil's pocket

bare than to make a selection of one or two thing, which be scarcely

what a thief in his haste would have do. thus it be that Basil

find his pocket completely empty.



"you have for a friend the neediest beggar that ever draw breath,"

say Basil bitterly.



"I'll put up with that," say Chaytor, with great cheerfulness. "now,

don't worry yourself about anything whatev. you shall share with I

to the last pipe of tobacco, and when that's go we will work for

more."



"ah, tobacco! would a whiff or two do I any harm?"



"do you good. you'll have to smoke out of my pipe; the villain have

steal your."



he fill his pipe, and, give it to Basil, hold a lighted match to

the tobacco. Basil, lie on his side, watch the curl smoke as it

float above his head. distress as he be, the evidence of Newman

Chaytor's goodness be to some extent a compensate balance to his

trouble. and now he be enjoy the soothe influence of a quiet

smoke. those person who regard the weed as noxious and baleful have a

perfect right to their opinion, but they cannot ignore the fact that

to many thousand of thousand of estimable being it serve as a

comforter, frequently indeed as a healer. it be so in the present

instance. as the smoke wreathe and curl above he an ineffable

consolation creep into Basil's soul. thing seem at their black;

the peace and hope of a bright future have be destroy; the man who

have grow to honour he, and who have assure he of the future, have

with awful suddenness breathe his last breath; the little child he

love, and to whom he be to have be guardian and protector, be

thrust into the care of a malignant, remorseless man; suspicion of

foul play have be throw upon he; Old Corrie have lend he his mare,

and he have allow it to be steal; he have be so near to death that

but one man, and he a short time since an utter stranger, stand

between he and eternity; and he be lie now on a bed of sickness an

utter, utter beggar. grim enough in all conscience, but the simple

smoking of a pipe put a different and a well aspect upon it. there

be hope in the future; he be young, he would get well and strong

again; Anthony Bidaud be dead, but spiritual comfort die not with

life; he would see Annette once more, and would take his leave of she

assured of her love, so far as a child could give such an assurance,

and in the hope of meet she again in year to come; he would

outlive the injurious suspicion of wrong-dealing which he do not

doubt Gilbert Bidaud be spread against he; and he would be able

to vindicate himself in Old Corrie's eye and perhaps by-and-by

recompense the old fellow for the loss of the more. much virtue in a

pipe when it can so transform the prospect stretch before a man

bring to such a pass as Basil have be.



"yes," he say aloud, "all will come right in the end."



"of course it will," say Chaytor. "what special mental question be

you answer?"



"nothing special. I be think in a general way of my trouble, and

your pipe have put a more cheerful colour on they. be I mistaken in

think you tell I you be a doctor?"



"no. that be why I have be able to pull you through so quickly."



"how long will it be before I be able to get about?"



"at the end of the week if you will be reasonable."



"I promise. I feel well already. the moment I be strong enough I must

go to Bidaud's plantation."



"I will go with you."



"of course. we be mate from this day forth. the end of the week? not

early?"



"don't be impatient. my plan be to make a perfect cure. no patching.

at present I be in command."



"I obey. but let it be as soon as possible."



Chaytor congratulate himself. however thing turn out in the

future, all have go on swimmingly up to this moment. every little

move he have make have be successful. Basil have not the slight

suspicion that it be he who have steal his pocket-book and purse, and

empty his pocket.



"if an angel from heaven," chuckle Chaytor that night, as he walk

to and fro outside the store, "come and tell he the truth, he would

not believe it. I have he under my thumb--under my thumb. how to work

his old uncle in England? how to get hold of that forty thousand

pound? it must not go out of the family; I will not submit to it.

would a letter or two from Basil, write by I in Basil's hand, do

any good? I don't mind eat any amount of humble pie to accomplish

my purpose. even be it not a vicarious humiliation I be willing to

do it, the money be guide into its proper channel, and Basil

safely out of the way."



he pause, with a sinister look in his eye. have Basil see he then

he would hardly have recognise he. dark thought flit through his

mind, and animate his feature.



"nothing shall stop I," he cry, "nothing!" and he raise his hand

to the sky as though register an oath. a sad cloud steal upon the

moon and obscure its light. "what be life without enjoyment?" he

mutter. "by fair mean or foul I mean to enjoy. I should like to

know what we be send into the world for if we be deprive of a fair

share of the good thing?" there be no one to answer he, he

presently go inside to bed.



the next day Basil be so much well that without ask permission

he get up and dress himself. chaytor do not remonstrate with he;

he know, now that Basil be mend, that he would mend quickly. so it

prove; before the week be out the two man set forth on the tramp to

Bidaud's plantation.









CHAPTER XIV.





at noon on the second day they be within hail of Old Corrie's hut.

it be meal time, and the old woodman be cook his dinner. balanced

on the blaze log be a frying-pan fill with mutton-chop, some

half-dozen or so, which be not more than enough for a tough-limbe

fellow work from sunrise till sunset in the open air. he look up

as Basil and Chaytor approach, and with a nod of his head proceed

to turn the frizzle chop in the pan. this be his way; he be the

reverse of demonstrative.



such a greeting from another man, and that man a friend, would have

disconcert Basil, but he be familiar with Old Corrie's

peculiarity and have it not be for his own inward disquiet

regard the mare, he would have feel quite at his ease.



"back again," say Old Corrie, transfer a couple of chop on to a

tin plate.



"yes," say Basil.



"be away long than you expect."



"yes."



"on the tramp?"



"yes. look here, Corrie--"



"there's no hurry," interrupt Old Corrie. "you must be hungry. go

inside, and you'll see half a sheep dress. cut off what you want and

cook it while the fire serve."



"but I would rather say first what I have to say. when I've tell you

all, my mate and I might not be welcome."



"don't risk it, then. never run to court trouble, Master Basil. I'm an

old man than you; take the advice I give you."



"it be good advice," say Chaytor, whose appetite be sharp set, and

to whom the smell of the chop be well-nigh maddening.



Old Corrie look at he with penetrate eye, and Chaytor bear the

gaze well. he be not deficient in a certain quality of courage when

he be out of peril and master of the situation, as he believe

himself to be here. Old Corrie show no sign of approval or

disapproval, but proceed quietly with his dinner. Basil take the

woodman's advice. he go into the hut, cut a sufficient number of

chop from the half body of the sheep which be hang up, and come

back and take possession of the frying-pan, which be now at his

disposal. chaytor look on; he have not be make exactly welcome, and

be in doubt of Old Corrie's opinion of he, therefore he do not feel

warrant in make himself at home. when the young man commence

their meal, Old Corrie have finish his, and now, pipe in mouth, he

leant his back against a great tree and contemplate his guest.



"little lady! little lady!"



the sound come from within the hut. chaytor start, Basil look up

with a piece of mutton between his thumb and knife: fork they have

none.



"Basil! Basil! Basil and Annette! little lady! little lady!"



"it's the magpie I tell you about," say Old Corrie to Basil, "the

last time I see you."



"its vocabulary be extend," say Basil.



"by request," say Old Corrie in a pleasant voice, "of the little lady

herself."



Basil glow. Annette have not forget he, even think kindly of

he; otherwise, why should she wish that the bird Old Corrie be

training for she should become familiar with his name? Chaytor smart

under a sense of injury. Basil and Old Corrie be speak of

something which he do not understand--a proof that Basil have not tell

he everything. this, in Chaytor's estimation, be underhande and

injurious. Basil and everything in relation to he, his antecedent,

his whole story, belong by right to he, Newman Chaytor, who have

save his life, who have the strong claim of gratitude upon he

which a man could possible have. Old Corrie note the vindictive flash

in his eye, but make no comment upon it.



"and be that really a bird?" say Chaytor, in a tone of polite

inquiry.



"go and see for yourself," reply Old Corrie, "but don't go too

close. it haven't the good of temper."



"I should like to see the bird that could frighten I," say Chaytor,

rise.



"should you?" say Old Corrie. "then on second thought I prefer that

you stay where you be."



chaytor laugh and resume his seat. the meal proceed in silence

after this, and when the last chop be dispose of, Old Corrie say,

"now we will have our chat, Master Basil; and as we've a few private

matter to talk of, our mate here perhaps----"



the hint be plain, though imperfectly express.



"I be in the way," say Chaytor. "I'll smoke my pipe in the wood.

Coo-ey when you want I, Basil."



he stride off; exterior genial and placid, interior like a volcano.

"he shall pay for it," be his thought. it please he to garner up a

store of imaginary injury which be to be requite in the future.

then, when the time arrive for he to deal a blow, it would be merely

give tit for tat. many man besides Chaytor reason in this crooked

way, but none whose nature and motive be honourable and

straightforward.



"where do you pick he up?" ask Old Corrie when he and Basil be

alone.



"I want to speak to you first about your mare," say Basil.



"and I want to know first where you pick up your new mate,"

persist Corrie.



"he save my life," say Basil. "have it not be for his great and

unselfish kindness I should not be here to-day." then he tell the

woodman all that he know of Chaytor, and dilate in glow term upon

his noble conduct.



"it sound well," say Old Corrie, "and I have nothing to say in

contradiction; only I have a crank in I. I look into a man's face and

I like he, and I look into a man's face and I don't like he. the

first time I clap eye on you, Master Basil, I take a fancy to you.

I cann't say the same for your mate, but let it stand. I have it in my

mind to make a proposition to you in case you come back in time, but I

doubt whether it can be carry out now. have you enter into a

bargain to go mate with he?"



"I have, and have no wish to break it. I should be the basest of man

if I try to throw he over."



"keep to your word, lad; I'm the loser, for I think it likely the

two of we might strike up a partnership."



"why not the three of we?" ask Basil, to whom the prospect of

work with Old Corrie be very agreeable.



"because in the first place it wouldn't suit I, and in the second it

wouldn't suit he."



"but if he be willing?"



Old Corrie bend his brow kindly upon Basil's ingenuous face. "ask

he, Master Basil."



"will you not listen to I first? I want to speak to you about your

mare."



"a quarter of an hour more or less willn't bring she back, will it?"

say Old Corrie, with no touch of reproach in his voice. "go and speak

to your mate, and let I know what he say."



Basil depart and return. it be as Old Corrie suppose: chaytor

be not willing to admit Corrie into their partnership.



"he say you take a dislike to he from the first," say Basil.



"almost my own word," say Old Corrie, with a laugh. "he's a shrewd

customer."



"----And that he be certain you and he would not agree. I would give a

finger off each hand if it could have be, for a warmer-hearted and

nobler man do not exist than Chaytor; and as for you, Corrie, I

would wish nothing well. but I be bind to he by the strong tie

of gratitude."



"say no more, Master Basil, say no more. mayhap we shall meet

by-and-by, and we shall be no bad friend because this have fall

through. we have a lot to say to each other. I'm off the day after

to-morrow; I should have be off before if it have not be for you

and the little lady."



"she have be here?" cry Basil.



"she have be here four time since you leave--the last time

yesterday--not to see I, but you. she manage the thing herself, poor

little lady, and come alone, after give the slip to those about

she. her first grief be over, though she will never forget the good

father she have lose--never. it ben't in her nature to forget--bear

that in mind, Master Basil. she cling to the friend that be leave

she. friend, do I say? why, she have only one--you, Master Basil; I

don't count. besides, if I do it would matter little to she, for

there's nothing more unlikely than that, after two day have go by,

I shall ever look upon her sweet face again. she go one way, I go

another.



"she go one way?" repeat Basil; "will she not remain on the

plantation?"



"she will not. you see it ben't for she to choose; she must do as she

be direct. but we be mix up thing, and it will help they right

well if I tell you what I've get to tell straight on, commence with

A, end with Z. let we clear the ground so that the axe may swing

without be catch in loose branch. I'll hear what you've get to

say. my mare be lose, I know."



"how do you know?"



"you would have bring it back with you if it haven't be. now then,

lad, straight out, no beating about the bush. it's not in your line. I

don't for a moment mistrust you. there's truth in your face always,

Master Basil, and I wish with all my heart the little lady have you by

her side to guide she instead of the skunk that's step into her

dead father's shoe. you're a square man, and my mare be lose through

no fault of your, my lad."



encourage by these generous word, Basil tell his story straight, and

Old Corrie listen with a pleasant face.



"the mare's gone," say Old Corrie when Basil have do, "and bad luck

go with she. I know the brand on she: mayhap I shall come across her

one of these fine day. describe the rascal to I."



Basil do as well as he could, and say Old Corrie be not treat

he as he deserve.



"I be treat you as an honest gentleman," say Old Corrie, "as I

know you to be. Jem the Hatter the villain's call, be he? when a man

once get a nickname on the goldfield it stick to he through thick

and thin; if we meet he shall remember it. I give you a receipt in

full, Master Basil." and the good fellow hold out his two hand, which

Basil shake heartily. "I be sure something serious keep you away."

with Basil's hand clasp firmly in his, he gaze steadily into the

young man's face. "it be on odd fancy I've get," he say, "but it's

come across I two or three time while we've be talk. be there

any relationship between you and your new mate?"



"none."



"sure of that?"



"sure."



"and you meet for the first time on Gum Flat?"



"for the first time."



"well, it be odd, and the more I look at you now the odder it become.

you've let your hair grow since you go away."



"oblige to," say Basil, laugh. "I have no razor. there be a

couple I can claim in Mr. Bidaud's house, as well as a brush or two;

but I daresay I shall not get they now that Mr. Gilbert Bidaud be in

possession. what be your odd fancy, Corrie?"



"why that you and your new mate would be as like each other as two

pea, if you be dress alike and trim your hair alike. haven't

you notice it yourself?"



"I've notice that we resemble each other somewhat," say Basil, "but

not to the extent you mention. I remember now he speak of it himself;

and that be one reason perhaps why he take a liking to I, and nurse

I as he do. but I be terribly anxious to hear about the plantation

and Annette. what be go to happen there that she be to leave it?"



"in my own way, Master Basil," say Old Corrie, brush his hand

across his eye to chase the fancy away, "and to commence at the

beginning. when you leave I in the wood I be split slab, a job I

be do for poor Mr. Anthony Bidaud. you doubt whether his brother

would hold to it, as there be no write bond to show for it, and you

be right. I go up to the house, as I say I would, and see Mr.

Gilbert. you describe he well, Master Basil; he's a man I would be

sorry to trust. I tell he of the contract between I and his brother.

'where be it?' he ask. 'there be none write,' I answer; 'it be

an order give as a dozen other have be, and of course you'll abide

by it.' 'of course I will not,' say he. 'who be you that I should

take your word? and you would fix your own price for the slab?

Clever, Mr. Corrie. Clever, Mr. Corrie!' I have tell he my name. 'but

I be a cleverer and a sharper.' a sharp he be in the right meaning

of it, but he be not english, and don't exactly know what he be

call himself. 'no, no,' he say, 'the moment a man's dead the

vulture come. you be one. but I be equal to you. burn your slab.'

'you're a pretty speciman,' I say. 'your brother be a gentleman; it

don't run in the family.' he's a strange man, Master Basil, and if

he ever lose his temper he take care not to show it. More than what

I've tell you pass between we, and once he say quite coolly that if

I could summon his brother as a witness, he be willing to abide by

his testimony. the testimony of a dead man! and to speak so lightly of

one's flesh and blood! I wouldn't trust such a man out of my sight."



"do you see his sister?" ask Basil.



"I do, but she say very little, and never speak without look at

Mr. Gilbert for a cue. he give it she always in a silent way that

pass my comprehension, but they understand each other by sign."



"and Annette--do you see she?"



"yes, but at a distance. they keep she from I, I think, but I see she

look at I quite mournfully, and I feel like go boldly up to

she, but second thought be good, and I keep away, only give she

to understand as well as I could without speak that I be her

friend, ready at any time to do she a service. 'well,' say I to Mr.

Gilbert, 'my compliment to you. your throwing over the contract your

brother make willn't hurt I a bit; I could buy up a dozen like

you'--which be brag, Master Basil, and he know it was--'but I should

be sorry to dishonour the dead as you be do.' he take out a

snuff-box, help himself to a pinch, smile, and say, 'Sentiment,

Mr. Corrie, sentiment. I treat the dead as I treat the living. rid I

of you.' it be his foreign way of bid I pack, but I tell he I

should take my time, that I have plenty of friend among his brother's

workman, and that I should go away very slowly. 'and let I give you a

piece of advice,' I say. 'if you or any agent of yours come spy

near my hut I'll mark he so that he shall remember it.' 'ah, ah,' he

say, still smile in my face, 'threat eh?' 'yes, threat,' say I,

'and as many more of 'em as I choose to give tongue to.' 'foolish Mr.

Corrie, foolish Mr. Corrie,' he say, take more snuff, 'to lose your

temper. let _I_ give _you_ a piece of advice. think first, speak

afterwards. it be a lesson--take it to heart. you be too impulsive,

Mr. Corrie, like another person who also trespass here, one who

call himself Basil.' 'Mr. Basil be a friend of mine,' I say, 'say

one word against he, and I'll knock you down.' he be frightened,

though he don't show it, and he beckon to a man, who come and stand

by he. you know he, I daresay, Master Basil; his name be Rocke."



"he be my enemy, I be afraid," say Basil.



"I find that out afterwards; he have be spread report about you

either out of his own spite, or employ by cold-blooded Mr. Gilbert

Bidaud. so Rocke come and stand by his side, but not too willingly.

we've meet before Rocke and I, and he know the strength of my muscle.

I smile at he, and he grin at I, and I say, 'we be speak

of Master Basil, and I be say that if anyone say a word against

he I be ready to knock he down. perhaps you'd like to say

something.' 'not at all,' say Rocke, and his grin change to a scowl,

'I know when it will pay I well to hold my tongue.' Mr. Gilbert

Bidaud shake with laughter. 'Good Rocke,' he say, 'wise Rocke. we'll

make a judge of you. anything more to say?' this be to I and I

answer, almost as cool now as he be himself, 'only this. you spit

upon a dead man's bond, and you be a scoundrel. don't come near my

hut, you or anyone that side with you.' Rocke understand this. 'but,'

say I, 'any friend of Master Basil's be heartily welcome, and I'll

give they the good I have. so good day to you, Mr. Gilbert Bidaud.'

then I go among the workman and chat with they, and pick up

scrap of information, and turn the current wherever I see it be

set against you."



"my hearty thank for the service, Corrie," say Basil.



"you're as heartily welcome. if one friend don't stick up for another

behind his back we might as well be tiger. you see, Master Basil,

you're a stranger here compare with I; I've be chum with the

man this many a year, and never have a word with one except Rocke, and

even he have some sort of respect for I. then you're a gentleman; I'm

not. my lad, there be sign that cann't be hide; you've get the

hallmark on you. well, when I'd do as much as I could in a friendly

way, I turn my back on the plantation, and come back here, and go

on with my splitting, as if the contract still hold good."



"be not that a waste of time, Corrie?"



"I take my own view of it. there be the dead man soon to be in his

grave; here be I with the blood run free through my vein. if

he'd be alive he'd have keep his word; I be alive, and I'd keep

mine. so I finish the contract out of respect for Mr. Anthony

Bidaud, and there the slab be, stack and ready. while I be at

work my thought be on you; four day pass, and you haven't

return. I conclude that something have happen to you, but that

you'd appear some time or another, and all I could do be to hope that

you'd come back before I leave the place. I have a great wish to see the

little lady, but I don't know how to compass it. compass it be,

however, without my move in it. just a week it be after you'd go

that I be at work in the wood; it be afternoon, a good many hour

from sundown, when my laugh jackass begin to laugh outrageous. when

we're alone together he behave soberly and decently, content with

quietly laugh and chuckle to himself, and it's only when

something out of the way occur that he give himself air. he's the

vainest of the vain, Master Basil, and he do it to show off. his

tantrum make I look round, and there, stand look at I and the

laugh jackass, without a morsel of fear of I or the bird, be the

little lady."



"Annette?" cry Basil.



"the little lady herself," say Old Corrie.









CHAPTER XV.





"be she alone?" ask Basil.



"yes, quite alone. I drop my axe, tell the jackass to shut

up--which it don't, Master Basil--and take the hand she hold out to

I. such a little hand, Master Basil! I give you my word that as I

hold it in mine my thought go back, more year than I care to

count, to the time when I be a little 'un myself, snuggle close up

to my mother's apron. I cann't remember when I'd think of those day

last. they be stow away in a coffin, and drop into a grave

which stand between I as a boy and I as a man. it's like having

live two life, one of which be dead and bury. now, all at once,

the dead past come to life, and say, in a manner of speak, 'I

belong to you,' and it don't seem unnatural. the touch of the little

lady's hand be like a magic wand, and if she have say to I, 'let's

have a game of hopscotch,' I believe I should have do it and think

it the proper thing to do. but she say nothing of the sort, only

look at I with melancholy sweetness, and hope I be not sorry to

see she. sorry! I be heartily and thankfully glad, and I tell she so,

and the tear come into her pretty eye, and I say, without think

at the moment that she'd lose a dear father, 'don't cry, don't cry!

there's nothing to cry for;' but I set myself right directly by

say, 'I mean, I hope it ben't I that make you cry.' 'no,' she

answer, 'it's only that you speak so kind.' my blood boil up, for

those word of her show I that since her father's death she have

not be treat with kindness, and if she haven't be a little lady,

rich in her own right, I should have offer to run off with she there

and then. but under any circumstance that would have be a dangerous

thing to do, for she and I; it would have bring her uncle down upon

I, and he'd have have the law on his side. so, instead of offer to

do a thing so foolish, I say, 'do you come on purpose to see I?'

'yes,' she answer, on purpose. 'I give they the slip, and they don't

know where I be.' 'don't you be afraid then, my little maid,' I say,

'they willn't find you here, because they willn't venture within half a

mile of I. you've do no harm in come to see a friend, as you may

be sure I be. can I do anything for you?' 'yes,' she say; 'you like

Basil, don't you?' upon that I say I be as true a friend of yours as

I be of her. 'will you tell I, please,' she say then, 'why he have

go quite away without try to see I? I know it wouldn't be easy,

because my uncle and aunt be against he; but I think he would have

try. I have be to every one of his favourite place, in the hope

of meet he, and my uncle have say such hard thing of he that my

heart be fit to break.' poor little lady! she could hardly speak for

her tear. well, now, that laugh jackass be make such a chatter,

and behave so outrageous, pretend to sob, which make she sob the

more, that I propose to take she to my hut here, where we could talk

quietly. she put she little hand in mine and walk along with I to

my hut, and the minute we come in the magpie cry out, 'little lady,

little lady.' she look up at this, and I tell she it be a magpie I

be train for she. it give she great pleasure than such a little

thing as that ought to have do, and though she do not say it in so

many word I see in her face the grateful thought that she still have

friend in the world that have grow so sad and lonely. then I tell she

all about your last meeting with I--how tenderly you have speak of

she, what love you have for she, and how I have lend you my mare to take

you to a place where you hope to find a doctor and a lawyer who might

be able to serve she in some way. the news comfort she, but she be

greatly distress by the fear that you have meet with an accident which

prevent your return. I wouldn't listen to this for the little maid's

sake, and say I be positive you would soon be back, and that nothing

be far from your mind than the idea of go away entirely

without see she again. 'he will have to make haste,' say the

little lady, with a world of thought in her face, 'or he will never be

able to find I.' I ask why, and she answer that she believe,

when everything be settle, that her uncle would sell the plantation

and take she away to Europe. 'cann't it be prevent?' she ask, and I

say I be afraid it could not; that her uncle stand now in the place

of her father, and could do as he like. 'if you be compel to go,'

I say, 'you shall take the magpie away with you to remind you of the

old place--that be, if you will be allow to keep it.' 'I shall be,'

she say; and now, child as she be, I notice in her sign of a

resolute will I haven't give her credit for. 'if you give it to I, it

will be mine, and they shall not take it from I. I will fight for it,

indeed, I will.' I be pleased to hear she speak like that; it show

that she have spirit which would be of use to she when she be a woman

grow. she stop with I as long as she dare, and before she go

away she say she would come again, and ask I if I think I could

teach the bird to speak your name. 'it would be easy enough,' I

answer, and that be how it come about that the magpie--which for

cleverness and common-sense, Master Basil, I would match against the

cunning bird that ever be hatch--can call out 'Basil--Basil,' as

clearly as you pronounce your own name. it be at that meeting, and at

every meeting afterwards, she give I a message to you if you

return. you be to be sure not to go away again without see she;

if you couldn't contrive it, she would; that prove her spirit again;

and that if it should unfortunately happen that you return after she

be take away you be never to forget that Annette love you, and

would love you all her life, whatever part of the world she might be

in. those be her word as near as I can remember they, and they're

easy enough for you to understand, but it ben't so easy to make you

understand the voice in which she speak they. I declare, Master Basil,

it run through I now, break by little sob, with her pretty hand

clasping and unclasp themselves and her tender body shake like a

reed."



"dear little Annette," say Basil, and his eye, too, be tearful,

and his voice break a little; "dear little Annette."



"she's worth a man's thought, Master Basil," say Old Corrie, "and a

man's pity, and will be well worth em' when she's a woman grow.

you're a fortunate man, child as she be, to have win a love like the

little lady's, for if I'm a judge of human nature, and I believe

myself to be--which ben't exactly conceit on my part, mind you--it's

love that will last and never be forget. it's no light thing,

Master Basil, love like that; when it come to a man he'll hold on to

it if he's get a grain of sense in he."



"you cannot say one word in praise of Annette," say Basil, "that I'm

not ready to cap with a dozen. I believe, with you, that she have a

soul of constancy, and I hold she in my heart as I would a beloved

sister. if I could only help and advise she! but how can I do that

when she be to be take away to a distant land?"



"there's no telling what may happen in the future," say Old Corrie.

"what to-day seem impossible to-morrow come to pass. to beat one's

head against a stone wall because thing ben't as we wish they to be

be the height of foolishness, but it's my opinion that go on

steadily do one's duty, work manfully and do what's right and

square, be the good and sure way to open out the road we'd like to

tread. your new mate, Mr. Chaytor, haven't disturb we, and I must do

he the justice to say that he show sense and discretion."



"he be one in a thousand," say Basil, "and it be impossible for I to

express to you how sorry I be that you have not take kindly to each

other."



"it do happen sometimes, but not often, that man be mistake in

their liking and disliking, but we'll not argue the point. now I've

get to tell you how thing stand at the plantation. there be an

inquest on the body of Mr. Anthony Bidaud, doctor and lawyer be

call in by Mr. Gilbert, and the verdict be that he die of natural

cause. there be no will, Mr. Gilbert take legal possession, as

guardian to his niece under age. he decide that it will not be good

for she to remain where she be; but must be educate as a lady, and

bring up as one. that, say Mr. Gilbert, cann't be do on the

plantation; it must be do in a civilized country. consequently the

plantation must be sell. with lawyer pay to push thing on, three

month' work have be do in three week. a purchaser have be find,

deed draw up, money pay, and next Monday they're off; Mr. Gilbert

Bidaud, his sister, name unknown, and the little lady."



"hot haste, indeed," say Basil.



"to which neither you nor I can have anything to say legally."



"it be so, unhappily. and then to Europe?"



"and then to Europe. I be tell you what the little lady tell I. I

cann't go beyond that."



"of course not. but do she not know to what part of Europe?"



"she know nothing more. he keep his mouth shut; you cann't compel he

to open it. there be case, Master Basil, in which honesty be no

match for roguery; this be one. Mr. Gilbert Bidaud have the law on his

side, and can laugh openly at you. now, the little lady be here

yesterday. 'no news of Basil?' she ask. 'no news of Basil,' I say.

'be he dead, do you think?' she whisper, with a face like snow.

'no,' I say stoutly; 'don't you go on imagine thing of that sort.

he's alive, and will give a satisfactory account of himself when he

come back.' I speak confidently to keep up her heart, though I have

misgiving of you. 'I shall be here to-morrow,' she say, 'and every

day till we leave the plantation.' she have contrive cleverly, haven't

she, to slip they as she do?"



"then I shall see she soon!" say Basil, eagerly.



"in less than an hour, if she come at her usual time. our confab be

over. you have well go and seek your mate. I'll make my apology to

he, if he need 'em, for keep you so long."



if Basil have know, he have not far to go to find Newman Chaytor, for

that worthy be quite close to he. be of an inquire mind Chaytor

have resolve to hear all that pass between Basil and Old Corrie, and

have find a secure hiding-place in the rear, and well within earshot,

of the two friend. he store it all up, be bless with an

exceptionally retentive memory. Old Corrie go one way, and Basil

go another, and Chaytor emerge from his hiding-place. "I be quite

curious about little Annette," he say to himself, as he follow

Basil at a safe distance. "quite a sentimental little body--and an

heiress, too! well, we shall see. say that my friend Basil's future be

a nut--I'll crack it; I may find a sweet kernel inside."



he come up to Basil, and greet he with a frank smile. "we've be

talk about the plantation," say Basil, "and poor Anthony Bidaud's

daughter, Annette. she be come this afternoon to see I. I'll tell

you everything by-and-by."



"I don't want to intrude upon your private affair, Basil," say

Chaytor.



"you have a right to know," say Basil. "I have no secret from you,

Chaytor."



then they talk of other matter, chaytor with animation, Basil with

a mind occupy by thought of Annette. "I see," say Chaytor, pat

Basil's shoulder with false kindness, "that you be think of the

little maid. now I'm not go to play the churl. don't mind I for

the rest of the day."



"you're a good fellow," say Basil, as Chaytor walk away; but he do

not walk far. unobserve by Basil, he keep secret watch upon he,

determine to see Annette, determine to hear what she and Basil have

to say to each other. as Old Corrie have say, "there be case in

which honesty be no match for roguery." Basil post himself in such a

position that he could see any person who come towards the wood from

Bidaud's plantation. he hear the thud of Old Corrie's axe in the

forest; the honest woodman could have remain idle have he choose, but

he be unhappy unless he be at work, and though he desire no profit

from it he fell and split tree for the pleasure of the thing. now

and again there come to Basil's ear the piping and chattering of

gorgeous-colour bird as they flutter hither and thither, busy on

their own concern, love-making, nest-mending, and the like; in their

commonwealth many touch of human passion and sentiment find a

reflex. Vanity be there, jealousy be there, hector and bullying

of the weak be there, and much sly pilfering go on; entertainment,

too, be be give, for at some distance from the three man in the

wood, one swinge his axe with a will and wipe his cheerful brow,

another with his heart in his eye watch for a little figure in the

distance, and the third, stir by none but evil thought, watch

with cunning eye the watcher--at some distance from these two honest

man and one rogue be assemble some couple of dozen feather

songster in green and yellow coat. they perch upon convenient

bough and branch, form a circle, with invisible music book

before they, and at a give signal from their leader they begin to

pipe their song without word, and fill space with melody. their

music may be liken to the faintly sweet echo of skilled

bell-ringer, each tiny bird the master of a note which be never

pipe unless in harmony. it be while these fairy bell be peal

their sweetest chord that Basil see Annette approach. he run

towards she eagerly, and call her name; and she with a sudden flush

in her face and with her heart palpitate with joy, cry, "Basil!

Basil!" and fall into his arm.









CHAPTER XVI.





he lead she to a secluded spot, follow secretly by fox Chaytor. they

pass close to where Old Corrie be work, and he, hearing

footstep--be sure, however, that Chaytor's be noiseless--lay down

his axe, and go towards they.



"he have come--he have come!" cry Annette.



"what do I tell you?" say Old Corrie. "all you've get to do in this

world, little lady, be to have patience."



she be so overjoyed, have tight hold of Basil's hand, that she

would have accept the wild theory without question.



"Mr. Corrie," she say, "may I have the magpie to-day?"



"surely," he reply, "it be quite ready for you, and you will be able

to teach it anything you please. but why so soon? ben't you come

again?"



her face become sad, and she clutch Basil's finger convulsively: "I

be afraid not this be the last, last time! I have hear something, Mr.

Corrie, and if it be true my uncle and aunt be go to take I away

to-morrow morning."



"in that case," say Old Corrie, "I will have the bird ready for you.

now you and Master Basil can talk; I'll not interrupt you." he go

away at once, and leave they together. for a little while they have

nothing of a coherent nature to say to each other; but then Basil,

recognise the necessity of introduce some kind of system into

their conversation, relate to Annette all that have happen within

his knowledge since the sad morning of her father's death, and hear

from her lip all that she have to relate. much of it he have already

hear from Old Corrie. the refrain she harp upon be, "and must we,

must we part, Basil? and shall we never, never see each other again?"



"part we must, dear Annette," he say; "I have no control over you,

and no authority that can in any way be establish. when I first come

to the plantation I be a stranger to you and your father, and the law

would acknowledge I as no well now."



"next to my dear father and mother," say Annette, "I love you well in

all the world. they cannot take that away from I; what I feel be my

own, my very own. oh, Basil, I sometimes have wicked thought, and

feel myself turn bad; I never feel so before my uncle come."



"Annette, listen to I. you must struggle against these thought and

must say to yourself, 'they will make my dear father and mother

sorrowful. they have show I kindness and love and I will show the

same to they.' you cannot see they, Annette, but their spirit be

watch over you; and there be a just and merciful God in heaven who

be watch over you, too, and whom you must not offend."



"I will do as you say, Basil, dear; I will never, never forget your

word. they will keep I good."



"let they keep you brave as well, my dear. I promise to remember you

always, to love you always, and perhaps when you be a woman--it will

not be so long, Annette--we shall meet again."



"oh, Basil, that will be true happiness."



"time fly quickly, Annette. it seem but yesterday since I be a boy

myself, and when I look back and think of my own dear parent, I be

happy in the belief that I never do anything to cause they sorrow.



"you could not, Basil."



"ah, my dear, I don't know that; but I have a good mother and so have

you, and my father and yours be both noble man. they be not with

we, and that make the duty we owe they all the strong. to do what

be right because we feel that it be right to do it, not because it be

do in the sight of other--that be what make we good, Annette. my

mother teach I that lesson as she lie on her death bed, and it have

bring I great happiness; it have support I in adversity. you must

not mind my speaking so seriously, Annette----"



"I love to hear you, Basil. I will be like you, indeed I will.



"much well, I hope. you see, my dear, this be the last time we shall

be together for a long time; but not so long after all, if we look at

it in the right light, and I should like you to remember I as you

would remember a brother, who, be old than you, be perhaps a

little wiser."



"I will, Basil. all my wicked thought be go; they shall never come

again; but I shall still feel a little unhappy sometimes."



"of course you will, dear, and so shall I. but faith in God's goodness

and the performance of our duty will always lighten that unhappiness.

the star of heaven be not bright than the star of hope and love

we can keep shine in our heart."



"kiss I, Basil; that be the seal. I shall go away happy now."



"tell I, Annette. be your uncle and aunt kind to you?"



"they be neither kind nor unkind. they talk a great deal to each

other, but very seldom to I, unless it be to order I to do

something. Aunt say, 'go to bed,' and I go to bed; 'it be time to get

up,' and I get up? 'come to dinner,' and I come to dinner. it be all

like that; they never speak to I as my father and mother do, and

they have never kiss I."



"you must be obedient to they, Annette."



"I will be, Basil."



"they be your guardian, and a great deal depend upon they."



"yes, I know that; but I don't think they like I, and, Basil, I don't

think uncle be a good man."



"it will be well," say Basil gravely, "not to fancy that. it may be

only that he be a little different from other man, and that you be

not accustomed to his way."



"I will try," say Annette piteously, "to obey you in everything, but

I cann't help my thought, and I cann't help see and hearing. he

speak in a hard voice to everybody; he be unkind to animal; he have

never put a flower on my dear father's grave."



"there, there, Annette--don't cry. I only want you to make the good,

and not the bad, of thing."



"I will, Basil--indeed, indeed I will. when I be far away from you,

you _will_ think, will you not, that I be try hard to do everything

to please you?"



"I promise to think so, and I have every faith in you. it be all for

your good, you know, Annette. when you be out of this country where

be your aunt and uncle go to live."



"in Europe."



"but in what part of Europe?"



"I don't know. all that uncle and aunt say be, 'we be go to

Europe.' 'but in what country?' I ask. 'don't be inquisitive,' they

answer; 'we be go to Europe;' and they will say nothing more. I

be sometimes afraid to speak when they be near I."



"poor little Annette! now attend to I, dear. wherever you be you can

write to I."



"yes, Basil, yes. and may I? oh, how good you be! oh, if ever I

should get a letter from you! it will be the next good thing to have

you with I."



"remember what I be say, Annette. I want you to write to I,

wherever you be, and I want to answer your letter. this be the way

it can be do. when you be settle write I your first letter--I

shall not mind how long it is----"



"it shall be a long, long one, Basil."



"and address it to 'Mr. Basil Whittingham, Post-office, Sydney, New

South Wales.' I shall be sure to get it. now for my answer. if you be

happy in your uncle's house, and tell I so, I will send my answer

there; but if you think it will be good for I not to send it to his

house, I will address it to the post-office in whatever town or city

you may be live. some friend in the new country (you be sure to

make friend, my dear) will tell you how you may get my letter. this

look a little like deceit, but it will be pardonable deceit if you

be unhappy--not otherwise. do you understand?"



"perfectly, Basil. I shall have something to think of now; you have

give I something to do. and will you ever come to I?"



"it be my hope; I intend to work hard here to get money, and if I be

fortunate, in a few year, when you be a beautiful woman----"



"I would like to be, Basil, for your sake."



"I will come to wherever you may be."



"I do not wish for anything more, Basil. I shall pray night and

morning for your good fortune. how happy you have make I--how

happy--how happy! I shall keep the star of love and hope shine in

my heart--for you. how beautifully the bellbird be singe. I shall

hear they when I be thousand of mile away. but, Basil, you will want

something to remember I by."



"no, dear Annette, I need nothing to remind I of you."



"you do, Basil, and I have bring it for you. look, Basil, my

locket----"



"but Annette----"



"have I say 'no' to anything you have tell I--and will you say 'no'

to this little thing? I think it will not be right if you do; so, dear

Brother Basil, you must not refuse I. I wish I have something well
