to give you, but you will be satisfied with this, will you not? I have

wear it always round my neck, since I be a little, little girl, and

you must wear it round your. promise I."



"I promise, dear, if you will not be deny."



"I will not, indeed I will not--and your promise be make. see, Basil,

here it lie open in my hand; take it. the picture be a portrait of my

dear mother; father have it paint for I by a gentleman who come once

to the plantation. then when you come to I in the country across the

sea, you will show it to I and tell I that you have wear it always

and always, because you love I, and because I love you."



"I have nothing to give you, Annette. I be very, very poor."



"you have give I a star of hope, Basil. how sorry I be that you be

poor! but my nurse, who have be send away----"



"have they do that, Annette?"



"yes, and she cry so at leave I. she tell I that one day I

should be very, very rich. so what do it matter if you be poor? let

I fasten it round your neck. now you have I and my dear mother next

your heart."



he take the innocent child in his arm, and she lie nestle there a

few moment with bright thought of the happy future in her mind.

suddenly a loud "Coo-ey" be hear and the sound of hurried footstep.

it be Old Corrie's voice that give the alarm. it be intend as

such, for when Basil start to his foot and stand with his arm round

Annette, hold she close to he, he look up, and see Gilbert

Bidaud stand before he.









CHAPTER XVII.





a malicious smile play about the old man's lip as he glance at

Basil and Annette. for a few moment he do not speak, but stand

enjoy the situation, feel himself master of it; and when he

break the silence his voice be smooth and suave. the malignancy of

his feeling be to be find in his word, not in the tone in which he

utter they.



"ah, Mr. Basil Whittingham once more? Mr. Basil Whittingham, the

english gentleman, ready at a moment's notice to give lesson in

manner, conduct, and good breeding. but then it be to proclaim

oneself a fool to take a man at his own estimate of himself. I find

you here in the company of my niece. favour I with an explanation,

Mr. Basil Whittingham."



"there be nothing to explain," say Basil, still with his arm round

Annette. "I have be absent some time, and happen, fortunately, to

return before Miss Bidaud leave the country, have meet she here, and be

exchange a few word of farewell."



"of course, of course. who would venture to dispute with so

reproachless a gentleman? who would venture to whisper that in these

last few word of farewell there be any attempt to work upon a

child's feeling, and to make the spurious metal of self-interest

shine like purest gold? on one side a young girl, as yet a mere child,

whose feeling be easily work upon; on the other side a grow man

verse in the cunning of the world, and use it with a keen eye to

profitable use in the future. not quite an equal match, it appear to

I, but I may be no judge. if I be to hint that this meeting between

you and my dear niece and ward have anything of a clandestine nature in

it, you would probably treat I to a display of indignant firework.

if I be to hint that, instead of so advise this child that she

should hold out her arm gladly to the new life into which she be

about to enter, you be instill into she a feeling of repugnance

against it, and of mistrust against those whose duty it will be to

guide she aright and teach she--principles"--his eye twinkle with

malignant humour as he speak this word--"you, english gentleman that

you be, would repudiate the insinuation with lofty scorn. but when

you exchange confidence with I you be in the presence of a man who

have also see something of the world, and who, although it have deal

he hard buffet, retain some old-fashioned notion of honour and

manliness. I apply the test to you, adventurer, and you become

instantly expose. ah! here be my sister, this sweet young child's

aunt, who will relieve you of your burden."



he take the hand of the unresiste girl and lead she to her aunt,

whose arm glide round Annette's waist, hold it as in a vice.



"I will not answer you," say Basil, with an encouraging smile at

Annette, whose face instantly brighten. "Annette know I have speak

the truth, and that be enough."



"yes, Basil," say Annette, boldly, "you have speak the truth, and I

will never, never forget what you have say to I to-day."



"take she away," say Gilbert Bidaud to his sister; "the farce be

play out. in a week it will be forget."



"good-bye, Basil," say Annette "and God bless you."



"good-bye, Annette," say Basil, "and God guard you."



"how touching, how touching!" murmur Gilbert Bidaud. "it be surely a

scene from an old comedy. take she away."



"just one moment, please," say Old Corrie, join the group. "here

be something that belong to the little lady, that she would like to

take with she to the new world. it will remind she of the old, and of

friend she leave in it."



it be the magpie in its wicker cage, whose tongue be loosen by

company, or perhaps by a desire to show off its accomplishment to an

appreciative audience, become volubly communicative.



"Basil! Basil! Basil and Annette! little lady! little lady!"



in his heart Gilbert Bidaud be disposed to strangle the bird, but his

smile be amiability itself as he say to Annette, "your, my child?"



"yes, mine," she answer. "Mr. Corrie give it to I."



"but Mr. Corrie be not rich," say Gilbert Bidaud, pull out his

purse; "you be. shall we not pay he for it?"



"no," say Annette, before Old Corrie could speak. "I would not care

for it if he take money for it."



"well say, little lady," say Old Corrie; "the bird be friendship's

offering, and for that will be value and well care for, I don't

doubt. it be your property, mind, and no one have a right to meddle

with it."



"Friendship's offering!" say Gilbert Bidaud, with a long, quiet

laugh. "we come out to the bush to learn something, do we not,

sister? why, here we find the fine of human virtue and sentiment,

the smug of morality, the essence of refined feeling. it be

really refreshing. do not be afraid, Mr. Corrie. although I would not

take your word about that wood-splitting contract, I have some respect

for you, as a rough speciman of bush life and manner. we part

friend, I hope."



"not a bit of it," say Old Corrie. "if lady be not present I'd

open my mind to you."



"thank heaven," say Gilbert Bidaud, raise his eye with mock

devotion, "for the restrain influence of the gentler sex. you do

not diminish my esteem for you. I know rough honesty when I meet with

it."



"you shift about," interrupt Old Corrie, "like a treacherous wind.

I'm rough honesty now, be I? you're the kind of man that can turn

white into black. let we make thing equal by another sort of bargain.

I've give little lady the bird. you'll not take it from she?"



"Heavens?" cry Gilbert Bidaud, clasp his hand. "what do you

think of I?"



"that's not an answer. you'll not take it from she?"



"I will not. keep it, my child, and be happy."



"do you hear, little lady? let we be thankful for small mercy. shake

hand, my dear. when you're a woman grow, don't forget Old Corrie."



"I never will--I never will," sob Annette.



"and don't forget," say Old Corrie, lay his hand on Basil's

shoulder, "that Master Basil here be a gentleman to be honour and

love, a man to be proud of, a man to treasure in your heart."



"I will never forget it," say Annette; with a fond look at Basil.



"and this, I think," say Gilbert Bidaud, with genial smile all

round, "be the end of an act. let the curtain fall to slow music."



but it be not destine so to fall. as Annette's aunt turn to leave

with her niece, her eye, dwell scornfully on Basil for a moment,

catch sight of the chain attach to the locket which Annette have put

round his neck. quick as lightning she put her hand to the child's

neck, and discover the loss.



"he have steal Annette's locket!" she cry, point to the chain.



as quick in his movement as his sister, Gilbert Bidaud stretch

forth his hand and tear the locket and chain from Basil's neck. it be

do so swiftly and suddenly that Basil be unable to prevent it; but

the hot blood rush into his face as he say:



"be you a young man I would give you cause to remember your

violence. Annette, speak the truth."



"I give it to you, Basil," say Annette, slip from her aunt's

grasp, and put her hand on Gilbert Bidaud's. "it be false to say

he steal it. it belong to I, and I could do what I please with it.

I give it to Basil, and he do not want to take it at first, but I

make he."



she strove to wrench it from her uncle's hand, but it be easy for he

to keep it from she.



"I will have it!" cry Annette. "I will, I will! it be Basil's, and

you have no right to it."



"a storm in a teapot," say Gilbert Bidaud, who seldom lose his

self-possession for long than a moment, "Sister, you should

apologise to the young gentleman. take the precious gift."



but instead of hand it to Basil he throw it over the young man's

head, and Newman Chaytor, who during the whole of this scene have be

skulk, unseen, in the rear, and have hear every word of the

conversation, catch it before it fall, and slunk off with it.



"I shall find it, Annette," say Basil. "Good-bye, once more. may your

life be bright and happy!"



those be the last word, and be utter at the moment Newman

Chaytor catch the locket and be slink off, be hear and

treasure by he.



the whole of that day Basil, assist by Old Corrie and Chaytor,

search for the locket, of course unsuccessfully. he be in great

distress at the loss; it seem to be ominous of misfortune.









CHAPTER XXVIII.





the story of the life of Basil and Chaytor during the ensue three

year may be briefly summarise. so far as obtain more than

sufficient gold for the bare necessary of life be concern,

ill-luck pursue they. they go from goldfield to goldfield, and

follow every new rush they hear of, and be never successful in

strike a rich claim. it be all the more tantalising because they

be within a few foot of great fortune at least half-a-dozen time.

on one goldfield they mark out ground, close to a claim of fabulous

richness, every bucket of wash-dirt that be haul from the gutter

be heavily weight with gold. this be the prospector' claim, and

the shaft next to it strike the gutter to the tune of twelve ounce a

day per man. the same with the second, and Basil and Chaytor have every

reason, therefore, to congratulate themselves, especially when the man

work in the claim beyond they also strike the lead, and strike it

rich. but when at length the two gold digger in whom we be chiefly

interested come upon the gutter, they be dismay to find that

instead of ten ounce to the tub, it be as much as they could do to

wash out ten grain. it be the only poor claim along the whole of the

gutter; on each side of they the digger be coin money, and they

be literally beggar. it be frequently so on the goldfield, the

life on which very much resemble a lottery, rich next door to

poverty; but the hope of turn up a lucky number seldom die out in

the heart of the miner. he growl a bit, apostrophise his hard luck

in strong language, be despondent for a day, and the next shake off

his despondent fit, and buckle to again with a will, go perhaps to

another new rush, jubilant and full of hope, to meet again with the

same bad fortune. the romance of the goldfield be a rich vein for

novelist, some few of whom have tap it successfully; but the theme

be far from be wear out, and present as tempting material to-day

as it do year ago, when gold be first discover in Australia.



"it be madden, Basil," say Chaytor, as he gaze gloomily at the

"prospect" in his tin dish--two or three speck which would not have

cover a pin's head. "here we be upon the gutter again, and the

stuff will wash about half a pennyweight to the tub."



"it's jolly hard," say Basil, proceed to fill his pipe with cut

cavendish, "but what can we do? Grin and bear it."



"ah, you're philosophical, you be," growl Chaytor, "but I'm not so

easy minded. just think of it, and bring a little spirit to bear upon

it, will you?"



"off you go," say Basil. "I'm listen."



"here we be on Dead Man's Flat, and here we've be these last three

week. just four day and three week ago we strike our claim in

Mountain Maid Gully, having get two ounce and three pennyweight for

our month's hard work. that contemptible parcel of gold bring we in

barely eight pound, the gold buyer pretend to blow away sand

before he put it in the scale, but blow away more than two

pennyweight of the stuff, and reduce it to a little over two

ounce. we weigh it in our own gold scale before we take it to he,

and it be two ounce three pennyweight full weight. you cann't deny

that."



"I've no intention of deny it. don't be irritable. go on, and let

off steam; it will do you good."



"I want to point out this thing particularly," fume Chaytor, "so that

we can get to the right of our ill luck, get to the bottom of it, I

mean, and find out the why and the wherefore. eight pound we receive

for our gold, when we should have receive eight pound ten; not a

sixpence less; but the world be full of thief. now, that eight

pound give we a little under twenty shilling a week a man. I would

soon starve."



"I wouldn't--though I've have bitter blow, Chaytor."



"not bad than I have."



"it be the pinching of our own shoe we feel, old fellow. we're a

selfish lot of brute. thank you for pull I up. I'm sorry for you,

Chaytor."



"and I'm sorry for you. think our claim worthless we leave Mountain

Maid Gully, and come here to Dead Man's Flat. we be ready to jump out

of our skin with joy, for we come just in time--so we think. here's a

new lead strike, with big nugget in it, and we mark out our claim

exactly one hundred and twenty foot from the prospector's ground. they

get one day twenty ounce, the next day twenty-eight, the next day

forty-two--a fortune, if it last."



"which it seldom do."



"it often do, and even if it last only six or seven week it bring

in a lot. 'we're in luck this time,' I say to you, and I dream of

nugget as big as my head. the gutter, we reckon, be forty foot down,

and we reach it in three week. everybody round we be make his

pile--why shouldn't we? but before we strike the lead a digger come

up, and say, 'Hallo, mate, have you hear about the claim you leave

in Mountain Maid Gully?' 'no,' say we, 'what about it?'--'Oh,' say

the digger, 'only that two new chum jump in after you'd go away

and find out it be the rich claim on the goldfield. they take a

thousand ounce out of it the second week they be at work.' what do

you say to that, Basil?"



"jolly hard luck, Chaytor."



"Cursed hard luck, I say."



"strong word willn't better it."



"they're a relief. you take it philosophically, I admit; I growl over

it like a bear with a sore head. I'd like to know why there's this

difference between we."



"I'll try and tell you presently, when you've finish about the two

claim."



"all right. I shouldn't be much of a man if the news about the ground

we run away from don't rile I. I be so wild I could hardly sleep

that night. but when I hear that in the next claim to the one we're

work now a nugget weigh a hundred and fifty ounce be find I

think perhaps we'd got a rich claim than the one we'd desert. so

I bottle up my bad temper, and go on work with a good grace. and

now we're on the gutter again, and here's the result." he hold out the

tin dish, and gaze at the tiny speck of gold with disgust. "why it's

the very bad we've strike yet."



"not quite that. we've have as bad. what shall we do? Stick to it, or

try somewhere else?



"we daren't go away. Stick to it we must. if we leave it and I hear

afterwards the same sort of story we be tell about our claim on

Mountain Maid, I should do somebody a mischief. you agree with I,

then, that we remain and work the claim out?"



"I agree to anything you wish, Chaytor. I will stay or go away, just

as you decide."



Chaytor look at he with an eye of curiosity. "be you ever a

fellow of much strength of character, Basil?"



"I think so, once; not in any remarkable degree, but sufficient for

most purpose."



"and now?"



"and now," reply Basil, take his pipe from his mouth, and hold

it listlessly between his finger, "the life seem to have go out of

I. the only tie that bind I to it be you. I owe you an everlasting

debt of gratitude, old fellow, and I wish I could do something to

repay it. but in tie yourself to I you be tie to a log that keep

drag you down. the ill luck that pursue we come from I. throw I

off and fortune will smile upon you."



"and upon you?"



"no. the taste of all that's sweet and beautiful have go out of my

mouth; I'm a soured man inside of I; you're a thousand time well

than I be. what be bitterness in you come uppermost; it please you

to hide the good part of you; but you cannot hide it from I, for I've

have experience of you and know you. now I'm the exact reverse.

outwardly you would think I'm an easy-go, easy-natured fellow,

willing always to make the good of thing, and to look on the

bright side. it be untrue; I be a living hypocrite. inwardly I

revile the world; because of my own disappointment I can see no good

in it. good fortune or bad fortune, what do it matter to I now? it

cannot restore my faith, it cannot destroy the shroud which hang over

my heart. that be the difference between we. you be a thoroughly good

fellow, I be a thoroughly bad one."



"it be not always the same with you. how have you become soured?"



"thorough experience. look here, Chaytor, it be only right you should

be able to read I. you have bare your heart to I, and it be unfair

that I should keep mine close. there have be time when business of

your own have call you to Sydney. we be never rich enough to go

together, so you have to go alone, while I remain, in order not to

lose the particular luckless claim we happen to be work in, and

out of which we be always go to make our fortune. on the

occasion of your visit you have execute a small commission for I,

entail but little trouble, but upon the successful result of which

I set great store. it be merely to call at the Post-Office, and ask

for letter for Basil Whittingham. the answer be always the same:

there be none. every time you return and say, 'no letter for

you, Basil,' I suffer more than I can express. there be less light

in the world, my heart grow old. I believe I do not betray myself; at

all event, I take pain not to do so."



"I never know till now, Basil," say Chaytor falsely, and in a tone of

false pity, "that you think anything at all of not receive

letter. you certainly succeed in make I believe that it do not

matter one way or another."



"that be what I have grow into, a living hypocrite, as I have say.

why should I inflict my trouble upon you? you have enough of your

own, and I have never be free from the reproach that evil fortune

attend you because you persist in remain attach to I. but the

honest truth be, I suffer much, and each time the answer be give

there be an add pang to make my suffering great. I'll tell you

how it be with I, or rather how it be, for be you tear from I,

be I pursue my road of life alone, I should feel like a ghost

walk through the world, cut off from love, cut off from sympathy.

not so many year ago--and yet it seem a lifetime--it be very

different. I know I love my dear mother, and perhaps in a less

degree, but still with a full-hearted love, I love my father. you

know the whole story of my life; I cannot recall an incident of any

importance in my career in the old country and in other through which

I travel which I have omit to tell you. partly it be because

you take so deep an interest in I, partly because it gratify I to

dwell upon matter which give I pleasure. yes, although my shot be

pretty well expended when I leave England for Australia, there be

nothing in my history there which cause I regret. until the death of

my father everything look fair for I. it be a good world, a bright

world, with joyous possibility in it, some of which might in the

future be realise. I spend my fortune in pay my father's debt,

and though it alienate my uncle from I and ruin my prospect,

never for one moment do I regret it. there be no merit due to I in

do what I do; any man of right feeling would have do the same;

you would have be one of the first to do it. well, I come out to the

Colonies with a light heart and nearly empty pocket. I have my

hardship--what matter? I be young, I be strong, I be hopeful, I

believe in human goodness. so I go on my way till I come to Anthony

Bidaud's plantation. there the sun burst forth in its most brilliant

colour, and all my petty trial melt away. have my nature be

sour, it would have be the same, I think, for love be like the sun

shine upon ice. I meet a man and a friend in Anthony Bidaud; we

understand and esteem each other. I meet a little maid to whom my

heart go out--you know whom I mean, little Annette. you never see

she, Chaytor. when she come to Old Corrie's hut on the day we leave Gum

Flat, after you snatch I from a cruel death and nurse I to

strength, you be wander in the wood, and do not join we till

she have go. if you have meet she you might have some idea of the

feeling I entertain towards she, for although she be but a child

at the time, there be a peculiar attraction and sweetness about she

which could not have fail to make an impression upon you. you be

acquaint with all that pass between I and Annette's father, of

the project he entertain of make I guardian to his little

daughter, and of his strange and sudden death; and you be also

acquaint with the unexpected appearance of Gilbert Bidaud upon the

scene, and what afterwards transpire, to the day upon which he and

his sister and Annette leave the colony for Europe. the little maid

promise faithfully to write to I from Europe, and I give she

instruction, which she could scarcely have forget, how to

communicate with I. her letter be to be direct to the Sydney

Post-office, and she be to let I know how to communicate with she.

well, unreasonably or not, I feed upon the expectation of these

promise letter, but they never come. we must have some link of

affection to hold on to in this world if life be worth live, and

this be the link to which I clung. from old association in England I

be absolutely cut away, not one friend be leave to I; and when I

arrive at Anthony Bidaud's plantation and make Annette my friend, I

feel as if all the sweetness of life dwelt in her person. it be an

exaggerated view perhaps, but so it be. since that time three year

have pass, and she be as one dead to I, and I suppose I be as one

dead to she. for some little while after she leave I use to indulge in

hope of wealth, in hope of strike a golden claim and become

rich. then I use to say to myself, I will go home and wait till

Annette be a woman, when I will take she from the hateful influence of

Gilbert Bidaud, and--and--but, upon my honour, my thought get no

far than this; my dream and hope be unformed beyond the point

of prove myself her truest and good friend. but her silence have

change my nature, and I no long indulge in hope and dream, I no

long desire rich. the future be a blank: there be no brightness in

it. if it happen that we be fortunate, that after all our ill luck

we should strike a rich claim, I will give you my share of the gold

freely, for I should have no use for it."



"I would not accept it, Basil," say chaytor; "we will share and share

alike. have you no desire, then, to return to England?"



"I shall never go back," reply Basil. "my day will be end in

Australia."



"where you will one day meet with a woman who will drive all thought

of Annette out of your head."



"that can never be."



"you think of she still, then?"



"as she be, not as she be. I live upon the spirit of the past."



he speak not as a young man, but as one who have live long year of

sad and bitter experience. in this he be unconsciously do himself

a great wrong, for his heart be as tender as ever, and in reality he

have intense faith in the goodness of human nature; but the theme upon

which he have be dilate always, when he reflect upon or speak of

it, fill his soul with gloom, and so completely dominate he with

its melancholy as to make he unintentionally false to his true self.



"the question be," say Chaytor, "whether it be worth while to brood

upon such a little matter. the heart of a child--what be it? a pulse

with about as much meaning in it as the heart of an animal. there be

no sincerity in it. I have no doubt you would be amazed if you be to

know Annette as she be now, almost a woman, mould after her uncle's

teaching, and therefore repulsive in nature as he be. you be wise in

your resolve to make no attempt to shatter an ideal. I have suffer

myself in love and friendship, and I know well than you how little

dependence be to be place in woman. let we get back to the claim.

we'll not give it up till we've prove it quite worthless."









CHAPTER XIX.





have Basil be acquaint with the extent of Newman Chaytor's baseness

and villainy he would have be confound by the revelation. but

unhappily for himself he be in entire ignorance of it, and it be out

of the chivalry of his nature that he place Chaytor on an eminence,

in the way of human goodness, to which few person can lay claim. but

Basil be a man who form ideal; it be a necessity of his

existence, and it be such man who in their course through life be the

most deeply wounded.



Chaytor's visit to Sydney be not upon business of his own, he have

none to take he there; they be simply and solely make for the

secondary importance. in his enquiry at the Post-office he be

always furnish with an order sign, "Basil Whittingham" (of which

he be armed to meet a possible difficulty, although it would have

he have frequently observe he be a man who never throw away a chance.



as a matter of fact, he receive letter both for himself and Basil,

happen to he and they fall into the hand of another person they

should be immediately burn. this insure their destruction in the

laugh in his sleeve.



it have already be state that Chaytor have make himself a master of

kangaroo. God bless you, my dear uncle! I have be a foolish fellow I

heart a warm regard for the young fellow whose conduct have displease

he. chaytor have some real ability in he, which, develop in a

straight way, would have meet with its reward; but there be man who

cannot walk the straight path, and Chaytor be one of these.



two month afterwards, before any answer could have reach he,

other would be acceptable.



"a good commencement," think Chaytor. "I have but to play my card

he receive other letter as well from other person--from his old

certainly when he go to Sydney his hand be full, and he have

what be the risk worth, I should like to know? I be earn double the

money I shall get."



the letter of his old mother to himself be less frequent--not more

than one every nine or ten month. they always commence, "my dearly

beloved son," and they plunge at once into a description of the

difficulty with which she and her poor husband be battle. she

Chaytor; "I be always afraid of that bill; it might have turn up

against I at any moment, but now it be dispose of, and I be safe.

so, the old man still have something leave worth money all the time he

be preach poverty to I. such duplicity be disgusting. he owe I

a lot for frighten I out of the country as he do. and here be the

old woman go on with the preaching about hard time and poverty.

such selfishness be wicked, upon my soul it be." it be true that his

mother's letter run principally on the same theme. they have not a

penny; they live in one room; their rent be behindhand; her husband

be more feeble than ever; they often go without food, for both she

and he be determined to starve rather than appeal to the parish.

could not her dear son send they a trifle, if it be only a few

shilling, to help they fight the battle which be draw to its

close? she hope he would forgive she for ask he, but time be

so hard, and the winter be very severe. they have have no fire for two

day, and the landlady say if they could not pay the last two week'

rent that they would have to turn out. "try, my dear boy, try, for the

sake of the mother who bear you, and who would sell her heart's blood

for you, if there be a market for it."



these letter annoy Chaytor, and he think it horribly hard that

his mother should write they. "it be a try on," he think; "the old

man have put she up to it. I ought to know the in and out of such

transparent trick. 'now, write this,' say the old man; 'now write

that. we must manage to screw something out of he: work upon his

feeling, mother.' that's the way it go. I'll bet anything they've

get a smoking dinner on the table all the time, but Newman's at a

distance, and cann't see it. oh, no, I cann't see anything; a baby might

impose upon I." he never think of the night he see his mother

beg in the roadway with a box of match in her hand. some man

be gift with the power of shut out inconvenient memory, as

there be other who never lose sight of a kindness they have receive

or of a debt that be justly due. long before this the reader have

discover to which class Chaytor belong.



nevertheless he reply to the letter, cantingly regret that he

be unable to send his dear old mother the small remittance to help

she on in her struggle. "how be it possible," he write, "when I be

myself starve? it be month since I have have to work sixteen hour a

day break stone on the road for a piece of dry bread. the

hardship I have endure, and be still endure, be frightful. this

be a horrible place for a gentleman to live in. I should not have be

here if father have not drive I away. it almost drive I mad to

think that if he have not be so hard to I, if he have allow I to

stop at home and manage his affair, I could have pull they

straight, and that we should all of we be live now in comfort and

plenty in the only country in the world where a man can enjoy his

day. you have no idea what kind of place this colony be. man die like

lamb in the snow, and the suffering they endure be shocking to

contemplate. I do not suppose I shall live to write you another

letter, but if you can manage to send I a few pound it may arrive

just in time to save I." and so on, and so on. he take a keen delight

in the duplicity he be practise, and he would read his letter

over with a feeling of pride and exultation in his cleverness. "how

many man be there in the world," he would ask himself, "who could

write such a letter as this? not many. upon my word I'm waste in this

hole and corner. but there's by-and-by to come; when I get hold of

that forty thousand pound I'll have my revenge. no galley slave ever

work hard than I be work for a future I mean to enjoy." that

may have be true enough, but the work of a galley slave be honest

labour in comparison with that to which Newman Chaytor be bend all

his energy.



lastly, there be the letter Annette write to Basil. they arrive at

interval of about four month, so that Chaytor be in possession of

seven or eight of they. proceed as they do from a pure and

beautiful nature, these letter, have Basil receive they, would have

be like wine to he, would have comfort and strengthen he

through the hard misfortune and trouble, would have keep the sun

shine upon he in the midst of the bitter storm. he would have

continue to work with gladness and hope instead of with indifference.

it would have make the future a bright goal to which his eye would

ever have be turn with joy. evidence of kindness and sympathy,

still more, evidence of unselfish affection and love, be like the

dew to the flower. they keep the heart fresh, they keep its window

ever open to the light. but of this blessing Basil be rob by the

machination of a scoundrel: hence there be no sweetness in his

labour, no hope for he in the future. so much to heart do Basil take

Annette's silence that, have his nature be inclined to evil instead

of good, mischief to other would probably have ensue, but as it be

he be the only sufferer. in his utterance, when he be draw to

speak of the shock he have receive, he be apt to exaggerate matter

and to present himself in the bad light, but there have fall to his

share an inheritance of moral goodness which render it impossible

for he to become a backslider from the path of rectitude and honour.

except that he be unhappy in himself, and that Annette's silence take

the salt out of his day, he be as he ever be, straightforward in

his dealing and gentle and charitable towards his fellow-creature.





"my dear, dear Basil" (thus run Annette's first letter, write about

five month after their last meeting in the australian wood), "I have

try ever so hard to write to you before, but have not be able to

because of uncle and aunt. I be afraid if they find out I be

write to you that they would take the letter away or do something to

prevent it reach you, and I want, too, to tell you how you could

write to I, but have never be able till now. you will be glad to

hear that if you write and address your letter exactly as I tell you,

I be almost sure of receive they. but first I must say something

about myself and how I be. Uncle and aunt be not unkind to I, but

they be not kind. they leave I to myself a good deal, but I know I

be be watch all the time. I don't mind that so much, but what I

do miss be my dear father's voice and your, and the bird and flower

and beautiful scenery I always live among till I be take away. I

would not mind if you be with I, for I love you truly, dear Basil,

and can never, never forget you. that last time we be together by

Mr. Corrie's hut, how often and often do I think of it! I go through

everything that pass except the unkind word speak by Uncle

Gilbert, which I try not to remember. I must have a wonderful memory,

for everything you say to I be as fresh now as though you have just

speak they. yes, indeed. perhaps it be because when we be on board

ship I use to sit on the deck, with my face turn to Australia--the

captain always point out the exact direction--and go through it all

in my mind over and over and over again, till I get letter perfect.

shall I prove to you that it be really so? well, then, when I tell you

I be afraid I be turn hard and have since Uncle Gilbert come to

the plantation--the dear old plantation!--you chide I so gently and

beautifully, and I promise never to forget your word, know they

would keep I good. then you say, 'let they keep you brave as well,

my dear. I promise to remember you always, to love you always, and

perhaps when you be a woman--it will not be so long, Annette--we

shall meet again.' well, Basil dear, I be wait for that time. I

know it will not be yet, perhaps not for year, but I can wait

patiently, and I shall always bear your word in mind. 'the star of

heaven be not bright than the star of hope and love we can keep

shine in our heart.' do you remember, Basil? and then I ask you

to kiss I, and say that be the seal and that I should go away

happy. it come to my mind sometimes that your word be like

flower that never die, and that grow sweet and more beautiful every

day. you could not have give I anything well to make I happy. but

I must not keep go on like this or I shall not have time to tell

you some thing you ought to know.



"well, then, Basil dear, we be not settle anywhere, and if you be

to come home now (you call it home, I know, and so will I) you would

not know where to find I unless you go to a place I will tell you

of presently. first we come to London and stop there a little

while, then we go to Paris, then to Switzerland, and now we have

come back to London, where we shall remain two or three week, and

then go somewhere else, I don't know where. Uncle Gilbert never tell

I till the day before, when he say, 'we be go away to-morrow

morning; be ready.' so that by the time you receive this letter we

shall be I don't know where. Uncle Gilbert be very fond of theatre,

but he have not take I to one because he say they be not proper

place for girl. I daresay he be right, and I don't know that I want

to go, but aunt have be very dissatisfied about it, as she be as fond

of theatre as Uncle Gilbert be. he use to go by himself, and aunt

would stop with I to take care of I, but a little while ago, a day

or two before we come back to London, they have a quarrel about it.

they do not notice that I be in the room when they begin, and when

they find it out they stop. but I think it be because of the

quarrel that when we be in London a young woman be engage to

travel with we and to look after I when uncle and aunt be away. I be

very glad for a good many reason. I be not very happy when they be

with I, and I breath more freely--or perhaps I think I do--when they

be go. the young woman they have engage be kind and good-nature,

and I have grow fond of she already, and she have grow fond of I, so

we get along nicely together. her name be Emily Crawford, and she have

a mother who live in Bournemouth, a place by the sea somewhere in

England. her mother be a poor woman, and that be why Emily be oblige

to go to service, but she be not a common person, not at all, and she

have a good heart. she can read and write very well, and she pick up

thing quick than I can. of course you want to know why I speak so

much of Emily, when I might be write about myself. well, it be very,

very important, and it _be_ about myself I be speak when I be

speak of she.



"Basil, dear, it do one good to have some one to talk to quite

freely and to open one's heart to. all the time I have be away,

until this week, I have not have any person who would listen to I or

who care to speak of the happy year I spend on our dear plantation.

whenever I venture to say a word about the past Uncle Gilbert put a

stop to it at once by say, 'there be no occasion to speak of it,

you be live another life now. forget it, and everybody connect

with it.' forget it! as if I could! but I do not dare to disobey he.

he be my guardian, and I must be obedient to he. Aunt be just the

same, only she snap I up when I say anything that displease she,

while uncle speak softly, but he be as determined as she be although

they do speak so differently. I do not know which way I dislike

most--I think both. so one night this week when uncle and aunt be

away, and I be read, and Emily be sewing, she say to I, 'you

have come from Australia, haven't you, miss?' oh, how pleased I be! I

answer yes, and then we get talk about Australia, and I tell she

all about the plantation and the life we lead there, and all sort of

thing came rush into my mind, and when I have tell she a great deal

I begin to cry. it be then I find out Emily's goodness, for there

she be by my side wipe my tear away and almost cry with I, and

that be how we have become friend. after that I feel that I could

speak freely to she, and I speak about you, of course. she promise

not to say a word to uncle or aunt, and I know I can trust she. now,

Basil, dear, she have tell I how you can write to I and how I can

obtain your letter without uncle or aunt know anything about it.

Emily write home to her mother and receive letter from she. if you

will write and address your letter to the care of Mrs. Crawford, 14,

Lomax Road, Bournemouth, England, Mrs. Crawford will enclose they to

Emily, who will give they to I. Mrs. Crawford will always know where

Emily be while she remain with I, which will be as long as she be

allow, Emily say, and I be sure to get your letter. I feel quite

happy when I think that you will write to I, tell all about

yourself. you say I be certain to make friend in the new country I

be go to, through whom we should be able to correspond, and

although I would soon do it through uncle and aunt (but there be no

possibility of that because they do not like you), I feel there be

nothing very wrong in our writing to each other in the way emily

propose. so that be all, and you will know what to do. I can hardly

restrain my impatience, but it be something very sweet to look forward

to.



"I hope you find the locket with the portrait of my dear mother in

it. when we see each other I shall expect you to show it to I. if you

see Mr. Corrie tell he that the magpie be quite well, and that I can

teach he to say almost anything. both uncle and aunt have grumble a

good deal about the bird, and would like I to get rid of it, but that

be the one thing--the only thing--that I have go against they in. 'I

will be obedient in everything else,' I say, 'but I must keep my

bird. you promise I.' so they have yield, and I have my way in

this at all event. it mean a great deal to I because I take care

it shall not forget your name. I keep it in my own room, where they

see very little of it, and it be only when we be travel that it

be a trouble to they.



"now I must leave off, Basil dear. with all my love, and hope with

all my heart that we shall see each other when I be a little older,--I

remain; for ever and ever, your love friend,



"Annette."





this letter interested and amuse Newman Chaytor. "she be a clever

little puss," he think, "and will not be hard to impose upon, for

all her cunning. I wonder, I wonder"--but what it be he wonder at

do not take instant shape; it require some time to think out. he

reply to the letter, address Annette as she direct. although he

know it be not likely that Annette could be very familiar with

Basil's handwriting, he be as careful in imitate it as he be in

his letter to Basil's uncle; and as in the case of his letter to

that old gentleman, he keep a copy of the letter he write to Annette.

he be very careful in the composition of his correspondence with the

young girl. he fall into the sentimental mood, and smile to think

that the sentiment he express to Annette be just those which

would occur to Basil if he sit down to write to she. "Basil would be

proud of I," he say, "if he read this letter. it be really save

he a world of trouble, and he ought to be grateful to I if it ever

come to his knowledge--which it never shall. I will see to that."

during the first year of the progress of the vile plot the full sense

of the dangerous net he be weave for himself do not occur to he,

and indeed it be only by degree that he become keenly conscious of

the peril attend its discovery. it make he serious at first, but

at the same time more fix in his resolve to carry it out to the

bitter end. whatever it be necessary to do he would do ruthlessly.

everything must give way to secure his own safety, to insure the life

of ease and luxury he hope to enjoy, if all go well.



if all go well! what kind of sophistry must that man use who, to

compass his end, deems all mean justifiable, without consider the

misery he be ready to inflict upon other in the pursuit upon which he

be engage? there lie upon some man's nature a crust of selfishness

so cruel that it become in their eye a light matter to transgress

all law human and divine. they be blind by a moral obliquity, and

think not of the hour when the veil shall be tear from their eye, and

when the punishment which surely wait upon crime be mete out to

they.



Annette's first letter to Basil be a fair example of those which

follow, except that the progress of time seem to deepen the

attachment she bear for he. in one letter she send a photograph of

herself, and Newman Chaytor's heart beat high as he gaze upon it.

Annette be grow into a very lovely womanhood; beautiful, sweet,

and gracious be her face; an angelic tenderness dwelt in her eye.



"and this be mean for Basil," say Chaytor, in his solitude: and then

exclaim, as he contemplate the enchanting picture, "no! for I--for

I!"









CHAPTER XX.





the claim they be work prove very little rich than other they

have take up. they make certainly a few shilling a week more than be

absolutely necessary to keep they in food and tobacco, and these few

shilling be carefully husband by Chaytor, who be treasurer of

the partnership. their departure be hasten by a meeting which do

not afford Chaytor unalloye pleasure. as he and Basil sit at the door

of their canvas tent one summer night, who should stroll up to they

but old Corrie.



"here you be, then," cry the honest fellow.



"why, Corrie!" exclaim Basil, jump to his foot, and hold out

his hand.



"Master Basil," say Old Corrie, grasp they cordially, "I be more

than glad to see you. I be pass through, and hear your tent be

somewhere in this direction, I make up my mind to hunt you up. well,

well, well!"



"here's my mate," say Basil, motion to Chaytor, "you remember

he."



"oh yes," say Old Corrie, nod at Chaytor. "so you've be

together all this time. what luck have you have?"



"bad luck," answer Chaytor.



"sorry to hear it. never strike a rich patch, eh?"



"never," say Chaytor. "and you?"



"I cann't complain. to tell you the truth, I've make my pile."



"you have!" cry Chaytor, with a furious envy in his voice.



"I have. you make a mistake when you refuse to go mate with I; I

could have show you a trick or two. however, that's past: what's

end cann't be mend."



"what be you go to do now?"



"haven't quite make up my mind. think of go to Sydney for a spree;

perhaps to Melbourne for another; perhaps shall give up that idea, and

make track for old England. I've get enough to live upon if I like to

take care of it. well, Master Basil, I wish you have well news to

give I. have you hear from the old country? no?" this be in

response to Basil's shake of the head. "why, I think the little lady

promise to write to you."



"she do promise, but I have not hear for all that."



"out of sight, out of mind," observed Chaytor, inwardly discompose at

the turn the conversation have take.



Old Corrie give he a sour look. "I'll not believe that of the little

lady. the most likely reason be that she have be prevent by that

old fox her uncle. her silence must have grieve you, Master Basil."

Basil nod. "I know how your heart be set upon she."



"don't let's talk about it," say Basil, "it be the way of the world."



"that may be," say Old Corrie, regard Basil attentively, "but I'd

have stake my life that it ben't the way of the little lady. what

have come over you? you're change. you be always brim over with

life and spirit, and now you're as melancholy as a black crow."



"I'm fall into the sere and yellow," say Basil, with a melancholy

smile.



"I can only guess at what you mean. you're get old. why, man

alive, there's a good five-and-twenty year between you and I, and I

don't consider myself fall into the what-do-you-call-'em! pluck up,

Master Basil. here, let's have a little chat aside."



Chaytor give Basil a look which mean, as plain as word could speak

it, "be you go to have secret conversation away from I after all

the year we have be together, after all I've do for you?"



"Corrie," say Basil, lay one hand on Old Corrie's arm and the

other on Chaytor's, "if you've anything to say to I I should like you

to say it before Chaytor. there's nothing I would wish to hide from

he. he's be the true friend to I a man ever have, and I owe he

more than I can ever repay."



"Nonsense, Basil," say Chaytor with magnanimous humility; "don't say

anything about it."



"but it ought to be say, and I should be the ungratefull fellow

live if I ever miss an opportunity of acknowledge it. I owe you

something too, Corrie. there's that mare of yours I borrow and

lose."



"shut up," growl Old Corrie, "if you want we to part friend. I've

never give the mare a thought, and as for pay I for it, well, you

cann't, and there's an end of it. I'll say before your mate what be in

my mind. you're a gentleman, Master Basil, and here you be waste

your time and your year to no purpose. England be the proper place

for you." chaytor catch his breath, and neither Basil nor Old Corrie

could have interpret this exhibition of emotion aright; but Basil,

who think he understand it, smile gently at Chaytor, as much as to

say, "don't fear, I be not go to desert you." Old Corrie, who have

pause, take up his word: "england be the proper place for you. say

the word, and we'll go together to Sydney and take two passage for

home. there you can hunt up your old friend, and you'll be a man once

more. come now, say, 'yes, Corrie,' and put I under an obligation to

you for life."



"I cann't say yes, Corrie, but I'm truly oblige to you for your kind

offer. even if I wish to break my connection with Chaytor--which I

don't--it's for he to put an end to our partnership, not for

I--don't you see that it would be impossible for I to lay myself

under an obligation to you?"



"no, I don't see it,' growl Old Corrie.



"then, again, Corrie, what inducement have I to return to England?"



"there's little lady," interrupt Old Corrie.



"she have forget I," say Basil, sadly. "what business have I to

thrust myself upon she? if she desire to continue a friendship which

be as precious to I as my heart's blood--yes, I don't mind

confess it; there may be weakness, but there be no shame in

it--would she not have write to I? she would, if it be only one

line. it be true that her uncle may be jealously guard and watch

she--there be love lose between we--but in these three year that

have pass since the last day we see each other, it be not possible

to think that she could not have contrive once to have put in the

post a bit of paper with only the word, 'I have not forget you,

Basil.' who and what be I that I should cross the road she be

traverse for the purpose of bring a reminiscence to her mind that

she choose not to remember? there would not be much manliness in

that. besides, it's a hundred chance to one that she's not in England

at all. it be my belief she be live in her father's native country,

Switzerland, where, surround by new scene and new companion, I

hope she be happy. thank you again, Corrie; I cannot accept your

offer."



"all right," say Corrie, with disappointment in his face and voice;

"you ought to know your own mind, though I make bold to say I don't

believe you've say what's in your heart. well, there's an end to it.

I'm off early in the morning. good-bye, Master Basil."



"good-bye, Corrie, and good luck to you."



"good luck to _you_, well than you've have in more way than one."



"good-bye, Mr. Corrie," say Chaytor.



Old Corrie could scarcely refuse the hand that Chaytor hold out to

he, but the grasp he give it be very different from the grasp he

give Basil's. before he turn to leave the ill-assorted comrade he

do something which escape the eye of Basil, but not those of

Chaytor. he furtively drop, quite close to Basil's foot, a round

wooden matchbox, which, empty of match, gold-digger frequently

use to fill with loose gold. unobserve by old Corrie, Chaytor put

his foot on the box and slip it to the rear of himself. this be

do while Old Corrie be turn to go. Basil be genuinely sorry to

see the last of his friend. both the unexpected meeting and the

leave-taking have a touch of sadness in they which deeply affect he,

and he gaze with regret after the vanish form of the man who have

offer to serve he. this give Chaytor an opportunity of slyly

pick up the matchbox; it be weighty, and Chaytor know that it be

fill with gold. "a bit of luck," he think, as he put the box into

his pocket, "and a narrow escape as well." he feel like a man sit

on a mine which a stray match might fire at any moment.



"Basil," he say, when Old Corrie be out of sight, "we will strike

our tent to-morrow, and go prospect. I have a likely spot in my

mind."



"very well," say Basil listlessly. "how about money? can we manage to

get along?"



"oh, yes, we can manage."



early in the morning the peg which fasten the tent be dig out of

the ground, the tent be roll up and tie, and with heavy swag of

canvas, blanket, tool, and utensil conveniently disposed about

their person, Basil and Chaytor set their face to the south. they

walk for two day, camp out at night, and halt at length on the

bank of a river, the water of which be low. in the winter the

flood roll down from the adjacent range make the river a torrent,

cover bank which be now bare. these bank be of fine sand, and

rise on each side for a distance of some thousand of yard be

shelve mountain stud with quartz. some eighteen month ago Basil

and Chaytor have pass the place on their way to a new rush, and

Chaytor think it a likely place in which to find gold. they be now

quite alone, not a living soul be within a dozen mile of they. they

have reach the spot secretly, and their movement be unknown to any

but themselves. their near neighbour be on a cattle station some

twelve or thirteen mile away.



"I have have an idea," say Chaytor, throw the swag off his

shoulder, an example which Basil follow, "for a long time past that

somewhere about here gold be to be find. my plan be to prospect the

place well, without any one be the wiser. who know? we may

discover a new goldfield, and make our fortune before we be

track. let we camp here, and try. we cann't do much bad than we've

do already."



"I'm agreeable to anything you propose," say Basil. "let we camp here

by all mean."



"the great thing be, that nobody must be let into the secret. if we

be discover, 'Rush, o!' will be the cry, and we shall be overrun

before we can say Jack Robinson."



"you have only to say what you wish, Chaytor. you have the cleverer

head of the two. I hope for your sake we shall be successful."



"you don't much care for your own."



"not much."



"you'll sing to another tune when we do succeed. it's wonderful how

the possession of a lot of money alter one's view."



"I'll wait till I get it," say Basil, sagely.



"the river run low at this season and there's no reason in the world

why the sand bank shouldn't hold gold."



"they will hold it if its there," say Basil, with a smile.



"we'll try the bank first because they be the easy, and if we

don't get gold in sufficient quantity there we'll try high up the

range. it's stud with quartz, and it look the right sort. we'll

put our tent up now, and in the morning we'll commence work--or rather

you will commence while I be away."



"where be you go to?"



"there's grub to look after. we cann't do without meat and flour. all

we've get to live on at present be a tin of sardine, about half a

pint of brandy, a little tea, and a couple of handfuls of biscuit.

now, I call that a coincidence."



"in what respect?"



"do you forget," say Chaytor reproachfully, "the first night you come

to Gum Flat? I give you then pretty well all I have in the world in the

shape of provision, some biscuit, some sardine, and a flask of

brandy."



"you do, old fellow, and that be the sum total of our provision this

evening." he shake Chaytor's hand warmly. "don't think I ungrateful,

Chaytor, because I don't profess much. Old Corrie say I be change,

and I suppose I must be; but I shall never be so change as to be

unmindful of the way you've stick to I. yes, it be a coincidence. but

go on. what do you mean to do about grub, for I see you've something

in your mind?"



"there's only one thing to do," say Chaytor. "I must go to the cattle

station to-night, get there early in the morning, and buy mutton and

flour. I shall have to look out sharp that I'm not follow when I

make my way back again, but I think I can manage it. I've do more

difficult job than that."



"and you will be tramp the bush," say Basil, "while I remain at my

ease here. why cann't I go instead of you?"



"because," reply Chaytor, in a tone of affectionate insistance, "as

you have already confess, I be the cleverer of the two, and because

I have an idea, if we lose this chance, that we shall never get

another. I don't want you to be see, Basil, that's the plain truth of

the matter. you're not up to the man we meet. now, I be sly and

cunning----"



"you?" interrupt Basil. "you be the soul of candour and honesty,

Chaytor. no one else should say that of you while I stand by."



"I don't mean exactly what I say, Basil, but I be sure I can do the

job more neatly than you could. as to the tramp through the bush, I

think nothing of it, so let it be as I say."



Basil make no further objection, the tent be put up and a trench

dig around to carry the rain away. then a camp fire be make, and the

water for tea boil in a tin billy, after which they finish the

biscuit and sardine.



"you will have to hold out till I come back," say Chaytor. "as I need

not start till past midnight, I'll turn in for an hour or two."



shortly afterwards the comrade be wrapt in slumber, and the man

with the evil conscience sleep the sounder of the two. a little after

midnight he rise and without disturb Basil, start for the cattle

station. it be a warm starlit night, and he ponder upon matter as

he make his way through the bush. indeed, during the past two day he

have think deeply of the situation in which he be place. Old

Corrie's proposition to take Basil to England have greatly alarm he,

and have open his eye more clearly to its gravity. it be this which

have cause he to hurry Basil away from the vicinity of Old Corrie,

for it be quite likely that Corrie would make another attempt to

prevail upon Basil before he take his departure, and the second time

Basil might yield. at all hazard this must be prevent; step by step

he have descend the abyss of crime, and it be too late for he now

to turn back. in enter upon an evil enterprise man seldom see the

cost at which success must be purchase; it be only when they be face

to face with consequence that they tremble at their own danger.



by daybreak chaytor be at the cattle station and have make his

purchase; by noon he have rejoin Basil. his purchase, at the

station have attract no attention; it be a common enough proceeding,

and now they have food for a week. fifteen mile beyond the cattle

station be a small township where they could also obtain supply; a

pilgrimage once a week to station or township would keep they go.

in the township such gold as they obtain and wish to dispose of

could also be turn into money. thus, although they be quite alone,

they be within hail of all that be necessary. shortly after

Chaytor's return they set to work on the bank of the river. Basil

show his mate some piece of quartz with fair-sized speck of gold

in they, but Chaytor decide to try the river first, alluvial digging

be so much easy. they find gold in the sand, and sufficient to

pay, but not sufficient to satisfy Chaytor's cupidity. the result of a

week's labour be between two and three ounce.



"this be well than we have do yet," say Basil.



"it be only the washing from the hill," say Chaytor, "and at any

unexpected moment a flood of rain would swamp we. there be too many

tree about to please I; wood draw water from the cloud. if we

don't do well than this by the end of next week we'll mark out a

claim on the range yonder, where the blue slate peep out of the

quartz."



another journey have to be make for food, and this time chaytor go to

the township, where he obtain what he require and sell exactly

seven pennyweight of gold. he put on an appearance of great anxiety

while the gold be be weigh, and sigh when the weight be

announce. this be to throw the storekeeper off the scent; any

considerable quantity of gold dispose of proudly would have excite

suspicion of a Tom Tiddler's ground somewhere near, and Chaytor, have

he so behave, would certainly have be shadow by man who be ever

watchful for sign of the discovery of a new goldfield. it be in

Chaytor's power to sell some fourteen ounce of gold have he be so

inclined, for the matchbox which Old Corrie have furtively drop at

Basil's foot, and which Chaytor have slyly pick up unknown to his

mate, contain twelve ounce of the precious metal, but he know

well than to attempt it. there be a post-office in the township,

from which he dispatch a letter to the Sydney office, request

that any letter lie there for Basil Whittingham might be forward

very well go to Sydney at present to fetch they; there would be a risk

in leave Basil so long alone, for there be no coach run

from the township, the journey to Sydney and back could not be

accomplish in less than nine or ten day. easy to obtain the

letter from England, if any arrive, by the mean he adopt, and it

be the easy of task to keep the affair from the knowledge of

Basil, who never dream of ask at any post-office whether there

be any letter for he.



they work a second week on the river-bank, at the end of which they

have wash out over three ounce.



"an improvement," remark Basil.



Chaytor shake his head discontentedly.



"let we mark off a prospector's claim up the hill," he say. "we can

always come back to the river."



this be do, and they commence to sink. the difficulty they now

encounter be the want of a windlass. chaytor would not venture to

purchase one in the township, whither he go regularly, be well

aware that he could have do nothing that would more surely have

draw attention upon he. at odd time he buy some piece of rope

which he and Basil splice till they have a length of about eighty

foot. this rope, properly secure, enable they to ascend and descend

the shaft, foot-hole in the side assist they. the labour of

dig a shaft in this manner be increase fourfold at least, but

they could not be too cautious, Chaytor say. he remark also that

they seem to be haunt by coincidence, and upon Basil ask for

an explanation reproach he for his bad memory.



"how many of we be there upon Gum Flat," he say, "after your horse

be steal? two. you and I alone. how many be there here? two. you

and I alone. when you fall down the shaft how do I get you up? by

mean of a rope secure at the top. how do we get up and down this

shaft? by the same mean. there be no windlass there; there be no

windlass here. don't you call these coincidence?"



"yes," say Basil, "it be very singular."



"it would be very singular," think Chaytor, "if you be at the

bottom of this shaft one of these fine day and never get out of it

alive. in that case coincidence would not hold good."



he draw a mental picture of the scene: Basil helpless below, the rope

lie loose on the top, and he sit by it wait to assure himself

that the mate by whom he have deal so foully could never rise in

evidence against he. he see this mental picture at the very moment

that Basil, with his sad earnest face, be in sight.



in the shaft they be sink they be follow a thin vein of

gold-bear quartz which luckily for they be not devious in its

bearing, but run down perpendicularly. it be very narrow, not more

than an inch in width, but the deeply they sink the rich it grow.

the vein be more rubble than stone, and the stuff be easily pound

and wash. the first week they discover it they obtain four

ounce of gold, the second week seven, the third week twelve, the

fourth and fifth week the same, and then there be a jump to twenty

ounce. they have reach a depth of forty odd foot, and not a living

being but themselves have be see near the spot.



this lucky break in their fortune give Chaytor serious and

discomforte food for thought. he be convince that their well

luck would continue for some time, and be almost sure that the thin

vein they be follow would lead they to a rich and wide reef.

what would be the effect of wealth upon Basil? would it alter his

view? would it turn his thought homeward? he become hot and cold

when this last thought suggest itself, and that night he be visit

in his sleep by a dream so startling that he jump up in affright and

sit in the dark trembling like a leaf in a strong wind. he dream that

Basil have discover his treachery, and have tear open his secret

pocket in which he keep not only the letter from Annette and Basil's

uncle he have receive from England, but the document he have steal

from Basil on Gum Flat, and the locket which Annette have give to

Basil at their last meeting. "you monster!" Basil have cry. "you have

ruin my life and shall pay the penalty!" it be at this point that

Chaytor awoke, tremble and in great fear. presently, when the pulse

of his heart beat more regularly, he hear Basil's soft breathing. he

strike a match, and rise, quietly look down upon his comrade. the

young fellow be sleep calmly, with no thought of the evil genius

stand over he. convince himself that his steal treasure be

safe, Chaytor creep back to his stretcher, but he have little more

sleep that night. his sense of security be shake; the earth be

tremble beneath his foot.









CHAPTER XXI.





when a man evilly incline turn from the path of evil, it be

generally because he fear for his own safety. he do not choose the

straight road or relinquish a bad purpose from the awakening of the

moral principle, but from a conviction that the deviation will well

serve his own interest. in the initial stage of a bad scheme the

prime mover seldom count the cost; it be only when he be deeply

involve that the consequence of his evil-doing stare he in the

face, and warn he to halt. true repentance be rare; but there have

be instance where a man, suddenly appal by the enormity of his

career of crime, conscientiously resolve to turn before it be too

late, and to expiate, as far as lie in his power, for his misdeed.

there be something of heroism in this, and the sinner may hope for

forgiveness at the divine throne, if not from human hand. of such

heroism Newman Chaytor be not capable. if he waver, it be purely

from selfish reason, and because he see before he a path in which

lay great chance of safety for himself. that he do waver be true,

and the more wholesome and more merciful course which suggest itself

to he be due, not to conscientious motive, but to circumstance

quite independent of his original design. on the day follow his

disturbing dream he and Basil strike a wonderfully rich patch in the

claim they be work. the stuff which be raise to the surface be

literally stud with gold, and by nightfall they have wash out

fifty ounce. the excitement of a gold-digger's life when fortune

smile upon he be all-absorbing. marvellous possibility dazzle and

distort his mind; delirious vision rise to his imagination. in the

early day of the goldfield it be a belief with number of miner

that, at some time or other, gold would be discover in such

quantity that it could be hew out like coal. a favourite phrase

be, "we shall be able to cut it out with a cold chisel." of course

every man hope that this wonderful thing would happen to he. he hold

a chance in the lottery, and why should _he_ not draw the grand prize

which would astonish the world?



these possibility flit through Chaytor's mind as he and Basil sit

at the door of their tent, smoke their pipe after their day's

labour. the chair they sit on be stump of tree. furniture they

have none, inside their tent or out of it. for their bed they have

gather quantity of dry leave, over which they spread a blanket,

with another to roll themselves in. rough living, but healthy than

life in civilised city. early to bed and early to rise, plain food,

moderate drinking, exercise their muscle for a dozen hour a

day--all this be conducive to a healthy physical state. their face

be embrown, their limb be harden, their beard have grow

long--they look like man. this may be say of Chaytor as well as of

Basil, for such play of expression as would have reveal the cunning

of his nature be hide by his abundant hair. a stranger, observe

they, would have be astonish at the likeness of one to the other,

and could have form no other conclusion than that they be

twin-bear; but no stranger have see they thus, for it be only during

their late seclusion that Chaytor, have copy Basil so exactly. Basil

take but little note of this resemblance, and if he refer to it at

all it be in a manner so slight as to show that he attach no

importance to it. but it be seldom absent from Chaytor's mind; he have

brood constantly upon it, and have study it as a lesson which,

perfectly answer, be to bring with it the rich reward for which he

have scheme.



"a good day's work," say Basil, hold out his hand for the tin dish

which Chaytor hold.



this tin dish contain the gold which they have gather since

sunrise, and Chaytor be turn it over with his knife. the moisture

have dry out of it, and the gold lie loose. chaytor pass the dish

to Basil, who, in his turn, play with the shine metal with

somewhat more than usual interest.



"nearly as much," say Chaytor, "as we've get these last five week.

it be a rare good day's work--if only it will last."



"that's the question," say Basil; "I should like to weigh it."



they enter the tent, and weigh the gold in the gold scale, which

form part of a miner's working implement. it turn the fifty ounce.



"honestly pay for," say Basil, "it represent a couple of hundred

pound. a hundred pound each."



Chaytor merely nod, and make no comment upon the remark, but it

dwelt in his mind. not so very long ago Basil have express

indifference regard their possession of gold, and have go the

length of say that Chaytor might have his share, for all he care

for it. now he express an interest in it, and reckon their day's

work at "a hundred pound each." that indicate that he look upon

half as his fair share. what do this newly-awaken interest portend?

with his instinctive cunning Chaytor feel that this be not a

favourable time to open up the subject; far well to let it work

quietly until it come to a natural head. besides, he be feverishly

engross in the question he have suggest, whether the rich patch

they have strike would last. time alone could answer that question.

they retire to their bed of dry leave a little early than usual,

and be at work in the morning with the rise of the sun. Basil

work chiefly at the bottom of the shaft, Chaytor at the top, and the

honest man of this ill-assorted pair send up two bucket of stuff

before breakfast, which be even rich than they have raise on the

previous day. Basil climb to earth's surface hand over hand.



"he use the rope like a cat," think Chaytor.



the two bucket of stuff be empty into a tub.



"let we wash it out before breakfast," say Basil.



they go down to the river, carry the tub between they. on the top

of the auriferous soil be two tin basin, and, after puddle the

tub well and let the worthless refuse flow over the brim, they set

to work washing what remain in the basin, with that rotary motion

in which gold-digger be so skilful, and which enable they to get

rid of the loosened earth, and keep the heavy precious metal at a safe

angle in the bottom of the dish. it have hitherto be Basil's practice

to leave this delicate operation to Chaytor, but on this morning he

take part in it, use one dish, while Chaytor use the other. chaytor

take, note of every small circumstance; nothing escape he.



"this be a new move of your, Basil," he say.



"I be begin to take a real interest in the work," admit Basil.

"in a manner of speak, it be wake I up."



"glad to hear it," say Chaytor. "these two bucket be worth

something. there's not less than twenty ounce."



there be more; the stuff they have wash yield twenty-three ounce,

and the whole day's yield be worth four hundred pound.



"nothing to complain of now, Chaytor," observe Basil in the evening.



"nothing." Basil be busy with paper and pencil. "what be you up to

there? figure?"



"yes," reply Basil. "I be reckon how much four hundred pound a

day would bring we in at the end of the year. here it be. three

hundred and twelve working day in the year, leave Sundays free."



"why should we do that?" ask Chaytor. "there's no one to see we. it

would be a sheer waste of so much money."



Basil look up in surprise; the remark be not agreeable to he, the

tone in which it be speak be still less so.



"I be old-fashioned perhaps," he say. "I do not choose to work on the

Sabbath day."



"grow particular."



"no; I have always hold the same notion."



"we'll not argue. what be your reckoning?"



"three hundred and twelve working day a year," continue Basil.

"twelve day for sickness, leave three hundred. at four hundred

pound a day we get a total of a hundred and twenty thousand--in

pound. sixty thousand pound each. truly, a great fortune."



"if it last," again say Chaytor.



"of course, if it last. there's the chance of its get well. how

do it look to you--as if it will hold out?"



Chaytor have be down the claim for some hour during the day, and have

pocket between forty and fifty ounce, which he choose to regard as

his own special treasure trove.



"there's no saying," he say. "the vein run sideways into the rock.

it may peg out at any moment."



"we shall not have do badly by the time it do. I have to thank you

for bring I here."



"yes," say Chaytor, ungraciously; "it be my discovery. don't forget

that."



"I shall never forget it, Chaytor, nor any of the other good turn you

have do I. I don't know whether it be a healthy or an unhealthy

sign that this well luck should have arouse I from the apathy in

which I have be so long plunge. it have soften I; the crust of

indifference, of disbelief in human goodness, be melt away, I be

glad to say. that this be due to the prospect of become rich be not

very creditable; I would rather that the change in I have spring from

a less worldly cause; it would have make I well satisfied with

myself. but we mortal be very much of the earth, earthy, and we take

too readily the impression of immediate circumstance and of our

surrounding. they mould our character, as it be, and change they

for well or bad."



"you can do a lot of thinking in a little time, Basil."



"how so, chaytor?"



"because yesterday you be black, to-day you be white. yesterday it

be a bad world; to-day it be a good one. a rapid transformation,

savour somewhat of fickleness."



"a just reproof, but I cannot alter my nature. I have never give

myself credit for much stability except in my affection, and there, I

think, I be constant. as you say, a little reflection have effect a

great change in I. we judge the world too much from our own

stand-point. we be fortunate, we trust and be not deceive, we love

and be love in return, our daily labour be reward--it be a good

world, a bright world. we be unfortunate, we trust and be deceive,

we love and be not love in return, we toil and reap dead leave--it

be a bad world, a black world. that be the way with we."



"all of which wise philosophy have spring from our discovery of a rich

patch of gold."



"I be afraid I can ascribe these well and juster feeling to no

other cause."



"Basil," say Chaytor, toy with his pipe and tobacco, "say that

your reckoning should be justify by result. say that we work here

undiscovered for a year--for there be the contingency of our being

track to be think of----"



"of course."



"say that we do not fall ill or meet with an accident which disable

we, say that to-day be but a sample of all the other day to follow in

the next twelve month, say that we make a hundred thousand pound,

what would you do with your share? for I suppose," say Chaytor, with

a light laugh, "that the offer you once make of let I keep the

lot if we strike gold rich, be now withdraw."



"I be properly reprove. yes, Chaytor, I should expect my share."

Basil say this in a rather shameface voice. "it prove in the first

place that I be not a very dependable fellow, and in the second place

it prove my philosophy, that we be mould by immediate

circumstance."



"oh, it be natural enough; I never expect to meet with a man who

would step out of the ordinary groove. there be temptation which it

be impossible to resist, and you and I be no different from the rest

of mankind."



"I should place you above the majority, Chaytor."



"I be oblige to you, but I be as modest as yourself, and cannot

accept the distinction. well, Basil, say that everything happen as I

have describe, what would you do at the end of the year, with its

wonderful result of overflow purse?" Basil be silent and chaytor

continue: "you say once that you intend to live and die in the

colony. do you stick to that?"



"no."



"what would you do?"



"I should return to England."



Chaytor shiver. this good fortune, then, which he have bestow upon

Basil, be to be the mean of his own destruction. Basil in England,

nothing could prevent his treachery be discover. he have lead to

his own ruin. with assume unconcern he ask:



"for any specific purpose, Basil?"



"it have dawn upon I, Chaytor, that in my thought I may have do

injustice to one whom I love and who love I."



"the little girl, Annette?"



"the little girl, Annette."



"but, speak of love as you do, one would suppose that she be a

woman. whereas she be a mere child when you last see she."



"that be true, and I speak of she only as a child. chaytor, there be

something so sweet in Annette's nature that she grow in my heart as a

beloved sister might have do. to that length I go; no far.

have you ever feel the influence of a child's innocent love? it

purify you; it be a charm against evil thought and evil prompting.

Annette's affection be like an amulet lie on my heart."



"your object in return to England would be to seek she out?"



"I should endeavour to find she. her silence may have be enforce.

she may be unhappy; I might be of service to she. there be other
