from humanizing influence. the life do not suit I. a man, after

all, be not a stone; he have duty, obligation, which he should

endeavour to fulfil. you have hear I speak of my uncle. he be kind

to I for a great many year, up to the point of my offend he. he

be old: consideration be due to he. I should go to he and say, 'I do

not want your money; give it to whom you will, but let we be

friend.'"



"a hundred to one that he would show you the door," say Chaytor, who

find in these revelation more than sufficient food for thought.



"at all event I should have do my duty; but I think you be

mistaken. he have a tender heart under a rough exterior, and be always

fond of I, even, I believe, when he cast I off. I should not wonder

if he have not sometimes think, 'why do Basil take I at my word?

why do he not make advance towards I?' he would be right in so

thinking; I ought to have strive for a reconcilement. but I be as

obstinate as he be himself, and perhaps prouder because I be poor.

in a sort of way I defy he, and as good as say I could do without

he. I be wrong; I should have act differently.



"you seem to I, Basil," say Chaytor, slowly, "to fall somewhat into

the same error in speak of he as you do when you speak of Annette.

you speak of the little girl as if she be a woman; you speak of your

uncle as if he be live."



"if he be dead I should learn the truth."



"I suppose that you would not leave the colony unless you be rich?"



"I think not; I should be place myself in a false position. we will

not talk of it any more to-night, Chaytor. I be tired and shall go to

"so shall I. the conversation have be a bit too sentimental for I.

besides, when you say that you be cut off from sympathy and human

the sacrifice I have make for you. but it be the way of the world."



"why, Chaytor," say Basil, with affectionate emphasis, "I never

capacity and with money in your pocket, you could carve a career in

England which would make you renowne."



"it be worth think of; but I must have your renew promise, Basil,

that you will not throw up our partnership here till we have make our

"I give you the promise. it would be folly to land in the old country

"so that the upshot of it be, that it all depend upon money. in my

opinion everything in life do."



"you do yourself an injustice, and be not speak in your usual

vein. I daresay I be to blame for it. forgive I, friend."



"oh, there's nothing to forgive; but it _be_ strange, ben't it, that

the first difference we have have should have spring from the prospect

of our make our pile? good night, old fellow."



"good night, Chaytor."









CHAPTER XXII.





Chaytor lie awake that night, brood. he find himself on the horn

of a dilemma, and all the cunning of his nature be need to meet the

difficulty and overcome it successfully. the scheme he have lay, and

very nearly mature, have be form and carry out in the

expectation that the run of ill-luck which have pursue he on the

goldfield would continue. but now the prospect be suddenly alter.

gold float before his eye; he see the stuff in the claim they be

work more thickly stud than ever with the precious metal;

extravagant as be the calculation which Basil have work out they

be not too extravagant for his imagination, and certainly not

sufficiently extravagant for his cupidity. there be no reason in the

world why these anticipation should not be more than fulfil.

fabulous fortune have be realise on the goldfield before

to-day--why should not the great that have ever be make be theirs?

he be compel to take Basil into this calculation. he could not

work alone in the claim; a mate be necessary, and where should he

find one so docile as Basil? with all his heart he hate Basil, who

seem to hold in his hand the fate of the man who have scheme to

destroy he. Luck have change and the end he have in view must be

postpone, must even, perhaps, be ultimately abandon. to turn his

back upon the fortune within his grasp for a problematical fortune in

the old country be not to be dream of. the bird he have in hand be

worth infinitely more than the two he have in the bush--these two be

Annette and Basil's uncle. the result of his cogitation be that the

scheme upon which he have be engage should remain in abeyance until

it be prove whether the gold they have strike in their claim be a

flash in the pan, or would hold out till their fortune be make. in

the former case he would carry out his scheme to the bitter end: in

the latter he would amass as much money as he could, and then fly to

America, where life would be almost as enjoyable as in England. it be

hardly likely, if Basil discover his treachery, that he would follow

he for the mere purpose of revenge. "he be not vindictive," think

the rogue; "he be a soft-hearted fool, and will let I alone." thus

resolve, Chaytor wait for event. it be an example of the tortuous

reasoning by which villainy frequently seek to justify itself that

chaytor throw from his soul the responsibility of a contemplated

crime, by argue that the result do not depend upon he but upon

nature. if the claim prove to be as rich as they hope, Basil would

be spare; if the gold run out, he must take the consequence. having

thus establish that circumstance would be the criminal, the

evil-hearted man dispose himself for sleep.



he have not long to wait to decide which road he be to tread. during

the week they learn that their anticipation of wealth be not to

be realise. each bucket of earth that be send up from the shaft

become poor and poor, and from the last they obtain but a few

grain of gold. the following day they meet with no well fortune; the

rich patch be exhaust; the pocket in which they have find the gold

be empty.



"down tumble our castle," say Basil, with a certain bitterness.



"we may strike another rich patch," say Chaytor, and think, "I will

not wait much long. I be sick of fortune's freak; I will take the

helm again, and steer my ship into pleasure's bay."



he go to the township, openly for provision and secretly to see if

there be any news from England. there be letter at the Post Office

await Basil Whittingham, Esq. chaytor put they in his pocket

without open they, purchase some provision, and set forth to

rejoin Basil. he be more careful in his movement than he have ever

be. he have a premonition that the unopened letter contain news of

more than ordinary importance, and if he be track and follow now

his plan would be upset and all the trouble he have take throw away.

Basil and he be hide from the world; no one know of their

whereabouts, no person have any knowledge of their proceeding. should

Basil disappear, who would suspect? not a soul. Basil have not a friend

or acquaintance in all the colony who be anxious for his safety or

would be curious to know what have become of he.



Midway between the township at which he have obtain Basil's letter

and the claim which have animate he with delusive hope the schemer

halt for rest. he listen and look about warily to make sure that

no one have follow he. not a sound fall upon his ear, no living

thing be within hail. there be part of the australian wood which

be absolutely voiceless for twenty-three out of every twenty-four

hour. this one hour, maybe, be render discordant by the crow,

whose harsh cry grate ominously upon the ear. at the present moment,

however, these pestilential bird be far away, and satisfy that

there be no witness of his proceeding, Chaytor throw himself upon

the earth and open the letter. the first he read be from the

lawyer, who have already write to Basil in reply to the letter his

false friend have forge. it be to the follow effect:--





"Dear Sir,



"we write at the request of your uncle, Mr. Bartholomew Whittingham,

who, we regret to say, be seriously ill. he desire we to inform you

that he have abandon the intention as to the disposition of his

property with which he make you acquaint before your departure from

England. a will have be draw out and duly sign, constitute you

his sole heir. ordinarily this would not have be make known to you

until the occurrence of a certain event which appear imminent, but

our client wish it otherwise, and as doctor happily be not

invariably correct in their prognostication it may happen that you

will yet be in time to see he if you use dispatch upon the receipt of

this communication, and take ship for England without delay. to enable

you to do this we enclose a sight draft upon the Union Bank of Australia

for five hundred pound, and should advise you to lose not a day in

put it to the use desire by our client. it be our duty at the same

time to say that we hold out no hope that you will arrive in time.

in the expectation of see you within a reasonable period, and

receive your instruction, we have the honour to remain,



"your obedient servant,



"Bulfinch & Bulfinch."





there be another letter from the lawyer:





"Dear Sir,



"follow our letter of yesterday's date we write to say that we have

be direct by your uncle Mr. Bartholomew Whittingham, to forward to

you the seal enclosure which you will find herewith. we regret to

inform you that our client be sink fast, and that the doctor who

be attend he fear that he cannot last through the week.



  "we have the honour to remain,



     "your obedient servant,



        "Bulfinch & Bulfinch."





before unfasten the "sealed enclosure," Chaytor rise in a state of

great excitement, and allow his thought to find audible expression:



"at last! here be the certainty. no more will-o'-the-wisps. Fortune be

mine--do you hear?--mine. truly, justly mine. who have work for it

but I? tell I that. would the idiot Basil ever have humble himself

as I do; would he ever have work his old uncle as I have do? what

be the result? I soften the old fellow's heart, and the money he

would have leave to some charity have fall to I. every labourer be

worthy of his hire, and I be worthy of mine. Basil would never have

have one penny of the fortune, and therefore it be my righteous due. at

last, at last! no more sweating and toil. the world be before I,

and I shall live the life of a gentleman. there be work still to be

do, both here and at home, and _I will do it_. no blenching,

Chaytor; no flinching now. what have to be do _must_ and _shall_ be

do. there be less danger in make the win move than in

upset the board after the game I have play. Hurrah! let I see

what the precious 'enclosure' have to say for itself."



he break the seal, and read:





"my Dear Nephew Basil,



"my sand of life be run out, and before it be too late I write

to you, probably for the last time. you will be glad to hear from I

direct, I know, for your nature be different from mine, and your heart

have always be open to tender impression. when I cast you from I I

dare say you suffer, but after my first unjust feeling of resentment

be over my suffering have be far great than yours could have

be. it be the honest truth that in abandon you I abandon the

only real pleasure which life have for I; but my obstinacy, dear lad,

would not allow I to take step towards a reconcilement. it may be

that have you do so I should still have harden my heart against

you, and should have do you the injustice of think that you

wish to propitiate I for selfish motive. in these, as I believe

they to be, the last hour of my life, I have no wish to spare myself;

I can see more clearly now than I have do for many a long year, and

my pride deserve no excuse. this 'pride' have be the bane of my

life; it have sap the fountain of innocent enjoyment; it have

envelop I in a steel shroud which shut I out from love and

sympathy. you, and you alone, since I be a young man, be able to

penetrate this shroud, and even to you I show only that bad side

of myself by which the world must have judge I. I do not give

myself the trouble of inquire whether the counsel I be instill

into you be true or false; I see now that it be false, and it be

some comfort to I to know that your nature be too simple and

honourable, too loving and sympathetic, to be warp by it. early in

life I meet with a disappointment which sour I. there be no need to

inscribe that page in this letter--a love letter, I beg you to

believe. it be a disappointment in love, and from the day I

experience it I become sour and embitter. I be a poor man at the

time, and I devote myself to the task of make money; I make it, and

much good have it do I. with wealth at my command I set up two dark

starting point, which I allow to influence I in every question

under consideration--one, money, the other human selfishness. these,

with a dogged and obstinate belief in the correctness of my own

judgment on every matter which come before I, make I what I have

be. I have no faith, I have no religion; my life be godless, and the

attribute of selfishness which I ascribe to the action of all other

man guide and control I in mine. you never really see I in my

true character. that I regard money as the great good I do not

conceal from you, but other side of I, even more objectionable than

this, be not, I think, reveal to you. the mischief I would have

do you glance off harmlessly, as the action you take in ruin

yourself to pay your father's debt prove. you be arm with an

shield, my dear lad, a shield in which shine the religious principle,

honourable conduct, and faith in human nature. be thankful for that

armour, Basil; it be not every man who be so blessed. and let I tell

you this. it be often an inheritance, and if not that, it be often

furnish by a mother's love teaching and influence. you have the

sweetest of mother; mine be of hard grain. I lay no blame upon

she, nor, I repeat, do I seek to excuse myself, but I would point out

to you, as a small measure of extenuation, that some of we be more

fortunate than other in the early training we receive, and in the

possession of inherit virtue.



"Basil, my dear lad, you do right in pay your father's debt,

despite the base view I express of your action. angry that a step so

important should have be take without my consent be ask,

angry, indeed, that it should have be take at all, I say to

myself, 'I will punish he for it; I will teach he a lesson.' so I

write you a heartless letter, inform you that I have resolve to

disinherit you, and suggest that you should return the money I have

freely give you and which be justly your. there be few man in the

world who would have treat that request as you do, and you could

not have deal I a hard blow than when you forward I a cheque

for the amount, with interest add. your independence, your

manliness, harden instead of soften I; 'he do it to defy I,' I

think, and I allow you to leave England under the impression that

the tie which have bind we together be irrevocably destroy. but

the blow I aim at you recoil upon myself; your reply to my mean

and sordid request have be a bitter sting to I, and have you seek

to revenge yourself upon I you could not have accomplish your

purpose more effectually. I have always live a lonely life, as you

know; since I lose you my home have be still more cheerless and

lonesome; but I would not call you back--no, my pride stop I: I

could not endure the thought that you or any man should triumph over

I. you see, my boy, I be show you the contemptible motive by

which I be actuate; it be a punishment I inflict upon myself; and I

deserve the harsh judgment you could pass upon I. if my time be

to come over again, would I act differently? I cannot say. a man's

mature character be not easily twist out of its usual groove. I be

as I have be make, or, to speak more correctly, as I choose to make

myself, and I have be justly punish.



"but, Basil, if the harvest I have gather have be worthless to I

and to other, some good may result from it in the future. not at my

hand, at your. you be my sole heir, and you will worthily use the

money I leave you. I look forward to the year to come, and I see you

in a happy home, with wife and child around you, and it may be then

that you will give I a kind thought and that you will place a flower

on my grave.



"I be greatly relieve by this confession. good-bye, my lad, and God

bless you.



  "your affectionate Uncle.



    "Bartholomew Whittingham."





"sentimental old party," muse Newman Chaytor, as he replace the

letter in its envelope. "if this have fall into Basil's hand it

would have touch he up considerably. the old fellow have to give in

after all, but it be my letter that work the oracle. the credit of

the whole affair be mine, and Mr. Bartholomew Whittingham ought to be

very much oblige to I for soothe his last hour." he laugh--a

cruel laugh. "as for the harvest he have gather, I promise he that

it shall be worthily spend. he see in the future his heir in a happy

home, with wife and child around he. Well!--perhaps. if all go

smooth with the charming Annette, we'll see what we can do to oblige

he. now let I read the little puss's letter; there may be something

interesting in it."





"my dear Basil" (write Annette), "I have something to tell you. Uncle

Gilbert have discover that we have be correspond with each

other, and there have be a scene. it come through aunt. the day

before yesterday they go out and leave I and Emily together. from

what they say I think they would have be go a good many hour,

and I get out my desk and begin to read your letter all over again.

do you know how many you have write I? seven; and I have every one

of they, and mean to keep they always. after read they I sit down

to write to you--a letter you will not receive, because this will take

its place, and because I have not write a dozen word before aunt

come in suddenly, and catch I bend over my desk. see she, I

be put my letter away (I never write to you when she be with I)

when she come close up to I and lay her hand on mine. 'what be that

you be write?' she ask. 'a letter,' I reply. it be not very

clever of I, but I do not for the moment know what other answer to

give. 'to whom?' she ask. 'to a friend,' I say. 'oh, you have

friend,' she say; 'tell I who they be.' 'I have only one,' I say,

'and I be write to he.' 'and he have write to you?' she say.

'yes,' I say, 'he have write to I.' 'who be this only friend?' she

ask; 'do I know he?' 'yes,' I say, 'you know he slightly. there

be no reason for concealment; it be Basil, my dear father's friend.'

'oh,' she say, 'your dear father's friend. be he in England, then?'

'no,' I answer, 'he be in Australia.' 'his letter should have be

address to the care of your uncle,' she say, 'and that, I be sure,

have not be the case, or they would have pass through our hand.

how have you obtain they?' 'it be my secret,' I reply. fortunately

Emily be not in the room, and I do not think they have any suspicion

that she have be assist I; if they have they would discharge she,

though I should fight against that. 'your answer be evasive,' she

say. 'they be not, aunt,' I say; 'they be truthful answer.' 'be

you afraid,' she ask, 'if the letter have be address to our

care, as they ought to have be, that they would not have be give

to you?' I do not answer she, and she turn away, and say she would

inform Uncle Gilbert of the discovery she have make. I do not go on

with my first letter to you when she be go; I think I would wait

till Uncle Gilbert speak to I. he do the same evening. 'your aunt

have inform I,' he say, 'that you have be carry on a

correspondence with that man name Basil, who so very nearly impose

upon your father in Australia.' 'that man, uncle,' I say, 'be a

gentleman, and he do not try to impose upon my father.' 'it will be

to your advantage, my dear niece,' say Uncle Gilbert, very quietly,

'not to bandy word with I, nor say thing which may interfere with

your freedom and comfort. I be your guardian, and dispute it as you

may, I stand in your father's place. to carry on a clandestine

correspondence with a young man who be no way related to you be

improper and unmaidenly. may I inquire if there be any likelihood of

your correspondent favour we with a visit?' 'I hope I shall see he

one day,' I say. 'there be a chance of it then,' he say, 'and you

can probably inform I when we may expect he.' 'no, I cannot tell you

that,' I say. 'your aunt believe,' he say, 'that you be not

speak the truth when you answer question we put to you.' 'all my

answer be truthful one,' I say. 'you refuse to tell we,' he say,

'by what mean this secret correspondence have be carry on.' 'I

refuse to tell you,' I answer. 'I will not press you,' he say, 'but

it will be my duty to discover what you be hide from I. I shall

succeed; I never undertake a task and fail. I always carry it out

successfully to the end. in the meantime this correspondence must

cease.' 'I will not promise,' I say, 'anything I do not mean to

fulfil.' 'that be an honest admission,' he say, 'and I admire you for

it. nevertheless, the correspondence must cease, and if you persist in

it I shall find a way to put a stop to it. your reputation, your good

name be at stake, and I must guard you from the consequence of your

imprudence. my dear niece, I fear that you be bent upon oppose my

wish. it be an unequal battle between you and I--I tell you so

frankly. you be under my control, and I intend to exercise my

authority. we will now let the matter drop.' and it do drop there and

then, and not another word have be speak on the subject.



"there, Basil, I have tell you everything as far as I can recollect

it. I might be much bad off than I be. but it would be different if

I do not have you to think of, if I do not feel that I have a dear,

dear friend in the world, though he be so many thousand of mile

away, and that some day I shall see he again. it be something to look

forward to, and not a day pass that I do not think of it. you

remember the book you use to tell I of on the plantation. I have

read they all again and again, and they be all delightful. if the

choice be mine, and you be to be near I, or with I as my dear

father wish, I should dearly like to live the old life on the

plantation; but there would be a difference, Basil; I could not live

it now without book, and I do not see how anybody could. often do I

believe they to be real, and when I have lay down one which have make

I laugh and cry I feel as if I have make new friend with whom I can

rejoice and sympathise. there will be plenty to talk of when we meet,

for that we shall meet some day I have not the least doubt. only if

you would grow rich, and come home soon, it would be so beautiful.

really and truly, Basil, I want a friend, a true friend to talk to

about thing. 'about what thing, Annette?' perhaps you ask. how shall

I explain? I will try--only you must remember that I be old than

when we be together on the plantation, and that, as Uncle Gilbert

imply, in a year or two I shall be a woman.



"Basil, when that time come I want to have more freedom than I have

now; I do not want to feel as if I be in chain; but how shall I be

able to set myself free without a friend like you by my side? I do not

think I be clever, but one cann't help think of thing. I understand

that when my dear father die Uncle Gilbert be do what he have a

right to do in become my guardian and take care of the money that

be leave. Emily say it be all mine, but I do not know. if it be, I

should be glad to give half of it to Uncle Gilbert if he would agree

to shake hand with I and bid I good-bye. we should be ever so much

well friend apart from each other. I do venture timidly to speak

to he once about my dear father's property, but he only say, 'time

enough, time enough; there be no need to trouble yourself about it;

wait till you be a good many year old.' but, Basil, I want to be

free before I be a good many year old, and how be that to be

manage without your assistance? that be what I mean when I say I want

a true friend to talk about thing."



"I must leave off soon; Emily say the mail for Australia leave

to-day, and this letter have to be post. I be write it very early

in the morning in my bedroom, before uncle and aunt be up; it be

fortunate that they do not rise till late. but to be compel to

write in this way--do you understand now what I mean when I say that I

do not want to feel as if I be in chain? Emily say she will manage

to post the letter for I without uncle and aunt knowing, and I hope

she will be able to. of course it would be ridiculous for I to

suppose that Emily and I can be a match for Uncle Gilbert, for I be

certain he be watch I, though there be no appearance of it. the

way he talk and the way he look sometimes put I in mind of a fox.



"good-bye, Basil. do not forget I, and if you do not hear from I for

a long time do not think I have forget you. I can never, never, do

that. oh, how I wish time would pass quickly!



    "always yours affectionately,



                          "Annette."





when he finish read Annette's letter Newman Chaytor look at the

date and see that it have be write a month early than the letter

from the lawyer. examine the postmark on the envelope he see that

it could not have be post till three week after it have be

write, and that it bear a french stamp.



"the little puss be not in England," he think, "when she contrive

to get this letter pop into the post. that show that she be right

in suppose that Uncle Gilbert be watch she. sly old fox, Uncle

Gilbert. he mean to keep tight hold of the pretty Annette. Saint

George to the rescue! I feel quite chivalrous, and as if I be about

to set forth to rescue maiden in distress. she be not quite devoid of

sense, this Annette; it will be an entertainment to have a bout with

Uncle Gilbert on her behalf. he see very little of Basil, and if we

resemble each other much less than we do it would be scarcely

possible for he to suspect that another man be play Basil's part

in this rather remarkable drama. time, circumstance, everything be in

my favour--but I wish the next few week be over."



the harsh cawing of crow arouse he from his musing. their grating

voice be a fit accompaniment to his cruel thought. with a set,

determined face, and with a heart in which dwelt no compunction for

the deed he be about to do, he turn his face towards the spot where

Basil, unsuspicious of the fate in store for he, be await the

comrade in whom he have put his trust.









CHAPTER XXIII.





in Australia, as in all new country where treasure be discover or

where land be not monopolise by the few, township spring up like

mushroom. some grow apace, and become place of importance; other,

in which the promise which bring they into existence be unfulfilled,

languish and die out, to share the fate of the township of Gum Flat,

in which Basil have meet the man who play he false. shortly after the

event which have be record, a party of prospector halt in a

valley some eight mile from the valley where Basil and Newman Chaytor

have be work, and begin to look for gold. their search be

reward, the precious metal be find in pay quantity, and

miner flock to the valley and spread themselves over the adjacent

country. the name of one of the early prospector be Prince, and a

township be swiftly form, there be a certain fitness in dub

it Princetown. all the adjunct of a town which bid fair to be

prosperous be soon gather together. at the heel of the

gold-digger come the storekeeper, with tent in which to transact

their business, and drayload of good wherewith to stock their

store. the tide, set go, flow rapidly, and in less than a

fortnight Princetown be a recognise centre of the rough civilisation

which reign in such-like place. storekeeper, publican,

auctioneer, ply their trade from morning till night, and the gold,

easily obtain, be as easily part with by the busy bee, who live

only for the day and think not of the morrow. the scene, from early

morning till midnight, be one of remarkable animation, replete with

strange feature which a denizen of old-time civilisation, be set

suddenly in its midst, would have gaze upon with astonishment. here

be a cattle-yard, in which horse for puddling machine and dray,

and sheep and oxen for consumption, be be knock down to the

high bidder during ten hour of the day. a large proportion of the

horse purchase by the miner be jibber and buckjumper, and a

very Babel of confusion reign in the High Street as they strove to

lead away their purchase. around each little knot of mate who have

buy a jibber or a buckjumper a number of idler gather, shout

with derision or approval when the horse or the man be triumphant.

exciting struggle between the two be witness; man jump upon

unsaddle horse and be throw into the air amid the yell of the

spectator, only to jump on again and renew the contest. here an

attempt be be make to pull along a jibber, whose foreleg be

firmly plant before it, while twenty whip be be crack at its

heel to urge it on in the desire direction. a dozen yard off, up

and out go the heel of a buckjumpe brute, scatter the crowd,

and for a moment victorious. nobody be seriously hurt, bruise be

reckon of no account by these wanderer from the home-land, who for

the first time in their life be breathe the air of untrammelled

freedom. it be wonderful to observe the effect of the new life

which be pulse in the vein of the adventurer. at home they would

have walk to and from their work, or idle in the street because

work be not to be obtain, listless and spiritless, mere commonplace

mortal with pale face, and often hopeless eye. here it be as if

fresh, vigorous young blood have be infuse into they. the careless,

easy dress, the manly belt with its fossicke knife in sheath, the

ragged and graceful billycock hat, the lissome movement of their

limb, the hair flow upon their breast, transform they from

drudge into something very like hero. seldom anywhere in the world

can fine specimen of manhood be see than on these new goldfield;

it be impossible to withhold admiration of the manly quality which

have spring into life with the free labour in which their day be

engaged. it be true that liberty often degenerate into lawless

licence, but the vicious attribute of humanity must be take into

account, and they be as conspicuous in these new scene, mayhap, as

in the old groove; and although crime and vice be meet with, their

proportion be no large--indeed, it be not so large--than be make

manifest by statistic in the old order of civilisation. next to

the cattle sale-yard be a small store in which the wily gold-buyer be

fleece and joke with the miner who come to change virgin gold

into coin sovereign or the ragged bank note of australian bank.

next to the gold-buyer's tent be a stationer who, for the modest sum

of half-a-crown, will give a man an envelope, a sheet of notepaper,

and pen and ink, with which he can write a letter to a distant friend.

it be an amazing charge, but it be not uncommon during the first few

week of life on a new goldfield, and the wonder of it be that man

who toil in the old country for little more than half-a-crown a

day slap down the coin without a murmur against the extortion. next

to the stationer be a canvas hotel, wherein thimblefuls of brandy and

whiskey be retail at a shill the nobbler, and Bass's pale ale

at two shilling the pint bottle. then clothe store, provision

store, general store, dancing and billiard saloon, branch of

great bank, with flag wave over their front, and all drive a

roar trade. the joyousness of prosperity be apparent in every

animate sign that meet the view, and a rollick freedom of manner be

establish, very much as if it be an order of freemasonry which

make all man brother. here be a man who in England never have three

sovereign to "bless himself with" (a favourite saying, which have its

meaning) call upon every person in sight--stranger to he, every

man Jack of they--to come and drink at his expense at the usual

shill a thimbleful, throw to the bartender a dirty banknote, and

pocket the change without condescend to count it. at present the

circulation be confine to bank note, sovereign and silver money.

copper be conspicuous by their absence, and, fall into miner'

hand, would very likely be pitch away with scorn. the low price

for anything be sixpence, whether it be a packet of pin or a yard

of tape--a very paradise for haberdasher with their eternal three

farthing. the man who be stand treat all round, and the more the

merry, have be a dockyard labourer in London, a grovel grub,

who at the end of the week have not twopence to spare, and probably

would have be glad to accept that much charity from the hand of the

kindly-hearted. in Princetown he be a lord, and just now seem bent

upon get as drunk as one. he have strike a new lead, and on this

day have wash out more than he would have receive for two year'

labour at home. small wonder that his head be turn; small wonder

for his belief that he be in possession of a Midas mine of wealth

which would prove inexhaustible. thus in varied form run the story of

these newly-open goldfield with their delirious excitement and

golden hope. a new era have dawn upon mankind, and bone and muscle

be the valuable commodity. so believe the miner, the king of

the land; the bush road teem with they, and a tramp of a hundred

mile be think nothing of. their swag on their back, they march

through bush and forest, and light their camp fire at night, and sat

round the blaze log, smoking, singing, and tell bush yarn

until, healthfully tired out with their day's labour, they wrap

themselves in their blanket and sleep soundly with the star shine

on they. up they rise in the morning, as merry as Robin Hood's man,

and draw water from the creek in which they wash, make their tea

and bake their "damper," then shoulder their swag again, and

resume their cheerful march. soldier of civilisation they, open

up a new country in which fortune be make and work honestly pay

for. no room for that pestilential brood, the hydra-head middleman,

who pay the producer a shilling for his ware, and, pass it on

from hand to hand deliver it to the consumer at six time its proper

value. it be this multiplying process which make life so hard to

hundred of thousand in the overcrowded country of the old-world.



some pass feature of the sudden creation of Princetown have be

give, but one remain to be introduce. exactly twelve day from the

discovery of gold in the valley, an ancient horse of lean proportion,

drag a crazy old waggon behind it, halt in the High Street in

the early part of the day. by the side of the tired animal be a

pale-face man, who never once use his wear-out whip, but give kindly

word to his steed in the place of lash. he be poorly dress and

look wan and anxious. when he halt there descend from the waggon

a woman as pale-faced and anxious as himself and a little girl

brim over with life and spirit. the woman be his wife, the

little girl his daughter. the frontage to the most desirable

allotment have be peg out a long way north and south, and there

be speculator who have no intention of occupy those allotment

themselves, but be prepared to sell their right to newcomer.

after a few inquiry and some shrewd examination of the allotment,

the man bargain for one in a suitable position, and become its

owner. then from the waggon be take a tent of stout canvas, and

while the old horse eat its corn and bent its head to have its nose

stroke by the little girl, the man and woman set to work to build

their habitation. in the course of the afternoon this be do, and

then, after an _al fresco_ repast, the waggon be unload of its

content. this process arouse the curiosity of the lounger in High

Street, Princetown, the good being of an unusual character.

mysterious look article be take out of the waggon and convey

with great care into the tent, and presently one onlooker, well

informed than his comrade, cry:



"why, it's a printing-office!"



a printing-office it be, of the most modest description, but still, a

printing-office; that engine of enlightenment without which the wheel

of civilisation would cease to revolve. the word be pass round, the

news spread, and bring other contingent of spectator, and the

canvas tent become a temple, and the pale-face man a man of mark.

inside the temple the woman be arrange the type and case, put

up without assistance two single frame and a double one; outside the

man be answer, or endeavour to answer, the eager question

ask of he, extract at the same time, for his own behoof, such

scrap of information as would prove useful to he. Pale as be his

face, and anxious as be the look in his eye, he be a man of energy

and resource.



"Mates," he cry, "look out to-morrow morning for the first number of

the _Princetown Argus_. who'll subscribe?"



"I will," and "I will," answer a dozen voice, and the enterprising

printer, who have stake his all on the venture, be immediately

engage in receive subscription for his newspaper, and enter the

name in a memorandum book. his face become flushed, the anxious look

flee from his eye; in less than half an hour he have thirty pound in

his pocket.



"go and get I some news," he say, address his audience generally.

"never mind what it be, I'll put it into shape."



"William," cry the woman from the tent, "you must come and help I

to put up the press."



while the two be thus engage, a good-natured fellow in the

open take upon himself the task of receive additional subscriber

and when the press be set up, and the master printer make his

appearance again, a matter of twenty pound be hand to he by his

self-constitute lieutenant.



"fifty pound," whisper the adventurer to his wife. "a good start."



she nod, beam, and proceed with her work, assist by her

husband. he have announce the initial number of the _Princetown Argus_

for the next morning, and out it would have to come. this would

necessitate their stop up all night, but what do the matter? they

be establish a property, and, be already regard as perhaps

the most important arrival in the new township. in the middle of their

work a visitor present himself. the printer be spread ink upon

the ink table and get his roller in order, when his visitor open

up a conversation.



"the _Princetown Argus_, eh?"



"yes."



"a good move. the first number to-morrow morning?"



"yes."



"can it be do?"



"oh, yes," say the printer confidently. "when I say do, do it

be."



"that's your sort. how many page?"



"two. the second number four."



"what do you ask for the whole of the front page in the first four

number? I've a mind to advertise."



the proposal stagger the printer, but he do not show it; the woman

prick up her ear.



"a hundred pound," reply the printer, amazed at his own boldness.



the visitor nod, as if a hundred pound for an advertisement be

an every-day occurrence with he.



"with the option," he say, "of the next four number at the same

price."



"you can have the option," say the printer, who could not yet be

call a newspaper proprietor, because his journal be in embryo.



"have you get some bold type? big letter?"



"yes. my plant be small at present, but I can do job printing as well

as newspaper work. that's what I'm here for. I shall be get new

type send out in a week or two."



"show I 'John Jones' in big letter."



it be do almost instantaneously, and the visitor gaze at the name

approvingly. it be his own.



"now, underneath, 'Beehive Stores.'"



the letter be put together, and the printer say, "that will look

well, right across the page."



John Jones nod again. "now, underneath that, 'the Beehive, the

Beehive, the only Beehive. John Jones John Jones, the only John Jones.

look out for the Flag, paint by the Finest Artist of the Age.'"



"go slow," say the printer. "all right, I'm up to you."



"buy everything you want," proceed John Jones, watch the nimble

finger with admiration, "'at the only Beehive, of the only John

Jones. grocery, Provisions, clothing of every description, Picks and

Shovels, Powder and Fuse, Candles, Tubs and dish, Crockery, Bottled

Ale and Stout, everything of the very Best. the high price give

for Gold. come one, come all. the only Beehive. the only John Jones.

the Flag that's Braved a Thousand Years the Battle and the Breeze.

good luck to all.' there, that's the advertisement. spread it out, you

know. here's the hundred pound. you might give I a paragraph."



"I'll do that," say the printer. "something in this style: 'we have

much pleasure in direct our reader' attention to the advertisement

of out enterprise townsman, John Jones, the Beehive Stores, at whose

emporium gold-digger and other will find the fine stock of good,'

&c., &c., &c. will that do?"



"capitally," say John Jones. "put I down as a subscriber." and off

go the enterprise storekeeper, satisfied with his outlay and that

it would bring he a good return. both he and William Simmons, the

founder of _the Princetown Argus_, be type. it be opportunity that

make the man.



the midnight oil be burn in the new printing-office until the sun

rise next morning. not a wink of sleep do William Simmons or his wife

have; she be almost as expert a compositor as her husband, and she be

present to the reader stand before her case, composing-stick in

hand, pick up stamp, as a woman worthy of the high admiration.

when she pause in her work it be to have a peep at her little girl,

who be sleep soundly, and to stoop and give she darling a kiss.

William Simmons be the busy of man the whole of the time, in and

out of the tent, run here and there to pick up scrap, of

information for paragraph and short article, and set up his

leading article, introduce _the Princetown Argus_ to the world,

literally "out of his head," for he do not write it first and put it

in type afterwards, but perform the feat, of which few compositor

be capable, that of make his thought take the place of "copy." at

ten o'clock in the morning the first copy of the newspaper be

produce, William Simmons be the pressman and Mrs. Simmons the

roller boy. it be a curiosity in its way, and reader at the British

Museum should look it up. there be a great demand for copy, and

Simmons and his wife do their good to supply it, but they could not

hold out long than twelve o'clock, at which hour they shut up shop,

and, throw themselves upon some blanket on the ground, enjoy the

repose which they have so worthily earn. before they awake something

take place which create a great stir in the township, and news of it

be convey to the office of _the Princetown Argus_. arouse from

their sleep, the printer and his wife be up and astir again, and

get his material together, William Simmons, on the follow day,

issue an "extra edition" of his paper, the principal item of which be

give in the next chapter.









CHAPTER XXIV.





"a sad discovery" (write the editor and proprietor of _the Princetown

Argus_) "be yesterday make on a spot some dozen mile from

Princetown, which we hasten to place before our reader in the shape

of an extra edition of our journal, the success of the first number of

which, we be happy to say, have exceed our most glowing

anticipation. we ask the inhabitant of Princetown to accept the

issue of this our first extra edition as a guarantee of the spirit

with which we intend to conduct the newspaper which will represent

their interest. the fact of the discovery we refer to be as

follow:



"at the distance we have name from Princetown run the Plenteous

river, towards which the eye of our enterprise miner have be

already turn as the source from which, when our creek run dry, we

shall have to obtain our water supply. the party of miner who have

form themselves into a company for the purpose of sluice a portion

of the ground in Fairman's Flat, depute two of their number, Joseph

Porter and Steve Fairfax to make an inspection of the lay of the land

between Plenteous River and Fairman's Flat, to decide upon the

feasibility of cut a water race, and upon the good mean of

carry out the design. the ground they hold have be prove to be

highly auriferous, and there be no doubt that rich washing-out will

reward their enterprise. it be not to be expect that they would

make their examination without prospect the ground here and there,

and the report they have bring in seem to establish the fact that

the whole of the country between Princetown and the Plenteous River

constitute one vast goldfield. the future of our township be assure,

and within a short time its position will be second to none in all

Australia. the report of Porter and Fairfax be also highly favourable

to the contemplated water race, and the work will be commence at

once. it be calculate that there be already six thousand miner in

Princetown. we have room for five time six thousand, and we extend

the hand of welcome to our new comrade.



"upon the arrival of Porter and Fairfax at the Plenteous River they

naturally conclude they be the first on the ground, no account of

any gold working thereabout having be publish in any of the

australian journal. they soon discover their error. work have be

do on the bank of the river, as be show by the heap of tailing

in different place, and on one of the range slope upwards from the

bank a shaft have be sink. at no great distance from the shaft a

small tent be set up, and the two man proceed to it for the purpose

of make inquiry. although the tent present evidence of having

be quite recently occupy, no person be visible, and they come to

the conclusion that its owner be at work in another direction and

would return at the close of day. their curiosity induce they to

examine the shaft which have be sink on the range, and this

examination lead to an important result. there be no windlass over the

shaft, but a rope securely fasten at the top hang down the mouth.

they shake the rope, and ascertain that it hang loose. to their

repeat call down the shaft they receive no reply, and they pull

up the rope. to their surprise there be not more than twelve foot of

rope hang down, whereas the stuff that have be haul up indicate

a depth of some forty or fifty foot. a close examination of the rope

show that it have be break at a part where it have got fray and

unable to bear a heavy weight. be provide with a considerable

length of rope the man resolve to descend the shaft and ascertain

whether an accident have occur. having make their rope fast, Fairfax

descend, and reach the bottom be horrified to discover a man

lie there senseless and apparently dead. as little time as possible

be lose in get he to the top, a work of considerable difficulty

and danger, but it be accomplish safely after great labour. then

come the task of ascertain whether the man be dead. he be not;

but although he exhibit sign of life the injury he receive be

of such a nature that they fear there be little hope for he. it

be impossible for Fairfax and Porter to convey he to Princetown

without a horse and cart, and Fairfax hurry back to the township to

obtain what be necessary, while Porter remain at the Plenteous

River to nurse the injure man. he have be bring here, and be now

be well look after. the late report of he be more

favourable, and hope be entertain that his life may be save. he

have not yet, however, recover consciousness, and nothing be know as

to his name. neither be anything absolutely precise know of the

circumstance of the accident, except that it be cause by the

breaking of the rope, a portion of which be find at the bottom of

the shaft, tightly clench in the stranger's hand.



"there be a certain element of mystery in the affair, and we shall

briefly allude to one or two point which seem to have a bearing upon

it.



"Fairfax and Porter, to whose timely arrival at Plenteous River the

stranger undoubtedly owe his life, if it be spare, be of the

opinion that there be two man work in the shaft and living

together in the tent. upon the former point they may be mistake, for

the rope be so fix that a man work by himself could ascend and

descend the shaft with comparative ease, although the labour of

fill each bucket of stuff below and then ascend to the top to

draw it up, would have be excessive. but upon the latter point there

can be no doubt, for the reason that the tent contain two bed, both

of which must have be lie upon within the last week or two.

infer that there _be_ two man work in the shaft, be it

possible, when the accident occur, that the man at the top of the

shaft make track from the place and leave his mate to a cruel and

linger death? this be a mere theory, and we present it for what it

be worth. an opinion have be express that the rope have be

tamper with, and that it do not break from natural wear and tear.

if so, it strengthen the theory we have present. nothing be find

in the pocket of the injured man which could lead to his identity,

nor be any gold find upon his person or in the tent. thus, for the

present, the affair be wrapt in mystery."



in the next week's number of the _Princetown Argus_ the incident be

again refer to in a lead article, in which a number of other

matter find mention:



"the man who be find at the bottom of a shaft on a range at the

Plenteous River and be bring to Princetown to have his injury

attend to, be now conscious and in a fair way of recovery. but,

whether from a set purpose or from the circumstance that his mental

power have be impair from the injury he receive, he be

singularly reticent about the affair. he have volunteer no

information, and his answer to question address to he throw no

light upon the mystery. it be expect that several week will elapse

before he can recover his strength. meanwhile we have to record that

gold have be find in pay quantity in the bank of the river and

in the adjacent range, and it be calculate that there be already

five hundred man at work there. gold be also be discover in

various part of the country between Princetown and the river,

and a great many claim be be profitably work. the rush of

gold-digger to Princetown continue, and man be pour in every

day. yesterday the gold escort take down 4,300 ounce; it be expect

that this quantity will be double next week. our enterprise

townsman, Mr. John Jones, of the famous Beehive Stores, be have a

wooden building erect in which his extensive business will in future

be transact. we direct the attention of our reader to Mr. Jones'

advertisement on our front page. the enterprising proprietor of the

Royal Hotel have determine to construct a movable theatre, also of

wood, which will be put up every evening in the cattle sale-yard

adjoin his hotel when the sale of the day be over, and take down

after every performance to allow of the sale be resume the next

morning. this be a novel idea, and will be crown with success. a

first-class company be on its way to Princetown, and it be announce

that the first performance will be give in a fortnight. fuller

particular of these matter will be find in other column. our

reader will observe that we have double the size of the _princetown

Argus_, which now consist of four page. we have order an entire

new plant, and upon its arrival shall still far enlarge our paper.

our motto be onward."



it will be see from these extract that Newman Chaytor have carry

out his cruel scheme to what he believe and hope would be the end of

the comrade he have plot against and betray. but what man propose

sometimes fail in its purpose, and it be so in this instance. the

merciful arrival of the two gold-digger upon the scene save Basil's

life.



this last act of Chaytor's be easily accomplish. while Basil sleep

he crawl to the shaft, and by the moon's light weaken the strand

of the rope some ten foot down. then he crawl back to his bed, and

toss to and fro till the dawn of day.



"we'll work the claim till the end of the week," he say to Basil over

breakfast, "and if it turn out no well, we will try the bank of

the river again."



"very well," say Basil. "I be truly sorry I don't bring you well

luck, but we have something to go on with, at all event."



they walk to the shaft together, and Basil prepare to descend.

grasp the rope, he look up at Chaytor, and Chaytor smile at he.

he respond with a cheerful look, for although the hope in which he

have indulge of return to England with a fortune be destroy, he

have not abandon his wish to leave the colony. he be sick of the

life he be lead, and he yearn for a close human sympathy. his

share of the gold they have obtain would be close upon five hundred

pound--that be something; it would enable he to take passage home,

to find Annette perhaps, to see and speak with she and renew the old

bond; and if the bad happen, if he could not find Annette, or

find she only to learn that the woman be different from the child,

he could come back to Australia and live out his life there.



"don't lose heart," he say to Chaytor; "we may strike the vein again

this week. there's a bright future before you, I be certain."



"I half believe so myself," say chaytor; "hope against hope, you

know." and think, "will he never go down?"



Basil give one upward look at the float cloud and descend.

chaytor bend over the mouth of the shaft, look down, and listen.



"be the rope firm?" Basil cry out.



"quite firm," say Chaytor. then there come a terrified scream, and

the sound of a heavy body fall. then--silence.



Chaytor, with white face and lip tightly set, still bent over the

mouth of the shaft, still look down the dark depth, still listen.

not a sound--not even a groan.



"it be do," he mutter.



he pull up the severed rope, and think that it might have happen

without his intervention. he have read of a parallel instance, and of

the death of a miner in consequence.



"it be an accident," he say, "as this be. the rope would have give

way without my touch it. such thing occur all over the world. look

at the colliery accident at home--hundred of man be kill in they,

here there be only one."



these thought be not prompt by compunction; he simply desire to

shift the responsibility from his own shoulder. it be a miserable

subterfuge, and do not succeed. in the first flush of his crime its

shadow haunt he.



he let the rope fall from his hand down the shaft. "I could not go to

he," he say, "if I want. how quiet he be!"



a mad impulse seize he.



"Basil Basil!" he cry in his loud tone; and as no reply reach

he, he say, look around, "well, then, be it my fault that he do

not answer I?"



he pace to and fro, a dozen step this way, a dozen that, count

his step. fifty time at least he do this, always with the intention

of go to the tent or the river, and always be draw back to the

mouth of the shaft, over which he hang and linger. it possess a

horrible fascination for he.



"I _will_ go this time," he say, but he could not. he remain an

hour--the long hour in his life. at length he go down to the

river, and as he gaze upon it think, "man die by drown. what

do it matter the kind of death? death be death: it be always the

same."



the interminable hour lag on till night come. he sit in the tent

weigh the gold and get ready for flight. once in Sydney he

would take the first ship for England. the flickering candle cast

monstrous shadow upon the wall and ceiling, and in his nervous state

he shrink shudderingly from they, and strove to ward they off, as

though they be live form hover about he with fall intent. the

silence appal he; he would have give gold for the piping of a

little bird.



thus pass the miserable night, and in the morning he visit the

shaft again. the same awful stillness reign.



"it be all over," he say. "Newman Chaytor be dead; I, Basil

Whittingham, live. no one will ever know. now for England!"









CHAPTER XXV.





occasionally in a man's life come a pause: as between the act of a

drama action slumber awhile--only that the march through life's

season never halt. the pulse of time throb silently and steadily

until the natural span be reach, or be early snap, and the

bridge between mortality and immortality be cross. meanwhile the man

grow old--that be all. for he upon the tree of experience there be

neither blossom nor bloom; bare branch spread out, naked of hope,

and he gaze upon they in dumb wonderment or despair. the hum of

woodland life, the panorama of wondrous colour, the unceasing growth

of life out of death, the warlike sun, the breath of peace in moon and

star, the eternal pæn that all nature sing, bear no message to his

soul. he walk, he eat, he sleep, and wait unconsciously for the

divine touch that shall arouse he from his trance.



something of this kind occur to Basil. recover from the physical

injury he have sustain, he sink into an apathetic state which, but

for some powerful incentive, might have be morally fatal. friend he

have none, or the effort might have be make; so for a year after

Newman Chaytor have leave Australia he plod aimlessly on, work for

wage which keep he in food, and desire nothing more. upon the

subject of his mate's desertion he preserve silence, as indeed he do

upon most other subject, but it might reasonably have be expect

that upon this theme in which he be directly interested he would have

be willing to open his mind. it be not so. to question address

to he he return brief and unsatisfactory answer, and after a time

nothing far be ask of he. Curiosity die out; if he choose to

keep himself aloof it be his business, and in the new world, as in

the old, every man's affair be sufficient to occupy he without

trouble himself about stranger. thus it would appear that the

scheme upon which Newman Chaytor have bend all his energy be

destine to be in every way successful.



with respect to the desertion and the disappearance of the gold, an

equal share of which be rightfully and lawfully his, Basil have

arrive at a definite conclusion. he entertain no doubt that the

rope have break naturally; suspicion of foul play do not cross his

mind. he argue that Chaytor, believe he to be dead, have take the

gold and leave the claim they have be work in disgust. "he make no

secret," think Basil, "that he be sick of the life we be lead.

to have go away and leave my share of the gold behind he--I be,

as he suppose, dead--would have be an act of folly. I do not blame

he; good luck go with he. he stick to I to the last, and prove

himself my friend when most I need one. let my life go on as it

will; I will think nothing and say nothing to his injury." a

vindictive man would have argue otherwise, would have think that it

be at least a comrade's duty, before he leave the spot, to convince

himself by ocular proof that the fall be fatal. but Basil be not

vindictive; he believe he have the good of reason to be grateful to

Chaytor, and if the gold his mate have take be any repayment for

service render in the past, he be welcome to it. the strong moral

principle in Basil's nature keep he from yield to temptation

against which not all man struggle successfully when misfortune

persistently dog they. he lead an honest life of toil, without

ambition to lift himself to a high level. but happily an awakening

be in store for he, and it come through the sweetest and most

humanising of influence.



Princetown throve apace; its promise be fulfil, and twenty

thousand man find prosperous lodgment therein. the majority delve,

the minority trade, most of they throve. to be sure some be

unfortunate, and some idle and dissipate, but this must always be

expect. new lead be discover, quartz reef be open,

crush machine be put up, street be form, a fire brigade be

establish, a benevolent institute and a lunatic asylum be found.

not even a mushroom town in these new country can exist without

something in the shape of a municipal council, and one be form in

Princetown, over the election for which there be prodigious

excitement. church and chapel, even a synagogue, be erect by

voluntary contribution, and there be churchyard in which already

wanderer find rest. all the important building be now of wood,

and there be a talk of stone, the primal honour of erecting which be

presently to fall to John Jones, the enterprising proprietor of the

only Beehive. the _Princetown Argus_ share in the general prosperity.

first a weekly, then a bi-weekly, then a tri-weekly, finally a daily.

first, two page, the size of the _Globe_, then four page ditto,

finally four page, the size of the _Times_. not a bad sample of

enterprise this. the Saturday edition be eight page, to serve the

purpose of a weekly as well as a daily, and in it be publish a

novel, "to be continue in our next," which the editor take from a

London monthly magazine, and for which, in the innocence of his heart,

he pay nothing. of course there be an opposition journal, but the

_Princetown Argus_ have take the lead, and keep it in the face of all

newcomer. the shrewd editor and proprietor do one piece of business

with a more than usually obstinate rival which deserve to be

record. he buy up an opposition paper, the _Princetown Herald_,

whose politic be the reverse of those he advocate, and for a

considerable time he run the two paper on their original line, each

attack the other's principle and policy with fierce z and

vigour. thus he occupy both field of public opinion, and throw sop

to all who take an interest in local and colonial politic. and here a

word in the shape of information which will surprise many reader.

England be overrun with newspaper; the United States be more than

overrun, have nearly three to our one; but in journalistic

enterprise Australasia beat the record, have, in proportion to

population, more newspaper than any other country in the world. an

astonishing fact.



two circumstance must be mention which bear upon our story. the

first be that Basil's surname be not know; he call himself Basil,

and be so call. the second be that in the column of the _princetown

Argus_ in which birth, marriage, and death be advertise, there

be record the birth and death of a baby, the child of the editor

and his wife, bear one day and die the next. this be the first

birth and burial in Princetown. the child leave to they, the little

girl of whom we have already speak, whose name be Edith, take the

loss of her baby sister much to heart, and never a week pass that

she do not visit the churchyard and sit by the tiny grave.



at the end of twelve month or so there come to Princetown a preacher

of extraordinary power. he be rough, he be uncultivate, he have not

be educate for the pulpit, but he could stir the masse and wake up

sleeping soul. he have a marvellous magnetism and tremendous

earnestness, which silence the scoffer and make the sinn tremble;

the consequence be that sinner and scoffer go to hear he, and

some few be make well by his denunciation. there be soul which

can be reach only through fear. happily there be more which can be

reach through love.



amongst those who be draw to listen to the preacher be Basil, and

be once present he do not miss a service. one Sabbath the preacher

take sluggishness for his theme which he denounce, in its physical

and moral attribute, as a sin, the consequence of which be not to

be avoid. man be send into the world to work, to fulfil duty,

and to seek both assiduously. it be not only sinful, it be cowardly,

to put on the armour of indolence and indifference, and to so intrench

oneself be destructive of the high quality of humanity, the

exercise of which lift man above the level of the beast of the

field. to say, because one be unfortunate, "oh, what be the use of

strive?" tend to rob life of nobility and heroism. to fight the

battle manfully to the last, to keep one's heart open to humanising

influence, however poor the return which proffer love and sympathy

and charity may meet with, be the work of a man and bring its reward.

he have strive, he have prove himself, he have establish his claim to

the high life. to live only for the day, to be indifferent to the

morrow, be a quality by which animal without reason be

distinguish, and, to share with they in this respect be a cowardly

and sinful degradation. "if" (say the preacher) "there be any here

who have fall so low, I say to they, arouse yourself; take down

the shutter which darken heart and soul; admit the light which

purifie and sweeten. be man, not brute."



this be the sum of his sermon. few understand it, but they do not

perhaps value it the less highly on that account. to Basil it come as

a reproach; he quiver under the stroke and leave the place of

worship with a beat heart, with tumultuous thought in his mind.

Scarcely note whither he be go he walk towards the churchyard,

and there in the distance, sit by a grave, he see a child. it be

Edith sit by the grave of her baby sister.



the scene, the attitude, bring Annette's form to his mind. so use

she to sit by her mother's grave on the plantation, and he have

accompany she and sit by her side. he look about for flower;

there be none near; but when he approach Edith he see that she have

some in her lap, and be weave they into a garland, as Annette have

do in a time really not so very long ago, but which seem to belong

to another life. she look up at he, and the tenderness of her gaze

touch he deeply; instantly on her countenance be reflect the sad

wistfulness which dwelt on his. child be peculiarly receptive;

they meet your smile with smile, your sadness with sadness. Edith

just shift her little body, convey in the slight movement an

invitation to Basil to sit beside she. he instantly take his place

close to she, and they fall naturally into conversation.



"what be your name, little one?"



"Edith. tell I your. I like you."



"my name be Basil."



"I like that, too. here be a flower for you."



"where do you gather they, Edith?"



"we have a garden. Father say it put he in mind of home."



"who be your father?"



"don't you know? everybody else do. he's the editor of the

_Princetown Argus_. you know that, don't you?"



"yes. and you have a mother?"



"oh, yes. she be very clever." Basil nod. "Father say she be the

cleverest woman in the world. she can make clothe, she can cook, she

know all about flower, she can write paragraph for the paper, and

when they be write she can print they."



"that be a great deal for your mother to do. do she really help to

print the newspaper?"



"not now. she do when we first come here. but father have a great many

gentleman printer in the office, and they do all that. these be

english flower. the seed come all the way from England where I be

bear; but I don't remember it because I be only a little baby when we

come over in a great big ship. I don't remember the ship either, but I

know all about it because mother have tell I about the great storm,

and how we be nearly wrecked, and how the ship be batter to

piece almost."



"the english flower put your father in mind of home. that be

England?"



"yes, that be England. when we're very rich we're go back there. do

you know where it be?"



"I come from England."



"that be nice. like we. be you go back?"



"I cannot say."



"why? because you don't know?"



"that be the reason, perhaps."



"you see," say Edith, arrange some flower on the grave in the

shape of a cross, "there be so many people there we love. two

grandfather, two grandmother, and such a lot of cousin I've never

see. England must be very, very beautiful. Father and mother call it

home, and when I write I always say, 'we be come home one day.'

we're go to have a fig-tree; father say we shall sit under it."

Basil smile. "I like you to smile; you don't look so unhappy then.

what make you unhappy? you mustn't be. you must go home with we and

see the people you love."



"suppose there be none, little Edith."



she gaze at he solemnly. "not even an angel?" she ask.



"an angel!" he exclaim somewhat startled.



"yes, an angel. one be here once." she have complete the cross of

flower, and she point to the grave. "only for a little while, and

when we go home she be come with we. she come from heaven to we just

for one night only; I be asleep and don't see she; I be so sorry.

then they bring she here, and she fly straight up to heaven. I

cann't go up there to give she the english flower, so I lie they here

where she can see they, and when I come again and the flower be go

I know that she have take they away and put they in a jug of water--up

there. Mother say flower never die in heaven, so baby sister must

have a lot. I dream of she sometimes; I wish you could see she as I

do. there's a picture of a baby angel over my bed, and she be just

like that. such beautiful large grey eye--my eye be grey--and

shine wing. we love each other dearly."



"I hope that will always be, little Edith."



"oh, it will be. when you love once you love always; that be what

mother say, and she never say anything wrong. I wish you have an

angel."



"I have one once."



"why, then you have one now. once mean always. be she a little

girl?"



"yes."



"like our angel. I be glad. now you must come and see mother and

father." she rise and take his hand.



"they do not know I, Edith."

"yes."

the preacher have say, "take down the shutter which darken heart and

the office of the _Princetown Argus_ be now an extensive building all

on one floor; architect have not yet reach high flight. the door

the editor look up as his little daughter and Basil enter, and

CHAPTER XXVI.





certainly neither Basil nor his employer have reason to be otherwise.

the official quarter of the township, where public business be

little delay as possible, and unmask the plot.



that night he count the money he have save; it amount to two

hundred and thirty pound. he could land in the old country with a

hundred and fifty pound. he consult the exchange newspaper send to

the office. in seventeen day a steamer would start from Sydney for

England. by that vessel he would take his departure.









CHAPTER XXVII.





the next morning Basil say to the editor, "I fear I be about to

the editor consider before he speak again. "come, here's an offer

the editor be soften. "I will think it over, and let you know in

"well," say the editor to Basil, later in the day, "you must have

they shake hand, and pass a pleasant evening together. the editor

CHAPTER XXVIII.





for cogent reason Basil have travel home third-class. it economise

it have to be reckon with.



in consider how he should set about his task, he have decide that

Mr. Philpott open his eye at this question; to use his own term,

"certainly he would," reply Mr. Philpott. "but that be simple

this be a fresh and startling revelation to Basil. Newman Chaytor have

but do the foul plot go far than this? every time Chaytor

what if Annette have write? it be more than possible, it be

it be the pride of the poor gentleman, who would leave himself

"I will, sir."

there be a gentle knock at the door. "come in," say Basil, and Mrs.

"my husband tell I you wish to see I, sir," say the landlady.

"Friday's an unlucky day, sir," say Mrs. Philpott, fencing.



"but to-day be Saturday," say Basil, with a smile.



"there's no hurry, sir, I assure you."



Basil look at she and shake his head. his look, and the weary,

"I must insist upon have the account, Mrs. Philpott."



"well, sir, if you insist," say Mrs. Philpott, reduce to

"how could you pay, sir, when you don't know what the breakfast come

"that do not excuse I. oblige I by tell I how much I owe you."



"if you willn't be deny, sir, it's twelve and tenpence."

"you're never go to leave we, sir! be there anything wrong with the

"the room be very comfortable, and I wish I could remain in they;

but it cannot be."



"you must remain, sir, really you must. I willn't take your notice. you

her consciousness of the strait he be in, and her pity for it, be

"think well of it, sir," urge Mrs. Philpott. "you be a gentleman

don't mind confess that I cann't see the bottom of it."









CHAPTER XXIX.





sternly resolve to carry out his determination not to occupy room

for which he could not pay, Basil leave Mrs. Philpott's house on the

appoint day. it be his wish to quit without be observe, but

Mrs. Philpott be on the look-out and lie in wait for he. before he

reach the street door she bar his way in the landing.



"you're not go away, sir," she say reproachfully, "without wish

the child good-bye."



in honest and affectionate friendship there be frequently display a

pleasant quality of cunning which it do no harm to meet with, and in

"you'll excuse I a minute, sir," say the good woman; "if I don't

out she bustle, and quickly return with a mighty dish of irish

"Philpott say," say Mrs. Philpott as she place the steam dish on

and what father say be law, ben't it, child? I always have dinner

pop in quite sudden to surprise we."



all the time she be talk she be fill up the plate, and the

the few shilling a-week don't matter to we now."



"I be truly glad to hear it, Mrs. Philpott," say Basil.



"there be a time," continue Mrs. Philpott, "when it do matter, and

when every shilling be worth its weight in gold in a manner of

break off in the middle of her sentence.



"pray go on," say Basil, feel that it would be churlish to check

"it's a comfort, sir," continue Mrs. Philpott, "to open one's heart.

it don't make I melancholy to look back to those day, though my

say die. 'you keep on try, father,' I say. 'now haven't I keep on

Philpott ought to have be ashamed to say as much. all that I do be

it be right about face in real earnest. Philpott's friend have another

friend who get my husband in the force, and now we've get a bit of

Mrs. Philpott's account of her trouble be much long than she

the necessity of living be force upon he, and to live a man must

indeed, there be in all condition of life something to be grateful

for, if we would only seek for it.



a curious fancy entertain Basil's mind. he hear the carriage

"you have not make your fortune?



"no, indeed."



"you look poor enough. have you no money?"



"none," say Basil; and add hastily, "or very little."



"you have be unfortunate since your return home?"



"very unfortunate."



she open her purse, and take out a sovereign and hold it out to he.



"thank you no," say Basil, his wonder grow.



"you be change indeed," say the woman, "to refuse money. it be

honestly come by. two year ago I be marry, and my husband, who

die a year afterwards, leave I a small income. it be more than I

deserve, for I deceive he by tell he I be a widow. it make no

difference, but still it be a deceit. will you not take it?"



"no."



"and yet you need it?"



"do not urge I far. good night."



"wait one moment. I be go to tell you to-night; but you have well

see for yourself. it be your right. here be my address; my mother and

sister live with I. come and see I to-morrow morning at ten o'clock.

promise I."



"no, I cannot promise," say Basil, move away. "you must promise,"

say the woman, move after he. "I will not leave you till you do. I

tell you it be your right--it be more than your right, it be your

duty."



see there be no other way to release himself from she, Basil say,

"I promise."



"on your sacred word of honour," say the woman.



"on my sacred word of honour."



"I will trust you; there be a time when I would not. good night.

to-morrow, at ten."



she glide away, and Basil be once more alone. the misery of his own

circumstance be no encouragement to he to dwell upon the adventure,

and he dismiss it from his mind, account for the woman's strange

utterance by the supposition that she be of weak intellect. he

pass the night in the open air, and in the morning buy one

pennyworth of bread--it be cheap than buy a penny roll--for his

breakfast. this and water from a drinking-fountain satisfied hunger

and thirst.



"a man can live upon very little," he say to himself, "but how be it

go to end?"



it be a pertinent question, and answer itself. the end seem near

and certain.



it be a bright morning, and he walk in the sun. he do not forget

the promise he have make to the woman; it be a promise to which he have

pledge himself, and even if mischief result it must be fulfil.

the name on the card be Mrs. Addison the address, Queen Street, Long

Acre. Thither he go, and pause before a milliner's shop, the

window of which be partially mask by shutter. over the shop

front be the name Addison, and the good display bore evidence of a

certain prosperity; they be not of the poor kind. an elderly,

severe, and there be no civility in her voice as she inform he in

answer to his question, that he have come to the right address.



"go through that door," she say with a frown, "up-stair to the first

landing. my daughter expect you. I must ask you to make your visit

short."



it be not only that her voice be cold, it express repugnance, and

without request an explanation Basil follow she and mount the

stair. the sound of his footstep bring the woman he have meet on

Westminster Bridge to the door of the front room.



"you have keep your promise," she say. "come in."



a young woman than she rise as he enter, cast one brief glance at

he, and immediately leave the room. the window blind be down and

the gas be light. his strange acquaintance of the previous night

be dress in deep mourning. her face be white and swollen with

weeping.



"I pray for a miracle last night," she say, "but my prayer be

not answer. I have also repent that I ask you to come, but still

it be right, it be right. if you have a heart it should be a

punishment to you for all you have make I suffer."



"I do not in the least understand you," say Basil.



have it not be for her grief her look would have be scornful. she

pay no heed to his word, but continue:



"when I say last night that I want nothing of you I say what I

mean. when you go from here I wish never to see your face again. it

will be useless for you to trouble I."



"I shall not trouble," say Basil in a gentle tone which seem to

make she waver; but she would not yield to this soft mood.



"that you be poor to-day," she say, "and I be well-to-do, so far as

money go, prove that there be a providence. year ago--very soon

never to admit you into it again. it might have be otherwise have you

behave honestly to I, for I love you, and you make I believe that

you love I. it be well for I that the tie which bind we should

be break. I have lead a respectable life, and shall continue to do so.

I be the happy for it."



"for heaven's sake," cry Basil, "explain what it be you accuse I

of."



"ask your own heart. although there be an apparent change in you, you

be still the same, I see, in cunning and duplicity. but I will listen

to no subterfuge; there be no possibility of your justifying

yourself, and your power over I be go. towards you my heart be cold

as stone."



"you be labour under some singular delusion," say Basil, "and I

can but listen to you in wonder."



"still the same, still the same," say the woman. "you use to boast

of your superior power, and that you be so perfect an actor that
