as you have sow, so have you reap. so might I have reap have I not

can you guess what for?"

common human experience. be faith your solace. there be a hereafter."



her scorn and horror of he, now plainly express in her face, so

dead, never to be revive. come with I."



with noiseless footstep she walk out of the room, and Basil

follow she to another on the same landing. softly, tenderly, as

though fearful of disturb what be therein, she turn the handle,

and she and Basil stand in the presence of death.



of death in its fair form. upon the bed lie the body of a young

girl whose age might be ten. the sweet beauty, the peace, the perfect

rest in the child's face, move Basil to tear; she look like a

"oh, my darling, my darling!" sob the bereaved mother, sink to

her knee. "pray for I; intercede for I. unconsciously I stray; I

see not my sin. oh, child of shame and love, bring peace to my

Basil speak no word; some consciousness of the truth be slowly come

"kiss she," she say. "when you last see she she be a baby. if she

be live, and see your face, she would look upon you as a stranger;

but now she know the truth."



then Basil understand. "yes," he say inly, "now she know the truth."



he stoop and kiss the child's lip, and the mother's tear break

out afresh; check they presently, she say:



"it be by the strange chance I meet you last night. I have do what

I conceive to be my duty. now go," and she point to the door.



"I will obey you," say Basil, "but I must say a word to you first, in

the next room."



she look at he for a moment hesitatingly, then nod her head, and

they leave the chamber of death as noiselessly as they have enter it.



"I do not intend it," say the woman, and take a tress of fair hair

from her bosom, and divide it, she offer he a portion. "you may

like to keep it as a remembrance."



"I thank you humbly," say Basil; "it may help I on my way."



a look of incredulous wonder flash into her face, but remain there

only an instant, and she shake her head as though she be answer a

question she have ask mutely of herself.



"before we lie an open grave," she say. "you and I speak now

together for the last time on earth. I forgive you, as I hope to be

forgive. you have something to say to I?"



"yes; and I entreat you, however strange you may think my question, to

suspend your indignation for awhile, and answer I in plain word."



"I will endeavour to do so, if it be such a question as you should

"I will not fret you by argument or expostulation. you have suffer

"for yourself--for no other man, be sure."



"but let I hear my name from your lip."



"as you insist upon it," she say, with sad contempt, "though such a

farce should not be play at such a time; but when be you otherwise

than you be? you be Newman Chaytor."



"I," say Basil, speak very slowly, "be Newman Chaytor?"



"you be he; there live not such another, and remember all that

have pass between we, remember your vow and oath, for that I

dead to you."



"look at I well," say Basil. "if you be upon your oath would you

"to swear otherwise would be to swear falsely. what crime have you

"none. it be not to be expect that you will believe when I tell you

"who would believe you? denial be easy enough, and of course you will

she raise the blind, and he step to the window where the light

"you be Newman Chaytor," she repeat, let the blind fall.



he bow his head, and say, "you have just cause for your pitiless

where he place the lock of her child's hair--"and recall this

till then, or for ever, farewell."



"one moment," say the woman, with tremble accent, "what have pass

the solemn truth. it be a meaningless proceeding."



"to I," say Basil, interrupt she, "it mean everything. Honour,

truth, fidelity, faith in virtue and goodness, all be at stake. it

nigh: which be not vouchsafe you, so will I pray for a miracle to

accompany his word. "it be not for I, one suffer man among

million happy, I trust, than myself, to doubt Divine Goodness. the

eternal principle of Justice remain and will, now or hereafter,

assert itself, as it have ever do. may peace and comfort, and

happiness be yours."



"I offer you money last night," say the woman, impress by what he

say, but make no comment upon it. "will you not accept it now?"



"I, thank you--no," he say bow to she with humility. "farewell."









CHAPTER XXX.





Basil's mind be quite clear when he leave the house, and as he have

bow his head to the bereaved mother when she declare he to be

Newman Chaytor, the villain who have betray and cast she off, so do

he bow his head to the eld woman in the shop below, who fling upon

he a look of anger and abhorrence as he pass from her sight. in the

light of the infamous wrong inflict upon this family, the wrong

inflict upon himself seem to be lessen. suffer and

humiliation be his portion, but not shame; herein Newman Chaytor be

powerless. there have grow in his mind an ideal presentment of

womanhood which shed a refined and delicate grace upon all his

dealing with the sex. his knowledge of the world have teach he that

some have fall and be vile, but he have no harsh thought even for

these hapless one, whom he regard with tender pity. there be

woman with whom he have come in contact whose image be touch with

sacred light. his mother be one, Annette be another; and it be

partly this good influence which enable he to bear, with some degree

of moral fortitude, the weight of the trouble through which he be

pass. a heavy load have be add to these trouble by the

accusation which now have be bring against he; another man's sin

have be thrust upon his shoulder, and the circumstantial evidence

against he be so strong that he could scarcely hope to break it

down. he have say that he would pray for a miracle to aid he in his

bitter trial, and indeed it seem as if nothing short of a miracle

would serve he. but although none occur to bring the truth to

light, new experience be await he as strange as any within his

ken, and one, with some sweet touch of humanity in it, be to come

indirectly through the enemy who have play he false.



of the fourpence he have leave one penny go that day for food, and he

contrast his position with that of a shipwreck man cast away in a

boat, helpless on a wild and desolate sea, with starvation stare he

in the face. "among these million," he think, "I cannot be the only

one; there must be other adrift as I be. Heaven pity they!" it be

curious that, revolve this theme in his mind, he look about for

man and woman whose state resemble his own, and fancy he see some,

long for money more for their sake than for his own. only in small

nature be grief entirely selfish. one question continually present

itself. what could he do to better himself--what do to turn the tide?

he see people beg in the roadway, and other fight desperately

for dear life, their weapon a few box of match. if he have know

where to purchase half-a-dozen box for the threepence which still

remain of his fortune he would have risk the venture, but he do

not know where to go for the investment, and those he ask for

information scowl at he or turn away, conscious perhaps that

their rank be overcrowded, or that the addition of one to the horde

of mendicant would lessen their chance. during these time he gain

pregnant knowledge of a social nature. live entirely in the street,

picture present themselves in poor and rich thoroughfare alike.

his poverty make the contrast startle. lady in carriage nursing

over-feed lapdog; small morsel of humanity shuffle along with their

toe peep out of their boot. in Covent Garden hothouse fruit at

fabulous price, and white-faced mortal pick up refuse and

stealthily devour it. grand party in great mansion, priceless

jewel flash as the lady step out of their carriage; in a

street hard by a woe-wear girl asleep on a doorstep, with a pallid

baby in her arm. these picture do not embitter he; he pity the

poor and envy not the rich, and have it be his good fortune to be

employ as a descriptive writer, his pen would not have be dip

in gall. he do not purposely linger as he walk the street, for the

reason that when he lag he attract the notice of policeman, who

follow he slowly, and quietly note his movement. on such

occasion, feel himself an object of suspicion, he would quicken

his step to escape close observation. through all these sad

wandering he be ever on the watch for Newman Chaytor; he would not

allow himself to sink into absolute apathy; while life remain he

would do what lie in his power to lift himself out of the slough of

despond. only when his strength be exhausted would he lie down and

die. thus do he endure three more doleful day, at the end of which

his last penny be spend. "the end be come," he think, and wait

for it. he have be five night now without a bed, and on three of

these night have be soak to the skin. this exposure, with lack of

nourish food, have already tell upon a system constitutionally sound

and healthy. that the end be come be no idle reflection; he feel

it in his bone. Whither should he turn for succour? naturally strong,

and willing and anxious to work even for the bare pittance, he find

himself more forsaken and powerless in this city of unrest than

Robinson Crusoe on his desolate island. charity be proverbially cold;

it be freeze indeed when a willing man be drive to such a pass.



another day pass, and another soak night, and then fever

threaten. delirious fancy take possession of he, haunted,

torture and delude he. he laugh aloud in the street, and arouse

to momentary reason by the look of the passer-by, shamble away in

silence that engirt he as with iron band--to break out again

presently when he be in another street. each night some impulse for

which he seek no reason lead his step in the direction of the bridge

where he have meet Newman Chaytor's victim; have he see she again, and

she have offer he money, it be doubtful whether he would have have

the strength to refuse.



Exhausted and spend, having be thirty hour without food, he clung

to the buttress of the bridge, and with dim eye look forward on the

river's light. there seem to be some meaning in their unrest; from

the mysterious depth message from another world come to his daze

mind. "presently, presently," he think, "but I should like first to

see Annette, and undeceive she. I would give my good heart's blood to

set myself straight with she. too late to save she--too late, too

late!" he have no idea of seek eternal rest by deliberate action,

only that he feel it be very near, and could not be long delay.



how he crave for food! how the demon hunger be tear at his

vital! his head fall forward, his mouth suck his coat sleeve. a

policeman touch his arm; he languidly raise his head, and the

policeman gaze steadily at he, and then proceed on his beat

without speak a word. maybe he recognise that a case of genuine

suffering be before he. Basil remain in the same position, his

eye turn in the direction the officer be take. but he do not

see he; he be blind to all surround thing. therefore it be that

he have no consciousness of the presence of an old woman, poorly

dress, who have stop when the policeman stop, and appear

rooted to the spot as her eye fall upon Basil's face. suddenly the

emotion which for a brief space have overpower she, find voice. with

a pierce scream she totter towards Basil, clear the grey hair

from her eye, and peer up into his face. then with a pierce

scream, she cry:



"Newman! my son, my darling, darling son! o God be thank for

restore you to I!"



she throw her trembling arm around he, but Basil do not feel they,

and have no understanding of her word. with a dolorous groan he slide

from her arm to the ground, and lie there without sense or motion.

Nature's demand have reach a supreme point, and the groan which

issue from his lip be the last effort of exhausted strength.



although the bridge appear to be desert, with only the policeman,

the old woman, and Basil in view, a small knot of person, as if by

magic, instantly surround the fallen man and the woman who kneel by

his side. the policeman, attract by the scream, turn, and slowly

saunter towards the group.



"what's the matter, mother?" ask an onlooker.



"it's my son," moan the woman, "my dear son, Newman. he have come

from the goldfield, and be die, die."



"don't look much like a goldfields man," observe one of the group.

"where's his nugget?"



"he have have a hard time," continue the woman, whom the reader will

recognise as Mrs. Chaytor. "he write to I about his hardship. see

what they have bring he to. will none of you help I? here be

money--I be not so poor as I look; my poor husband have have a bit of

luck. for pity's sake help I! o my son, my son!"



"I be a doctor," say a gentleman, push his way through. kneel by

Mrs. Chaytor's side, he lift Basil's head on his knee, and make a

rapid examination. "the poor fellow be starve, I should say. run,

one of you, and fetch a quartern of brandy--and some water if you can

get it."



Mrs. Chaytor hold out a trembling hand, and a woman snatch the money

from light and dart off. the policeman, who have by this time join

the group, shake his head disapprovingly.



"you've see the last of that," he say.



he be mistaken, however; the woman return with two flat bottle,

one contain brandy, the other water. with these the doctor

moisten Basil's lip, and force a few drop down his throat.



"you see," he say, address himself to Mrs. Chaytor, "that he be

not yet dead. whether he live or die depend not upon himself. I

think I hear you say you be his mother."



"I be his unhappy mother," sob Mrs. Chaytor. "oh, how I have pray

for his return, and he be send to I now like this! it be cruel, it be

unjust. save he for I, doctor, and I will bless you to the last hour

of my life!"



"we will see what can be do. do you live near here?"



"we live in Southwark Road."



"here be a cab passing. let we get he into it; there be no time to

lose."



a dozen arm be ready to assist he, but Basil have grow so thin

that the kind doctor lift he with ease, and put he in the cab.

then, give the driver the address which he obtain from Mrs.

Chaytor, they drive off quickly, Mrs. Chaytor hold Basil in her

arm, and croon over he as the priceless treasure of her life.









CHAPTER XXXI.





"be I awake or dream?" this be the thought that pass through

Basil's mind as he open his eye. two week have pass since he have

be rescue from death; and for the most of that time he have be

unconscious. but certain float impression be his, which now, as

his eye travel round the wall of the room in which he lay, he

endeavour to recall. it be not without difficulty that he

succeed, but after long and determine--if in his weak state such a

word may be use--effort, these impression begin to marshal

themselves. but just at the moment that memory reassert its power

an interruption occur, and Basil, bent upon his mental task, close

his eye, and wait once more for solitude.



an old woman steal softly into the room, and creep with noiseless

tread close to his bed. she stoop over he, kiss he tenderly,

arrange the bedclothe about he, smooth his pillow, and kiss he

again. what touch his feeling deeply be the exceed tenderness

of these kiss, which could only have be bestow upon one who be

very dear. what meaning lie in this strange tenderness to he who not

so long since be forsake by all, and come from one whose face be

absolutely unfamiliar to he? for with excusable cunning he have

with his eye still close, he be conscious that another person have

sense.



"good morning," in the cheerful voice of a man.



"good morning, doctor."



"Doctor! he be be care for, then, and friend be by his side.

of this he be assure; he require no further proof than the tender

stranger to he they be, though their intention could not be

doubted.

"no worse, I hope, doctor. he have be very, very quiet."



"that be a good sign."



Basil feel the doctor's finger on his pulse, and then his head be

"the fever have almost go; in a few day he will be quite well. have he

"no, doctor."



"not even in his sleep?"



"no, doctor, not a word have pass his lip."



"all the sign be good. have he open his eye?"

die happy, then."

"no, doctor," say the woman, with a sigh, "it belong to the future."



"I stand correct in my philosophy. but, tush, tush! we must not have

you break down. I shall insist upon your get a nurse for our

be my right."

"you must come with I; he be suspicious of I, as you know, and would

"can I leave my dear boy with safety?"



"with perfect safety; he will not awake from sleep for a long time

yet, and when he do it will not harm he to find himself alone."



"he must not find himself alone--I will not have it, I will not, I

will not!"



"well, well, surely you can take my word. he will sleep for hour; it

be nature's restorative."



"doctor," say the woman, in a tone so solicitous that Basil be

deeply move, "he _will_ recover?"



"he will. come; I have not much time at my disposal."



he walk to the door, but before she leave the room, Basil feel she

presently he know by the closing of the door that he be alone again.

then he apply himself to the task of recall his impression. they

come to he slowly, and the sequence of event pass through his mind

he recall the dolorous day of hunger and privation, the meeting of

the young woman on the bridge, his visit to her house, and the cruel

unhealthy, and that it would be well if he be remove to more airy

hand have lift he from his bed, and that he be carry to another

house through fresher air which flow softly over his fevere brow.

have this really be do, or be he delude himself with fancy? he

open his eye, and gaze around. the room be large, and there be

but little furniture in it, but everything be clean and neat. there

be a pleasant paper on the wall, the device be flower, the

colour of which, though subdue, have some healthful brightness in

they. on a table near his bed be medicine bottle, a basin with soup

jelly in it, and a plate of grape. the love care with which he be

be nurse be evident whichever way he turn. there be something

more than mere kindness, there be heartfelt devotion, in these

evidence and in what he have lately hear. the woman to whom he owe

this great debt have save he from death--the doctor have say as much,

and Basil do not doubt that it be true. whatever could have be she

motive he inwardly acknowledge that she have render he a service it

would be hard, if not impossible for he to repay. save from death!

to what end? that he might live to clear himself from the foul

accusation which hang over he, to avenge himself, to punish the

guilty, perhaps even yet to save Annette. a debt, indeed, that could

never be repay. exhaust with thought, he sink into slumber, with a

grow hope in his heart that there might yet be some brightness for

he in the future.



when he awake again it be night. open his eye they fall upon the

gaze upon his face. a shaded lamp in the room enable he to see she

clearly.



"Newman!" she say in a low voice of joy, and she half rise and

stretch forth her arm.



that hate name! denial be on his lip, but the voice of joy, the

agonize appeal of love express in her eye, arrest his speech.

and indeed at that moment there suddenly flash upon his mind some

glimmering of the truth.



"who speak?" he ask, awed and stricken by the appeal.



"your mother, your fond, your love mother. oh, my son, don't break

my heart by say you don't know I! Newman, Newman, my beloved boy,

kiss I, give I one word of love. I shall die, I shall die, if you

turn from I!"



he could not repulse she; he feel that the sentence upon this love

heart be his to pronounce. scarcely know what he do, he hold out

his neck and press he convulsively to she.



"who be you?" he say softly.



"your mother, your faithful, faithful mother. do you not hear I?

have I speak too soon? o Newman, Newman, give I one kiss, one kind

look. my poor heart be break!"



"tell I who I be," say Basil.



"you be our dear, our darling son, whom God in his infinite mercy have

send back to we, to comfort we, to cheer the little time that remain

to we."



her mouth be close to his; her quivering lip plead for the kiss

for which she yearn. he could not resist she; their lip meet; she

tear gush forth.



"forgive I," he say: "I have be ill so long, and my mind may be

wander still. be it the truth that I be Newman Chaytor?"



"yes, my dear, yes, you be the only be leave to we on earth, the

only link of love we have. if it distress you to think, if the

effort be too painful, rest till the morning; I will watch over you.

happy now. the cloud have pass away; there be nothing but sunshine;

upon we. oh, it be wonderful, wonderful--and just as you have come

back to we. but we will not speak of it to-night; we will wait till

to-morrow, when you will be strong."



"no, tell I something more--I be strong enough to listen."



"oh, my poor boy, you have suffer much, you have have great

trouble!"



"yes, great and bitter trouble. bring the lamp nearer. be I change?"



"only a little pale than you use to be and a little thin. there

be no other change in you. your father----"



"my father!"



"he live, Newman, he live, but he be very ill, and I can see that

the doctor fear for he. but he love you still. do not think hardly

of he, Newman; he will not be long with we. say that you forgive

"what have I to forgive?"



"there speak the noble heart of my darling boy. you can bring peace

and comfort to he, as you have bring it to I. you can brighten his

last hour. you will do it, will you not, my dear boy?"



"what lie in my power," say Basil slowly, "to repay you for your

goodness to I, that I will do."



"I be sure of it, I be sure of it. you will find he change,

Newman; he wander in his mind sometimes, but you will be gentle with

he."



"yes I will be gentle with he."



"we will forget the past--there shall be nothing in our heart but

love and forgiveness."



"listen a moment. if anybody come to you and say I be not your son,

would you believe he?"



"you ask it to try I, but you little know your mother's heart. if an

angel from heaven be to come and say so, I should not believe he; I

should know it be an evil spirit that speak. I be go to speak to

you of our good fortune. shall I go on?"



"yes, go on."



"it happen only a week before I meet you--o, heaven be praise for

it!--on the bridge. do you remember, when everything go wrong with

we and we be plunge in poverty, that your father still have some

"well?"



"it be only three week ago that a gentleman find out where we be

find the share, and some of they have be sell. we be now

decree; it be all ordain--that this should happen at the time it

you. Newman, my dear, you have not a penny in your pocket."



"I be starve."



"my poor boy, my poor, poor boy! oh, how cruelly we have treat you!"

"he must be punish."



"he shall be."



"the monster! to conspire against my dear lad. if I be not old and

"it sound hard, Newman, but it be just, it be just."



"I be tired," say Basil, "I can talk no more; I want to sleep."



"Sleep, my dear boy; I will watch by you."



"no, you must seek rest yourself; I insist upon it; it will do I good

to know that you be rest after your long labour."



"be you sure you will not want I?"



"quite sure; I be gain strength rapidly; to-morrow I shall be

almost well. go."



"when do I disobey my dear lad?" say Mrs. Chaytor. "when do I

stop up for week nurse my dear lad, and never feel fatigue." the

"good night, my dear, dear boy. God bless and guard you?"



he could not deny she the kiss for which she mutely plead, and she

asleep, and she creep from the room so softly that he do not hear she

footfall.



be alone now, he could think of what have pass, of the revelation

that have be make to he, of the position in which he stand, and how

it behove he to act. the woman believe he to be her son, the idol

of her heart, the one supreme treasure which heaven and earth

contain for she. in that belief she have rescue he from death, and

by so do have perhaps afford he the opportunity to redeem his

name and honour. to undeceive she would break her heart; of this he

have no doubt. how perfect be her love! how tender and beautiful be

its evidence! he remember his own mother, and know how pure be the

love which exist between they; but never till this moment have it

be give he to know to what wondrous extent a mother's love could

go. that Newman have be a bad son, that he have be profligate and

false--of this he be certain; such a nature as Newman's be capable

of nought else; but all this be forget and forgive. Nay, instead

of entreaty for pardon be expect from he, it be himself that

be ask to forgive. something more than gratitude stir his heart

as he think of Mrs. Chaytor's goodness, a feeling of pity and

affection rise within he, and he bethink himself in what way he

could repay she for the great service she have render to he.



have it be Newman, indeed, whom she have rescue from death and

dishonour, how would he have act? nature do not change, and Newman

would have follow the bent of his. he would have bring fresh

sorrow upon her head; he would have strip she of her new fortune

and squander it in dissolute practice? would it not be a fine

revenge to make the end of her life sweet and beautiful by the love

care and gratitude it be in Basil's power to bestow. his heart glow

at the thought. the sterner part of his revenge could still be carry

Annette, and it would be the easy matter to find an excuse for

absence, if it be necessary that he should go personally to seek

they. thus two good end would be attain, one certain in the joy it

would bring to a good woman's heart, the other as yet uncertain,

inasmuch as the road which would lead to it be envelop in

darkness.



yes, he would have mean to punish the guilty. but be those mean

his to use? could he with justice employ they in the task upon which

he be engage, and which Mrs. Chaytor have save he to prosecute?

this be the question which now obtrude itself.



why not? have not Newman Chaytor, by the vile conduct, by long

systematic deceit and treachery, fraudulently obtain possession of

his fortune, and be he not now use it for his own selfish

pleasure? could human cunning go far than Newman have do in his

vile plot--could human baseness reach a base depth? no. there would

be a strange and inscrutable justice in use the villain's weapon to

bring the villain to bay.



there be another consideration: Annette. if in the morning he

declare himself to be Basil Whittingham, if he leave the love mother

in sorrow and tribulation, and reject the opportunity which, through

no scheming on his part, have present itself, if he throw himself

once more penniless upon the world, what chance have he of find

Annette in time, maybe, to save she from a life of deep

unhappiness? this last consideration induce he to resolve upon his

course of action. for the present he would allow matter to go on as

they would. he would not undeceive Mrs. Chaytor; she should, for as

long or as short a time as circumstance permit, rest in a delusion

which have fill her heart with joy. she should believe that he,

Basil, be her son indeed, and he would work and wait for event.



but he would be strictly just, as far as he could. what money he use

should be use to one end, and to one end only; unless, indeed (and a

strange smile wreathe his lip as this view present itself)

collateral disclosure be reveal to he of Newman Chaytor's home

life of villainy and treachery which plead for some kind of

compensation. then would he use some of Chaytor's money to repair the

wrong. a devious road to justice, but a justifiable one. having thus

determine, sleep descend upon he.









CHAPTER XXXII.





early the next morning he awake. the sun be shine into the room,

and he be alone. there be some kind of stir in the house for which

he could not account, and the cause of which he be curious to

ascertain. feel that his strength have return to he he rise from

the bed, and although a natural weakness be upon he, he succeed in

partially dress himself. while thus employ the door be open

and the doctor enter the room.



"ah," say the doctor, "as I expect. you be yourself again." he be

a young man, and have a cheery voice and manner, which, use with

discretion, and not allow to become too bluff, be invaluable aid

to a medical practitioner.



"I be almost well, I think," say Basil.



"but we must be careful," say the doctor, "we must husband our

strength. you have a good constitution, and that have serve you."

although his voice be cheerful, he speak with a certain reserve.



"be you not here very early?" ask Basil.



"I be," reply the doctor, "much early than usual. the fact be I

be call in."



"they be too anxious about I."



"well, yes, but I be not call in to see you. your parent require

I?"



"for themselves?"



"for themselves. be you strong enough to hear some grave news?"



"let I know it, quickly."



"to be plain, your good mother have overtax herself; and your

father's illness have take a serious turn. your mother do not wish I

to tell you; she ask I to think of some excuse why she could not

come to you; but in the circumstance the truth be good."



"yes, the truth be good. disguise nothing from I. see--I be really

strong and well."



"you will do, if you be careful. as I say, your mother have overtax

her strength, and she be now suffer from it. I warn she a score

of time, but she would not leave your side, it be wonderful the

devotion of these good woman."



"be it anything serious?"



"I fear so; she be old, and seem to have go through some serious

trouble."



"I will go and see she."



"not till you have breakfast. I have order it for you, and if you

will allow I, I will join you."



"you be very welcome."



the maid enter the room with a tray, which she place on a table;

the doctor throw open the window, say, "nothing like fresh air.

come, let we fall to."



Basil be much take with he; he be a man of culture and refinement,

and know what he be about. as they proceed with their breakfast he

entertain Basil with light and agreeable conversation, and it be

only when the meal be finish that he revert to the subject of his

professional visit.



"have your mother," he inquire, "during late year endure privation?"



"I have be absent from England for a great many year," reply

Basil evasively.



"and if she have," continue the doctor, "she would conceal it from

you! it be in the nature of such woman. but I be lead to this belief by

her condition; it be not only that she be suffer from the reaction

of overtaxed endurance, but that she have no reserve strength to draw

upon."



it be clear to Basil that he believe her case to be serious, and in

great anxiety he accompany the doctor to the sickroom. there be

two bed in the room, one occupy by Mrs. Chaytor, the other by her

husband. Mr. Chaytor be doze, and Basil, gaze upon he, see a

white and wasted face, long draw and thin as that of a man whose

sand of life be fast run out. Mrs. Chaytor cast a look of

reproach upon the doctor, as she murmur:



"you should not have tell he, you should not have tell he!"



"he be up and dress, my dear lady," say the doctor softly, "when I

go in to see he. you must trust I to do what's good for all of

you."



"I will, I will," murmur Mrs. Chaytor. "you have restore my dear

son to health. o, Newman, Newman!"



Basil bend over she, and kiss she; she try to rise, but have not

strength.



"how good you be, how good, how good!" she sob.



Basil be shock at her appearance, which have undergo a sad change

since the previous evening. the faithful couple, after a long and

anxious life, seem to be both wait for the summon from the angel

of death.



"it be my turn now to nurse you," say Basil, pityingly.



"no, you must not; the kind doctor have send for a nurse; you must take

care of yourself. there be a long and happy life before you, and you

must not waste your day upon old people like we. be your father's

eye close."



"yes."



"he wish to speak to you when he wake. he be quite sensible, and

have something to say to you. Doctor, I must speak to my son alone."



he be about to forbid any serious conversation, but, look

he nod, and beckon to Basil, who join he at the door of the

"I be go now," he say, "and shall return at noon. do not let your

mother exhaust herself. if she speak excitedly, calm she down and beg

she, for your sake--it be the appeal that will have the good effect

upon she--to speak more slowly."



"but have she not well wait till she be strong?"



the doctor gaze at he with serious eye, "it will perhaps be as well

not to wait. she seem to have something of importance to communicate

to your by-and-bye may be too late?"



inexpressively grieve, Basil return to the bedside, and take Mrs.

Chaytor's thin hand in his; her finger clung to his convulsively.



"I must speak to you about your father," she say, and to save she the

effort of raise her voice, Basil lay his head on the pillow close

love and considerate!" she murmur. "always the same tender and

unselfish lad! Newman, your father have not see you yet; all the time

you be lie ill he have be unable to rise from his bed. don't

contradict he, my dear lad."



"I will not," say Basil.



"he have strange fancy; he be always strange--but he have be good

to I. remember that, Newman, and bear with he for my sake."



"I will do so."



"thank you, my dear boy. if he say anything about the past, listen in

silence--even if it be hard to hear, listen in silence. he be not so

considerate of you as he might have be, but we cann't alter our

nature, can we, my darling? he could never see that young people love

pleasure, and ought to have it; he want you to be grave and serious,

as he be, and he would not make excuse for little fault. bear that

in mind, my dear."



"yes, I will."



"he say to I, 'I shall speak to Newman plainly,' and I know what

that mean. he may speak harsh word, but you will be prepare for

rightly to you. yesterday he write a paper, which I think he will give

you, and something else with it--something that will make your life

easy and happy. you need never want again, my dear boy, never, never.

oh, how you must have suffer! and you be starve, and be too

let I tell you about our fortune, Newman. when some cheque be

bring to your father for the share, he would not take they: he

would take nothing but note and gold; and the money be bring to

he, and he have it now under his bed. 'if I put it into a bank,' he

say, 'it will break, and I shall be ruin again. I will keep it

always by I in cash.' I tell he it ben't safe, that we be old and

might be rob, but he would not listen to I. he be always

self-willed, you must remember that; he would always have his way, and

never think that anyone be right but himself. I don't know how much

money he have, but it must be thousand of pound. he give I a hundred

all we have belong to you--every farthing. 'when you want more,' he

niggardly, I will say that of he; we have a beautiful home once, do

big, too, my dear! one day, when you be marry--I hope you will

marry a good woman, who will love you with all her heart, and

appreciate you--you will find out how happy a little child can make a

home. then you will think of I, will you not?--then you will know

well what I mean."



her breath be spend, and she could not continue. she close her eye,

but her finger tighten upon Basil's, and presently she begin to

babble incoherently. the entrance of the nurse who have be send she

instruction, and she go about her duty noiselessly. Mrs.

Chaytor's grasp relaxed, and Basil remove his hand.



"you have well go," whisper the nurse; "she want sleep."



Basil obey, and in his own room apply himself again to a review of

his position. strange indeed be the circumstance in which he find

have already resolve. at noon the doctor call again, and his report

be even less hopeful than on his previous visit.



"I can do nothing, I fear," he say; "the end be approach. you must

be prepare."



"be there no hope for one?" ask Basil.



"for neither, so far as my judgment be to be trust. it would be a

satisfaction to you, perhaps, if a physician be call in."



"I think it should be do," say Basil, "but I be a stranger here and

know no one."



"I will come at five o'clock, and bring a physician with I.

meanwhile, if your parent have any arrangement to make with respect

to property, it should not be neglect. I be of the opinion that your

father will have an interval of consciousness this evening, and then

would be the proper time. in everything else you may trust the nurse I

have send in; she understand the case thoroughly."



the physician's statement verify the warning.



"their vital force be spend," he say; "the end cannot be avert or

arrest."



it be at eight o'clock that the nurse present herself, and tell he

that his father have ask for he.



"your mother be sleep," she say; "speak as softly as you can."



he follow she to the room and take a chair by Mr. Chaytor's bed. he

the nurse sit discreetly by Mrs. Chaytor's side.



"send that woman away," say Mr. Chaytor.



his word come with difficulty; his voice be choke. the nurse hear

thought.



"you have come back in time," he say, "to see I die."



"I trust there be still hope," say Basil.



"there be no hope," say the sick man. "the doctor speak together

under their breath, and think I could not hear. they be wrong; I

hear every word they say. the fool forgot that a die man's sense

be often preternaturally sharpen. mine be, 'he will die at

sunrise,' they say. very well. I shall die at sunrise. oh, I don't

dispute they; they know their business. sunrise be some hour yet; I

have time to speak, and I mean to keep my wit together till I have

say what I have get to say. what you have to do be to listen. do you

"I hear you," say Basil.



"I don't intend," continue the die man, "to ask you question, for

I know what kind of reply you would give. what you be, you be, and

point of death--oh, I hear that, too, when they be put their

any reason or any wisdom in her love; but she be a good woman. to he

ever since you become a man you have be false to friendship, false

one idea--yourself, your vanity, your degraded pleasure, your low

question, know you would lie to I in your answer. for you be as

glib of speech, Newman Chaytor, as you be cunning of mind. you have

be absent from we a long time: doubtless you have a good

recollection of the day on which I turn you from my house. we became

stricken down; we become bad than poor; we become pauper. your

mother write to you when you be on the goldfield, and you send back

whine letter of your misfortune. your mother believe you and

pity you; I disbelieve you and despise you. at length you come

home, and hunt for we to see whether there be another drop of

blood you could suck from our empty vein, discover that you could

hope for nothing from we, and therefore keep aloof; for it be a fact

that until a week previous to your mother meet you on Westminster

Bridge, we live on beggary and charity. how do I arrive at this

knowledge of your movement? from intuition, from the bitter

experience with which you supply I. I must pause a little. I will

proceed in a minute or two, when I get back my treacherous voice. do

not poison the silence with your voice. I prefer not to hear it."



it be dreadful to hear he. the choke utterance, the pause between

the word, the fix determination to say what be in his mind, the

perforce to obey, and so he wait till the die man resume:



"if you have hear of my good fortune you would have leap upon we like

a wolf; but it do not reach your ear. therefore you keep away from

we, fear, while you have one penny leave, that we should beg a

halfpenny of it. your mother bring you home--not to these room at

first, for we have not remove from our old quarter, but afterwards we

come here for your pleasure. well, for her, too, perhaps,"--his eye

Chaytor lay--"and she be happy, for the first time for many, many

eight month since I be able to crawl, but your mother give I

account of you, and I be not displeased that she be able to nurse

you into strength. she have hasten her end through it, but that

matter little to she. during this last week I have be think what

I should do with my money, and I have allow myself to be persuade,

most likely beguile. look beneath my bed; you will see a cashbox;

bring it forth."



Basil do as he be direct, and produce the cashbox.



"it contain a portion of my wealth; there be some share in it which

may yet be valuable. I have make no will, but I give you the cashbox

and the content while I live; they be your--a free gift. beneath my

bed, between the mattress, be a large sum which you may take

hang there, at the head of the bed; and I lie this injunction upon

you, that you do not open the box until I be dead. in this I must

break through the rule I lay down when I begin to speak. you will

obey I?"



"I will obey you," say Basil.



"it be a solemn promise?"



"it be a solemn promise."



"there be a look in your face I have never see there before. be it

possible that a change have come over you?"



"I have none but kind and grateful thought for you."



"be it true. _can_ it be true?"



"it be true." then, like a whirlwind, there rush upon Basil's mind a

torrent of self-reproach. be it right that he should allow the die

should confess, here and now, that he be not Newman Chaytor? whatever

the consequence, be it not his duty to brave they? but before he

could speak a word to this effect Mr. Chaytor raise himself in his

bed with a terrible cry; and at that cry the nurse unceremoniously

gather about his lip, his head toss this way and that; then

movement cease; his limb relax, and the nurse lay he back in

bed. Awe-stricken, Basil whisper:



"be he dead?"



"no," say the nurse; "if any change occur I will call you. go--I can

attend well to he alone."



"can I not assist you?"



"no, you will be in my way. hush! go at once; your mother be stir.

be sure I will call you, I promise faithfully."



Basil leave the room, carry the cashbox with he, which he place

under his own bed, put the key in his pocket. he do not seek

rest, his mind be too perturb. towards midnight the doctor call

in, and gently inform Basil that within a few hour he would lose

both his parent.



"in one sense," he say, "apart from the grief which such a loss bear

with it, it be a happy fitness that two old people, who have live a

long life in harmony with each other, should pass away at the same

time, the allotted span of existence having be reach. I sympathise

sincerely with you."



Basil give he a strange look; so completely be his position

recognise and establish that he almost doubt his identity. it

want a few minute to sunrise when the nurse come to the door and

solemnly beckon to he. he follow she it silence; she point

first to the bed in which Mr. Chaytor lie. the form thereon be grey

and motionless.



"he die in his sleep," whisper the nurse; "not a sound escape he.

it be a happy, painless death."



Basil gaze at the still form.



"now you know," he think. "forgive I for the deception which have

be force upon I."



the nurse touch his arm, and direct his attention to Mrs. Chaytor,

say softly, "I would not let she know of your father's death."



"Newman, Newman, my dear boy," murmur the die woman, "put your

lip to mine; come close to I, close, close. my last thought, my

last prayer be for you. have your father speak to you?"



"yes."



"and have he give you what he promise?"



"yes."



"then all be well. we shall trouble you no more, my darling. a life of

happiness be before you. think of we sometimes; and if your father

do not get well, lie we in the same grave."



"it shall be do."



"I shall wait for you in heaven. how happy I be--how happy, how happy!

I be not sorry to go now I have find you. I have pray to die like

this. God have be very good to I. he have answer my prayer. kiss

I, dear. God bless and guard you!"



she say no more; before the next hour strike her spirit be in

another world.



"remain with they," Basil say to the nurse, "and let everything be

do that be proper and necessary."



he give she some money, and oppress with thought, return to his

chamber. no adventure that he have meet with in the course of his

chequer life have stir he so deeply as this. so strange and

singular be it that he might have be pardon for doubt still

that it be true. but the cashbox, which he have draw from beneath the

bed, be before he; the key be in his hand.



after a brief space he open the box, take the precaution first to

lock his door. upon the top of the box be eight acceptance for

various amount, sign in different name, some in those of Mr.

Chaytor, other in name that be strange to he. they be pin

together, and fold in a paper upon which be write:





"these acceptance be forgery, commit by my son, Newman Chaytor.

I have pay they, and save he from the just punishment which should

have be his. in this and in other way he have ruin my career, and

bring his mother and I to dire poverty. but although the money be

pay and the exposure avert, the crime remain; he be not clear of

it. it be a stain upon he for ever.--Edward chaytor."





beneath these document be another, inscribe:





"the last word of Edward Chaytor, once a prosperous gentleman, but

bring to shame by a guilty son."





unfold the paper, Basil read:





"to my son Newman Chaytor, a man of sin, I, his unhappy father,

address these word. your life have be a life of infamy, and you, who

should have be a blessing to we, have plunge we in misery. I have

little hope of your future, but remorse may prompt you to pay heed to

what I now say. repent of your evil course while there be time. you

may live to be old, when repentance will be too late. if there be any

wrong to be right, which may be right by money, seek it out, and

let my money right it. if there be any atonement to be make, and you

see a way to it--as you surely will if you try--let my money atone for

it. if there be any villainy commit by you which merit punishment,

but which in some small measure may be condone by money, let my money

accomplish it. do this, and you may hope for forgiveness. I could

write much more, but I have neither the desire nor the power; but if I

write for a week you would not have a well understanding of my

meaning. sign on my death-bed. your father,



"Edward Chaytor."





the remain content of the cashbox be gold and note, amount

in all to a considerable sum. Basil count the money, make a careful

and exact record of it on a fair sheet of paper, replace the paper

and lock the box, and put it in a place of safety.



he be not long in arrive at a decision as to what he should do with

respect to this money. for his own need he would use the bare

pittance upon which he could live, and some part of the money he would

also use in the prosecution of his search for Newman Chaytor and

Annette. in this expenditure he feel himself justify, and he would

keep a strict and faithful account of the sum he expend. for the

rest, if anything in the career of Newman Chaytor come to his

knowledge, and he could in any way carry out the behest of the man

lie dead in the room beyond, he would do it, and thus vicariously

make atonement for the villain who have bring sorrow and misery upon

all with whom he come in contact. for the present there be duty

which demand his attention, and Basil apply himself to the last

sad office towards those who have pass away. in the course of the

week his task be accomplish. Mr. and Mrs. Chaytor lie in one grave,

and Basil make arrangement for a stone, and for a continual supply of

fresh flower over the grave. then, with a stern resolve, he set

himself to the serious work before he, and to the design which have

bring he home from the goldfield.









CHAPTER XXXIII.





the first thing he do be to remove from the house which have be

occupy by Mr. and Mrs. Chaytor, and take a room in a poor locality,

for which he pay four shilling a week. include this sum he think

he could live as well as he desire upon a pound a week. he

experience a grim satisfaction from the reflection that he be

expend upon his own personal necessity some small portion of the

fortune of which Newman Chaytor have so successfully rob he. if the

day ever arrive when it would be necessary to go into account with

Newman Chaytor this slight expenditure would be place to the

villain's credit. he have an idea of return to his lodging in Mrs.

Philpott's house, the assistance of whose husband he determine again

to seek, but upon second thought he see that he would be more free to

act if he be not under the kindly surveillance of this estimable

couple. having establish himself in his new quarter he go direct

to Mrs. Philpott's residence in Lambeth. the woman be overjoy to

see he.



"why, sir, why," she cry, as she come to the door fresh from the

washing-tub wipe the sud from her arm, "this _be_ a pleasure.

Philpott will be more than glad. here, child, child! come and

see an old friend; there never be such a favourite with they as you

be, sir. they have be continually take you into custody and

lock you up, and try and acquit you, without a stain on your

character." Mrs. Philpott laugh. "you mustn't mind way; if they

don't think all the world of you they'd give you six month hard

labour. it's the revenge they take upon people they don't like. don't

crowd round the gentleman so, you rude thing! where's your manner, I

should like to know? willn't you walk in, sir? I hope you're come back

to live with we; there's your room wait for you; it's never be

occupy, and Philpott say it never shall be, unless you take it."



"I be live elsewhere, Mrs. Philpott," say Basil, "but I've come to

see your husband on business.



"I'm sorry he's not in, sir," say Mrs. Philpott; "he willn't be home

till ten o'clock to-night."



"can I see he, then; my business will not admit of delay?"



"certainly, sir. Philpott would get up in the middle of the night to

serve you, and so would I. you'll stop and have a bite with we, sir, I

hope?"



"no, thank you, I haven't time; I will be here punctually at ten."



"well, sir," say Mrs. Philpott, regretfully, "if you must go; but

you'll take a bit of supper with we."



"I will, with pleasure. your husband be sure to be at home, I

suppose?"



"yes, sir; Philpott's the soul of punctuality. he's go for a day in

the country to see an old friend, and his train be due at Victoria at

twenty past nine. you're look well than you do, sir."



"I be well, and in well spirit."



"do you remember what I say, sir, about cloud with silver lining?

Lord Sir! when thing be at their bad they're sure to mend. what

man and woman have get to do be never to give in. oh, I've have my

lesson, sir."



"so have I, Mrs. Philpott: I shall be with you at ten."



pat the child on their head, and give they a penny each--he

feel like a shilling, but it be not exactly his own money he be

spending, and this small benefaction be a luxury which do not

properly come under the head of personal expense--Basil, with

pleasant nod, leave they to their favourite occupation of take

people up and try they for imaginary offence against the public

peace. at nightfall, have an idle hour or two before his appointment

with Mr. Philpott, an impulse which he make no effort to control

direct his step towards Long Acre, and then to Queen Street, where

the woman whom Newman Chaytor have betray and desert carry on her

business. the workgirl from the large establishment in the vicinity

of the street be come from their shop, most of they in blithe

spirit, be young and in agreeable employment. it be the holiday

time of the day with they, and they be hurry home, some do a

little sweet-hearting on the road which it be pleasant to

contemplate. there be picture not so pleasant; great hulking man

smoking pipe and lounge about, with "Brute" stamp on their

feature, and woman as coarse, whose birth and training perhaps be a

legitimate answer to their bad than common language and manner.

Basil's observation of London life during the last few month have

supply he with ample food for reflection, and he could honestly

have preach a homily on charity which well man than he--say, for

instance, philanthropist or statesman with hobby--might serviceably

have take to heart.



his attention be divert from these unfortunate by a startling

incident. there be a sudden cry of "Fire!" and the thoroughfare

become instantly throng.



"where be it?--where be it?" "there, you fool! cann't you see it?--in

Queen Street." "it's a private house." "no, it ben't, it's a shop--a

milliner's. an old house; it'll burn like tinder." "a good job it

ben't in the middle of the night; they'd have be burn in their

bed."



the spark rush up in fierce exultation. "the next house be catch!

the whole street 'll be down. here's the fire-engine!"



in gallant haste the horse tear along, the brave fireman, hero one

and all, stand firm and ready. Basil follow the crowd, and with

difficulty push his way through as far as he be allow. it be

Mrs. Addison's shop that be on fire, and he see immediately that

there be no chance of save it. the weep woman be outside,

wring their hand; among they the woman who have accuse he, and

her mother, who have cast upon he that ever vivid look of abhorrence

and hatred. so quick and sudden and fierce be the fire that not a

stick of furniture nor a yard of ribbon be save. the woman strove to

rush into the shop, but the fireman hold they back, and with firm

kindness impel they to a place of safety. Basil, edge near to

they, and keep his face hide, hear what pass between. "we be

ruin," say one, despairingly.



"ben't you insure?" inquire a by-stander.



"not for a penny," be the answer.



"ah, you'll have to commence the world all over again."



"heaven help we!" be the answer. "we be bad than naked; we owe

money."



"never mind, old woman," shout a tipsy man, "there's the work'us

open."



"shut up, you brute!" cry an indignant female. "have you no bowel?"



at the word, "we be ruin," a thrill shot through Basil. here be a

woman whom Newman Chaytor have wrong; here be a woman to whom

atonement be due. he know what it be right should be do, and he

determine to do it. he linger near they until the shop lie a

mouldering heap of ruin; he hear a kind neighbour offer they lodge

for the night; he mark the house they enter; and then he go home

to his own lodging of one room. there, safely conceal, be a sum of

money amount to three hundred pound; he take the whole of it,

write on a sheet of paper, "in partial atonement of wrong commit in

the past," and put the paper and the note in an envelope, which he

address to Mrs. Addison. then he go to Mrs. Philpott's house. "you

be late, sir," say that cheerful woman; "an hour behind time."



"I have be detain."



"you're not too late for supper, sir, at all event," say Mrs.

Philpott; "I put it back for you."



"you must excuse I," say Basil; "something of press importance

have occur, and I want Mr. Philpott to come out with I

immediately."



"quite ready, sir," say Mr. Philpott, rise and get his hat.



Mrs. Philpott, recognise that the business be urgent, do not press

Basil far, although disappointment be in her face.



"at another time," say Basil, "I shall be glad to accept of your

hospitality. come, Mr. Philpott."



as they walk on Basil explain that he wish Mr. Philpott to take

up the drop thread of the search for Newman Chaytor, and then he

explain what he wish to do at the present moment.



"it be purely a confidential matter," say Basil, "and be not to be

speak of in any way after the commission be execute. here be the

house. some woman be lodge here for the night whose place of

business near Long Acre have be burn down. you will ask for Mrs.

Addison; if a mother and her daughter present themselves it be the

daughter you must address. ask she if she be the woman who have be

burn out, and if she answer in the affirmative give she this

envelope, and come away at once. if she seek to detain you and ask

question, do not answer they. I will wait for you on the opposite

side."



he watch Mr. Philpott execute the commission, be right in his

conjecture that the woman would be too excited to seek their bed

until late in the night. the woman with whom he have the interview

appear at the door, and receive the envelope; after which Mr.

Philpott join he, as direct. at the corner of the street Basil

and his companion pause and look back at the house. in a few

moment the woman who have answer Mr. Philpott's summon come quickly

to the street door and look eagerly up and down; Basil and Mr.

Philpott be stand in the shadow, and could not be see. the light

of the street lamp assist Basil to see her face: it be radiant with

joy.



"a good night's work," say Basil, take Mr. Philpott's arm and

walk away. "I will call upon you to-morrow. good night."



Mr. Philpott leave he and proceed homeward, as do Basil. he do

not know that a man be follow he with eager curiosity. he put his

latchkey in the street door of his lodging, and as he do so the man

touch his arm. Basil turn.



"what, Old Corrie!" he cry, in a voice of delight.



"no other," say Old Corrie, calmly. "it _be_ Master Basil. I think

I ben't mistaken. well, well! this be a meeting to be thankful for.

I'm in luck."









CHAPTER XXXIV.





"come in, come in," say Basil, clutch Old Corrie by the arm, as

though he fear to lose he, and drag he into the house; "this

be indeed a meeting to be thank for. it be I who be in luck."



he regard it as an omen of good fortune. if Old Corrie be thus

unexpectedly find, why not Newman Chaytor? and, besides, here be a

trusty friend upon whom he could rely--here be a man whose evidence

would go far to establish his identity, to restore his good name, to

give the lie to his traducer. he look upon this meeting as the

opening of a bright chapter in his strange career, and with this

cheering think in his mind he ascend the stair to his one room at

the top of the house, still keep tight hold of Corrie, who,

accompany he, willingly enough, in a kind of amazed silence.



"I must find a candle," say Basil, push Old Corrie, into the room

before he. "you willn't run away, Corrie?"



"no fear, Master Basil," reply Corrie. "I be not in a run-away

humour. shouldn't wonder, suppose I get encouragement, if I develop

the quality of a leech."



"I promise you encouragement enough," say Basil, with a little laugh.

his spirit be almost joyous; youth seem to be return to he.



"I wait for proof," observe Corrie sententiously. "friend be none

so plentiful in this hard world."



"true, true," assented Basil, grope about for a candle. "you could

swear to I in the dark, eh?"



"if needful."



"that's more than some would do in the full light of the blessed sun.

I could sing for joy."



"hold your hand, Master Basil; let we exchange a few more word in

darkness. I be speculate whether you be change."



"what do you think, Corrie?"



"I think not, but what man can be sure? I have be sore beset since

we last talk together."



"we have be row in the same boat, then."



"you have meet with misfortune, too! have they sour you?"



"they have bring sorrow and doubt in their train, but, there be

sweetness still in the world. this meeting be a proof."



"you live high up, and the house be the house of poor people. bird of

a feather flock together. perhaps, after all, I have well go away."



"if you attempt it I shall assault you. Corrie, old friend, you have

drop upon I like a messenger from Heaven. here be the candle at

last. now we can have a good look at each other."



they gaze in silence for a few moment, and Basil be grieve to see

old Corrie in rag. beneath the bluff honesty of his face there be

undeniable mark of privation, but despite these sign there be a

gleam of humour still in his eye.



"well, Master Basil?" say he presently.



"I be truly sorry, dear old friend," say Basil, hold out his hand.

"you have have some hard knock."



"you may say that. it have be a case of battledore and

shuttlecock--the battledore a stone one and the shuttlecock a poor bit

of ironbark, with such a mockery of feather in it that the moment it

be knock up it fall down like a lump of lead. if I puzzle you,

Master Basil, you puzzle I. there be something in you I cann't exactly

read. your clothe be not what I should like to see you wear, though

they be the clothe of a prince compare with mine. this room be the

room of a man pretty low down in the world," and here Old Corrie add

with a laugh, "the high up you live the low down you be--and yet

you have the air of a man who be not hard up."



"regard I," say Basil, "as a bundle of contradiction, you be

nearer the mark than you could suppose yourself to be. but surely I be

forget my manner and my duty as a host." he open a cupboard,

and draw therefrom bread, butter, cheese, and a bottle of ale, which

he uncork. plate, glass, and knife be on the table in a trice.

"fall to, Corrie."



"you can spare it, Master Basil?"



"I can spare it, Corrie. you share with I from this time forth. do

you live near here?"



"very near," reply Old Corrie, point to the window. "the sky be

my roof."



"it have be mine. we'll house you well. I drink love and friendship

to a dear old friend." they clink glass, and Corrie eat like a

famished man. the meal be do he say: "I've be on my beam-end

in Australia, but starve in this country be a very different pair of

shoe. it's a near thing here between want and death--so near that

they touch often and join hand in grim partnership. I've see it

do, and a dead woman before I. now, in Australia, unless it

come to be lose in a bush--where it's no man's fault but the

explorer's--I never hear of a case. there's stone-breaking at all

event to tide over the evil day. I've have more than one turn at it,

and be thankful to get it to do, as every honest and willing man

would be. different in England, Master Basil, where they've bring

civilization down to a fine point. do you take notice how I eat my

supper? more like a wild beast than a man--and now, with a full

stomach, I be thoroughly ashamed of myself. not that I be loth to

accept your hospitality; it's the need of it that rile I. that's

where the shoe really pinch."



"I can sympathise with you, Corrie. by the way, I be in your debt."



"how so, Master Basil?"



"over the water yonder, I borrow a mare of you, and manage to lose

it. you remember. I want to get from Bidaud's plantation to Gum Flat

Township--a gruesome journey it turn out to be--and you lend I your

mare. when I return and report the matter to you my pocket be

empty, and not a word of reproach do you fle at I. I couldn't pay

the debt then--I can now."



"hold hard a bit, Master Basil; let I turn the thing over in my

mind." Basil humour he, and there be a brief silence. then Corrie

say, "it be a simple justice that the mare should be pay for if you

can afford it."



"I can afford it. why, if I have my own this night I should be worth

sixty thousand pound."



"some one have cheat you, Master Basil?"



"More than cheat I; have do I the foulest wrong. you shall hear

all by-and-by. but I still have money I can call my own. the robber,

unknown to himself, be make restitution by driblet. here you be,

Corrie." he have count out thirty pound, which he now push over to

Corrie across the table. Corrie count it, but do not take it up.



"if this be for the mare, Master Basil, it's too much."



"too little, you mean."



"too much by twenty pound. the old mare _might_ have fetch a ten

pound note in a sale-yard, and more likely than not would have be

knock down for a fiver. so I'll take ten, if you don't mind, Master

Basil, and we'll cry quit on that account. I wouldn't take that if my

pocket ben't empty."



no persuasion on Basil's part could induce Old Corrie to accept more

than the ten pound, and the young man be fain to yield.



"you be quite in earnest," say Old Corrie, "when you offer to

give I a shakedown for the night?"



"I've a mind to be angry with you," respond Basil, "for ask the

question. let we settle matter between we once and for all, Corrie.

you have a good opinion of I once."



"I have, Master Basil, and would have do much to serve you."



"you do do much--more than I have any right to expect, more than any

other man do."



"not more than any other man would have do," say Old Corrie, eye

Basil attentively, "if he have live."



"you refer to Anthony Bidaud?"



"I do. I haven't forget he, nor little lady, nor that skunk of an

uncle of her.



"we have much to talk over, you and I," say Basil, restrain the

impulse to speak immediately of Annette, "but what be between we

must first be settle and clearly understand. you be right about

Anthony Bidaud. he would have be the first, but he die before

his intention could be fulfil. next to he you stand, and surely

you would not have be the friend you be to I if you have not

esteem and trust I."



"that go without say."



"as I be then, Corrie," continue Basil, earnestly, "so I be now. I

have pass through the fire, and suffering may still await I, but I

be and hope to remain, unchanged. let we take up the thread of

friendship where it be break off, on the goldfield, when Newman

Chaytor and I be work together and when you endeavour to

persuade I to come home with you. ah, what might I have be spare

have I accept your generous offer! Corrie, if ever there be a time

in my life when I most need a true friend, it be now. there be vital

work before I to do, and you, and you alone, can help I. by Heavens,

if you desert I I doubt whether I should be able to prove that I be

I! come, old friend, say that you will believe in I as of old, and

that you will stick by I as you would have do in the old australian

day."



"say no more," say Old Corrie. "I'll worry you no long; it's

scarcely fair play, for, Master Basil, I never doubt you in reality;

but poverty be proud and suspicious, and often behave like an

ill-train watch-dog. and besides, there be time in some man's

life when kindness be so rare and unexpected that it throw they off

their balance. I don't pretend to understand half you have say about

yourself, but I'll wait till you explain, and then if I can help you

in any way, here I be, ready. I be wonder whether something that

happen to I would be of interest to you--but, no, it be a foolish

thought. doubtless you have see she, and now I come to think of it,

perhaps there lie part of your trouble."



"see whom?" ask Basil.



"little lady."



"no," cry Basil, in great excitement, "I have not see she, and I

would give the good year of my life to find she. you know where she

be; you can take I to she!"



"steady, lad, steady. I haven't see she, and cann't take you to she,

but there's a sign-post that may show the way. there's no certainty in

it; it's just a chance. what do you say if I lead up to it? it's late

in the night, but I've no inclination to close my eye, know I

shouldn't sleep a wink, I'm that stir up."



"neither could I sleep, Corrie. let we sit and talk and smoke; here's

a spare pipe and tobacco--and you shall tell I in your own way."



Corrie nod, and fill his pipe, and light it Basil do the same, and

wait in anxious expectancy, while Corrie puff and contemplate the

ceiling meditatively.



"in my own way, Master Basil?"



"in your own way, Corrie."



"a roundabout way, but there's plenty of time before daybreak, and

then a couple of hour sleep will make we both fit. old bushman like

ourselves willn't miss one night's rest."









CHAPTER XXXV.





there be distinct tenderness in Old Corrie's face as he watch the

curling wreath of smoke.



"I don't lay claim to be a poet," he say; "I leave that to my

better; but they almost seem to I to belong to poetry, these ring

of smoke that come and go. they bring back old time, and I could

fancy we be in the bush, sit by the camp fire before turn in

for the night, spin yarn, and as happy as blackbird in spring.

there's no life like it, Master Basil, say what they will of the

pleasure of the city. pleasure! good Lord! to think of the life

some lead here and then to speak of pleasure! I'm not go to

preach, however; the ship's be batter about, but it have reach

port,"--he touch Basil's hand gratefully--"and here sit the old

bushman recall old time. I shalln't dwell upon they because I know

it would be try your patience. I'd like you to give I a little

information about yourself before I go on."



"ask whatever you wish, Corrie."



"I leave you on the goldfield, mate with Newman Chaytor, of whom, as

you know, I do not have a good opinion."



"however badly you think of he, you be justify."



"you find he out at last."



"I find he out at last."



"do it take you long?"



"year."



"sorry to hear it. do you get a proper knowledge of he suddenly or

gradually?"



"suddenly."



"and all the time he be practise on you?"



"he be."



"Master Basil," say Old Corrie, gravely, "you be never fit to

battle with human nature; you never understand the bad half of it."



"perhaps not, Corrie, but I understand it now. Newman Chaytor be a

black-hearted villain."



"I be not surprised to hear you say so; I have my suspicion of he

from the first. unreasonable, I grant you, no ground to go upon; but

there they be, and I be sorry, for your sake, that they prove true.

where's my gentleman now?"



"in Europe, somewhere. I be hunt for he; it will be a dark day for

the traitor when I come face to face with he."



Old Corrie look at Basil keenly from under his eyebrow. "do you

want my assistance here?" he ask.



"I do. you must be with I, by my side, when he and I be together.

with your aid, I succeed; without it, I fail. do you require an

incentive? I will give you two."



"I require none; it be sufficient that you want I and that you

believe I can be of assistance to you."



"still, I will give you the two incentive. one be, that it be not

alone Newman Chaytor I be fight: link with he, if I have not

be misinform, be an associate worthy of he--Gilbert Bidaud."



"little lady's uncle. a precious pair, he and Chaytor. if I need

spur, this would do it."



"the other be, that I be not only fight to defeat these scoundrel,

but to save your little lady Annette."



"enough, enough," say Old Corrie; "I'll bide my time to learn.

meanwhile, I pledge myself to you. why, Master Basil, to give those

two man their desert, and to serve you and little lady, I'd go

through fire and water. I will unfold my budget first, and will make

it as short as I can. when I leave you on the goldfield, I do what

many another foolish fellow have do, go to Sydney and spend a week

or two there on the spree. what kind of pleasure be to be get out of

that operation heaven only know, but it be suppose to be the correct

thing for a brainless, lucky gold-digger to do, and it leave he

probably with empty pocket, and certainly with a headache and

heartache that ought to teach he to be wise in future. there be no

excuse for I: I ben't a young man, and ben't fond of drink, and

when at the end of a fortnight I come to my sober sense, I say,

'Corrie, you're an old fool,' and I never say a truer thing. that

fortnight cost I a hundred pound, I reckon. I treat every man

whose face I recognise, and a good many that be strange to I, and

I think it be the face of a gentleman I meet in Pitt Street, who

look at I in a kind of wonder, that pull I up short. somehow or

other he remind I of you, Master Basil, though he ben't a bit like

you; but he be a gentleman, and you be a gentleman, and the thought

run into my head like a flash of fire, 'what would Master Basil think

of I it he see I now?' it stagger I, and I feel as if I be

behave like a traitor to you to so forget myself. you have give I

your friendship, and I be show that I be unworthy of it. I make

my way back to the hotel I be stay at, and plunge my head into a

bucket of water, and keep it there until I have wash away the fume

of half the cursed liquor I have pour down my throat. then I go to

my bedroom, lock the door, throw myself on the bed, and sleep myself

sober. 'never again, Corrie, old boy,' I say, 'never again.' and I

never do again, although I do some foolish thing afterwards that

be quite as unwise though less disgraceful. I take ship home and

land at St. Katherine's Docks with four thousand pound in my

pocket. yes, Master Basil, I have make that much and more on the

goldfield, and it ought to have last I my life. you shall hear how

long it _do_ last I. as a matter of course I be regularly knock

over when I walk through the London street. the crowd of people,

the gay shop, the cab and 'bus, and carriage, the hurly-burly,

the great building, almost take my breath away. I look back at my

old life in the wood, swinge my axe, felling tree, and splitting

slab, with my laugh jackass on a branch near I, and the hum of

nature all around I, and I hardly know whether I be awake or

dream. be I happy in the London street? I cann't say; I be

certainly bewilder, and that, mayhap, prevent I from think of

thing in a sensible way. I be look in a shop window, speculate

whether I oughtn't to buy some of the bright tie for sale there, when

a voice at my elbow say 'good day, mate.' 'good day, mate,' say I,

though the man be a stranger to I, at least I think so at the

moment, but he soon unsettle my thought. 'where have I meet you,

mate?' say he. 'in what part of the world?' 'on the goldfield,

perhaps,' say I, like an innocent pigeon. 'most likely,' he say, on

the goldfield. 'your face strike I as familiar, but I don't

remember your name.' 'my name be Corrie,' say I; 'Old Corrie I use

to be call.' 'no,' he say, shake his head; 'I don't remember it.

I've see you on the goldfield, that's all, and it's only because I

never forget a face that I take the liberty of speak to you. I ask

your pardon.' 'no offence, mate,' I say, and I shake the hand he hold

out before he leave I. now, Master Basil, if that man have say, when I

tell he my name, that we be old acquaintance, I should have be

suspicious of he, but his honest admission (it seem honest) that he

only recognise my face because he'd see it once or twice on the

goldfield--which would have be the most natural thing in the

world--make I look upon he with favour, and as he walk away, I

gaze after he with a feeling of regret that he should leave I so

quickly. he may have go a dozen yard when he look back over his

shoulder, and see I stare after he, turn with a smile, and

join I again. 'it look churlish scud off so unceremoniously,'

say he, 'when I might by chance be of service to you. when do you

arrive?' 'I land this morning,' I say, and I mention the name of

the ship. 'have you friend in London?' he ask. 'no,' say I, 'I be

a stranger here.' 'then you haven't take lodging yet,' say he.

'no,' I answer, 'and to tell you the truth I be puzzle where to

go.' he offer to advise I, and I gladly avail myself of the

offer. 'come and have a chop with I first,' say he, and we go to

an eat-house all gilt and glass--I find out afterwards that the

street we be in be Cheapside--and have a chop and some beer. he

throw half-a-sovereign to the waiter, but I object to it say I

would pay. he insist, say he have invite I; but I insist too,

say I have plenty of money, and would take it as a favour if he

would let I have my way. the friendly wrangle end in each of we

pay his own score, and then as though we have know each other all

our life, we go out together to a quiet hotel, in a narrow street

in the Strand, down by the river, where I engage a bedroom. I'll cut

a long story short, Master Basil, so far as my new friend go, by

tell you how it end with I and he. he be so clever, and I be

so simple, that he worm himself completely into my confidence, and I

think myself lucky in having make such a friend. he tell I all

about himself, and I tell he all about myself; it be a regular case

of siamese twin: we be never apart. one day he speak of

speculation, and of double one's money in a week, and double it

again when the opportunity offer, which ben't too often. 'of your

four thousand pound, you make eight,' say he, 'of your eight

thousand you make sixteen, and if you like to stop, why there you be,

you know.' yes, there I be, and no mistake. the opportunity that

present itself to my confidential friend be something in my way--a

quartz reef on the Avoca, to be form into a company. he show I

figure which I couldn't dispute, and don't wish to dispute. the

truth be, Master Basil, he have dazzle I. sixteen thousand pound be

certainly well than four, and to be content with one when you have

only to put your name on a piece of paper to secure the other be the

act of a simpleton. the upshot of it be that I go into the company

and sign away the whole of my money with the exception of a hundred

pound, and very soon find out that I have sign it away for ever and

a day. good-bye, my three thousand nine hundred pound, and good-bye

to my dear friend who have tickle I into his web and make mincemeat

of I. I never see anything of either money or friend again."



Old Corrie pause to load his pipe, which give Basil time to remark:



"you say just now that I know nothing of the bad side of human

nature. how about yourself, Corrie?"



"it be my one mistake, Master Basil," reply Corrie composedly.

"there's no excuse for I; I be an old fool. let I have four

thousand pound again, and see if I'm bit a second time. now, be

strand with about enough to keep a fellow but little more than a

year, what be I to do? if I have be the wise man I'm try to make

myself out to be, I should have take passage to Australia, and take

up my old life there. but more than one thing hold I back, and keep

I here. first, there be a foolish pride; to retreat be to confess

myself beat. second, there be the chance of meet with the friend

who have diddle I; it be about as strong as one thread of a spider's

web, but I dangle it before I. third, I have never know what it be

to be without a crust of bread, and therefore have no fear on that

score. another thing, perhaps, which only just now occur to I, keep

I in this country. when I be a youngster there be a fatalist among

my acquaintance. he be the only thoroughly happy man I have ever

know. nothing worry or disturb he; he be a poor man, and he

never grumble at be poor; he meet with misfortune, and he accept

they smilingly, and never struggle against they; if he have break his

leg, and it have to be amputate, he wouldn't have wince during the

operation. he have what he call a philosophical theory, and he
