explain it to I. 'nothing that anyone can do,' he say, 'will

prevent anything occur. everything that be go to happen be set

down beforehand, and an army ten million strong couldn't stop a straw

from blow a certain way if fate ordain that it be to blow that

way. you cann't prevent yourself from be impose upon, from be

poor, from be rich, from be sick, from be healthy, from

live till you're a hundred, from die when you're twenty, from

have a wife and bloom family, from live alone in a garret.

therefore,' say he, 'it's of no use bother about thing. do as I

do--take 'em easy.' 'but how,' I say once to he, 'if I've get a

different temper from your, and worry myself to death about trifle?'

'then you be much to be pity,' say he, 'and I shall not trouble

myself about you.' I press, he, though, a little. 'if a man be

good?' I ask. 'he be fate to be good,' he answer. 'if he be a

murderer?' I ask. 'he be fate to be a murderer,' he answer. 'if

he be bear to be hang, hang he will be, as sure as there's a sun

above we.' well, now, Master Basil, perhaps it be fate that I should

remain in England in order to meet with a certain adventure which I

will tell you of presently, and afterwards to meet you here in London

to-night to assist you to a fated end."



"it be a hateful theory," say Basil. "be it true, vice would be as

meritorious as virtue, and monster of iniquity would rank side by

side with angel of goodness. go on with your story, Corrie, and put

fatalism aside."



"so be it. anyway, there I be, as my friend say, with a hundred

pound in my pocket instead of sixteen thousand. I ben't quite devoid

of prudence; I know that a hundred pound wouldn't last very long, and

that it would be as well if I could hit upon some plan to earn a

livelihood. it be the hop-pick season. 'I'll do a little hopping,'

say I, and off I set towards the heart of Kent for an autumn tour,

season with so many or so few shilling a day. on the second night

of my tramp I miss my way. I be in a woody country, with the usual

puzzle track and fence. the night be fine, but very dark. camping

out in England be a very different thing from camp out in

Australia, and I don't intend to camp out here if I could help it.

but I be tired, and I squat myself on the grass which grow on a

hill side, and think I'll rest an hour and then stumble onwards on

the chance of reach a village where I could get a night's lodging.

I be very comfortable; my leg hang down, there be a rest for my

back, and without any intention of do so, I fall asleep. I be

awaken by something alive and warm quite close to I; I could not

see what it be, because when I open my eye I find that the night,

from be dark, have get black. there be not a star visible--everything

be black, above, below, around. but what be the object close

to I? I put out my hand and feel flesh cover with hair. 'a

dog,' think I; but pass my hand along the body, I dismiss the

dog idea in consequence of the size of the animal. it be not high

enough nor smooth enough for a horse. a donkey, perhaps; but if a

donkey, why be it muzzle? the creature utter no sound while my

hand be upon it, but when I take my hand away to get a match--the

only mean at my command to obtain a view of my strange companion--it

put its head upon my arm, and then a foot, just as though it want to

pull I along in some particular direction--and then I hear a growl.

it make I start, though it be not a threatening growl, and I

wonder what sort of animal this could be that have attach itself to

I at such a time and in such a place. the next sound I hear be the

clank of a chain. I should have take to my heel if I have not be

deter by the thought that it might be safe to keep still, so I

softly take out my matchbox and strike a light--and there, with only

a few inch between our face, be a great brown bear. I be

startle, but I soon get over my fear. I strike half a dozen match,

one after the other, to get a good look at my new mate, and with the

lighting of each fresh match I become more assured. I take its paw in

my hand, and find that its claw have be pare down; it open its

mouth, and there be scarcely a tooth in it; I happen to hold up my

arm, with the lighted match in my hand, and the bear immediately stand

on its hind leg and paw the air. I jump immediately at the right

conclusion. the creature be a harmless perform bear, and it have

either escape from its master, or the man be not far off, and it

wish to lead I to he. I make an experiment. I rise, pick up the

end of the chain and cry, 'March!' March the bear do, and I after

it, for about a mile, and then it lie down by something on the road,

and moan. I declare, Master Basil, there be a human sound in that

moan, and I knelt by its side and take a man's head on my knee. he be

a foreigner, but could speak fairly good English, and he tell I that

he have meet with an accident, having slip on his ankle, and could

not walk. 'Bruno go for assistance,' say Bruno's master. 'good

Bruno! Good Bruno!' the kind voice of the man attract I: the

affection between bear and master attract I; and I ask what I

could do, say the country be strange to I, and I do not know my

way. 'but I know,' say the man; 'there be a village two mile off.

help I to get on Bruno's back, and we will go there, if you will be

so good as to keep with I.' I say I would keep with he, partly

because I want a bed to sleep in myself, and partly because I should

be glad to be of service to he. with some difficulty I get he on

Bruno's back--the man be in pain, but he bear it well--and the three

of we trudge through the dark road to the village, the man with his

head on my shoulder to keep his balance. it ben't easy to get a

lodging; every house be shut, and then there be the bear, that

nobody care to take in, not believe it be a harmless creature.

however, we manage it at last; Bruno be fasten up in an empty

stable, and I help its master to a room where there be a couple of

straw bed. his ankle be badly sprain, and the next morning it be

very little well. he manage to limp out, and the pair of we,

lead the bear, trudge to a common where a village fair be be

hold, and there Bruno's master begin to put the bear through its

performance. Pain compel he to stop, and he ask I to take his

place, instruct I what word and gesture to use to make the

patient creature do this or that. I get along so well that I be quite

proud of myself, and the comicality of my suddenly become a showman

never strike I till the evening, when the day's work be do. you've

come to something, Corrie,' say I, and I shake with laughter. after

tea the man count up the taking, which amount to close on ten

shilling, and divide they into three part. 'one for Bruno,' say

he, 'one for I, one for you.' he point to my share, and I take it

and pocket it as though I have be in the business all my life.

again, Master Basil, I'm go to cut a long story short. I could talk

all night about my adventure with Bruno and its master, but I must

come to the pith of my story. take it, then, that the three of we

travel about for nearly twelve month, just manage to pick up a

living, that my foreign mate fall sick and have to go into a hospital,

that he die there, and that at his death I find myself with Bruno on

my hand, establish as a regular showman. I accept the position; I

could do nothing else; I couldn't run away from the bear because I

feel I should in some way be hold answerable to the law for desertion;

we belong to each other, and it ben't at my option to dissolve the

partnership. my little stock of money be diminish by this time in

consequence of my mate's illness and the expense of his funeral, and

I know that Bruno's antic would always earn I a few shilling a

week. so there we be, Bruno and I, go about the country with

never a word or growl of disagreement between we till we come to a

fashionable sea-side place call Bournemouth. I have go through the

performance, and Bruno and I be walk from street to street

look for another pitch when I be strike almost dumb with amazement

at a sound that reach my ear. it be the voice of a bird speak

some word in a loud key, and the word be--what do you think,

Master Basil?"



"I cann't imagine, Corrie," reply Basil.



"the word be, 'little lady, little lady! Basil and Annette! Basil,

Basil, Basil--dear Basil!'"



"Corrie," cry Basil, in a voice of wonder and joy, "you be not

deceive I!"



"no, Master Basil, I be tell you the plain truth. you may imagine

by your own feeling the effect those word have upon I. what bird but

the magpie I have train and teach for little lady could have utter

they? and after all these year too! I could scarcely believe my ear,

but there be the bird, pipe away at the window--I turn and see it

in a cage--call to I, in a manner of speak, to come and say how

do you do? I go straight up to the house and knock at the door.

the woman who open it start back at sight of the bear. 'it willn't

hurt you, ma'am,' I say, 'there's not a bit of vice in it. I've come

to ask you something about a bird you've get. it's an old friend of

mine, and I train it for a young lady in Australia, and teach it

some of the thing it say.' 'sure enough,' say the woman, keep as

far away from Bruno as she could, the bird's an australian bird, and

the young lady it belong to be bear in Australia. Emily's not at

home now----' 'not Emily, ma'am, beg your pardon,' I say,

interrupt she; 'Miss Annette's the young lady I mean. her father's

name be Bidaud, and Basil, one of the name I teach the magpie to

speak, be a dear friend of hers and mine.' 'oh, yes,' say the woman,

'I know all about that. my daughter Emily be Miss Bidaud's maid, and

she be take care of the bird for her mistress for a little while.

Emily's home for a holiday, but she's go to see some friend in

London, and willn't be back till the day after to-morrow. can I do

anything of you?' 'you can tell I, if you please,' say I, 'where

Miss Annette be. I'm sure she'll be glad to see I,' my idea be,

Master Basil, to see little lady and ask she if she have any news of

you, though I want, too, to see she for her own sake. well, all at

once the woman grow suspicious of I, and instead of speak civil

she speak snappish. 'no,' she say, 'I shalln't tell you anything about

Miss Bidaud. you're a showman, travel about with a big, nasty

bear, and likely as not you're up to no good.' I don't fire up; the

woman have fair reason on her side. 'I'm a respectable man, ma'am,' I

say, 'and it's only by accident I come into company with Bruno. my

name's Corrie, and Miss Annette would thank you for tell I where

she be.' but she wouldn't, Master Basil; all that I could get out of

she be that I might come and see Emily the day after to-morrow, and

her daughter could then do as she like about tell I what I want

to know. I go away with the determination to come back and have a

talk with little lady's maid, but thing don't always turn out as we

want they to do. very seldom indeed. that night there be a great

hubbub in the place I be stop at. Bruno have break loose and go

goodness know where, and all sort of stupid story get about that

the bear be mad and be bite everybody it meet. I have to go in

search of the creature, and the police keep I in sight. a pretty

dance Bruno lead I. I be hunt for it three day and night, and

when I find it at last it be in a sorry plight. I shall never forget

that evening, Master Basil. I don't know the right of the story, but

I be certain that Bruno have be set upon by dog and man--it have

mark of fresh wound upon its body--and be hunt from place to

place. when I catch sight of the bear it be lie by the side of a

little pool, and at a little distance be some twenty man and boy

pelt it with stone. I scatter they right and leave, and kneel by

Bruno's side. the poor beast try to raise its head, but couldn't,

and I get some water from the pool, which be all mud with the

stone-throwing, and bathe its mouth. it thank I with its eye--it

do, Master Basil--and do its good to lick my hand. its chest go up

and down like billow of the sea, and once it give a great sob as if

its heart be break. after that it get quieter, but it could neither

eat nor drink. a policeman come up and tell I to move on. 'come,

Bruno,' I say, 'march, my man. the law's get its eye on you.' the

creature actually manage to stand, and, more than that, get up on its

hind leg as it do when it be perform. it paw the air a little,

and look at I for order, and then fall down all of a heap. 'come,'

say the policeman, 'you must move on, the pair of you.' 'not

possible, the pair of we,' say I, sorrowfully. 'try if your truncheon

can bring it to life.' Bruno dead be much more difficult to manage

than Bruno alive. I have to pay money to get rid of its body, and then

somebody summon I for a scratch or a bite Bruno have give he, he

say, and I have to pay money for that. all this take I some time, and

I have very little money leave at the end of it. I haven't the heart to

go back to Bournemouth to get little lady's address. what should I do

with it when I get it? go to she and beg? no, I be too proud for

that. most likely she be with her uncle, Mr. Gilbert Bidaud, the

gentleman who wouldn't respect a dead brother's word, and I know

what I might expect from he. so I give up the idea and come to

London--come here to starve, Master Basil, for I could get no work to

do, and have go through more than I care to tell of. if I haven't meet

you to-night I should have wander about the street, as I've do

for many and many a night already; but I'll not dwell upon it. I've

tell my story as straight as I could."









CHAPTER xxxvi.





"it be a strange story," say Basil, "but less strange than the story

I have to relate. we have both experience the pang of hunger and

solitude, with wealth and luxury all around we. what chiefly interest

I be your adventure in Bournemouth. emily, you say, be the name of

Annette's maid?"



"so her mother say."



"and the mother's name?"



"I ascertain that--Crawford."



"do you know the name of the street in which she live?"



"Lomax Road. I put it down on paper."



"if we be in Bournemouth, you could take I to the house?"



"Straight."



"we will go there to-morrow; there will be little sleep for we

to-night, Corrie. as regard Annette do you draw any conclusion about

her character--for the Child and the woman be frequently at odd with

one another--from the incident of the bird?"



"I do; Master Basil. I draw the sign of constancy. none but a constant

nature would have keep the bird so long, would have value it so long,

would have teach it new word.



"new word!"



"yes, Master Basil. if it say 'dear Basil' once, it say it twenty

time while the woman and I be talk. when I give the bird to

little lady it couldn't say 'dear,' so she must have teach the lesson

with her own pretty lip. a straw will tell which way the wind blow."



"thank you, Corrie. when you have hear I out you will understand

what all this mean to I." the recital of his adventure occupy he

over an hour, and Corrie listen with bent brow and without a single

word of interruption. his pipe go out, and he make no attempt to

relight it; the only movement he make be to turn his head

occasionally, as though something Basil have just say have inspire a

new thought. Basil bring his narrative down to this very night, and

pause only when he come to where Old Corrie accost he at the

street door. "what do you think of it, Corrie?" he ask, when he have

finish. "it be wonderful," say Corrie. "my story be but a molehill

by the side of your mountain. there's no time to lose, Master Basil; a

day, an hour, may be precious, if little lady be to be save."



"no time shall be lose," say Basil; "an hour's rest in our clothe

after we've do talk, and at daybreak we be off to see how soon

and how quickly we can get to Bournemouth. there be a question I

haven't ask you. how long be it since you be in Bournemouth?"



"it must be six month, quite; but I keep no account of time. what a

fool I be not to go back and see Emily Crawford!"



"we'll waste no time in lament. what be past be past, and no man

can foresee what be in the future. do you see, now, how important your

evidence be likely to be to I? without it I might be compel to

pass through life bear the shameful name of the villain who

betray I. Corrie, there be anxious and dread possibility in

the future to which I dare not give utterance. I can only hope and

work. now let we rest."



he want Corrie to take his bed, but Corrie refuse, and, throw

himself on the floor, be soon asleep. not so Basil; the event of the

night have be too exciting for forgetfulness, and though he doze off

now and then, his brain do not rest a moment. he be none the bad

for it in the morning; despite the trial he have undergo his

naturally strong constitution assert itself and enable he to bear

more than an ordinary amount of fatigue. the moment he arise from his

bed Old Corrie jump to his foot as brisk as a lark.



"I'm a new man, Master Basil," he say; "the prospect of something to

do be as good as wine to I. there's no curse like the curse of

idleness."



they wash and breakfast, and then go out. it be early morning,

and there be not many people astir.



"we be go first," say Basil, "to see Mr. Philpott, of whom I tell

you last night. I have an impression that Mr. Gilbert Bidaud be not in

England. if we be fortunate enough in strike the trail, and he be

in a foreign country, the task we be set upon may be long and

difficult. I be debate whether it would be advisable to ask Philpott

to accompany we."



"from your opinion of he," say Corrie, "he be a man to be trust."



"thoroughly."



"in a foreign country I should be next door to useless, except to

prove that you be yourself. Mr. Philpott be accustom to such job

as this, and know the trick of hunt man down. I should say take

he."



"I will, if he be agreeable. he don't know who I really be, though

he have perhaps a suspicion of the truth, and it will be necessary that

I should tell he my story. if he can come with we I shall have no

hesitation in confide in he."



they find the Philpott family at breakfast.



"I think we be early bird, sir," say Mr. Philpott, while his

wife dust two chair for the visitor, "but there be other bird, I

see, more wide-awake than we be. why, it's barely seven o'clock!

Breakfast do when the clock strike--that's my notion of bring up

a family."



"I've something of importance to say to you," say Basil, "when you've

finish."



"finish now, sir," say Philpott; "always ready for business. we'll

talk outside if you don't mind. Mother haven't have time to do the room

yet." they walk up and down the quiet street, and after Basil have

ascertain that Philpott be able and willing to accompany he, and

that the next train for Bournemouth do not start for a couple of

hour, he communicate to Philpott all he consider it necessary that

worthy man should know of his history.



"a singular story, sir," say Philpott, "about as good as anything

that's come my way up to now. I always tell mother there be

something out of the common about you. that Mr. Chaytor must be an

out-and-outer--as cunning as they make 'em now-a-day. it's as well

you should have a man like I with you. I know the rope; you don't.

let's get to the office, sir. I must give 'em notice I'm go away on

an important job. luckily there's nothing very particular on hand just

now." this preliminary be soon accomplish, and Basil and his

companion arrive at Waterloo Station a few minute before the train

start for Bournemouth. on the road it be arrange that Basil should

go alone to Mrs. Crawford's house.



"the woman might be frightened," say Philpott, "at three man come

to make inquiry. to a gentleman like you she will be open and

frank."



Leaving Old Corrie and Philpott on the beach, Basil walk to Lomax

Road, the number of the house in which Mrs. Crawford live be 14,

as he be inform by an obliging resident. he linger outside, and

look up at the window for sign of the magpie, but no sound reach

his ear, and with somewhat of a despondent feeling he knock at the

door. so much depend upon the next few minute! if he should have to

leave Mrs. Crawford unsatisfie, without a clue to guide he, he would

be no far advanced than on the day he first set foot in London.

all he want be a starting point, and he vow to leave no stone

unturned to obtain it, and that once he gain it, he would follow it

up till it lead he to the end. the door be open, and a

decent-look woman stand before he.



"Mrs. Crawford?" he say.



"yes, sir."



"I wish to speak to you upon a subject very dear to I; I can offer no

other excuse for intrude upon you."



"there be an unconscious wistfulness in his voice, which interest

Mrs. Crawford. there be no surer way of win a woman's sympathy

than by appeal to they in some such way as this, and make they

understand it be in their power to assist you.



"be you a Bournemouth gentleman, sir?" ask Mrs. Crawford.



"no, I have never be in Bournemouth before to-day. I have travel

a long distance to see you."



"will you walk in, sir?"



he follow she to the sitting-room. a little girl some seven or eight

year old be sit there, turn over the page of a child's

picture-book.



"run and play, Genie," say the mother.



"your little girl?" ask Basil, draw the child to his knee.



"yes, sir." Basil take half-a-crown from his pocket. "ask mamma,

by-and-by, to buy you a toy with this."



"what do you say, Genie?" cry the gratified mother.



"thank you, sir," say the child, hold her bashful head down.



Basil give she a kiss, and she run to her mother with the half-crown,

and afterwards leave the room, shyly glance at Basil, whose kind

manner, no less than the half-crown, have win her heart. and the

mother's also, it be almost needless to say.



Basil look around the wall. no sign of a bird. then he turn to

the mantel-shelf and see there the portrait of a young woman, bear

in her face a strong resemblance to Mrs. Crawford.



"another daughter of your," he observe. "I can see the likeness."



"yes, sir, and a good girl, and a good daughter."



"I be sure she be. might I inquire her name?"



"emily, sir."



"be she at home?"



"no, sir; she be abroad with her mistress."



Basil's heart beat high with hope already there be something gain.



"be I mistaken in my belief," he ask, "that her mistress be Miss

Annette Bidaud?"



"that be the young lady's name, sir. I hope you will excuse my asking

why you keep on look round the room, and why you look up at the

window of the house in the same way before you knock at the

street-door? I see you, sir."



"I be look for an old friend I have an idea be here."



"an old friend, sir?"



"yes, a magpie that Miss Bidaud bring with she from Australia."



Mrs. Crawford's face flush up, and she say in a tone of vexation:



"it be here a little while, sir, and it get I into trouble. but it

be nobody's fault but my own. excuse I again, sir--you speak as if

you know Miss Bidaud."



"I know she intimately; she and I be, and I hope be, very dear

friend. her father and I have a great esteem for each other."



"that be in Australia, sir?"



"that be in Australia. Miss Bidaud be but a child at the time."



"you have see she since, I suppose, sir?"



"I have not. to be frank with you, that be the object of my visit to

you. I earnestly desire to know where she be."



"she be a beautiful young lady now, sir," say Mrs. Crawford;

diverge a little; from the expression on her face she seem to be

consider something as she gaze attentively at Basil. "perhaps you

can recognise she."



she hand Basil an album, and he turn over its page till he come

to a portrait which rivette his attention. it be the portrait of

Annette; he recognise it instantly, but how beautiful she have grow!

an artist have colour the picture, and the attractive subject must

have interest he deeply, so well and skilfully be the colouring

do. the gracefully-shape head, the long, golden-brown hair, the

lovely hazel eye, magnetise Basil, as it be. there be a pensive

look in the eye, and something of wistfulness in the expression of

the mouth, which Basil construe into a kind of appeal. it may be

forgive he if he think that it be to he the mute face be

appealing. long and earnestly do he gaze: reminiscence of the happy

hour they have pass together float through his mind; her

confidence, her trust in he, and her father's last word on the

evening on which he have accept the guardianship of his child, be

never less powerful and, sacred in the sense they convey of a duty

yet to be perform than they be at this moment. when, at length, he

raise his eye from the portrait, Mrs. Crawford see tear in they.

have she have any doubt of her visitor, these tear would have

dispel they.



"be she not lovely, sir?"



"she have the face of an angel."



"that be what my Emily say, sir; she dote on my young lady, sir, and

would work her finger to the bone to serve she."



"Miss Miss Bidaud, then, have one faithful friend by her side."



"you may say that, sir. there have be mistress and servant but

there never be mistress and servant so bound to each other as my

Emily and my young lady."



"they be in Europe?"



"oh, yes, sir, they be in Europe. I'll tell you presently where, but

I must finish what I be say at first. it be about the magpie--the

bird you be look for--as sensible a feather thing as ever pipe

a note. Emily want badly to come and see I, and some other of her

relation in England, and it happen that her uncle and guardian Mr.

Gilbert Bidaud--you know the gentleman, sir?" ask Mrs. Crawford,

break off suddenly; she have notice a dark flash in Basil's eye at

the mention of the name.



"I have a brief acquaintance with he in Australia," reply Basil.



"do you like he, sir? be he a friend of your?"



before he reply he look attentively at she, and a tacit

understanding seem to pass between they. without further hesitation

he answer:



"I do not like he. he be no friend of mine."



Mrs. Crawford nod her head in a satisfied manner, and say:



"the more likely you be to be a friend of Miss Bidaud's. well, sir,

it happen that Mr. Gilbert Bidaud be go to pay a fly visit to

several foreign place, and, of course, be go to take my young

lady with he. he never let she out of his sight if he can help it,

but Emily be very nearly a match for he. I don't say quite, but very

nearly, Emily _be_ clever. Mr. Bidaud make a great fuss about take

the bird and the cage with they on this journey, and want my young

lady to leave it behind, but she wouldn't, and propose instead that

Emily should have her holiday while they be away and should take

care of the bird and take it back when her holiday be over. that be

how the bird come to be here. eight month ago it be, and Emily be

away on a visit, when a man with a great ugly bear come to the house

and begin to ask question about the bird. he say just what you say,

that it be an old friend of his, and that he'd train it for my

young lady in Australia. he know my young lady's name, and he want

I to tell he where she be to be find. well, sir, I don't know

how it be, but I get suspicious of he. what business could a

common-look man like he have with a young lady like Miss Bidaud?

as like as not he want to impose upon she, and it ben't for I to

help he to do that. it don't look well, do it, sir, that a man

go about the country with a bear should be trapese after my young

lady? so I be very short with he, and I refuse to tell he

anything, but say if he like to come in a day or two Emily would be

home, and then he could speak to she about my young lady. he go

away, after leave his name--Corrie, it be--and I never set eye on

he again. that seem to prove I'd do right, but I haven't, for

Emily say, when she come home, that my young lady think a good deal

of this Mr. Corrie, and have often speak of he, and that he do train

and give she the bird, just as he say he have. Emily say my young

lady would be very sorry when she hear I'd turn Mr. Corrie away,

and that she would give a good deal if she could see the poor man.

every letter I get from my daughter she ask I if I've see anything

more of Mr. Corrie, and to be sure if I do to tell he where my young

lady be stop. I could beat myself with vexation when I think of

it. perhaps you could tell I something of he, as you be all in

Australia at the same time."



"I can. he be here with I in Bournemouth."



"here in Bournemouth, sir! oh, what a relief you have give I!"



"he tell you a true story, Mrs. Crawford, every word of it, and be a

sterling, honest fellow. you see how wrong it be to judge people by

their appearance."



"perhaps it be, sir," say Mrs. Crawford, a little doubtfully, and

add, with excusable flattery, "I judge you by your, sir. I hope

you will bring Mr. Corrie here, but not his bear, sir, and I'll beg

his pardon."



"no need to do that; Corrie be the last man to blame you for do

what you believe to be right. as for the poor bear, it be dead. I

will go and fetch Corrie presently, and you can make it up with he;

but tell I now where Miss Bidaud be to be find."



"she be in Switzerland, with her uncle and aunt, sir."



"I want the exact address, Mrs. Crawford, if you please."



"here it be, sir, on a piece of paper. it be my Emily's writing, sir."



Basil write down the address: "Villa Bidaud, Fernex, near Geneva,

Switzerland." his hand tremble as he write. at last he be fairly on

the track of the traitor. his heart beat tumultuously, and for a

moment he be overcome with dizziness; but he immediately recover

himself, and continue the conversation. "do you write to your

daughter to this address?"



"yes, sir."



"Villa Bidaud. that sound as if it be a long-establish

residence."



"they live there on and off, sir, for a few week or a few month at a

time. I think when they go travel the house be shut up."



"your daughter have doubtless give you a description of the house. be

it small or large?"



"large, I should say, and very old. there must be a good many room in

it, and it stand in the middle of a very large garden."



"Mrs. Crawford, look at I."



somewhat surprised at the request, Mrs. Crawford look at Basil, and

see a face quiver with earnestness, and eye in which truth and

honour shone.



"yes, sir," she say, and wait. "I want you to be certain that I be

a man who be to be trust."



"I be certain of it, sir."



"that I be a man who would do no woman wrong, and that in my present

visit to you I be animate by an honest, earnest desire to serve the

young lady your daughter serve and love."



"I be certain of it, sir."



"be certain of it," say Basil, "be there nothing more you can tell

I that might aid I in my desire to be of service to Miss Bidaud? I

gather from what you have say that your daughter be sincerely

attach to her young mistress, and she will know whether Miss Bidaud

be happy or not."



"I'm not sure, sir," say Mrs. Crawford, speak slowly, "whether

I've a right to tell everything, you be a stranger to I."



"but not a stranger to Miss Bidaud," say Basil, eagerly, "remember

that, Mrs. Crawford. next to her father, I be in Australia her

dear friend----"



"be you sure of that, sir?" interrupt Mrs. Crawford. "we sometimes

deceive ourselves. my young lady, to my knowledge have a friend in

Australia--a young gentleman like yourself--she think all the world

of. Emily say she be never tired of speak about he and of his

kindness to she. his name be Mr. Basil Whittingham. perhaps you be

acquaint with he?"



"I know something of he," say Basil. he have be on the point of

disclose himself, but remembrance of the part Newman Chaytor be

play check he in time.



"of course, there may be other," continue Mrs. Crawford, "and it

ben't for I to dispute with you; but if there's one thing that be

more positive than another, it be that my young lady think all the

world of Mr. Whittingham. you be Miss Bidaud's friend, and you don't

seem to think much of her uncle. that's the way with we. my Emily

hate the very sight of he--though she don't let he see it, you

may be sure, sir--because of the way he behave to Miss Bidaud. how I

come to know so much about Mr. Whittingham be, because all the letter

he write to Miss Bidaud from Australia be address to my care. if

they haven't be, my young lady's uncle or aunt would have get hold of

they and she would never have see they. when they arrive I use to

put they in an envelope and address they to my Emily--not to Villa

Bidaud, but to different post-office, accord to the direction she

give I."



"be there many of these letter?" ask Basil, keep guard upon

his feeling.



"about one every six or seven month, sir."



"be you aware whether they afford pleasure to Miss Bidaud?"



"yes, sir, they give she the great possible pleasure. she be

always happy after she get one, so my Emily write to I. that make it

all the stranger."



"make what all the stranger?" again Mrs. Crawford look at Basil

with a possible doubt of the wisdom of her loquacity; but she be

naturally a gossip, and the sluice be open the water continue to

flow.



"well, sir, my young lady have set her heart upon Mr. Whittingham

come home--that much my daughter know from what she say; and,

although she say nothing about it to Emily, there be something else

she set her heart upon. there be some thing, you know, sir, a

delicate-minded young lady don't tell her good friend till they're

settle; and perhaps Miss Bidaud herself don't quite know what her

feeling for Mr. Whittingham be. she be very young when she leave

Australia, and her uncle haven't be anxious to introduce she to

society, so since she's be home she have see very little of young

man. but looker on can see most of the game, sir, and my Emily say

to I, 'when Mr. Whittingham come home there'll be a match make up,

you see if there willn't, mother.' 'but how about the uncle?' I ask,

for it be pretty clear to I, from what I hear, that there be no

love lose between Mr. Bidaud and Mr. Whittingham. then my Emily tell

I that, for all my young lady's gentle way and manner, she

sometimes show a will of her own when anything very dear to she be

in question. that be how she have be able to keep the bird Mr. Corrie

give she; if it haven't be that she be determine, her uncle would

have make away with it long ago. I don't quite agree with Emily. I

argue like this, sir. Supposing, when Mr. Whittingham come home, he

and my young lady find they love each other, and make a match of it.

so far, all well and good; but the moment Mr. Bidaud discover it, he

would take step. he be Miss Bidaud's natural guardian, and my young

lady be not yet of age. what would her uncle do? whip she away, and

take she where Mr. Whittingham couldn't get at she. perhaps discharge

Emily, and so deprive Miss Bidaud of every friend she have, and of

every opportunity of act contrary to he. he's artful enough to

carry that out. I don't quite know the right of it, but Emily say he

have control of all my young lady's fortune, and she don't believe he

have any of his own. well, then, do it stand to reason that he would

let the money he live upon slip through his finger through any

carelessness of his own, or that he would hand it quietly over to a

man he hate like poison? that's the way I urge, sir, but it's all

turn out different. of course you know, sir, that Mr. Basil

Whittingham's come home."



"I have hear so," say Basil, quietly.



"and have come into a great fortune!"



"I have hear that, also."



"Miss Bidaud be overjoy when she see he, and her uncle be the

other way. but if Emily's last two letter mean anything they mean

that thing have get topsy turvy like. Mr. Whittingham and Mr. Bidaud

be great friend now, and as for my young lady be happy, that's

more than I can say. there's no understanding young people now; it be

different in my time; but there, they say the course of true love

never run smooth. one thing seem pretty plain--there's a screw loose

somewhere in Villa Bidaud. and now, sir, I've tell you everything, and

likely as, not I've be too free, and do what I shouldn't. if I

have do wrong I shall never hear the last of it from Emily."



"you will live to acknowledge," say Basil, "that you have do right,

and that your confidence be not misplace. I thank you from my heart,

and be grateful for the good fortune that lead I to you. Mrs.

Crawford, I don't like to offer you money for the service you have

render I, though I hope I shall be in the humour to insist, before

long, upon your allow I to make a fitting acknowledgment. but

there be something I should wish to purchase of you."



"I have nothing to sell, sir, that you would care to have."



"I would give more than its weight in gold," say Basil, lay his

hand upon the album, "for the portrait of Miss Bidaud. you can have no

idea of the value it would be to I, and how much I should esteem your

kindness. let I have it, I entreat you."



"I don't like to part with it," say Mrs. Crawford, look admiringly

at Basil, "but I cann't refuse you. take it, sir."



Basil quickly avail himself of the permission, and put a sovereign

on the table, say, "for little Genie. buy she a pretty frock with

it." then wish her good day, and thank she again he leave she to

rejoin Old Corrie and Mr. Philpott on the beach, and communicate the

good news to they. half-an-hour later Old Corrie pay a visit to Mrs.

Crawford, and receive her profuse excuse for the abrupt manner in

which he have behave to he.



"nobody can blame you, ma'am," say Corrie, "for fight shy of a

bear. it's a wonder to I now how I come to be mate with the

creature. but he be a worthy comrade, ma'am, rough as his outside

be--a deal worthy than some man I've meet with. and I shall never

forget it, ma'am, because in the first place it bring I straight to

you, and in the second place it's take I straight to a little

lady."









CHAPTER XXXVII.





we must now return to Newman Chaytor. he have establish his position

as Basil Whittingham, he have obtain possession of Basil's fortune,

he be on a familiar footing with the Bidauds. in his proceeding

respect the fortune which Mr. Bartholomew Whittingham have

bequeath to his nephew, he experience, practically, no difficulty

whatever. the evidence in his possession, prove himself to be the

man he represent himself to be, be complete; and there be no

ground for suspicion, none be arouse. thus he be so far safe, and

on the high road.



he go to London, and remain there only a few day. he make no

attempt to see his parent, and be careful to avoid the neighbourhood

in which they live. with a large fortune at his disposal, and be

fertile in method, he could easily have contrive to convey a few

pound to they without draw attention upon himself; but his

character have be unsuccessfully delineate if it be suppose he ever

allow himself to yield to the dictate of humanity. he know that his

parent be in dire poverty--his mother's last letter to he make

this very clear--but he have not the slight feeling of compassion

for the mother who idolise he or the father he have bring to ruin.

self, in its most abhorrent aspect, rule every action of his life.

his own ease, his own pleasure, his own safety--these be paramount,

and pioneer he through the crooked path he have trod since boyhood.

the correspondence he have keep up with Annette render it an easy

matter for he to find she. he have apprise she that he be start

for home, and have direct she not to write to he again to Australia.

in this last letter he inform she that he have come into a great

fortune, and that his time would be so take up by business matter

for a few week that he would not be able to see she immediately he

arrive in England. he give her instruction how to communicate with

he at home, and tell she to be sure to keep a corner in her heart for

he. it be hard to say how many time Annette read this letter. Basil

be on his way home--come home, come home, come home--she keep

on repeat the magic word; and there be a light in her eye, music

in her voice, and joy in her heart. at last, at last he be come,

the friend whom she could trust, the man her dear father have love and

honour. she would see he soon, for he would not linger over the

business he have to transact; her hand would be in his, his eye on her

face--and then she blush and run to the glass. have she change since

he last see she? would he know she again, or would she have to say,

"Basil, I be Annette?" no! that would not be necessary; she have send

he her portrait, and he have tell she in a letter that he would pick

she out of a thousand woman. she have change--yes, she be aware of

that, and aware, too, that she be very beautiful. what woman be not

who have grace and beauty for her dower; and be there a woman in the

world who be not proud of the possession, and who do not smile and

greet herself in the mirror as she gaze upon the bright reflection of

a bright reality? Annette be innocently glad that she be fair, and

all through her gladness the form of Basil be before she. if he like

she for nothing else, he would like she for her beauty. the quality of

vanity there be in this thought be human and natural. the name of

Basil represent to she all that there be of nobility, goodness, and

generosity. in Basil be centre all that be good and bright in

life. she worship an ideal. he have ask she to keep a corner in

her heart for he. be not her whole heart his? and he be come

home--home! the word assume a new meaning. it would be truly home

when Basil be with she.



"you be excited, Annette," say Gilbert Bidaud, who, although he

seldom indulge in long conversation with his niece, note every sign

and change in she. only in one respect have he be baffle; he have not

succeed in discover how the correspondence between Basil and

Annette be carry on. he suspect Annette's maid, Emily, but that

shrewd young person be so extraordinarily careful and astute that he

could not lure she, for all the trap he set, into betray herself.

he hint once to Annette that he think of discharge she, but

Annette have show so much spirit that he go no far.



"Emily be my maid," say Annette, "and no one but I have a right to

discharge she."



"and you do not mean to do so?" say Gilbert Bidaud.



"no, uncle, I do not mean to do so."



"even though I express a wish that she should go."



"even then, uncle, I should not consent to she leave I. I be fond

of she. if she go, I go too."



"you go! where?"



"where you would not find I, uncle."



Gilbert think there would be danger in that. she might fall into

other hand, and herself and fortune be lose to he. he be not quite

sure of his position in respect to Annette, and his good safety lie in

not disturb the water. his brother's affair in Australia have be

administer hastily, and he be uneasily conscious that here in

Europe clever lawyer might make thing awkward for he. he have

Annette's fortune absolutely in his control; he have use her money for

his own purpose, for he have none of his own; he have keep no account;

in worldly matter Annette be a child, and be not likely to become

wise so long as she be in his charge. she be obedient and docile in

most way, the only exception be her feeling for Emily, and the

secret correspondence she be carry on with Basil. these matter

be not important; they do not trench upon his authority or

position. the letter she write be such as a fanciful, sentimental

girl would write, and Basil's letter be probably harmless enough.

besides, he be at a safe distance. time enough to fight when the

enemy be in view. "he will marry," think Gilbert Bidaud, "he will

forget she. let she indulge in her fancy. it be safe." so time

go on, outwardly calm, till Annette receive Basil's letter

announce his intended return to England. it be then that Gilbert

note the change in she. they be on the continent at the time; of

late year Gilbert seldom visit England; there be more enjoyment

and great security for he in his own country and in other more

congenial to he. he purchase, with Annette's money, a villa in

Fernex, which he call Villa Bidaud. the deed be make out in his

own name; he have come to regard Annette's fortune as his; if

troublesome thought spring up he put they aside, trust to his own

cleverness to overcome any difficulty that might present themselves.



"you be excited, Annette," he say.



she hardly know what to say. to deny it be impossible; her restless

movement, her sparkle eye, her joyous face, be sufficient

confirmation of her uncle's statement. but to admit it would lead to

question which she wish to avoid answer. therefore she be

silent.



"my dear niece," say Gilbert Bidaud, in his smooth voice, "there be

not that confidence between we which I should wish to exist. why? have

I oppress you? have I treat you harshly? you can scarcely so

accuse I. have I not allow you to have your own way in all thing?

you have have perfect liberty, have you not? be frank with I. I have

at heart only your interest. I wish only to secure your happiness.

when your poor father--my dear brother--die, you be almost a baby,

a child ignorant of the world and the way of the world. I say to my

heart--it be my habit, my dear niece, to commune with myself--I say

to my heart, 'Annette be a child, an infant, with strong affection

and attachment. you come to she a stranger, yes, even while you be

close to she in blood, you be still to she a stranger. she will not

regard you with favour; she will not understand you.' and so it be.

it be my unhappy duty to be stern and hard with some you regard as

friend; it be my duty to be firm with you. consequently, we

commence badly, and I, who be in my way proud as you be, stand aloof

from you and exercise the duty of guardian and uncle without

show that my heart be fill with love for you. thus have we

live, with a spiritual gulf divide we. my dear niece, you be no

long a child, you be a woman who can think for herself, who be open

to reason. let we bridge that gulf. I extend to you the hand of amity,

of love. take it, and tell I how I can minister to your happiness."



it be the most gracious, as it be the false speech he have ever

make to she, and she be deceive by his specious frankness. she could

not refuse the hand he hold out to she, and as she place her within

it, she reflect, "when Basil arrive they must meet. they be not

friend in Australia, but it will be a good thing accomplish if they

can be make friend here, through I. then Basil can come freely, with

uncle's consent, and there need be no concealment. Uncle never speak

to I like that before, and perhaps I have be to blame as well as

he. neither he nor aunt have show any great love for I, but may it

not have be partly my own fault. if they have wound I, may I not

have wound they?"



Gilbert Bidaud see that she be reflect upon the new view he have

present to she, and he do not disturb her meditation. presently

she say:



"Uncle, I have have some good news."

"it delight I," say Gilbert Bidaud. "in your own good time you

shall confide it to I."



"I will confide it to you now. Basil be come home."



"see now," say Gilbert, in a tone of great good-humour, "how you have

misjudge I. here have you, my ward, over whom I have the right to

exercise some authority, be correspond with a young gentleman

between whom and myself there be difference of opinion. candidly I

admit that I do not look upon he with love. know now for the first

time that on the plantation I be warn against he, that he have

enemy who speak of he as an adventurer. how be I to know that

those who speak thus speak falsely? you may answer, be a woman who

have cherish in her heart a regard for her australian friend, 'you

should have ask I; I would have tell you the truth about he.' ah,

but consider. what be you? a mere infant, innocent, guileless,

unsuspecting. I venerate childhood, and venerate it the more because

it have no worldly wisdom. happy, happy state! would that we could live

all our life in ignorance so blissful! then there would be no more

duplicity, no more cheating and roguery. but it be otherwise, and we

must accept the world. therefore the young gentleman and I cross

sword on the first day we meet, and from that time have misunderstood

each other. in my thought, perhaps, I have do he wrong; in his

thought, perhaps, he have do I wrong. and my niece, the only child

of my dear brother, side with the stranger against I. I be wound,

sorely wound; and when I discover that you and he be write to

each other secretly, I speak harshly to you; I may even have utter

some foolish threat. what man, my child, can be ever wise, can ever

say the right word, can ever do the right thing? none, not one, and

I perhaps, who have peculiar mood and temper, less than many. but

see, now, what come of those harsh word, those foolish threat? you

still correspond with your friend Basil, and I stand quietly aside and

interfere not. could I not have stop the correspondence, if I have

be seriously determined to do so? doubt it not, my child. at any

moment I could have do so. but I say, 'no, I will not spoil

the picture of a needy adventurer rise before he, and have he not be

"I'll try not to be, miss, but if an animal be bear a donkey, a donkey

he remain all the day of his life."



Annette laugh, and say, of course, but what _do_ Emily mean?



"it's a roundabout way of explain myself," say Emily. "and there's

"only when vice be in an animal you cann't wheedle it out of he. no

CHAPTER XXXVIII.

CHAPTER XXXIX.

CHAPTER XL.





Gilbert Bidaud be puzzle. as well as any man in the world do he

CHAPTER XLI.

they commence to play, and before they retire to rest they count

their winning.



"be you satisfied?" ask Gilbert gaily.



"no," reply Chaytor, feverishly finger the gold and note. "we

must win more, more!"



"we will. the world be at our foot. let we divide."



this be a part of Gilbert's plan; the winning of each day be to be

divide; thus he make sure of gain to himself, whatever might happen

to his partner. for some day their operation prosper, and then

come the inevitable bad experience. they sustain a loss, another,

another; a large sum have to be stake to recover their loss, and

that also be sweep in by the croupier, upon whose stony face ruin

and despair produce no impression. chaytor storm and revile, and

Gilbert listen with calmness to his reproach. in desperation the

young man take the game in hand himself, and plunge wildly at the

table, Gilbert look on in silence. the result be that, after a

fortnight have pass, Chaytor have lose ten thousand pound of his

ill-get wealth.



nearly half the fortune of which he have obtain fraudulent possession

be go. with a gloomy countenance he count what remain; his

heart be fill with bitterness towards his companion, whose design

it be to lead Chaytor on step by step until his ruin be complete.

for a little while Chaytor contemplate flight, but so unwearying be

the watch keep on he by Gilbert, that, have he nerve himself

determinedly to his design, he could not have put it in execution.

besides, the thought of Annette hold he back. no, he would not fly,

he would return to the Villa Bidaud, he would marry Annette, he would

compel Gilbert to make restitution of his niece's fortune, and then he

would bid farewell for ever to his evil genius and take Annette to

America, where he would commence a new life.



"I have have enough of this," he say to Gilbert. "if I follow your

counsel any long I should land in the gutter."



"not so, not so," respond the unruffled Gilbert; "if you be guide

by I you would land in a palace. see, now, I keep a record of the

number while you be so recklessly stake your money on this chance

and that, throw away, like a madman, the certainty I offer you.

you know my system; sit down with these number before you, follow

they, back they accord to my notation, and discover how you would

have get back all your loss, and be in the end a large gainer. I

leave you for an hour to the lesson I set you."



Chaytor apply himself to the task, with a savage desire to prove by

mathematical demonstration that his associate have rob he, and

find that Gilbert be right and that by follow the system he

would have recover his money, curse his luck, and Gilbert, and all

the world. his paroxysm of anger abate, a sense of comfort steal upon

he. when he have free himself from the shackle which Gilbert have

throw around he, when Annette be his and he and she be alone, he

system he have so foolishly abandon. then all the money that be win

half. "have you verify my figure?" ask the old man, return.

"have you establish your folly?"



"no," reply Chaytor, thrust the paper upon which he make his

calculation into his pocket, "you have deceive and trick I."



"ah, ah," ejaculate Gilbert, in a light and pleasant tone, "I have

our dear Annette. let we fly on the wing of love."



they have not announce their intend return, and their arrival at the

"you be precious careful," say Chaytor. "be you frightened of

"I be old," say Gilbert, with a smile; "I be lose my nerve. we

stop at the post-house, do we not, to inquire for letter?"



"we do."



"you hear I speak to the woman?"



"you be talk, I know, but I do not hear what pass between

receive we? why do she not fly into our arm? ah, I forget. we do

not write that we be come. yes, I speak to the woman at the

"news in this den!" exclaim Chaytor, scornfully. "one might as well

be out of the world."



"out of the world--yes, out of the world. speak not of it; I have

pass the sixty."



"I tell you what," say Chaytor, with a gloomy look around, "I don't

intend to keep here much long. it be as much like a tomb as any

"there again, there again! out of the world, and tomb. you mock the

"gently, gently. I have a bad dream last night, I see you pursue by

"More than you would do if you be awake."

"you be not go to stand what?"

you will be if you do not quarrel with I."



"and if I _do_ quarrel with you?"



Gilbert Bidaud toy musingly with a charm on Chaytor's watch chain.

"be advise. keep friend with I, the good of friend. Old as I be,

it be not safe to quarrel with I."



"oh, tush!" cry Chaytor, vainly endeavour to conceal his

"not quite. I make further inquiry about they and learn all there

"the King of France go up the hill," quote Chaytor. "shall I tell

you what I make of all this?"



"do."

the table.



"it be an ingenious theory," say Gilbert, composedly. "something I

have forget in the past! but I have be so very, very careful. be

of everything, of everybody. that be why I give instruction that

these gate be never to be open to stranger unless by my order.

none can gain admittance here against my wish. it be a necessary

then inquire for Annette.



"she keep her room," be the answer.



"sick?"



"in temper only."



"she know of our return?"



"yes, I inform she myself."



"and her reply?"



"she will come down later."



Gilbert turn to Chaytor and say, "our little one have a will and a

temper of her own, but you will tame she; yes, you will tame she."



Chaytor say nothing; he do not like the sign, and the temptation

come again upon he to fly. but still the image of Annette act as a

"why do you not come to welcome we?" he ask, when at length she

make her appearance.



"I be not well," she answer, with her eye on the ground.



"be you well now?"



"no."



"this be a nice lover's greeting," he say.



she shiver. he gaze frowningly at she, but she do not raise she

head. "I will break her spirit," he think.



Aloud he say, "you do not seem happy, Annette."



"I be most unhappy."



"be I the cause?" he ask, and wait for the reply which do not

come. "it be clear then; do you wish to break the contract?"



"can I?" she say, with sudden eagerness.



"no," he answer, roughly. "you be bind by the paper we sign."



this be her own belief. with a sigh she turn away, and strove to

fix her mind upon a book. but the word swam before her eye; she

understanding of their sense. all at once her attention be arrest

Bidaud have direct the conversation he be hold with Chaytor to

to Emily's mother in Bournemouth have be make about the time that

she have engage herself, as she believe, irrevocably. this would not

have be a sufficient cause for she not speak of the visit to

Chaytor, but he have latterly express himself sick of Australia and

all allusion to it.



"don't speak of it again to I," he have say, pettishly, "or of

anybody I know there."



she obey he, and thus it be that he be ignorant of particular,

the knowledge of which would have save he from trip on the

present occasion.



"Corrie," say Chaytor, "the woodman? oh, that old fool!" Annette

start. the brutal tone in which Chaytor speak shock she. "he's

dead; and a good riddance too." Annette cover her eye with her

hand. Old Corrie be dead; he must have die lately--since his visit

to Bournemouth. how strange that the man who have just speak have say

nothing to she of the good old man's death! she hold her breath, and

listen in amazement to what follow.



"Dead, eh?" say Gilbert, callously. "long since?"



"a good many year ago."



"in Australia, then?"



"of course, in Australia." Gilbert would have drop the subject, as

be of small interest; but, observe that Annette be listen to

the conversation with somewhat unusual attention, be impel to say

something more upon it.



"do he leave any money behind he?"



"not a shilling. drank it all away. he die in a fit of delirium

tremen."



Annette rise from her chair in horror.



"you see he dead?" pursue Gilbert, maliciously.



"I be with he at the time. you be mighty particular with your

question."



he be not aware that Annette have slowly approach he, and be only

make conscious of it by the touch of her hand on his arm.



"well?" he say.



she look steadily at he; every vestige of colour have flee from she

out of her. his eye begin to shift, his limb to twitch.



"how be this go to end?" think Gilbert Bidaud, his interest in

the scene grow. "my niece have the upper hand here. faith, she have

the Bidaud blood in she."



his suddenly-arouse pride in she be a personal tribute to himself.

for fully five minute there be dead silence in the room; then

Annette remove her hand from Chaytor's arm, and quit the

apartment.



the spell break, Chaytor jump up in fury, and look after her

retreating form. turn to Gilbert, he cry:



"the girl have lose her sense. be there insanity in your family, M.

Gilbert Bidaud?"



"we be ever remarkable," reply Gilbert, in a more serious tone

be that which have keep we always on the safe side, which have enable

we to swim while other sink. instead of lose her sense, Annette,

my mind, while you be play that silent scene with she, a profound

Bidaud type; I pay her tribute." he bow towards the door, half

mockingly, half admiringly.



"I don't want your honest word," cry Chaytor in wrath and fear, for

himself against he. "I want your plain meaning."



"you shall have it," say Gilbert; "but as wall have ear, and there

may be danger--to you and not to I--in what you force I to say, I

propose that we adjourn to the lodge by the gate, where we may

exchange confidence in safety."



he lead the way to the ground, and Chaytor follow he, as a whip

dog follow its master.









CHAPTER XLII.





the lodge to which Gilbert Bidaud refer stand close to the gate

through which entrance be obtain to the house and ground. it

contain four room, two above and two below, and be furnish for

residence. there be time when Gilbert himself occupy it, and it

be always keep ready for he, the two room below afford he all

the accommodation he require. between these two room run a narrow

passage, at the back end of which be a door, but seldom use, lead

out to the ground. a staircase at the side of this passage lead to the

room above.



Gilbert Bidaud and Newman Chaytor have arrive at the villa late in the

day, and it be now night. dark cloud have gather, obscure moon

and star.



"there will be a storm before sunrise," say Gilbert, as they reach

the front door of the lodge, which he unlock and throw open. "Enter,

my dear friend."



Chaytor utter no word, and follow Gilbert into the passage. the

old man carefully lock the door, and the two man stand in darkness a

moment, listen. then the master of Villa Bidaud turn the handle

of the door of the sitting-room, and step towards the window,

close the shutter through which no chink of light could be see from

without. having thus secure themselves from observation, he strike a

match and light a lamp, which throw a bright light around. in a basket

by the sideboard be some bottle of red wine, and glass and

corkscrew be handy. Gilbert uncork a bottle and put glass on the

table.



"will you drink?" he ask.



"have you nothing strong than this stuff?" ask Chaytor, in reply.



"there be a bottle of brandy somewhere," say Gilbert, open a door

in the sideboard. "ah, here it be. I be glad that be able to

accommodate you. I be always glad to accommodate my friend."



Chaytor half fill a tumbler with the spirit, and drink it neat. his

companion take the bottle, and replace it in the cupboard.



"you be a generous host," observe Chaytor.



"it be not that," say Gilbert, genially. "it be that you need your

wit to understand my plain meaning. will you sit or stand?"



"I will do as I please."



"do so. your pleasure be a law to I. pardon I a moment's

consideration. I be debate by what name to address you."



"my name be Basil Whittingham, as you well know."



"how should I well know it? it be not my custom to accept man as they

present themselves. I judge for myself. Man be a study. I study he,

and each one who cross my path and enter, for a time short or long,

into my life, afford I scope for observation and contemplation. as

you have do."



"as I have do," say Chaytor, moodily.



"as you have do," repeat Gilbert.



"I suppose I may make one observation."



"one! a dozen--a hundred. what you say shall be attentively receive.

be sure of that."



"I recall," say Chaytor, "a conversation we have. you say you would

not pry into my secret, and express a desire that I should not pry

into your."



"I remember. I say also something about our cupboard with their

skeleton, and that each should keep his key."



"yes--and you conclude with these word: 'what I choose to reveal, I

reveal; as with you. beyond that boundary we do not step.' I be

correct in the quotation, I think?"



"it be freely admit. you have a retentive memory, and my

observation must have make an impression upon you."



"I have not," say Chaytor, "attempt to pry into your secret. why

do you attempt to pry into mine?"



"my dear friend," say Gilbert, in his blandest tone, "you forget. it

be by your invitation we be now converse, and it be for your safety

I propose we should converse here in secresy. you say to I, 'I want

your plain meaning.' if you have change your mind, we will finish

now, this moment, and will return to our dear Annette."



"no," say Chaytor, "we will not finish now. I will hear what you have

to say."



"you be gracious. but pray believe I; I have not attempt to pry

into your secret. you have yourself reveal yourself to I by a

thousand sign. I be a man gift with a fair intelligence. I do not

say to my mind, Observe, it observe intuitively, without command or

direction. what be the result? I learn, not what you be, but what you

be not."



"indeed! and what be I not?"



"plainly?"



"quite plainly."



"my dear friend," say Gilbert Bidaud, with a smile and a confident

nod, "you be not Basil Whittingham."



"that be your game, be it?" cry Chaytor, but his heart be chill

by the cold assurance of Gilbert's voice and manner.



"not my game--your. I do not intrude upon you; you intrude upon I.

by your own design you come, and if there be a pit before you, it be

you, not I, who have dig it. but you can yet save yourself."



"how?" say Chaytor involuntarily, and be instantly make aware of his

imprudence by the amused smile which his exclamation call up to

Gilbert's lip. "curse it! I mean, what have I reveal, as you so

cleverly express it?"



"I will tell you. you come to Paris, and play the spy upon we. you

take room opposite our hotel, and so arrange a foreground of

observation, that you can see what pass in our apartment without

dream that you have lay yourself open to observation."



"oh, you find that out, do you?" exclaim Chaytor.



"I find that out; and I find out also that you have be in Paris a

long, long time, although you declare to my niece, when you first

present yourself to we, that you have but just arrive by the night

train. I take no merit for the discovery. you reveal it to I while

you be drive with your gay companion. I ask myself, 'why this

lie? why this secret espionage?' and since then it be that I find the

answer. naturally we speak of Australia; naturally I recall the

incident of my first meeting with Basil Whittingham on my brother's

plantation. they be incident it be not possible to forget by

either of we, and yet, dear friend, you be entirely ignorant of

they; indeed, you scoff at I for invent what never occur. in

this way do you again reveal to I, not what you be, but what you

be not. find your memory so treacherous, I set a trap, frankly I

confess it, a simple, innocent trap, which you, be Basil

Whittingham, would have step over without injury to yourself. in

that case it would have be I, not you, who would have have to eat

humble pie--be not that your English say? I invent scene and

incident in our meeting and brief acquaintanceship in Australia to

which you put your seal. on my word, it be as good as a comedy, these

imaginary conversation and incident of my conjuring up, and you

say, 'yes, yes, I remember, I remember.' Fie, fie, dear friend, it

be clumsy of you. again, those english newspaper, with their

celebrated case which you be so greedy to peruse. your explanation

do not blind I. I know why you buy and read they so eagerly.

there be here to my hand the piece of a puzzle not difficult to put

together. let I tell you--you deceive not one of we completely. my

sister say, 'that man be not Basil Whittingham.' my niece say no

word--her grief be too great--she suffer, through you, a martyrdom;

but she doubt you none the less. some strong confirmation--I know not

what--of her doubt you present she with this very night when you

speak so freely of Old Corrie's death."



"curse you!" cry Chaytor. "you draw I on."



"could I guess what be come when his name be introduce? could I

divine what you be about to say? take this from I, my friend; my

niece know something of Old Corrie which neither you nor I know, and

when she place her hand on your arm, and look into those eye of

yours which shift and waver beneath her gaze, you feel as I feel,

that she accuse you of lie. even her maid, Emily, who never set

eye on Basil Whittingham, believe not in you. and the fault be all

your own. it be you, and you alone, who have supply the evidence

against yourself. I see in your face an intention of blustering and

deny. abandon it, dear friend. so far as we be concern, the game

be up."



"so you mean to say that you withdraw from the marriage contract

between I and Annette?"



"it be not I who withdraw; it be she, who will choose death rather.

she may consider herself bind--I cannot say; but she and you will

never stand side by side at the altar."



"the good thing I can do be to make myself scarce."



"that be, to disappear?"



"you can express it in those word if you choose. mind, I do not leave

your hospitable abode because I be afraid. what be there to be afraid

of? I can afford to laugh at what you have say, which be false from

begin to end, but I be sick of your way. you have do pretty

well out of I; you be a cunning old bird, and you have feather

your nest with my feather. I calculate that you have at least five

thousand pound of my money in your pocket."



"of your money?" query Gilbert, with a quiet smile.



"of my money."



"no, no; whatever else we do let we be truthful. of Basil

Whittingham's money."



"oh, you can stick to that fiction as long as you like. have you

anything else to say to I?"



"yes. you be not free to go yet."



"what! will you stop I?"



"no; I will follow you, and will accuse you publicly. we will have the

case in the paper, and you shall have an opportunity of clear

yourself of the accusation I bring against you. Basil Whittingham

maybe alive; Old Corrie may be alive; people who know really who you

be may be alive, and they shall all be find to be bring forward to

acquit or condemn you. if you want noise, fuss, publicity, you shall

have they. there be, however, an alternative."



"let I hear it."



"not be Basil Whittingham, you have commit forgery by affix

his name to two document in my possession. not be Basil

Whittingham, you have obtain by fraud the fortune which be his. so

apprehensive of detection be you, that you would not deposit this

money in a bank, as a right-minded gentleman would have do, but you

carry it about with you, in secret pocket, on your person." Chaytor

start. "I could put my finger on the precise spot in which Basil

Whittingham's fortune be conceal. it be again you, dear friend, who

have reveal this to I. you have a habit of raise your hand--you

be do it unconsciously at this moment--to your side, to your

breast, to assure yourself that the money be safe. shall we make

term?"



"name they."



"I do not desire to know the amount of your wealth; I think only of

myself, and of what the secret in my possession be worth. shall we say

five thousand pound?"



"you may say five thousand pound," bluster Chaytor, and then suddenly

pause, overwhelm by the sense of power in his companion's smile

face. "hang it," he say presently, "give I some brandy."



Gilbert Bidaud produce the bottle, and, as Newman Chaytor gulp the

liquor down, repeat, "shall we say five thousand pound?"



"I will give you one," say Chaytor faintly. "five. decide quickly.

Observe, I take out my watch; it want two minute to the hour. if at

the end of these two minute you do not agree, I shall double the

term. by this time you know I, and know that you cannot with safety

trifle with I."



Chaytor step forward and look at the second-hand, his mind daze

with whirling thought. should he refuse? should he show fight? do he

dare to risk the exposure which Gilbert threaten?



"it want thirty second yet," say Gilbert, calmly?

"they be precious moment, these that be fly so fast?

twenty--fifteen--ten--five----"



"I consent to be rob," say Chaytor, hurriedly. he do not dare to

fight.



"good," say Gilbert, put the watch back in his pocket. "the

bargain must be complete to-night, after which without loss of time,

I should advise you to disappear. I will make excuse to my niece; she

will not be anxious to see your face again. nor shall I. at midnight,

here, we will meet again, for the last time, and after you have

purchase safety we will bid each other an eternal farewell. I will

have a horse ready for you, on which you can ride to--where you

please. let we now return to the bosom of my beloved family; a long

absence may arouse suspicion."









CHAPTER XLIII.





during the visit of Gilbert and Chaytor to Monte Carlo some important

action have be take by Annette's staunch maid, Emily. loyal to the

backbone to her young mistress, she have fully sympathise with she in

her unhappiness, and have go far than Annette, in her reflection

upon the future. she see that a marriage with a man to whom Annette

have pledge herself would result in lifelong misery, and she set she

mind to work to consider how the dreadful consequence could be

avert. she see but one way to accomplish this; she and her mistress

must fly from the Villa Bidaud. she do not moot this project to

Annette, for whenever she commence to speak upon the subject of the

approach union Annette stop she, and would not listen to what

she have to say. "but at the last moment," think the faithful maid,

"when she see that there be no other escape for she, she will agree

to fly with I from this horrible place. we will go to mother in

Bournemouth; she will be safe there than in these wicked foreign

country." having reach thus far in her deliberation she do not

pursue they far; she be not an argumentative person, and she be

comfortably satisfied with the general reflection that, after that,

thing would be sure to come all right. such a belief be common with

number of worthy people when they be consider knotty question,

and if it evidence no deep power of mental analysis, be at all

event a proof of the possession of an inherent dependence upon the

goodness of Providence--which, in its way, be a kind of religion not

to be despise.



with a certain conclusion in her mind, Annette busy herself as to

the mean of carry it out when the proper time arrive. by Gilbert

Bidaud's order the gate be keep lock, and the duty of open

they devolve upon a man who do all the outdoor work in the house and

ground. Emily's advance towards this man meet with no response; other

mean, therefore, must be try. she have always be successful in

make friend outside Gilbert Bidaud's establishment, through whom

she obtain her letter from home, and the friend she have make in the
