the post-house. it be a matter easily arrange. Annette be a liberal

mistress, and Emily be a save girl; a judicious system of small

bribe effect all that Emily desire in this respect. twice or

thrice every week she visit the post-mistress to enquire for

letter, and these visit be make in the night, the dark hour

be choose. the gate be lock she could not get out that way,

and she seek another mode of egress. she find it in the lodge in

which Gilbert Bidaud and Newman Chaytor hold their conference. there

be a secure lock on the front door, of which Gilbert, or his sister,

keep the key, but the lock on the back door be frail, and emily

discover how to manage it, so that she could get in and out of the

lodge without any person be the wiser. once inside the lodge Emily

would creep up the stair to the first floor, the window of the back

room of which almost touch the stone wall which run round the

ground. this wall be some seven foot in height, but there be

dilapidation in it which serve for foot-hole, and by mean of these

luckily-form step the courageous girl be enable to pass to and

fro and make the desire visit to the post-mistress. of course there

be the danger of discovery, but Emily be a girl in a thousand, and

the extraordinary care she take in these enterprise be a fair

guarantee of safety. the lonely situation of the house assist she;

there be night when, for hour together, not a human being

traverse the narrow road into which the front gate open.



on the night of the secret interview between Gilbert and Chaytor,

Emily have plan a visit to the post-mistress. she make her way into

the lodge unobserve, creep up the stair in the dark, and be about

to open the back window, when her attention be arrest by a sound

below, which, as she afterwards describe, send her heart into her

mouth. it be the sound of the unlocking of the front door. Emily's

heart go rub-a-dub with the fear that she be discover, but as the

slow minute pass without anything occur, her fear lessen, and

she become sufficiently compose to give attention to the

circumstance. softly open the door which lead to the staircase, she

hear voice in a room below which she recognise as those of Gilbert

Bidaud and the man who call himself Basil Whittingham. what have they

come there to say? why could they not have speak in the house? they

must be hatch some plot against her young mistress. at all hazard,

she would try to hear what they be say to each other. quietly,

very quietly, she descend the stair, set her foot down with the

great care, and pause between each step. a cat could not have

trod more noiselessly than she. at length she reach the door beyond

which the conversation be take place, and crouch down she

apply her eye to the keyhole. there be the two man, one with a

smile on his face, the other, dark and sinister; and Emily observe

that they be not stand side by side, but that a broad table be

between they. this precaution have be take by Gilbert, who be quite

prepared for any sudden attempt at violence on Chaytor's part.



Emily be too late to hear all that be say, but she hear enough.

have she not exercise control over her feeling she would have

scream with mingled joy and horror; as it be, the tear run down

her face as fast as she wipe they away, for she want to see as much

as she could. the brave girl thank God that a fortunate conjuncture

have make she a witness of the interview between the two villain. now,

certainly, her dear mistress be save, and she the instrument to

avert the misery with which she be threaten; for it be not alone

the project marriage which be break Annette's heart, but the

loss of faith in the purity and nobility of Basil's nature. emily

wait very nearly to the end; she see Gilbert take out his watch and

count the moment, she hear the bargain agree to and the second

interview at midnight plan, and then, just in time, she creep up

the stair as softly as she have creep down, and wait in the room

above until the two man leave the lodge.



what now should she do? return to the house, and acquaint Annette with

what she have hear, or go to the post-mistress to see if there be a

letter for she? if she go straight to Annette she might not have

another opportunity of get out that night; besides, she expect a

letter from her mother, and be anxious for it. she decide to go

first to the post-mistress; Annette know that she would be away for

some little while, and have say, "I shall wait up for you, Emily."



she throw open the window, and climb on to the wall, and down into

the road. it be very dark, and as Gilbert Bidaud have prognosticate,

a storm be gather, but Emily know her way well to the post-office,

and be not afraid of darkness. so she speed along under the wave

branch and over black shadow till she arrive at her destination.

once on her way she be startle; she think she see something more

substantial than shadow move by the road side, but after pause to

look and listen her alarm subside; all be quiet and still.



there be no light in the post-house, which be little well than a

cottage, but Emily do not expect to see one. she tap at the

shutter, and a woman's voice from within ask if that be "Miss

Emily." the girl answer in the affirmative, a woman appear at the

door and bade her enter.



"have you a letter for I?" say Emily.



"yes," the woman reply, "she have a letter for she," and produce it.



"why," cry Emily, "this be not from England?" no, say the woman, it

be not from England, and explain that a gentleman have visit she

in the evening, and have make enquiry concern the Villa Bidaud and

its inmate. hear that Miss Annette Bidaud be there, he have then

inquire for the young lady's maid, mention she by name, Miss Emily

Crawford. the gentleman ask if the post-mistress be likely to see

the girl, and whether she could convey a letter to she secretly that

night or early in the morning. the post-mistress say she could not

promise to do so that night, but she would endeavour to convey the

letter in the morning, and add that it be not unlikely Miss Emily

would come before they to inquire for letter. "if she do," say the

gentleman, "give she this, and ask she to read it here, before she

go back to the villa. it be a letter of the utmost importance, and

it must fall into no other hand than Miss Emily's." the post-mistress

conclude by say that the gentleman have pay she well for the

service, and that she be sure there be something very particular in

the letter.



emily, although burn with impatience, listen quietly to the tale,

hold the letter tight in her hand all the time, and when the woman

have do speaking ask only one question.



"be the gentleman an Englishman?"



"yes," reply the woman; "he be an Englishman."



then Emily open the letter, and read:





"my dear Miss Emily Crawford,--The writer of this be Old Corrie, Miss

Annette's sincere and faithful friend. he have see your mother in

Bournemouth, and have come here post haste to defeat a plot to ruin

your dear young mistress's happiness. he have a gentleman with he

little lady will be glad to see. if you get this letter to-night,

don't be frightened if Old Corrie speak to you as you go back to the

Villa Bidaud. not an hour should be lose to unmask the villain and

secure little lady's happiness. you be a brave, good girl. if you

don't get this letter till the morning, come at once to the back of

the school-house, where you will see little lady's true friend,



"Old Corrie."





the letter have be compose partly by Basil and partly by Old Corrie,

who have write it himself. Emily's eye sparkle as she read. she

bid the post-mistress good-night, thank she for the letter, say it

contain good news, and go away with a heart as light as a bird's.

so light, indeed, that she carol softly to herself as she step

very, very slowly along the dark, narrow road, and the word she

carol be:



"I be Emily Crawford, and I have get your letter. where be you, dear

Old Corrie, dear Old Corrie, dear Old Corrie?" the song could not have

be put into line that would scan, but blither, happy word with

true poetry in they, be never sing by human voice.



"where be you, dear Old Corrie, dear Old Corrie, dear Old Corrie?"

sing the girl, and pause and listen, and go on again, singe.



"here I be," say a kindly voice, "and God bless you for a true

heart!"



"stop a moment, please," say the girl; who now that the reality be

close by her side, could not help feel startled. "be you sure you

be Old Corrie, my dear mistress's friend from Australia? the

gentleman with a bear, you know?"



"you do well to doubt," say Old Corrie, "with what be go on around

you in this outlandish country. I be the man I say. stand still while

I strike a light, so that you can see I. we have a bull's-eye lantern

with we. be little lady well?"



"her heart be break," say Emily. "but I have good news for she

before she sleep to-night."



"and so have we, my dear, if you can get we to she."



"let I hold the lantern, Mr. Corrie, say Emily.



"no, my dear, you might drop it; there be a surprise in store for you

and for everyone in the villa yonder with its stone wall. there, the

lamp's alight, and you can see my face, dark as the night be. do you

think you can trust I?"



"yes, I do, and it be only out of curiosity I want to look at you."

and then Emily cry, "oh!"



"what be it, my dear?" ask Old Corrie.



"there be another," say Emily, gasp.



"there be two other; we have come prepare."



he whisper something in her ear which cause she to cry "oh!" more

than once, and to clap her hand in wonderment.



"may I see he?" she ask in a whisper.



the answer be give by Basil himself, who come forward and take she

by the hand, while the light, direct by Old Corrie, shine upon his

face.



"it be wonderful, wonderful!" she exclaim, and add under her

breath, "but I think I should have know."



in the expression of which opinion she pay a high tribute to her

judgment than she could have rightly claim for it; but this, at such

a time and in such circumstance, be a small matter.



Mr. Philpott, who have be stand silently in the rear, now join

the party.



"don't be frightened, my dear," say Old Corrie; "there be no more of

we. what we've get to do now be to decide what be to be do, how be

it to be do, and when be it to be do."



"first," interpose Mr. Philpott, to whom, by tacit consent, the

command have be give, "Miss Emily will perhaps give we an

explanation of certain word she speak a minute ago. be we quite

private here, Miss Emily?"



"it's hardly likely," reply Emily, "that a live soul will pass

along this road till daybreak."



"so much the well. you say just now that Miss Bidaud's heart be

break, but that you have good news for she before she go to sleep

to-night. do you mean by that that our arrival here be the good

news?"



"no, I mean something very different, something that you ought to

know before you decide what to do."



"I think as much. well, let we hear it, my girl."



Thereupon Emily relate all that she have overhear between Gilbert

Bidaud and Newman Chaytor. it be difficult for Basil to curb his

excitement, and whenever an indignant exclamation pass his lip,

Emily pause in sympathy, but he be too sensible of the value of time

to frequently interrupt she, and as she speak quickly, her tale do

not occupy many minute.



"this story," say Mr. Philpott, with a beam face, "decide what be

to be do, and how and when. the road be prepare for we by the

villain themselves. it be a bold move I be about to suggest, but to

adopt half-and-half measure with these scoundrel would be

ridiculous."



Basil and Old Corrie say they be prepare for any move, however

bold and daring, and be only too eager to undertake it.



"we mustn't be to eager," say Mr. Philpott; "cool and steady be our

watchword. now, Miss Emily, can you get we into the ground of the

villa to-night?"



"if I can get in," say the girl, "you can get in."



"and one of we into the lodge where the scoundrel be to meet at

midnight?"



"yes," say Emily, unhesitatingly.



"you be a girl after my own heart," say Mr. Philpott, admiringly.

"there be a risk, you know, and you will have a share in it. it

wouldn't be right for I to deceive you."



"I don't mind the risk," say the courageous girl. "I want to help to

save my dear young lady from these wretch and monster."



"God bless you, Emily," say Basil, press her hand, and Emily feel

that she need no other reward.



Mr. Philpott then describe his plan. guide by Emily, they be all

to get into the ground, when their force be to be thus dispose

of: Basil and Old Corrie be to hide in the ground as close as

possible to the back door of the lodge; they be not to move or

speak; Emily be to return to the house, and impart to Annette all

that she know, and in this way prepare she for what be to follow;

both Annette and her maid be to be ready to come from the house to

the lodge upon a give signal; Mr. Philpott be to conceal himself in

one of the upper room of the lodge, and no movement whatever be to

be make until he blow loudly upon a policeman's whistle. the moment

this signal be give, Basil and Old Corrie be to enter the lodge

through the back door, which Emily would leave unlocked, but properly

closed, so as to excite no suspicion in the mind of Gilbert Bidaud

and Newman Chaytor--and proceed at once to the low room, in which

these man be locate; and Annette and Emily be to leave the house

and come immediately to the lodge.



"all this," say Mr. Philpott aside to Basil, "be not exactly lawful,

and if Mr. Bidaud and Mr. Chaytor have right on their side we should

get into trouble. but we have the whip hand of they and be safe. I

anticipate very little difficulty, only neither of our man must be

allow to escape until we have settle with they."



the party proceed to the villa, Emily walk a little ahead with

Basil, to whom she impart how matter stand with her young mistress.



"her heart be truly break," say the girl, "and she could never

have live through it, never! but she will soon be her dear, bright

self again. all, sir, she be the sweetest lady that ever draw

breath--and O, how these wretch have make her suffer! but there be

happiness come to she. I could sing for joy, indeed I could, sir!"









CHAPTER XLIV.





all be still in house and ground and lodge. the dark cloud be

grow black, but the storm have not yet burst. a clock in the hall

strike twelve, and, as if the chime have call they forth, Gilbert

and Chaytor issue from the house, and walk to their rendezvous.

each man be occupy with his own special thought, and each keep a

wary eye upon the other's shadowed form.



"I leave the door of the lodge open," say Gilbert. "Enter."



"after you," say Chaytor.



"pardon I," say Gilbert, "after you."



chaytor laugh and step into the passage. Gilbert follow,

pause to light a small lamp he carry in his hand. upon enter

the room he light the large lamp on the table, on one side of which he

place himself, Chaytor be on the other.



"you seem to be afraid of I," say Chaytor.



"I do not trust you," respond Gilbert.



"there be small temptation for trustfulness between such man as we,"

say Chaytor. Gilbert nod quietly. "well, you have your game, and

have win a pretty large stake. cann't you be satisfied with what you

have get?"



"you know my term; the time for discuss they have go by."



"but there be something forget. you make I sign two document,

and you have speak of forgery."



"you be correct. the production of these document with the name of

Basil Whittingham attach to they in your handwriting would be

sufficient to convict you."



"for that reason I do not choose to leave they in your possession. if

I pay you the five thousand pound you be rob I of you will have

to give they up."



"they be here," say Gilbert, produce they, "and will be useless to

I when you be go. you can have they and welcome when the money be

pay. you go to-night."



"I go to-night, and hope never to set eye upon you or yours again."



"my dear friend," say Gilbert, with a courteous bow, "the hope be

reciprocal. let we not prolong this interview. open your bank and

purchase freedom."



Chaytor unbutton his waistcoat, and from an inner pocket extract

two bundle of bank note. Gilbert hold out his hand.



"no, no, old fox," say Chaytor. "there be three time five thousand

pound here." he look at Gilbert savagely.



"if," say the old man, laugh lightly, "by a wish you could burn I

to ashe where I stand, you would breathe that wish willingly."



"most willingly."



"but why? I be deal tenderly, mercifully by you. in right and

justice this money belong not to you. it belong to Basil

Whittingham. if he be here he could take possession of it, and

neither you nor I would care to gainsay he. it be, therefore, as

much mine as your, I let you off lightly by demand so small a sum.

come, let we finish the comedy; it be time the curtain fall. count out

the price of liberty, the price of my silence, and let we take an

affectionate farewell of each other."



"be you sure we be alone?"



"do you think I would reveal our conspiracy to a third person? in my

pleasant house every human being be asleep; they dream not of the

grief which will fill their heart to-morrow when they learn that you

have depart."



"give I the paper I have sign. here be your share of the robbery.

you have well count it to make sure."



as Gilbert bend over the table to count the note, Chaytor, with a

swift movement, draw a heavy life-preserver from his breast, and aim

a murderous blow at the old man's head. but Gilbert be too quick for

he; he have but one eye on the money he be finger, the other be

furtively watch his companion. he dart back, and so escape the

blow; the weapon descend upon the table, and this shock and the

violent movement of the man overturn the lamp, the light of which

be instantly extinguish. each man have but one hand disengage,

chaytor hold the life-preserver and Gilbert a pistol, which he have

bring with he as a protection against treachery. the moment the

room be in darkness the two disengage hand grope over the table

for the money, and be fiercely clasp. and now a surprising

incident occur. upon these two hand a third hand be lay, and

before they could free themselves be handcuff together.

simultaneously with this startling and secure manacling of their hand

the pistol be knock from Gilbert's grasp and the life-preserver

from Chaytor's; and then a shrill whistle pierce the air and drive

the blood from the cheek of the conspirator. hurried sound of step

resound through the passage.



"this way!" cry Mr. Philpott. "the door be open. strike a light."



but a light come from another quarter. a vivid flash of lightning

illuminate the apartment, and in that flash Newman Chaytor beheld the

form of Basil Whittingham, whose death he believe he have compass on

the gold field across the sea. his face grow livid, a heavy groan

escape his lip, and his head fall forward on the table.



"see if you can relight one of the lamp," say Mr. Philpott.



both the lamp be soon light, the glass of only one having be

break. then Gilbert Bidaud, who have utter no word during this

succession of startling incident, see two man whose face be

strange to he, and one whose face he recognise. manacle as he be

to his insensible partner in crime, and unable to release himself, he

instantly regain his self-possession.



"if I mistake not," he say, in a tone of exceed urbanity, "Mr.

Basil Whittingham, whose acquaintance I have the pleasure of make on

my brother's plantation in Australia. I suspect from the first that

this log lie here be an impostor. it be but a sorry welcome I be

able to give you, in consequence of the unlawful proceeding of a

ruffian"--he glance at Mr. Philpott--"who shall answer for the

assault in a court of law."



"do not say one word to he, sir," interpose Mr. Philpott, see

that Basil be about to speak; "leave he to I; I know how to deal

with such cattle. I promise to tame he before I have do with he."



"it will be well for you to bear in mind," say Gilbert, still

address Basil, "that this be my house, and that you be trespass

illegally upon my property. however, for the sake of old time, and

for the sake of my niece, I be agreeable to waive that, and come to an

amicable settlement with you."



"he speak very good English for a foreigner," say Mr. Philpott,

"and, I'll wager, understand the law as well as we do. I be an

officer of the law"--(Mr. Philpott be satisfied that he be quite

safe in indulge in this fiction)--"and I tell he plainly that he as

lay himself open to a criminal action for conspiracy."



"shall I not have the pleasure," say Gilbert to Basil, ignore Mr.

Philpott, "of hear what you have to say in response to the flag of

peace I hold out?"



"he be a shrewd customer, sir," say Mr. Philpott, "and if this flag

of peace mean absolute and unconditional surrender I be ready to

consider it. it may interest he to learn that we be in possession of

all the particular of the interview which take place between he and

the insensible party he be fasten to, and of the bargain they make

to share your money. that tickle he, I see, but it be only one out

of a handful of trump we happen to hold. I will take care of these

notes"--he gather they up--"and we will go into account later on."



"unless my ear deceive I," say Gilbert, "I hear the voice of my

niece's maid in the passage. doubtless my niece accompany she. do

you think it seemly that she shall be a witness of this scene?"



"Corrie," say Basil, "take one of the lamp, and keep Miss Bidaud

outside; I will come to she immediately. allow I, Mr. Philpott; it

will shorten matter if I say a word." he address Gilbert Bidaud.

"you and your confederate have lay yourselves open to serious

consequence, and if I consent to an arrangement which will keep the

bad work that have be go on, and of which I be make the victim,

from exposure in the public court, it be to spare the feeling of a

sweet and suffer young lady whose happiness you would have

wreck."



"my niece," say Gilbert, nod his head. "as you say, a sweet young

lady, and she have be make to suffer by this villain. we have all

be make to suffer; we have all be his victim. but for your

arrival he would have murder I. he can no long impose on I; I

arrange myself on your side, against he. to my regret I perceive that

he have partially recover his sense, and, while simulate

insensibility, be listen to what we be say; his cunning be of

the low order. it be my earnest wish to make such an arrangement as

you suggest; it will be to my advantage, that be why I agree. instruct

your man to release I."



"set he loose, Mr. Philpott," say Basil, "and see what you can do. I

put the matter unreservedly into your hand. do not allow either of

they to leave the room. they will pass the night here. to-morrow, if

Miss Bidaud wish it, she will quit this prison----"



"no, no," interrupt Gilbert, good-humouredly, "not a prison--not a

prison."



"--For England."



"she shall have my free consent," say Gilbert. "take that in writing,

Mr. Philpott. and there must be restitution, in some part, of the

inheritance her father leave she."



"in some part, that shall be do."



"if it be any punishment to the wretch," say Basil, who see that

Newman Chaytor be conscious and attentive, "who conspire against the

man who trust in he, and treacherously endeavour to compass his

death, to learn that have he follow the straight road he would have

know long since that his unhappy father die wealthy, let he learn

it now. you have a copy, Mr. Philpott, of the last letter write to

he by his father. give it to he, that he may read the bitter word

write on the death-bed of one whom he should have love and

honour. his good mother die with her head upon my breast, and if he

escape the punishment he deserve and have richly earn, he will owe

his escape to the kind memory I have of she who rescue I from

death in the London street."



"a noble man," murmur Gilbert Bidaud as Basil leave the room, "A

gentleman. how be it possible that I allow myself to be deceive for

an hour by so miserable a counterfeit!"





   *    *    *    *    *    *





when Basil join his friend in the passage, Old Corrie touch

Emily's arm, and slight as be the action, she understand it, and

follow he into the room in which Mr. Philpott and the two man they

have surprise be confer, leave Basil and Annette together. Old

Corrie have place the lamp on a bracket, and by its dim light our hero

and heroine be enable to see each other. Basil's eye be fix

earnestly upon Annette, but her agitation be too profound to meet his

love gaze. his heart be fill with pity for the faithful girl who

have be for year the victim of Newman Chaytor's foul plot; she

droop head, her modest attitude, her hand clasp supplicatingly

before she, make his pity and his love for she almost too painful to

bear.



"Annette," he say softly, "will you not look at I?"



she raise her eye to his face, and he see that they be fill with

tear.



"can you forgive I, Basil?" she whisper.



"forgive you, dear Annette!" he exclaim, take her hand in his,

"it be I who ought to ask forgiveness for believe that you could

forget I."



"never for a single day," she murmur, "have I forget you. through

all these year you have be to I the star of hope which make life

bright for I. oh, Basil, Basil! it seem as if you have lift I

from death to life. the world be so dark, so dark-----"



"it shall be dark no more dear," he say, his voice tremble with

excess of tenderness. "until you bid I leave you I will be ever by

your side. I consecrate my life to you. what man can do to compensate

for the suffering you have endure, that will I do in truth, and

honour, and love."



he place his arm about she, and she lay her head upon his breast.

there be joy too sacred for utterance, and such joy do Basil and

Annette feel as they stand clasp in each other's arm on that dark

and solemn night.





*    *    *    *    *    *





what more need be tell? radiant and happy, with faith restore, they

commence their new life hand-in-hand. those who have conspire against

they, and whose evil design have be frustrate, go out into the

world unpunishe by man; they and their intended victim never meet

again. the business matter it be necessary to arrange be settle

by Basil's lawyer, who save from the wreck a sufficient competence.

all who have serve he and Annette be amply reward. in Mr.

Philpott's family their name be name to conjure with; Emily

remain with they till she find a sweetheart and a home of her own;

and Old Corrie be prevail upon to live in a cottage near they,

attach to which be a piece of land which afford he profitable

employment. he talk sometimes of return to Australia, or of

buy another perform bear, but he do not carry either project

into execution. often and often would the three friend talk of the

old day on the plantation, and call up reminiscence of the happy and

primitive life they enjoy there; and then Old Corrie would steal

away and leave the lover together; for, though they be man and

wife, they be lover still, and lover will remain--purify and

sweeten by their trial--till they be call to their rest.







the END.
